1.1.3.3.4.4.5.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.3.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.3. Con: There is a way and it is called a free will. Free will is not a valid argument against the classical problem of theodicy, since nobody will wish an evil to himself. But in dharmic religions there is a concept of karma, which means that reactions to our actions are delayed. The purpose is to educate us about what is good and evil, by a personal experience of what is favorable and what is not. Karma explains why is there evil alongside with loving God.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.3.1. Con: There are many forms of suffering that cannot be attributed to karma. Terrible things happen to even the most innocent and good people.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.3.2. Con: If the purpose of suffering is to educate us then an omnipotent God could have just created us with that education in the first place, thereby avoiding the need for anyone to suffer.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.3.3. Con: Dharmic religions also have the concept of "Brahman"... the ultimate reality in the universe. According to Chandogya Upanishad 3.14.1, "This whole universe is Brahman. In tranquility, let one worship It, as Tajjalan \(that from which he came forth, as that into which he will be dissolved, as that in which he breathes\)."

If all is Brahman, evil and good are essentially identical. Karma cannot explain how to differentiate between two identical things.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.3.4. Con: According to the doctrine of Karma, all evil that happens to us is brought upon us by our own past evils.

This cannot describe how the first evil was done to us, before we had performed an evil deed. It requires an infinite regress of past lives, or a non-karmic evil to start the chain of karma, which denies the premise that all evil is the result of past evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.3.5. Con: Free will does not justify the ability to execute the will to cause harm, just like free will does not grant the ability to fly \(unaided\) to those who want to fly.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.4. Con: There is no reason they are mutually exclusive.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.4.1. Con: A good and able God would both want and be able to stop evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5. Con: The concept of evil could not exist without a moral standard. The existence of God creates a moral standard to define good and evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1. Con: The mere existence of God doesn't create a moral standard of good and evil. It would be God's words and actions that would define good and evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1. Con: It's possible that, through His nature, God embodies, rather than commands, what is right. In this view, God and morality are the same thing.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1. Pro: If moral judgments have objective truth, there must exist an objective standard or anchor point to justify them. Such a morally perfect, objective, rational, and transcendent anchor would be considered God.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.1. Pro: The discipline of ethics shows that morality is considered a rational pursuit, that is that it requires reason to reach certain ethical conclusions. As such, any embodiment of the moral standard must also be rational.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Morality does not require embodiment, it just requires minds capable of reason to idealize those moral principles - thus it requires humans, not God.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: Humans often idealize behaviors, situations and objects and those idealizations don't need to \(and perhaps never actually do\) correspond to reality.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.1.1.2. Pro: The idea of a line does not correspond to anything real, it is an idealization based of real characteristics which are not a \[perfect\] embodiment of the concept of line.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.1.1.2.1. Con: An abstract line doesn't measure anything. It has no dimension of width or height. It's length is infinite, so it is rather useless as a standard of measurement.

A meter stick on the other hand is useful precisely because it idealizes a standard for measurement.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.1.1.3. Con: Morality is not a list of rules, but a standard of behavior. Any standard is something by which another thing is measured, and must idealize the quality which it measures. Therefore, the moral standard must idealize the quality of behavior, which implies a person.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.1.1.3.1. Pro: You cannot measure the length of a line using color; rather, the standard used must have length. "24 blue" is meaningless with regard to the length of a line. "24 inches" is meaningful because the standard idealizes the quality of length.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.1.1.3.2. Pro: A standard is not subject to being measured in terms of the quality that it idealizes. In other words, it is illogical to say a meter is not a meter. It must be a meter by definiton.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2. Con: Rationality itself is a sufficient source.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1. Con: Without values, rationality only deals with what is, whereas morality is concerned with what ought to be/what we ought to do. [It isn't possible to derive "ought"s solely from "is"s](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Is–ought_problem).
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.1. Con: Economics, as an example, derives what ought to be from what is valuable, and possible.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.1.1. Con: Economics relies on a presupposition of value \(i.e. that property is valuable\). The field itself is concerned only with how best to obtain what is already considered to be valuable, but doesn't compel anyone to give personal value to property.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.1.1.1. Pro: Some people - such as hermits and communists - don't consider property valuable nearly as much as others.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con: All people consider property valuable, even hermits, and communists. Those groups just treat possession differently.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.1.1.2. Con: Economics only requires that each individual actor values something, not necessarily that they want the same things, or value them the same.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.1.1.2.1. Con: This is still a presupposition of value, so economics fails to produce "ought"s with no presupposed "ought"s.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.1.1.2.1.1. Con: Value to the individual is objective, they have a need, and the thing they want can sait it, so this is an is, not an ought.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.2. Con: [Natural law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_law), is a body of philosophical thought that derives ought \(at least partially\) from is.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.2.1. Con: Secular conceptions of natural law still rely on axiomatic "ought"s - for instance, that one ought not infringe upon another's rights.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.2.2. Con: All ethics is concerned partially with what is, using "is"s to supplement "ought"s and create more specific or complex "ought"s. However, no ethical system can successfully reach "ought"s without at least one axiomatic "ought" as its basis.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.2.3. Con: Natural law is a system that has done great harm to the world, by hindering the rights of women, trans individuals, and homosexuals, repeatedly come up with false conclusions, like that women should have no autonomy, and homosexuals are evil, and that is based on a false interpretation of reality, that things were created with a purpose instead of evolving to adapt to the world. It is by every measure a failed moral system. As such, it has not derived real 'oughts'.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3. Con: Morality was observed before it was studied, those observations are the 'is's upon which we built our systems.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.1. Con: In order for morality to be observed it must first exist. If morality exists, then the moral arguments hold true.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.1.1. Con: The existence of morality is only one of the assumptions needed for the moral arguments to prove the existence of God.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.2. Con: Morality being built upon prior observations of morality is circular - it attempts to ground morality in itself.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.2.1. Con: It was seen, then it was studied, once we saw it, we moved to figure out why it was, and how it worked, the observations are the starting point of moral studies, but not the bases of it.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.3. Con: Observations of what humans think is moral do not indicate that those humans are correct. The fact that I think it is good to exercise does not prove that it is good to exercise.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.3.1. Con: If morality was not observable, we would not know that it exists.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.3.2. Con: That morality exists, and that morality should be followed are two different statements.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.3.2.1. Con: Definitionally, morality is the means to determine what one should do. The statement "one should follow morality" is equivalent to "one should do what one should do," which is tautologically true.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.3.2.1.1. Con: [morality: a doctrine or system of moral conduct](https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/morality)
[moral: sanctioned by or operative on one's conscience or ethical judgment](https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/moral)
neither of these definitions disallow someone from acting upon another measure of what they should do, like their
[self interest](https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/self-interest), or says that doing one is preferable to the other.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.4. Con: If morality is based solely upon an observation of what humans think is moral, then moral progress would be impossible, because it would never be possible to be more moral than what is currently thought to be moral.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.3.4.1. Con: We started with what we saw of morality, and built upon it, just as we did with science, learning it's in's and outs, and applying them to the world around us.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.4. Con: we have to use rationality to determine what values deserve to be valued.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.5. Con: Selection \(via survival\) favors a kind of morality that indicates as prescriptive certain norms and values: a moral framework which exists independently of any deities and independently of whether survival is right or not outside of said framework.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3. Con: If God is morality, then God can not choose what they do, as they must always do what is moral, and thus lacks libertarian free will, and is merely a force of the universe. As such, God would not be omnipotent, as they could not choose.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1. Pro: Since God under this definition would be just a force, we could manipulate, and control them, as we do other forces, which would ultimately make us more powerful then God. If we can be more powerful then God, God can not be Omnipotent.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.1. Con: We don't manipulate or control other forces. For instance, we can only predict and work around gravity and electromagnetism, not change them - this kind of utilization certainly doesn't imply that we are more powerful than these forces.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.1.1. Con: We can defy gravity, or harness it for our own ends, gravity in turn can only gravitate. We are thus the stronger.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.2. Con: Not all things that lack libertarian free will are "just a force".
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.2.1. Pro: Even if He lacked libertarian free will, God could still think and act \(even if those thoughts and actions are determined\), making Him a conscious agent and thus greater than a force.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.2.1.1. Con: If those thoughts and actions are all predetermined, then God would still be nothing more then an animal, or a machine.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.2.2. Con: Not all things that lack libertarian free will are forces, some are objects, animals, or machines, but as morality is an ever-present entity that acts upon us, it, and thus, under this definition, God, must be a force.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.3. Con: In order to utilize God in a way similar to electromagnetism He would have to be completely predictable in His actions. Lacking libertarian free will doesn't mean that something is completely predictable.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.3.1. Pro: Basic quantum events are fundamentally unpredictable, but don't have "free will" in any meaningful sense.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.3.2. Pro: Since God is outside the universe and, at least to some extent, unknowable, it would be impossible to predict His actions even if He was unfree.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.3.3. Con: God in this instance is definite by the rules of an absolute morality, which is by it's nature perfectly predictable if you know the rules.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.3.3.1. Pro: It is do in no small part to morality that out civilization has grown so expansive, so if God is morality, then we are already using God to our ends.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.1.3.4. Con: A force doesn't have to be perfectly understood to be utilized, we were using compasses many years before we discovered electromagnetism.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2. Con: According to [compatibilism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compatibilism), an individual will freely choose according to his nature. Therefore, given the same situation and the same individual, the same free choice will be made in all cases.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.1. Con: Compatibility would argue that a rock has free will, as it is not put under distress to make a choice, and that clocks choose what time they tell. Rocks do not have free will, and clocks do not make choices.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.1.1. Con: Free will is a quality only found in minds.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.1.1.1. Con: By either definition of free will, it is conceivable that it would cover true artificial intelligence if such a thing would be developed, as an AI is not a mind in the traditional sense, a mind is not a necessary condition of free will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.1.1.1.1. Con: If such AI should be developed with all of a mind's faculties and fully independent it could be argued that it has a mind in the "traditional" sense.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con: It could be argued that an AI is just an over-complicated clock. Where the mind necessary for soft deterministic  free will begins is still a valid question.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.1.1.1.2. Con: No such AI has been developed yet. Until then, it is not safe to affirm that AI's can have a mind.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.1.1.1.2.1. Con: We can conceptualize such an AI, which is sufficient to judge whether or not they would be able to have free will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.1.1.1.2.1.1. Con: If it is conceivable, you should be able to conceive of it.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.1.1.2. Pro: In order to be able to make a choice, one must have certain qualities \(such as consciousness, perception, thinking, judgement\) essential to a mind.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.2. Con: Compatibilism exists as a response to incompatibility, and answers the dilemma posed to them by changing the definitions of choice and free will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.3. Con: As Ted Honderich explained, the mistake of the compatibilistic definition of free will is to assert that nothing changes as a consequence of determinism, when clearly we have lost the life-hope of origination.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4. Pro: Harry Frankfurt has shown that [alternate possible choices are not required for free will](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frankfurt_cases).
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4.1. Pro: Consider additionally a rock climber high up a cliff face whose harness breaks and whose footholds crumble. The climber is left hanging on by one hand, and with only one other handhold within reach - this limits the climber to either grabbing the other handhold, or plummeting to her death. She will definitely not choose to die, so she only has one possible choice - however, we don't say that she doesn't in that moment have free will. Therefore, many choices are not necessary for free will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4.1.1. Con: A rock climber with a broken harness at risk of falling to her death may appear to have no option but to climb up, but she has at least 3, let go, do nothing, or climb up, and internally, a myriad or options to choose as a motive for her choice. One of the 3 courses of action is clearly more preferential, at least from the information we have, but her choice is not certain, and her motives are not locked, what motive she chooses now will probably effect her in the future.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4.1.1.1. Pro: Say, for example, she thinks of the face of one of her loved ones to find the strength to pull herself to safety,it is quite likely that after she recovers from this event, she will seek out that person, and bond with them as a result, of this occurrence, had she picked a different loved one, she would have instead been more likely to bond with the other loved one.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4.1.1.2. Pro: Consider she thought of some goal in her life she wanted to accomplish, and used that to will herself to safety, she would probably, as a result, be more committed to that goal.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4.1.1.3. Pro: Say she thought of how her passing would effect others, under this scenario, she would be likely to be more careful in the future, perhaps to a fault.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4.1.1.4. Pro: Consider the scenario where she personified the cliff, and saw this as the cliff challenging her, defying her to conquer it, if she took this as a motive, she would likely feel more accomplished for her triumph, and push to tackle greater challenges in the future.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4.2. Con: The Harry Frankfurt argument does not move to show that alternate possible choices are not required for free will, but instead argues that responsibility is not dependent upon choice. ["The best definition, by his reckoning, is this: "\[A\] person is not morally responsible for what he has done if he did it only because he could not have done otherwise.""](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frankfurt_cases)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.1.2.1.1.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.6. Pro: [Free will](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_will) is the ability to choose between different possible courses of action unimpeded.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.6.1. Pro: The definition of free will doesn't include the need for different outcomes for the same individual in the same situation.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.6.2. Con: This view of  soft determinists, as coined by William James is nothing but an effort to create a quagmire of evasion by stealing the name freedom to mask it's underlying determinism. As Immanuel Kant pointed out, it is nothing more then wretched subterfuge, and jugglery. It is nothing more then attempting to treat the disease by masking, not treating, masking, the symptom.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.6.2.1. Con: Determinism isn't "underlying" in compatibilism, it is both admited and thought to be compatible with free will the way it has been defined.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.6.2.1.1. Con: The way it has been defined by compatablists, which is the way nobody else defines it. Pop-Culture is littered with examples of people exploring the concept of libertarian free will, or as they call it, free will, and how it conflicts with determinism, but nobody but compatablists uses the definition compatablitisest use.`
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3. Con: If God's nature is identical to morality, then God is not compelled by anything, since morality is simply defined as "what God would do." Under this definition, the statement is "God must always do what God would do," which isn't a compulsion, only a tautology.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.1. Con: If whatever God does is arbitrary defined as good, then morality is arbitrary, and no more valid then what any random individual does as the bases of what should be done.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2. Pro: An inability to contradict His own nature does not count against God's omnipotence.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.2.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2. Pro: Freedom to defy one's nature is logically impossible, because an agent's decisions are necessarily the result of its nature. An agent couldn't decide to defy their nature, because that decision would itself be following its nature.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2.1. Con: All observed creatures with free will \(humans\) have dual natures, which at least in part contradict each other, and can not universally be followed in all instances. For example, one's economic self interest, against moral behavior.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2.1.1. Con: The conflict between desire for wealth and desire to behave morally is an example of contradicting desires, not contradicting natures. One's nature is what allows one to choose between conflicting desires.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2.1.1.1. Con: The same individual in the same situation may very well make different choices, all factors equal.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2.1.1.1.1. Con: Different choices being made in the same scenario doesn't demonstrate that a free agent contradicting their own nature is possible.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2.1.1.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2.1.1.2. Pro: A person's nature isn't any of their desires, in the same way that a sheet of piano music isn't any of the keys on the piano. Instead, the nature is how someone chooses between their desires - and without it, no such choice could be made.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2.1.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.1.2.1.1.1.1.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2.2. Pro: According to [Arthur Schopenhauer](https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer), "Man can do what he wills but he cannot will what he wills."
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2.2.1. Con: People do consciously change their nature, alcoholics work hard to recover from their addiction, and so can racists, it's not easy, but it can be done.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.2.2.1.1. Con: People can consciously change their thoughts and desires, but they cannot change their nature consciously. Their nature is what allows them to make the decision to change their thoughts/desires.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.3. Con: God under this definition loses not only the ability to defy his nature, but the ability to determine it.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.3.1. Con: In order to change one's nature, one would have to defy one's nature first. The change would, by definition, oppose the agent's nature.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.3.1.1. Con: If changing one's nature is defying it, then libertarian free will exists, as being able to defy one's nature must be possible, as people do change their nature as they grow.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.3.1.1.1. Con: People's nature may change, but this change is not due to a conscious decision to change their nature.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.3.1.1.1.1. Con: A change must be internal, or external. The change here is clearly not external, so it must be internal. Since the change is internal, the nature changed itself. It has previously been assumed that a nature could not change itself, and that to do so it would have to defy itself. Since the nature would, under these assumptions, defy itself, free will must exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.3.2. Con: God's nature is determined by necessity. He must be the way He is, otherwise He would not be God, leaving no room for modification.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.3.2.1. Con: If God's nature is determined by necessity, an act that does not allow them to be omnipotent, then God, as defined in this discussion, can not exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.3.2.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.2.1.2.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.3.3. Con: -> See 1.1.3.1.2.1.1.1.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4. Pro: Free will only implies the freedom for one to act based on one's nature, not the freedom to defy one's nature. If perfect morality is a part of God's nature, it isn't necessary or expected that He be able to defy it.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.1. Con: Freedom, the legal interpretation of free will, has long been referred to as the ability to make one's own mistakes.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.1.1. Con: The legal interpretation of free will is irrelevant.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.1.1.1. Con: the legal definition is tied to the actual definition, what one says tells us a lot about what the other says.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.2. Con: Following commands is not free will, one's nature is a form of command, therefore, if one is only capable of following their nature, they do not have free will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.2.1. Con: Free will is only contradicted when one is constrained by commands external to oneself. One's nature is internal to oneself, and so following it doesn't contradict free will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.2.1.1. Pro: My brain commands my fingers to move to type this sentence. I wouldn't be more free if my fingers didn't follow that command - in fact, I'd be less free, since I wouldn't be able to act upon my will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.2.1.1.1. Con: If one's finger acted independent of oneself, they would be less free, but their finger would be more free, the part is not the whole, and the whole is not the part.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.2.1.2. Con: A computer holds many commands within itself, and acts upon the majority of them without external stimulation of any kind, but a computer does not have free will. As such, even internal commands are not sufficient to define free will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.3. Con: People have the ability to do things they were not instructed to do, or even instructed not to do, and that are against their best interest, not due to a mistake, not even only for a greater good, but simply because they can, an action that would require them to have libertarian free will. When we read the story of Pandora's box, or Eve, and the Apple, we don't blink an eye, we empathise with the characters, because it's not outlandish, it is the natural result of a creature with free will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.3.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.1.7.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.3.2. Con: The text of Genesis doesn't claim that the tree of knowledge was intended as a test of free will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.3.2.1. Con: The "tree as test" interpretation of the story, along with a similar one of Pandora's box, is somewhat popular.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.3.3. Con: One example of free will making mistakes, doesn't show that making mistakes is a necessary requirement for free will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.3.3.1. Con: Everyone makes mistakes, so even with only one example, the evidence is in favor of the necessity.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.4.6. Pro: If we ignore the first sentence, then I agree that it is not valid to expect from one aspect of God's perfection to deny other perfection. That is a contradiction in logic, not in God.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.2.1.2.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.6. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.1.2.1.1.1.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.3.2.7. Con: To contradict one's nature is an ability. The lack of such an ability is a limit to God's power and therefore 'counts against' His omnipotence.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4. Pro: In order for morality to be objective, its source must also be objective and unchanging. The universe and all of its contents are contingent and therefore not objective. So it follows that the moral law-giver must be transcendent and necessary.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1. Pro: The nonexistence of the universe is entirely possible and has, in fact, occurred.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1. Pro: The Universe \(i.e. our universe or whatever multiverse of which it may be part\) began to exist \("began to exist" defined [here](https://www.reasonablefaith.org/writings/scholarly-writings/the-existence-of-god/must-the-beginning-of-the-universe-have-a-personal-cause-a-rejoinder/) at definition A5\)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.1. Pro: A universe like ours that did not begin to exist is philosophically incoherent because it implies the formation of a concrete actual infinity by successive addition of events in time
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.1.1. Con: According to the [b-theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B-theory_of_time) of time, all time exists at once and therefore there is no need to traverse it.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: -> See discussion #17746: A-theory or B-theory of time?
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.1.2. Pro: It is impossible to traverse an infinite number of past events of finite size. Consider:

A. If one started today to count an infinite number of days, they will never reach the end of that sequence. Every day counted would leave them an infinite number of days away.

B. In an infinitely old universe, a day must exist that is an infinite number of days prior to now. 

These two statements are mutually-exclusive and cannot both be true, yet both must be true in an infinitely old universe.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2. Pro: A universe like ours that did not begin to exist is philosophically incoherent because it implies the concrete existence of an actual infinity
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.1. Con: Actual infinities are near-unanimously accepted as a legitimate concept of mathematics leaving no room to doubt the possibility that it could be instantiated in the real world
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.1.1. Con: Near-unanimous acceptance as a mathematical concept is irrelevant.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.1.1.1. Pro: Strict logical or mathematical coherence is not identical to metaphysical possibility, which is restricted by additional intuitions, conceivability considerations, or various other things depending on your epistemology of modality
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.1.2. Con: There is not a consensus in mathematics. Intuitionism, constructivism, and anti-platonism all do not consider concrete actual infinities to be possible for one reason or another
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2. Con: Actual infinities exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.1. Pro: Continuum - If space is continuous, there are an infinite number of infinitesimal points in any space interval. If time is continuous, there are an infinite number of instants in any time interval. \(see [plato.stanford.edu](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/continuity/) for a scholarly summary and or [www.quora.com](https://www.quora.com/Is-a-line-made-up-of-infinite-points) for a popular summary\)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.1.1. Con: We can treat a line as logically proceeding its division into parts and points such that points only exist after we specify them and therefore will always be finite.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.1.2. Con: This assumes a controversial view of space-time structure when space and time might be quantized \(see [www.scientificamerican.com](https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/is-time-quantized-in-othe/) for a popular summary\)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.1.3. Con: Though worded to describe physical things, these are examples of numerical infinites, not actual infinites.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.2. Pro: Spacetime - the four-dimensional universe might be infinite in its 4D volume given that science suggests the universe will expand indefinitely into the future
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.2.1. Con: Scientific evidence can never justify the future being endless. All theories will fail to be predictive beyond some finite time in the future. Said another way, it will always be consistent with empirical evidence that the universe will come to an end given enough time
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.2.2. Con: This assumes a [B-theory of time](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/time/#TheBThe) and would be undermined on an A-Theory of time
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.2.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.3. Con: An actual infinity could never be observed to exist in principle \(i.e. our experience can only be of finite things\), leaving only the usefulness of infinity in some theory to be potential evidence that one exists
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3. Con: Actual infinities are entailed by theism.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.1. Pro: God’s omniscience involves an actually infinite number of thoughts in God’s mind or the grasping of an infinite number of propositions
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.1.1. Con: God's thoughts are a unified whole and not something consisting of parts
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.1.2. Con: It is not apparent than there are an actually infinite number of true thoughts.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.1.2.1. Con: The proposition "1 is less than 2" is a true and is a thought that one might have. The same goes for "2 is less than 3" and so on. If each of these is considered a single thought in the mind of God, than God has an actually infinite number of true thoughts given that the integers form an abstract actual infinity and God hold to all these thoughts.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.1.3. Con: It is not obvious how one would isolate one single thought so that it might be counted.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.1.3.1. Con: As a general claim for all sentient creatures this is false. The notion that humans can "focus" or "concentrate" implies that they have distinguishable thoughts that can be more or less paid attention to. Or consider how counting to 10 involves at least 10 separate thoughts entering one's mind. While an exact line cannot be drawn between thoughts, that doesn't imply the thoughts are not distinguishable enough to be numerable.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.1.4. Con: A thought is an immaterial entity; immaterial entities are not subject to the physical bounds of time and space which constrain actual infinities of physical entities.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.2. Pro: Heaven is defined to be eternal and thus time in heaven must go on infinitely into the future. Nothing distinguishes such an infinite future from an infinite past in a relevant way. Therefore, accepting the possibility of the former implies one accepts the possibility of the latter.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.2.1. Con: It is incorrect that an infinite past is the same as an infinite future. Past infinity is an absolute infinity; future infinity is only a potential infinity.

If the past is infinite, then an actually-infinite number of seconds has already elapsed. But if the past is finite and the future is infinite, then the total number of seconds elapsed will always be a finite \(albeit very large\) number.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.2.1.1. Con: This must assume a controversial [growing block view](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Growing_block_universe) of spacetime \(i.e. an infinite past is a static actually-infinite block while the future doesn't exist\). On presentism and standard B-theory views of spacetime, the future and past are metaphysically identical, equally non-existent or equally real respectively. For these more mainstream views, the use of tensed language \(e.g. "has already elapsed"\) is not metaphysically meaningful. \(see [Wes Morrison paper](https://pdfs.semanticscholar.org/d08b/f8e00b0cf4a9f61febf951cbf44778e074ee.pdf), [Bill Craig Rebuttal](https://www.reasonablefaith.org/writings/scholarly-writings/the-existence-of-god/taking-tense-seriously-in-differentiating-past-and-future-a-response-to-wes/), [Wes Rebuttal](http://spot.colorado.edu/~morristo/reply2craig-forthcoming.pdf)\)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.3. Pro: If infinities do not exist then God cannot be omnibenevolent nor omnipotent.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.3.3.1. Con: This commits a category error. Omnipotence and omnibenevolence are qualitative properties; an infinite regress of events is a quantitative property.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4. Pro: The possibility of an actual infinity leads to absurdities as [various thought experiments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Category:Paradoxes_of_infinity) can illuminate for any sincere inquirer \(e.g. Hilbert’s Hotel, Infinite Library, Benardete’s Big Read, various supertasks\)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.1. Con: A thought experiment is not a stand-alone argument. An informative articulation of the source of the absurdity must occur \(e.g. formulate into a deductive argument\) instead of simply relying on vague gut reactions to a story
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.1.1. Con: These thought experiments are often paired with such articulations of the source. For example, one thing illustrated by the Hilbert's Hotel paradox is the following deductive argument: \(1\) If an actual infinity is possible, then set A being a proper subset of set B does not imply A is smaller than B; \(2\) A being a proper subset of B does imply A is smaller than B; \(3\) Therefore an actual infinity is not possible
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.1.2. Pro: Paradoxical ideas about actual infinities stem from their counter-intuitive nature and not from any actual/physical impossibility.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.2. Con: The examples, if they cannot be resolved, only exclude certain kinds of actual infinities and cannot be generalized to the type of infinity that an eternal past is or the types of infinity entailed by an eternal past, if it entails any other infinities at all.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.3. Con: [Thompson's Lamp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomson%27s_lamp) has a solution: the lamp is on or off randomly \(with a probability of 50%\) after being on and off at the same time \(via being turned on and off infinitely fast - ie. simultaneously\). Thus this paradox is one of either lack of information or erasure of information, and can have a logical solution while assuming the existence of infinities.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.4. Con: The [Ross–Littlewood](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ross%E2%80%93Littlewood_paradox) paradox has various solutions depending on the exact variant, but it has perfectly consistent solutions \(one for each variant or the problem\) when we turn to physics. It is therefore not a problem with infinities.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.4.1. Pro: After adding infinite objects  we will have infinite objects. After adding 2 objects and subtracting one over and over for an infinite time, we have infinite objects. After adding n objects, if we add and subtract an equal number of objects we end up with n objects. Thus all conclusions are self consistent and the paradox resides in equivocating different physical actions under the same abstract concept, which leads to the erring assertion that their outcomes should be the same.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.5. Con: The [Grand Hotel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert%27s_paradox_of_the_Grand_Hotel) "paradox" is provably true, therefore not illogical \(and therefore only paradoxical in the sense of being counter-intuitive\).
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.6. Con: [Galileo's paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galileo%27s_paradox) poses no issue with infinities, it just shows that they are unquantifiable \(which is perfectly in line with the meaning of infinite\). It also shows that some infinities are faster moving/growing than others \(ie. someone who runs forever will always be ahead of someone who walks forever\), which is compatible with our experience and with the definition of infinity.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.7. Con: [Zeno's paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zeno%27s_paradoxes) has been demonstrated fallacious \(and ends up not being paradoxical at all\) - as the sum of the infinitesimal quantities add up to a finite quantity disproving it's solution and achieving a solution which is perfectly compatible with our every day experience.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.7.1. Con: Zeno’s paradox shows that there really isn’t an infinite number of spaces between two points.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.4.7.2. Pro: Zeno assumes an infinite number of tasks cannot be completed in a finite amount of time. This assumption is false \([supertasks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supertask#Zeno)\).
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.5. Pro: An actual infinity is metaphysically absurd because its mathematical validity requires accepting the non-obvious [axiom of infinity](https://www.encyclopediaofmath.org/index.php/Infinity,_axiom_of) and rejecting the mundane notion of [proper subsets](https://mathinsight.org/definition/proper_subset) having a smaller [cardinality](https://brilliant.org/wiki/cardinality/)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.6. Con: The Kalam argument presumes at least the causation principle \(in the same way science does\), which allows for infinite regress, even though this thought be abhorred.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.6.1. Pro: Democritus artificially introduced the atom to stop the abhorred infinite regress into ever smaller sizes, but atoms can be and have been split, showing at least this infinite regress to be possible.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3. Pro: [Big Bang theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Bang), the most widely accepted cosmological model, holds that the Universe began to exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.1. Con: Even if the age of the universe is finite \(which we do not know\), that would not suggest or support in any way the existence of a god.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.2. Con: The argument uses misleading language, as 'began to exist' does not mean what one would think it does.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.3. Pro: The [Borde-Guth-Vilenkin theorem](https://arxiv.org/PS_cache/gr-qc/pdf/0110/0110012v2.pdf) shows that any system which is, on average, expanding throughout its lifetime, must have had a temporal beginning in its past. This applies regardless of the system's properties.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.3.1. Con: The class of cosmologies satisfying [Borde-Guth-Vilenkin](https://arxiv.org/PS_cache/gr-qc/pdf/0110/0110012v2.pdf) are not limited to inflating universes. Of particular interest is
the recycling scenario  in which each comoving region
goes through a succession of inflationary and thermalized
epochs.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.3.1.1. Con: In some versions of the cyclic model the brane spacetimes
are everywhere expanding, so [this theorem](https://arxiv.org/PS_cache/gr-qc/pdf/0110/0110012v2.pdf) immediately
implies the existence of a past boundary at which boundary conditions must be imposed.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.3.1.2. Con: In other versions, there are brief periods of contraction, but the net result of each cycle is an expansion. For null geodesics each cycle is identical to the others, except for the overall normalization of the affine parameter. Thus, as long as Hav \> 0 for a null geodesic when averaged over one cycle, then Hav \> 0 for any number of cycles, and [this theorem](https://arxiv.org/PS_cache/gr-qc/pdf/0110/0110012v2.pdf) would imply that the geodesic is incomplete.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.3.1.3. Con: When accounting for quantum phenomena the universe is predicted to have had [no beginning](https://phys.org/news/2015-02-big-quantum-equation-universe.html).
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.3.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.3.1.3.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.3.3. Con: The Universe is not necessarily expanding as once thought. There are other potential explanations for the red-shift in light that we observe from distant bodies.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.3.3.1. Pro: The Earth is moving spirally at a high velocity. The solar orbital velocity, 66,000 mph, must be added or subtracted to/from the galactic orbital velocity, 483,000 MPH, and the galaxy itself is moving at 1.3 Million mph. Our relative velocity cycles from 751 kmph - 1,849 kmph. [astrosociety.org](https://astrosociety.org/edu/publications/tnl/71/howfast.html)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.4. Pro: If our universe was infinitely old it would have undergone heat death long ago, and it hasn't.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.4.1. Con: Our universe would not be heat dead if it didn't have activity for an infinitely long period of time \(ie. by being quantum mechanically infinitesimally probable to suffer any change during it's infinitely long initial stage\).
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.4.1.1. Con: "Infinitely long" implies that time was existent during this time, which is philosophically impossible.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.4.1.2. Con: This ignores [Brownian motion](https://www.britannica.com/science/Brownian-motion). If the assertion is that even Brownian motion did not happen, then it appears that the universe was already in heat death.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.4.2. Con: Our universe would not be heat dead if it undergoes a cycle that restores it to a state of low entropy.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.4.2.1. Con: Without an external source of energy, such a cycle would lose energy after every iteration, like a basketball losing energy after every bounce unless it is being dribbled.

After a long, but finite number of cycles, the universe would indeed suffer heat death. In an infinitely old universe, this would have happened an infinitely long time ago.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.5. Con: Cosmologists propose a beginning for the current timeline of the universe, not for the universe as a whole.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.5.1. Pro: The current timeline of the universe is defined as events which occurred since the rapid expansion. It is not known if the universe as a whole extend beyond this point. The current timeline is the universe as we know it today, whereas the universe as a whole may possibly extend past that point.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.5.1.1. Con: The "compact universe" before its expansion still needs a cause for its existance.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.5.1.2. Con: The universe's expansion requires a cause. Otherwise it would have stayed in its state.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.5.2. Con: It is unclear that there is a meaningful distinction between "current timeline of the universe" and "the universe as a whole."
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.5.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.5.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6. Con: There is at least one cosmological model that does not hold that the universe began to exist and is better supported by current evidence.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.1. Pro: [Steady state models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steady_state_model) do not hold that the universe began to exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.2. Pro: [Oscillating models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyclic_model) do not hold that the universe began to exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.3. Pro: [Vacuum Fluctuation models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Tryon#Is_the_universe_a_vacuum_fluctuation?) do not hold that the universe began to exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.4. Pro: [Chaotic Inflationary models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eternal_inflation) do not hold that the universe began to exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.5. Pro: [Quantum Gravity models](https://arxiv.org/abs/0909.2566) do not hold that the universe began to exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.3.1.3.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.6. Pro: [String cosmology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/String_cosmology) does not hold that the universe began to exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.7. Pro: [Ekpyrotic cosmologies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ekpyrotic_universe) do not hold that the universe began to exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.8. Pro: The [Gott-Li model \(Closed Timelike Curves\)](https://arxiv.org/abs/astro-ph/9712344) does not hold that the universe began to exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9. Con: Multiverse theories fail by every philosophical measure.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.1. Pro: Multiverse theories have low explanatory scope, since they don't explain any data other than the fine-tuning of the universe. In contrast, theism explains the existence of the universe and of objective morality, as well as many miracle claims and subjective experiences.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.1.1. Con: The multiverse theory can be treated as part of a larger philosophy of naturalism that also aims to explain all things theism attempts to explain. Then they would have the same explanatory scope.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.2. Pro: Multiverse theories are very unparsimonious, since they posit a bafflingly large number of entire universes that cannot be observed. Theism only proposes a single extra entity.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.2.1. Con: This assumes parsimony concerns limiting the number of entities as opposed to the number of types of entities. God would represent a significantly distinct class of entities not present in naturalism. Many people's intuitions may consider this less parsimonious than adding more universes like our own.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3. Pro: Multiverse theories are ad hoc, since the existence of causally disconnected universes isn't very plausibly supported outside of its use as an answer to fine-tuning. Theism is supported by other arguments, as well as by the subjective experiences of many.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.1. Con: Max Tegmark's [mathematical universe hypothesis](http://lesswrong.com/lw/1zt/the_mathematical_universe_the_map_that_is_the/) gives a philosophical framework for the nature of existence that implies a form of multiverse.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.1.1. Con: It's not clear that consciousness can be simulated by purely mathematical processes.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.1.1.1. Pro: [Quantum mechanics has shown](http://henry.pha.jhu.edu/aspect.html) that consciousness isn't describable by purely physical processes.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.1.1.2. Pro: The [Chinese room argument](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_room) holds that simulating consciousness within non-conscious systems is impossible.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.1.1.2.1. Con: The Chinese room argument is a hypothetical scenario, and merely purports to show how the illusion of consciousness may not be actual consciousness, not that creating, or even simulating consciousness is impossible
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.1.1.2.1.1. Con: By [Occam's Razor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occam%27s_razor) \(parsimony\) there would be no need for the additional entity of conscious experience to explain the behavior of the machine or Room.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.1.1.2.1.1.1. Con: There is no explanation for the behavior in the scenario, simple or otherwise.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.1.1.2.2. Con: The Chinese room thought experiment demonstrates an entity that is incapable of performing a task, speaking Chinese, can have components added that allow it to appear to perform this task. It extends this argument to say the same is true of computers that appear conscious. However, this ignores that the thing between the first entity, and the audience, is itself an entity. And this entity as a whole can perform this task, thus could be considered conscious.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.2. Con: Multiverse theories build on principles of cosmology developed and verified while explaining various phenomena within our universe. Therefore, extending these ideas to explain the phenomena of fine-tuning and the origin of our universe is motivated hypothesis building as opposed to pure ad hoc stipulation.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.3. Con: This assumes the existence of God is supported by other arguments \(see Cons for the head claim of this debate \(i.e. God exists\) as well as those underneath the Pros for the head claim\)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.4. Pro: See the Pros for the head claim of this debate \(i.e. God exists\)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4. Pro: Multiverse theories have low explanatory power: P1. A perfectly random system is more likely to produce simpler results than more complex ones P2. There are simpler possible universes than ours which contain conscious beings P3. We should expect to find ourselves in the most common situation that produces observers \(Copernican principle\) C. Under multiverse theories, our existence in this universe is unlikely
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1. Pro: By contrast to the multiverse theory, a conscious creator could have many valid reasons to create a more complex universe like our own.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1. Pro: Since God isn't limited in time, resources, or motivation, He would have no reason to prefer a simpler/smaller universe, and so simple created universes are no more likely than more complex ones.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1. Con: There is no reason God would make things unnecessarily complex, especially without taking full advantage of what has been created, of the two possibilities, simpler makes more sense.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.1. Con: It is possible that a simpler/smaller universe would result in fewer saved souls or overall less moral goodness in the world.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.1.1. Con: An omnipotent being could save all souls if it wanted to, so it does not make sense that adding more would decrease the number of souls saved.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Salvation requires free choice by the saved. It is definitionally impossible to force a creature to freely choose something, so God would not be able to do this.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.2.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.2. Con: an omniscient God that would be able to know that a bigger universe would mean less saved souls would be able to direct the universe in such a way that more souls were saved.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.2. Con: The complexity/size of the universe may have been intended to show the smallness and fragility of human life, encouraging humility.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.2.1. Con: A huge inhabited universe would do this better, as there would be more life, more civilizations to show just how insignificant the individual was.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.2.1.1. Con: Insignificance of the entire human race may be preferred, to encourage humans to seek fulfillment in a larger purpose, rather than individual insignificance which can be remedied by economic or governmental power.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.2.1.1.1. Con: More sentient life in the universe does not necessarily require more human life.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.2.1.1.2. Con: The bigger things are the harder they are for someone to control through personal power in any form.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.2.2. Con: There is no need for such a Universe to achieve that effect. Plenty of mundane, earthly experiences encourage humility much more easily than exploring the cosmos.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.3. Con: The complexity/size of the universe may have been intended specifically to add weight to the teleological argument and bring more people to belief.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.4. Con: The complexity/size of the universe results in great beauty, which a perfect creator would prefer.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.4.1. Con: A creator would prefer a beautiful, densely inhabited universe more.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.4.1.1. Con: There may be any number of reasons why an arbitrarily large number of inhabitants is not preferable to the creator.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.4.1.1.1. Con: Any reason why God would not value a large number of inhabitants works equally well for God not valuing any inhabitants.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.4.1.1.1.1. Con: "I want the inhabitants to have an arbitrary large and unchallenged space to develop during eons" is a reason that works for valuing a single source of inhabitants and not for zero inhabitants.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.2.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.3.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.4.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.2. Con: The universe looks complex to us because we are a part of it, and one can't accurately, and completely model something they are a part of
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.2.1. Con: This doesn't show that the universe is not complex or that simpler, consciousness-producing universes cannot exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.2.2. Con: One doesn't need a complete or fully accurate model of something in order to judge its complexity.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.2.2.1. Con: If our brains were so simple that we could understand them, we would be so simple that we could not understand them, because this is an example of something trying to model itself. Any model that we could make would need to necessarily be quite crude, because an accurate one would need to be all encompassing, and anything we want to model that can model itself needs to be able to do other things.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.2.2.1.1. Con: This statement isn't true in all possible worlds. In a universe with different laws, much simpler intelligent systems are conceivably possible.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.2.2.1.1.1. Con: Suppose a computer has a total of N possible states. Suppose there is something outside of the computer in the universe, so the universe has at least N+1 possible states. With zero overhead, each state of the computer can correspond to a state of the universe, but since the universe has more states than the computer, some states of the universe will map to the same state of the computer, in which case the simulation will not be able to distinguish between them.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.3. Con: Just because there are simpler "possible" universes than ours does not mean that the universe is all that complicated. The rules guiding this universe are relatively simple.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.4. Con: A simpler model that allows for sentient beings has not been presented.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.4.1. Con: A [Boltzmann Brain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boltzmann_brain) type universe allows for sentient beings and is simpler than the multiverse.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.5. Con: Multiverse models are not required to be completely random in the universes they generate. They can, in principle, have motivated constraints on the types of universes generated within the multiverse which would lead to universes like ours being among the most probable.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.5. Pro: Multiverse theories are unfalsifiable, since it is impossible by definition for a causally disconnected universe to interact with our own. Theism is at least possibly falsifiable, since God would be able to interact with the universe.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.5.1. Con: The multiverse theory [predicts](http://140.119.115.32/sa/pdf.file/en/e032/e032p066.pdf) that the vacuum energy is probably nearly as far from zero as possible for a universe capable of supporting sentient life, whereas a single finely-tuned universe ought to have it at exactly zero; this constitutes a testable prediction.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.5.1.1. Con: There is no reason to assume that a creator would be compelled to make the vacuum energy zero.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.5.2. Con: Most multiverse models allow for causal interaction between universes. At the very least there is interaction between our universe and the universe within/from which our universe arose. Depending on this process, various marks might be left on our universe to hint at what came before. No such evidence has been observed allowing us to falsify various multiverse models.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.10. Con: If there is a cosmological model of a universe with an infinite past, that is better supported by current evidence, it would replace the Big Bang as the mainstream theory.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.4. Con: [A5](https://www.reasonablefaith.org/writings/scholarly-writings/the-existence-of-god/must-the-beginning-of-the-universe-have-a-personal-cause-a-rejoinder/) is not the natural definition of 'began to exist'. \(A1 is the natural definition\). Definition A5 was deliberately created so that the Kalam cosmological argument could retain its [appealing-to-common-sense form](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kalam_cosmological_argument#Form_of_the_argument).
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.4.1. Con: A5 may be philosophically technical but it has to be in order to capture all the various usages of phrases like "begins to exist", "the creation of", or "came into existence" that appear in various popular sources discussing cosmology as well as our mundane usage in everyday life. One can find this phrase being used in a non-A1 sense by [cosmologists](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FgpvCxDL7q4) \(skip to 5:40\) or [generic science blogs](https://www.space.com/13347-big-bang-origins-universe-birth.html) with no interest in the Kalam argument
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.2. Con: According to the [b-theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B-theory_of_time) of time the universe has always existed.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2. Pro: No argument has been put forth for the impossibility of the universe's nonexistence. In the absence of such an argument, our default assumption should be that since the universe's nonexistence is conceivable it is logically possible, and therefore it is contingent.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.1. Con: The default position is "I don't know." Any other claim should not be the default position and requires its own support.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.2. Pro: The claim "x is logically possible" is equivalent to the claim "x contains no logical contradictions." Evidence cannot be given for a lack of logical contradictions, except by disproving any proposed contradictions.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.3. Pro: According to [Sankara's dictum](http://alexanderpruss.com/papers/Samkara.html), anything which is logically impossible should be imperceivable and inconceivable, even within our imaginations.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.3.1. Con: We can conceive of many things that are impossible. [Like an eternal staircase.](https://encrypted-tbn0.gstatic.com/images?q=tbn:ANd9GcTY6fwSBxH2BrGaaDTRHN_ugELsaBwpLpfuvea9rdwpODmVhhIL) Or [a shape with 3 prongs but only two bases, and no splits.](https://i.ytimg.com/vi/FfqcjGXTtKs/maxresdefault.jpg)
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.3.1.1. Con: Impossible-shape illusions can be drawn and seen as two-dimensional representations, but not perceived mentally as actual three-dimensional objects.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.3.1.1.1. Con: The reason illusions are  illusion is because our mind has rendered the objects, we are very good at filling in missing or incomplete information, what we see is not the 2D image that is actual represented at all, but rather a 3D object created in our mind.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.3.2. Con: We can imagine a world without logic where 1+1=3.
It would be chaotic and nonsensical to us, but we can imagine things that are nonsense.
The idea that we can only imagine things that are possible or logically possible or coherent is evidently false.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.3.3. Con: If this dictum were true no one would ever make a logical error. If we can't imagine things that are logically impossible we'd never make a logical error.
People are certainly capable of conceiving things and discovering later that they are logically impossible.
Indeed they still capable of recalling them and why they thought them possible even after discovering they are not.

Sankara's dictum is obviously false.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5. Con: Morality can't be transcendent, because different moral standards apply to different historical contexts.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1. Pro: Cultural relativism states that the morality of an action is determined solely by its cultural contexts.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1. Pro: Cultural relativism is supported by moral disagreements between cultures.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1. Pro: Widespread disagreement in morality undermines the idea of "universal morality".
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.1. Con: Scientific disagreement is also everywhere, but it doesn't follow from this that the outside world doesn't follow reasonable rules, only that some people are mistaken about scientific truths.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Scientific claims rely on observable, verifiable evidence. Moralistic claims rely on relativistic interpretation based on cultural references. Science is objective, whereas morality is subjective. 

The outside world, and its rules, are neither all reasonable nor all unreasonable. It's a mixture of both. Filled with both objective scientific truths and a mix of subjective and often conflicting moralities, it follows that people would have trouble reconciling scientific truths and their faith.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2. Con: Most general statements on morality are universally agreed upon by healthy societies. For instance, unjust murder is always considered to be wrong, as is thievery. Even if morality on the whole isn't universal, these general statements still require explanation.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2.1. Con: Humans all have many similar traits, it is therefore coherent that humans would also share many the same relative morals.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2.1.1. Con: Morality must be something more than shared human opinion. In 18th-19th century America, slavery was widely considered to be moral. If morality is merely "shared relative morals", then in their time and place, slavery was in fact, moral. Yet we rightly consider those persons today to have been morally flawed, even in their own time.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.3. Con: Universal morality isn't required for the moral argument to hold, only that moral statements have truth value and things are objectively right or wrong.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.3.1. Pro: Morality can have objective truth even if no one knows of it.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.4. Con: The fact that people might disagree on a further point does not mean that originally they felt the same inclination towards agreeing with \(or disagreeing with\) that moral point. It could be \(and probably often is\) that a person convinces himself/herself of a particular moral point, thereby then finding evidential arguments for their holding of that moral view.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.5. Con: Disagreement about a concept "A" is warranted if and only if A actually exists. Therefore moral disagreement supports, rather than undermines, the idea that objective morality exists.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.6. Con: Disagreement in morality often derives from factual, not moral, error. For instance, witch hunts were once conducted on the erroneous assumption that witches could have negative effects on others and the world.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.6.1. Con: The contentious issue of abortion demonstrates disagreement in morality.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.6.1.1. Con: The debate of the morality of abortion largely comes down to two points, one of which is clearly a factual disagreement, rather than a moral disagreement;

Is the fetus meaningfully alive?

The other question, which is debatable less factual in nature, is if a pregnant individuals right to choose outweighs the right to life the fetus may, or may not have, but many people consider rights to be based in facts.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.2. Con: The divide between cultures isn't clear or well-defined - as such, the decision of which cultural moral framework to follow would depend on overarching moral rules. Those rules separating cultures cannot themselves be decided by any culture, and as such, at least some objective moral standards would have to exist.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.3. Con: Under cultural relativism, the moral framework decided on by a culture is true, and anyone who defies it is morally wrong. However, we agree that certain figures such as Martin Luther King acted morally, even though they defied the moral framework of the culture they lived in. This is known as the reformer's dilemma.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.6. Pro: All attempts to generalize morality into uncontroversial and exceptionless broad rules have failed to match perfectly with our moral judgments. As such it seems likely that morality isn't simplifiable or reducible to general axia, and requires situational judgments. Therefore, if morality is objective, it must be contained in an external mind able to make such judgments - and such a mind would be considered God.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.6.1. Con: Situational judgments are made by the minds of people, thus do not require God.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.6.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.5.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.7. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2.1.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.8. Con: Morality may not have "objective truth." For example, controversial issues such as abortion and ethical dilemmas such as trolley problems and Singer's drowning child demonstrate that humans greatly disagree on certain moral issues.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.2. Con: Embodiment of a characteristic is distinct from being the cause of it. Regardless of whether or not God embodies what is right, this concept of what is right can and does exist independently of God. Thus, there is no distinct proof for the existence of God based on the existence of morality. Ultimately, then moral arguments for God still fail.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.2. Con: Atheist also have moral standards. Morality is not founded upon religion alone.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6. Con: It is impossible to know a priori whether an omnipotent and omnibenevolent God could co-exist with evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6.1. Pro: To rule out a prior the compatibility of divine omnipotence, divine omnibenevolence, and real evil, one would need to prove that there are in principle no justifiable reasons for God to permit evil to exist. To know this would require knowing all possible outcomes of evil in the world, knowledge which no human has or can have.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6.1.1. Pro: It's impossible to say with any acceptable certainty that the state of the world isn't justified from God's perspective.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6.1.1.1. Pro: Any given "evil" circumstance has results that can continue to affect other circumstances long after, and far away from, the original circumstance. [Chaos theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chaos_theory) shows that accurately predicting, and therefore judging the weight of, these consequences is far beyond the scope of human intelligence.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6.1.1.1.1. Con: There is no reason that an all powerful god would have to utilize chaotic systems to realize a certain outcome.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6.1.1.1.2. Con: If God is omnipotent then God can do anything. If God can do anything, then good does not have to come from evil unless God decides that it must be the case. If God is omnibenevolent, God would not allow evil. Therefore, God would not require evil for good.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6.1.1.1.2.1. Con: It may not be possible, even in principle, to have a physical reality with good and without evil.
