1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6.1.1.1.4. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.1.5.2.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6.1.1.2. Pro: We cannot know what God is thinking.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6.1.1.2.1. Con: We can if He choose to share.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.6.1.1.3. Con: Moral standards should be acceptable, for making a sensible claim. If God bothered to give us moral standards, then He should have also told us why He violates them. Jesus taught by example and by explanation. God should explain the contradiction, why is He not the way He was supposed to be. Suspicious silence about Himself, while being vocal about human moral  issues raise a reasonable doubt, that there is no any justification at all.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.7. Pro: An uncaring God is compatible with the existence of evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.8. Pro: If a God is all knowing and good, it should also know the future. If it is the case and it gives free will to people, knowing already that people will do bad, and then punishes them, this is either sadistic or perverted, so not good.
1.1.3.3.4.4.5.9. Pro: A fictional, non-existent God is compatible with the existence of evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.6. Con: If God were to prevent Evil, this would disrupt the laws of cause and effect, and cause greater unimaginable harms.
1.1.3.3.4.4.6.1. Con: This doesn't satisfactorily challenge the problem of evil. If god chooses not to prevent all evil, then he is not all good or not all powerful, no matter his reasoning.
1.1.3.3.4.4.6.2. Con: The claim that preventing evil would disrupt cause and effect is unsubstantiated.
1.1.3.3.4.4.6.3. Con: If evil were prevented, nothing could be harmful \(by definition\).
1.1.3.3.4.4.7. Con: The concept of evil only works if God does exist to define right and wrong. Without that, it's only an opinion.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1. Pro: Evil is a problem for atheism as well, because it is not clear what an atheist's moral basis for calling something evil is in a purely physical world.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1. Pro: The fact that people presuppose evil exists in the acceptance of using it to refute a particular god suggests that they presuppose an objective moral arbitration, which requires a god.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.1. Con: At least some of our morals are rooted in some goal, or valuable concept of value.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.1.1. Con: Opposition to a subjective goal is not an example of objective evil. The existence of activity which opposes some person's subjective goals would in no way be considered evidence for the non-existence of a classical theistic god.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.1.1.1. Con: There is no evidence to support the assumption that all forms of morality that don't really on God are subjective.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.2. Con: Kant's moral dignity, social contract, utilitarianism, and virtue ethics; these are just some moral theories that we have come up with that don't require a god.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.2.1. Con: Kant's moral theory explicitly necessitates practical belief in god.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.2.1.1. Con: You can accept most of Kant's theory without agreeing that it necessitates a belief in god.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.2.2. Con: The mere existence of theories about morality does not imply their correctness and applicability to the real world. Although these views might govern the morality of a possible world, they do not account for morality in the real one.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.2.2.1. Pro: Moral theories do not make a claim to the authority of their ideas of morality. They merely suggest possible explanations to behavior that is already observable and operative in the world.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.3. Con: The existence of morality doesn't imply an objective or absolute morality. Subjective morality is still morality.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.2.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.3. Pro: Another problem related to this is how humans are surprised by pain or feel it doesn't fit, in an age and world were pain is a common certainty: even some young children are only a bit upset to see animals torn apart by other animals, but are annoyed at minor injustice like littering. We would not expect this from a car: we know it fails in good time. But we often apply perfect standards to moral issues. To me this indicates a desire not explained by natural selection without a perfect guide.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.3.1. Con: If anybody expects others to act perfectly moral at times, this belief will either be quickly corrected by experience, excepting others as who they are because they either can't afford not to, or because they morally realize they can't do anything about it, or they will become a monster, seeing corruption everywhere but in themselves, and generally act like Judge Claude Frodo from the Hunchback of Notre damn, because even major moral failing are everywhere.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.3.2. Con: A car doesn't have feelings like me. I do have feelings and I do feel pain, which can be fully explained by evolution.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.3.3. Con: Being surprised by pain is a matter of not being used to it.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.3.4. Con: People have more empathy for people, because we are more used to people's expressions and also because understanding other people is evolutionarily advantageous.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.3.4.1. Pro: Understanding other people's pain and identifying \(even feeling\) that pain drives us to help members of our populations, directly increasing the chances of survival.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.4. Con: Atheism does not have a problem in defining evil. Empathy - which enables us to determine how we would feel about the experience - can be used to evaluate actions as good and bad.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.4.1. Pro: In addition to atheists, many religions also derive a sense of morality from the same basic impulse - empathy - which is why these religions are similar to each other.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.4.2. Con: Empathy may be useful to discover the moral state of an action, but it does not provide an ontological basis for evil. It may explain what is evil, but it seems inadequate to explain why an action is evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.5. Con: The existence of evil does not require a moral law beyond man, simply morals.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.2.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.5.2. Pro: Even devoid of some system, someone can believe things right, or wrong, which in turn would mean there are good, and evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.5.3. Con: A Universal position held by all men, \(such as "murder is wrong"\), suggests a Universal Mind.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.6. Con: Evil is defined as that which goes against humanity by humanists who do not necessarily believe in a god.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.6.1. Con: Humanists do not have the moral authority to make such a distinction.
If morality is defined by man himself, then man himself is free to rewrite the rules of morality as he pleases, whether he cares for social contracts, utilitarianism, or any other social theory or not.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.6.1.1. Con: Those who claim to be acting morally without reason do so without authority, but Utilitarianism is based on reason, social contract is based on reason, Kant's work is based on reason, these systems, and many more draw there authority form the reason, and logic on which they were built.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.6.1.2. Con: Humanism is not defined by man himself. It is defined by a rational exploration of what hurts and helps humanity. It exists independent of any given mind.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.7. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.2. Con: Evil can be defined in terms of threats and degradation of human wellbeing. In the problem of evil. If you're enduring something avoidable and unpleasant, that is evil. 

Compare this to pain. Some people enjoy pain. So it's not suffering. It's actually thrilling or stimulating. Such people might not enjoy ice-cream. Thus being made eat some would be a low degree of suffering. It an objective description of subjective experience.

God doesn't define evil or suffering, our experience does.
1.1.3.3.4.4.7.3. Con: Indeed, it might just be an "opinion." Many atheists do not accept an objective moral standard. However, classical theists generally subscribe to such a standard, and this can still be used against them in an argument by contradiction.
1.1.3.3.4.4.8. Con: The description of the problem of evil leaves out all self-determining life besides God. If humans are created in God's image, that includes self determination and free will. For God to 'do away with all sin' requires limits to be placed on self determination, essentially changing life from 'created in God's image' to 'automatons obeying the will of God'.
1.1.3.3.4.4.8.1. Con: There is a distinction between a perfect agent and a perfect automaton. It is supposed that in heaven, humans will all maintain their free will while simultaneously being free of evil. God could have created humanity like that in the first place. In other words, rather than creating humans that can't sin, God could have created humans that won't sin.
1.1.3.3.4.4.8.1.1. Con: Those would not be free willed agents. They would be good because they know nothing else, not because of their choices. Humans are said to be created in God's image, requiring them to have the ability to choose. Access to heaven is contingent on sacred choices in the face of evil, not sacred choices in the absence of any other possibility.
1.1.3.3.4.4.8.2. Con: "Self-determination" and free will are illusions. All human behaviour emerges, ultimately out of things that were not "determined" or chosen by the human themselves. 

Genetics, upbringing, brain chemistry, literally the entire universe, is not chosen, they are determined by something preceding the "self" on every single occasion. 

This, doesn't change by the way with a "soul", you also don't choose your soul.
1.1.3.3.4.4.8.3. Con: This argument is self-defeating. If an inability to sin places limits on free will, then God has no free will. Hence he should be able to create humans without it too. If instead an inability to sin does not limit free will, then God should be able to create humans with free will and without sin.
1.1.3.3.4.4.8.4. Con: This ignores ['natural evil'](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_evil) where natural disasters etc cause huge amounts of suffering.
1.1.3.3.4.4.8.5. Con: This condition is already set by classical theism's definition of God. An omniscient, omnipotent being must necessarily be the only morally responsible agent in existence. The freedom of other beings to exist or do anything at all is contingent upon the approval \(or ignorance\) of the one who can make and unmake at will.
1.1.3.3.4.4.9. Pro: The moral argument for the biblical god seems to run into the problem of the existence of evil. The god of the bible also does not set a good moral example, decimating cities and inciting people to dash out children's brains or asking them to sacrifice their children as a test of loyalty and many other torturous acts besides.
1.1.3.3.4.4.10. Pro: The Epicurean Paradox: If God is willing to prevent evil, but is not able to then he is not omnipotent.
If he is able, but not willing  then he is malevolent.
If he is both able and willing then whence cometh evil.
If he is neither able nor willing then why call him God? [Epicurus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epicurus)
1.1.3.3.4.4.10.1. Con: It is not true that an omnipotent, omnibenevolent God must always and at all times remove all evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.10.1.1. Con: The idea that god follows utilitarian theory is let down by babies and infants dying of medical conditions, such as cancer, where there is no societal benefit.
1.1.3.3.4.4.10.2. Con: Evil is anthropocentrically ill-defined and hugely subjective; things that we think are natural Evils to us \(like diseases, natural disasters and death itself\) serves themselves as Good to the world, abiding by the scientific laws that govern them.
1.1.3.3.4.4.10.2.1. Pro: God should not change the science of the world specifically for us; if the whole of Creation is good \(Genesis 1:31\) then God doesn't owe his sustenance to us, but equally to everything in His Creation, all of which are products of His designed Science.
1.1.3.3.4.4.10.2.2. Con: The Epicurean argument does not assume that some things are objectively evil. A subjective concept of evil is enough to make the argument work.
1.1.3.3.4.4.10.3. Con: God may be able and willing to stop evil, and may in fact be doing so in His own time and in His own way for reasons of His own.
1.1.3.3.4.4.10.3.1. Con: According to the standard christian doctrine of hell, God does not even terminate evil, ever.
1.1.3.3.4.4.10.3.2. Con: The Epicurean paradox is concerned with the prevention \(and not the termination\) of evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.10.4. Con: Some goods may only be actualizable by allowing the possibility of evil.
1.1.3.3.4.4.10.4.1. Pro: Love, to truly exist, must be given freely. It cannot be forced from someone. To permit love to exist, one must allow for it to be withheld.
1.1.3.3.4.4.11. Pro: -> See discussion #1960: Because of the existence of evil, there cannot be a monotheistic God \(as traditionally conceived\).
1.1.3.3.4.4.12. Con: This argument limits existence to duality but ignores that Reality is fundamentaly non-dual.
1.1.3.3.4.4.12.1. Pro: Light and darkness is dual experience, but darkness has no existential basis - it is just subjectively experiencing lack of light. This means that in reality there is just light and that means reality is non-dual.
1.1.3.3.5. Con: Humans are said to be made in God's image - as free willed, self determining creatures. Enacting limits on that self determination - such as 'eliminating sin' - requires God to limit free will. Of the two - the ability to choose for oneself, and the choice of divine over profane behaviour - the former is the more fundamentally 'divine' characteristic. Moreover, this world may be no more than a testing ground - where humans learn the consequences of choices via trial and error.
1.1.3.3.5.1. Con: Free will is already limited: just try really hard turning into a guinea pig.
1.1.3.3.5.2. Con: This answer suggests that 'sin' is something that needs to exist without God having a say in the matter. However if God created everything, \(s\)he could have \(and given omnipotence and omnibenevolence, would have\) created the 'set of possible actions in the world' such that 'sin' wasn't in it. In that case, 'free will' would not be compromised.
1.1.3.3.5.3. Con: This argument is self-defeating. If the inability to sin constitutes a limit on free will, then God himself has no free will, and the "ability to choose for oneself" is certainly NOT divine. If instead the inability to sin does not limit free will, then God certainly should have created humans without sin and eliminated all suffering in the world.
1.1.3.4. Con: Anything that God can't do isn't problematic as the only things that God cannot do are acts contrary to his nature.
1.1.3.4.1. Con: Omnipotence is a matter of having the power to do anything, whether or not one chooses to use that power. God's desire to do an act is irrelevant when considering his ability to do an act.
1.1.3.4.2. Con: If you claim that omnipotence is the ability to do anything within one's nature, any human could claim to be omnipotent. Can't lift a rock? No problem. It simply wasn't within the nature of the person to be that strong. Of course, one could argue that every being, including the classical gods, are omnipotent, but that rather defeats the purpose.
1.1.3.5. Pro: God cannot do what He does not will to do. Likewise, God can only do what He wills to do.
1.1.3.5.1. Con: This is contradictory, because people can manipulate their will, so to be held back only by your will is not to be held back at all.
1.1.3.6. Con: Examples of things that God cannot do are logically nonsensical and meaningless \(e.g. make a square circle\).
1.1.3.6.1. Con: If a square circle is illogical and meaningless, then so is God. as his very nature \(outside space & time\) is also logically nonsensical and meaningless.
1.1.3.6.1.1. Con: This is a [non-sequitur](https://www.logicallyfallacious.com/tools/lp/Bo/LogicalFallacies/136/Non-Sequitur). Square circles are impossible because the two terms are mutually exclusive.  The claim that God exists outside of space and time has no inherent contradiction.
1.1.3.6.1.1.1. Pro: Many things exist outside of space and time - numbers, for example, or pieces of music, stories, abstract properties.
1.1.3.7. Pro: God cannot be all powerful because he can't prove his own nonexistence.
1.1.3.7.1. Con: All powerful God might be able to prove false statements. This is contradictory. By [principle of explosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principle_of_explosion?wprov=sfla1), this would allow him to prove any statements.
1.1.3.7.2. Con: "His Omnipotence means power to do all that is \[possible\], not to do the \[impossible\].... meaningless combinations of words do not suddenly acquire meaning simply because we prefix to them the two other words 'God can.'... It is no more possible for God than for the weakest of his creatures to carry out both of two mutually exclusive alternatives; not because his power meets an obstacle, but because nonsense remains nonsense even when we talk it about God." -- C.S. Lewis, The Problem of Pain
1.1.3.7.3. Pro: If God was all-powerful and therefore could prove his own nonexistence, and his proof would be correct and realistic, he would not exist. This is a contradiction.
1.1.3.7.3.1. Con: The argument begs the question by assuming the conclusion. "If God... could prove his own nonexistence, and his proof \[is\] correct... he would not exist."

Of course if he correctly proves that he does not exist, he would not exist!
1.1.3.7.3.1.1. Con: No the claim does not assume its conclusion. Just because certain proofs exist doesn't mean the reality agrees with the proof. Nor does it mean the proof is correct. But due to that, each step of the proof must be explicitly stated and justified.
1.1.3.7.3.1.1.1. Con: That the proof is correct is a given in the grandparent claim.
1.1.3.7.3.1.1.1.1. Con: Actually that part had to be explicitly assumed, otherwise the claim would not be sufficiently justified. But this is not in any way circular.
1.1.3.7.3.2. Con: It has not been shown that an all-powerful being can do illogical tasks, such as proving their own non-existence.
1.1.3.7.3.2.1. Con: By definition of all-powerful, a being is not all-powerful, if there exists tasks which the being is unable to perform.
1.1.3.7.3.3. Con: It has not been shown that an omnibenevolent being could lie by proving their non-existence when they actually exist.
1.1.3.7.3.3.1. Con: The claim is about whether all-powerful entities can do certain contradictory actions. The claim is not about omnibenevolence, which is unrelated.
1.1.3.7.3.3.1.1. Con: The claim is about the God of classical theism, who is omni-benevolent and cannot lie. If the grandparent claim imagines that God can lie, then it fails because it addresses a straw man god, not the God of classical theism.
1.1.3.7.3.3.1.2. Pro: Only omnipotence of God is needed to prove the contradiction. Omnibenevolence cannot avoid this problem because every notion of God that assumes God is omnipotent would be subject to same proof by contradiction.
1.1.3.7.3.4. Con: Even if an all-powerful being can do illogical tasks, it does not follow that He would do illogical tasks.
1.1.3.7.3.4.1. Con: The God of classical theism is not simply an all-powerful being, but He is also the source of morality and cannot lie.
1.1.3.7.3.5. Pro: It is not necessary for the proof that God actually performs any illogical actions. If God is able to perform illogical actions, then these must be consistent. Even if God never actually does such actions. By [Compactness theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compactness_theorem?wprov=sfla1), If they are consistent with the universe then assuming their occurrence cannot cause contradiction.
1.1.3.7.3.6. Pro: Clearly the abilities of an all-powerful being would be limited, if he cannot do such illogical action. The definition of "[all-powerful](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omnipotence?wprov=sfla1)" would reject such limitation by definition, at least in their most strong form. Therefore all-powerful entities that can perform all, even absurd, kinds of illogical actions cannot exist. Hence, a God that is in such sense all-powerful does not exist.
1.1.3.7.4. Con: An all-good God would not lie, and therefore would never attempt to prove his own nonexistence.
1.1.3.8. Con: A better understanding of God's omnipotent nature is that: God can do anything that can be done, and that anything that can be done cannot be limited to my understanding of it.
1.1.3.9. Con: This is not a contradiction in the definition of God, rather in \(some of\) the theological systems devised to support certain religions. God is not the same as religion.
1.1.4. Pro: God's interactions with the world is inconsistent with God's divinity.
1.1.4.1. Pro: The tone of many religious texts is inconsistent, simultaneously stating that God is a perfect being, and yet requiring that God is praised. A perfect being would not need praise.
1.1.4.1.1. Con: God could request praise for the sake of God's devotees, rather than God's own sake.
1.1.4.1.2. Con: There are conceptions of God in classical theology, such as in the flood story, when God is conveyed as imperfect, or as having the ability to make mistakes.
1.1.4.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.1.7.
1.1.4.1.3. Pro: A perfect entity needs nothing as it is, by definition, totally developed and unchangeable.
1.1.4.1.3.1. Con: An unchanging permanence is the definition of a statically perfect \(complete\) entity. God can be viewed as a dynamically perfect 'entity' - such as the water cycle on Earth. Since the water cycle arises out of basic principles - the transition of a specific compound \(water\) between solid-liquid-gas states in response to changing energy levels \(temperature\) - it has existed for billions of years, but still incorporates constant change.
1.1.4.1.4. Con: Religious texts incorporate centuries of human politics, not to mention the inefficiencies of transcribing, printing and publishing such documents on a mass scale without the aid of machines. The King James Bible, for instance, is probably pretty different from the one \(ones?\) that existed around 500 AD. Also, in a lot of verses/ quotations/ scriptures \(and in many religions\), 'praise' could also be read as 'worship' - an injunction to practice the preaching.
1.1.4.1.5. Con: Classical theists would not say that God "needs" praise, but is utterly self-sufficient.
1.1.4.1.5.1. Pro: According to the Christian Bible, God does not need our praise. However it is the right response when we understand who we are in relation to God and what he has done for us.  \(See [Acts 17:24-25](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=acts+17%3A24-25&version=NIV)\)
1.1.4.1.6. Con: -> See 1.1.4.1.5.1.
1.1.4.2. Pro: If God responds to prayer, then this undermines his transcendence \(total independence from all else\).
1.1.4.2.1. Con: The use of anthropomorphisms regarding God is a common practice throughout the Bible, probably used metaphorically to help readers understand some of God's actions and attributes, rather than as a literal description of God's nature.
1.1.4.2.2. Con: Most modern theologians agree that intercessory prayer isn't effective, since it relies on the assumptions that God can learn something new \(invalidating His omniscience\) and/or change His mind \(invalidating His perfection\) based on your request. As such the rejection of intercessory prayer does not impact the existence of God.
1.1.4.2.3. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.1.7.
1.1.4.2.4. Con: Prayer does not inform God or change God's mind. It is God's eternal unchanging will that prayers be rewarded.
1.1.4.2.5. Con: Transcendence does not equate to independence - it means to “go beyond” or surpass. Therefore God can transcend creation yet still be in relation to it
1.1.4.2.6. Con: Mercy is an attribute of God. So are consciousness and will.
1.1.4.3. Pro: The apparent definition of God negates God as an existent being.  If God is the creator of all things, then God cannot be in the same category of the things created.
1.1.4.3.1. Con: God isn't in the same category of created things \(contingent\). By definition, God is eternal and necesary.
1.1.4.3.1.1. Con: The logical string that God could not have failed to exist only works if you believe he created the universe. God must exist only if you believe in God. If you don’t believe in God it is wholly possible that he cannot exist.
1.1.4.3.1.1.1. Con: If there is no necessary being to give origin to contingent beings, then there is no way contingent beings could have come to existence. Since contingent beings exist, there must be a necessary being to bring them to existance.
1.1.4.3.1.1.2. Con: Not so. God could exist even if He did not create anything.
1.1.4.3.2. Con: Not everything that exist is created.
1.1.4.3.3. Pro: The definition of God is self-contradictory in that he is beyond space but also omnipresent.
1.1.4.3.3.1. Con: God may affect space, even if space may not affect God.
1.1.4.3.3.1.1. Con: If God affects space, then God reacts to space and, therefore, space affects God.
1.1.4.3.3.2. Con: Omnipresent does not mean existing "within" space.
1.1.4.3.3.2.1. Pro: Modern understanding of space is within a space-time continuum. They cannot be addressed separately. 'God is beyond space' is strictly what Abrahamic religions claim. Illustration for an alternative opinion can be found in another monotheistic religion, Vaishnavism, where God says that He Himself is time [Gita 11:32](https://asitis.com/11/32.html) . In modern language, God is space/time.
1.1.4.3.3.3. Con: "Beyond space yet omnipresent." These are different expressions conveying the same idea. God is to his creation as an author is to his novel. Nowhere, yet everywhere.
1.1.4.3.4. Pro: The definition of God is self-contradictory in that he is beyond time but also eternal.
1.1.4.3.4.1. Con: God may be linked in some way to time and also be beyond it in other ways.
1.1.4.3.4.2. Con: God created time. It's like saying a relational human is self-contradictory just because they created unfeeling robots.
1.1.4.3.4.3. Con: Being beyond, or outside of time implies being eternal.
1.1.4.3.5. Con: God is in his own category. Everything that exists does so in a hierarchy between similarity and dissimilarity with God. A rock is like God only in the fact that it exists. A human is much more like god in the fact that they reason. But God himself is inherent to reality and the only entity that is such.
1.1.4.3.6. Con: If God is assumed to be the universe itself \(an oft repeated claim in religious and spiritual documents\), then at the instant of the big bang, there was nothing but God - the physical and metaphysical laws that govern everything we know - and an enormous amount of energy. From that initial point, we have evolved to the universe of today, in which everything that we know to exist is a result of what appeared in that first instant. Thus God is both creator of all things and within everything.
1.1.4.3.7. Con: The claim of God being the creator of all things implies a degree of common sense in its understanding. Namely, God created all things other than himself. The Bible asserts that God is eternal: "from everlasting to everlasting"; without creation; and therefore not in the same category as created things.  \(See Psalms 90 verse 2\)
1.1.4.3.7.1. Con: This negates the creation of reality, itself. Either God is the creator of reality, and he was outside of reality during its creation \(i.e., is not real\), or was within reality, and thus not the creator of all things.
1.1.4.3.7.1.1. Con: Reality isn't a "thing" per se, but only a metaphysical concept - the property of being real. As has been stated, God could not create reality while also being real, so there's no contradiction in saying that God did not create reality.
1.1.4.3.7.1.1.1. Pro: Classical theism doesn't claim that God created every concept and entity, only that He is the necessary cause for all contingent things. This is consistent with metaphysical concepts such as reality not being created by God.
1.1.4.3.7.1.1.2. Con: Concepts are things. If God didn't create them, then he didn't create everything.
1.1.4.3.7.1.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.4.3.7.1.1.1.
1.1.4.3.7.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.4.3.7.1.1.1.
1.1.4.3.7.1.3. Con: God exists in at least 6 or 7 dimensions this claim only makes sense when bound to 3.
1.1.4.3.8. Con: In classical theism, God is not the creator of all things simpliciter, but of all contingent things.
1.1.4.3.9. Con: Classical theists do not believe that God is in the same category as things created, and even His existence is qualitatively different from all other kinds of existence. Thomas Aquinas, a famous classical theist, conceived of God not as "a being" but as "Being Itself."
1.1.4.4. Pro: There are many cases where God is defined as good but claimed by believers to have done things which those same believers would consider bad if done by anyone other than God \(or someone claiming to be doing God's will\).
1.1.4.4.1. Con: As the author and rightful owner of all creation it is God's prerogative to do things that us, mere members of that creation, may not.

As [Isaiah 45:9](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Isaiah+45%3A9&version=NIV) says, "Does the clay say to the potter, ‘What are you making?’"
1.1.4.4.1.1. Con: A sentient being can not be owned.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1. Con: A sentient being can be owned if the entity owning the sentient being is as supreme as God is understood to be.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1. Pro: There is no proper analogy between God's right as an "owner" of his creation, and our rights as "owner" of our creations.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: When we create something we create it from "borrowed" ingredients. Everything down to the smallest particle was made by God and for God. We can not make anything the same way He made everything.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: The argument that 'when we create something we create it from "borrowed" ingredients. Everything down to the smallest particle was made by God and for God. We can not make anything the same way He made everything" is somewhat undercut by the fact that it is a thing created without the use of any of these particles purely by a human.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: If the Christian God exists, then the power of reason and logic are part of his nature and can then be said to belong to him. When we use these capabilities we are borrowing from God.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: If the Christian God does not exist, we can still use our faculties of reason and logic. It's just that we would not describe it as "borrowing from God".
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.1.2. Con: Believing in a God as a presupposition to justify using reason and logic is rationally worse that presupposing reason and logic directly. [Occam's razor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occam%27s_razor).
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.1.3. Pro: Colossians 1:16 explains that the Christian understanding of God is that "by him \[God\] all things were created, in heaven and on earth, visible and invisible, whether thrones or dominions or rulers or authorities—all things were created through him and for him."
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.2. Con: If the Christian God exists, then our mental substance \(whether material or immaterial\) was created by God and when we exercise this mental substance to formulate arguments and thoughts we are borrowing from God.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.1.3.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1.2. Con: If a person were to conjure a sentient sandwich from the void, it would still be immoral to eat it.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.2. Con: Saying 'God is a special case, we can't hold them to the same standard' is the logical fallacy of special pleading.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.2.1. Con: Special pleading is making an *arbitrary* exception to a rule. God is in a unique position of authority and supremacy to every other being. It is a case of equivocation to assume that God is merely a human in different clothes.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.2.1.1. Con: Special pleading is not an adequate defense of the practice of special pleading.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.2.1.2. Con: Positing a God with unique position of authority and supremacy is arbitrary, no matter how ancient the notion.
1.1.4.4.1.1.1.2. Con: It is special pleading and a cope out to make an exception for God. In effect and in the absence of the contrary, no degree of "supremacy" can prevent a sentient being from being its own individual.
1.1.4.4.1.2. Pro: The only person who can rightfully play God, is God.
1.1.4.4.1.2.1. Pro: The popular colloquialism "don't try to play God" is evidence that there is an idea of a being called God that holds proper authority to do actions that other beings do not hold.
1.1.4.4.1.2.1.1. Con: What people say, when not backed by adequate reason, is not a sufficient reason to do things, as people say things like that a mirror being broken containing one's reflection is bad luck, or will damage one's soul.
1.1.4.4.1.3. Con: The contradiction is not in the authority or capability to do something but the good or the bad property of something.
1.1.4.4.1.4. Con: Either an action is good, or it is not, the doer of the deed does not change this fact.
1.1.4.4.1.4.1. Con: The doer of the deed does sometimes have an impact on the morality of the action.
1.1.4.4.1.4.1.1. Pro: For a doctor with the proper medical know-how, cutting into someone's chest to save their life is moral. If the average Joe does the same thing then it is recklessly immoral.
1.1.4.4.1.4.1.2. Pro: If a police officer, acting under authority of the State locks up a prisoner in a jail cell, it is moral. If an average Joe does it, it is kidnapping.
1.1.4.4.1.4.1.3. Pro: If a soldier in wartime situation kills an enemy combatant, it is moral. If an average Joe kills the same person on a street corner in peacetime it is murder.
1.1.4.4.1.4.1.4. Con: The only examples of 'the does' changing the morality of the situation involve factors that fundamentally change the situation, an enemy combatant is not comparable to some Joe on the street, nor is a criminal awaiting do process, and random flailing is a substantially different act then performing a surgery one is qualified to perform.
1.1.4.4.1.5. Con: Power is not authority.
1.1.4.4.1.6. Con: If the sandwich you made suddenly grew a mouth, and started talking and thinking, it would not be moral for you to eat it, even though that was the purpose it was made for. This is true even if you knew it would become sentient.
1.1.4.4.1.6.1. Con: Us making a sentient sandwich is not analogous to the  supremacy of God's power in creating us.
1.1.4.4.1.6.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.4.4.1.1.1.1.1.
1.1.4.4.1.6.2. Con: There is no clear reason to believe that sentience is the criteria for moral consumption.
1.1.4.4.1.6.2.1. Con: Almost every moral system values moral actors.
1.1.4.4.1.6.2.1.1. Con: Sentience does not automatically imply moral agency.
1.1.4.4.1.6.2.1.1.1. Con: If sentience does not, it is unclear what does.
1.1.4.4.1.6.2.1.1.1.1. Con: Some animals appear to be sentient in a rudimentary sense but are not considered moral agents \(e.g. certain high primates\).
1.1.4.4.1.6.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con: This does not address the problem, if sentience does not demonstrate value, it is unclear what does.
1.1.4.4.1.6.2.1.1.2. Con: Almost every moral system values moral actors in some capacity.
1.1.4.4.2. Con: "Goodness" and "badness/evil" are irrelevant if they are only subjective constructions. One may consider something "bad", but be mistaken. For the argument to succeed, the action must be objectively bad.

Objective moral values and duties cannot exist unless God exists.
1.1.4.4.3. Con: It is possible that God exists but did not do what some believers claim that He did.
1.1.4.4.3.1. Pro: Many of the [genocides](https://zippycatholic.wordpress.com/2014/09/13/literal-insanity/) attributed to God are actually commanded by humans \(such as Moses\), and carried out by humans, not God.
1.1.4.4.3.1.1. Con: In many cases where God does not explicitly order the genocide, he encourages it or does nothing to stop it. This still makes God complicit in these genocides.
1.1.4.4.3.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.
1.1.4.4.3.1.2. Con: In the particular case of Amalekites \(1 Samuel 15\) God is unambiguously responsible for the genocide.
1.1.4.4.3.1.3. Con: In their historical contexts, it is likely that the biblical accounts did in fact intent to attribute these commands to God.
1.1.4.4.4. Con: It is possible for an omniscient and benevolent being to do things which would be immoral for finite creatures because of His ability to see and guide all ends.
1.1.4.4.4.1. Con: The ends do not justify the means.
1.1.4.4.4.1.1. Con: Ends can justify means, so long as they are sufficient to do so.
1.1.4.4.4.1.1.1. Pro: In the Bible, Rahab told a lie to the Jericho soldiers to protect Israelite spies. She is later spared by God during the attack because of her help. Joshua 2
1.1.4.4.4.1.1.2. Pro: If Nazi's are banging on your door, lying to protect the Jews that are hiding in your basement is justified.
1.1.4.4.4.1.1.3. Con: No end can justify the things God does, like the ordering of genocides, commuting genocides, and eternal torture.
1.1.4.4.4.1.1.4. Con: Examples involving lying seem to only suggest that lying is a justifiable means, so long as the end is sufficient, or perhaps that lying on it's own is not immoral, but they do not show that in general, ends can justify means, so long as they are sufficient to do so.
1.1.4.4.4.1.2. Con: There is no ending to something that is not yet structured completely or completed.
1.1.4.4.4.1.3. Con: The morality of a "being" that is so much more than what the sum of all humanity could ever be could never be as simple as humanity's morality. This sentence is debating humanity's morality not God's morality and it does so from a humane point of view, for we are not God if God exists and we do not have his moral compass.
1.1.4.4.4.1.4. Pro: For a human, the ends should not justify the means.
1.1.4.4.4.2. Pro: Yet the end may not be known unless it's past is already known therefore predetermined.
1.1.5. Pro: The definition of God as absolutely good is contradictory because 'goodness' is relative to one's moral views and there cannot exist a person or character that would seem good to every sentient being.
1.1.5.1. Con: What "seems good to every sentient being" is not the same as "absolutely good".
1.1.5.1.1. Con: This is the precise reason why absolutely good doesn't exist. A calamity for humans is very good for the animals that they would consume. Also, a destroyer of the solar system could spurn the creation of another with other life forms. For them, the destroyer is a good thing.
1.1.5.1.1.1. Con: If you claim that the only "good" that matters is the "good" of humans \(for example\), absolute good could exist in that sense.
1.1.5.2. Con: For a sentient being to recognize that some things are "good" and some are "evil" implies a recognition of a moral standard by which to distinguish between the two. Only God's nature is sufficient to be that standard.
1.1.5.2.1. Con: Morals are, to at least some extent, evolutionary. As such, no standard is required.
1.1.5.2.1.1. Con: God would be complete, therefore not evolutionary. The whole spectrum of possible evolutions of morals would be contained in God.
1.1.5.3. Con: Seeming good and being good are two different things. I personally feel most vegetables taste awful, though I know for a fact they're good for me.
1.1.5.4. Con: Maybe if the definition was rephrased – god being the absolute good, not being absolutely good – one would not run into a relativistic aporia.
1.1.5.5. Pro: Any interpretation of a God's actions would have to be made by humans, who are fallible. No person would be truly privy to the mind of God.
1.1.5.5.1. Con: Human interpretation is, however, the filter through which we perceive everything. To dismiss such human ability is to dismiss - among everything else - the very possibility of knowing whether God exists and also whether God does not exist.
1.1.5.6. Con: The concept of relative morality is incoherent.
1.1.5.6.1. Pro: If morality is relative, then each person gets to decide what is moral. However, this renders each of us incapable of saying what is moral or immoral for someone else. Such a morality is incapable of informing us of how we should treat others--yet, how to correctly interpersonal relationships is a huge part of our moral framework.
1.1.5.6.2. Pro: Relative morality cannot explain why any person would ever consider something that they did to be immoral or wrong.
1.1.5.6.2.1. Pro: Subjective, relative morality requires that the correct answer to any moral question be found within oneself. For this to be true, no objective moral truth can exist to override the subjective moral opinion of the individual.
1.1.5.6.2.2. Pro: If one's subjective moral opinion is the ultimate arbiter of morality, then when a person believes they have done something "wrong" or "immoral", they have only violated their own internal standard.
1.1.5.6.2.3. Pro: Given subjective, relative morality, one's moral standard cannot be applied to anyone other than oneself. To do so would be to assert that said morality is objective and universal.
1.1.5.6.2.4. Pro: When a consistent moral relativist believes they have done something immoral, they can only mean that they have violated their own internal code. This raises the question of why they would ever believe any action \(especially their own action\) to be immoral to begin with. The value of a moral code that can only apply to oneself is questionable in the extreme. It is of little value to believe that oneself should behave in a given manner, while not holding others to any standard at all.
1.1.5.6.3. Pro: Relative morality cannot explain why, in the absence of an objective morality, one would even have a moral sense to begin with.
1.1.5.7. Con: The good suggested by classical theism is the creator of the universe, and as such, would be an objective arbiter of morality unlike entities within the created universe which would only have a subjective view.
1.1.6. Con: Possible contradictions in classical theism may appear from transcription difficulties and errors introduced over centuries of existence.
1.1.6.1. Pro: The source ideas for classical theism - the Bible, the works of Plato, Aristotle, etc - had already existed in documented form for centuries before the classical period. The oldest Bible, for example, [is from 330-360 AD](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/religion/5749555/Worlds-oldest-Bible-published-in-full-online.html). The multiple rounds of transcription and retranscription that preserved those source documents over the thousands of years to today are likely to have introduced errors/ caused loss of subtlety in the original conceptions.
1.1.6.1.1. Con: The oldest document of the Bible is the Septuagint with fragments dating back to 300BCE. \(Of course consisting only of the Old Testament as the events of the New Testament have not yet happened\).
1.1.6.1.1.1. Con: The Septuagint is itself the [translation into Greek of a number of earlier texts in Hebrew](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Septuagint). While there are fragments of \(Hebrew\) Biblical text dating back to 300 BC \([and before](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_Bible#Origins_of_the_Hebrew_Bible_and_its_components)\), the oldest complete Bible \(compared to the book in its current form\) would probably be one containing both Old and New Testament - hence the date of 300 AD. In either case, an older Bible makes an even stronger case for transmission/ transcription errors.
1.1.6.1.2. Con: The original domcuments of the Bible were transcribed in several different locations, by several different monasteries, each with detailed systems in place to ensure as accurate a transcription as possible. An error made in one transcription did not infect the others due to their isolation. Therefore, we can cross examine these documents to identify and eliminate errors.[thebibleproject.com](https://thebibleproject.com/podcast/making-bible-part-2/)
1.1.6.1.2.1. Con: Comparing multiple independent transcripts does help with scribal error, but it does not guarantee *perfect* transmission. Another possible problem comes from the choice of certain sources as more authoritative than others. The [Masoretic Text](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Masoretic_Text), for instance, is considered the source book for the Hebrew Tanakh \(Bible\). Though there may be good reason to choose that source as more correct than others, this also ensures that errors/ interpretations in the Text itself become part of future canon.
1.1.6.2. Pro: One apparent contradiction in classical theism is that of a theistic God changing its mind. God, per [Plato](http://www.mc.maricopa.edu/~davpy35701/text/plato-god-immutable.html), is changeless because a\) nothing can change God, and b\) God would not change, because it is already perfect. Parts of the Bible, like God repenting the creation of humanity, therefore seem to contradict theism. The problem might exist because today's Bible carries an edited version of the source documents of the Old Testament. 'Repent' might not carry the subtleties of the original text.
1.1.6.3. Con: An omnipotent God should be able to keep the record of itself clear enough for it to be not contradictory.
1.1.7. Con: Apparent contradictions in classical theism may appear from translation difficulties.
1.1.7.1. Pro: The ideas within classical theism come from a number of thinkers, including those as far back as Plato and Aristotle. These ideas were not developed in English, and even in their native languages, were reliant on the context of their times for the subtleties of meaning involved. The version of theism we’re debating, off Wiki, is already a compressed version of a complicated concept developed in multiple languages and over several centuries. It would be more extraordinary if there were no errors.
1.1.7.2. Con: In theory, a perfect God would be capable of communicating to man in such a way that all possible translations of the original message would still be accurate. In other words, He could make the "word of God" fool-proof; a message so utterly perfect, no man could ever misconstrue or spoil it.
1.1.8. Con: The possibility of God existing in another dimension allows for the possibility of both immanence and transcendence.
1.1.8.1. Con: An 'alternate dimension' implies a space which is causally disconnected from ours. Therefore, even if an omnipotent being exists in such a place, it would be impossible for it to interact with us; nor could we identify such a being or place.
1.1.9. Pro: Omniscience, omnipotence and omnibenevolence are mutually exclusive.
1.1.9.1. Con: Because we have no full concept and no experience of these terms, perhaps they could actually be contingent on each other. 'Knowing all' may require 'all-power', and 'omnibenevolence' could be decided upon by the entity that fits this description.
1.1.9.2. Con: "Science" and "potential" are two sides of the same coin: Being aware of phenomena versus directly affecting phenomena. These two are NOT mutually exclusive, rather they are complementary to each other, therefore omniscience and omnipotence \(all-knowing and all-powerfulness\), as the supreme forms of both attributes, relate to each other in the same way.
1.1.9.3. Con: God's omnibenevolence can be taken for granted.
1.1.9.3.1. Pro: God decides what is good, therefore God's goodness is uncontestable.
1.1.9.3.1.1. Pro: God only answers to God.
1.1.9.3.1.1.1. Con: This paints God as a weight-pushing authority figure, which doesn't coincide with the definition of God as being completely good and benevolent.
1.1.9.3.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.3.5.2.
1.1.9.3.1.3. Con: God does not decide what is good. This is one horn of the [Euthyphro dilemma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euthyphro_dilemma), and would make good and evil arbitrary.
1.1.9.3.1.3.1. Con: Everything is arbitrary to the ultimate God. Primary principles of omniscience, omnipotence and omnipresence ensure that. However, God doesn't change \(immutability\), and this results in the definition of an absolute point of moral reference.
1.1.9.3.2. Pro: God has unlimited knowledge to determine his own goodness eternally, and he has unlimited power to become absolutely good eternally, therefore God is absolutely good.
1.1.9.3.2.1. Pro: The human concept of "good" does not apply to God.
1.1.9.3.2.1.1. Pro: The human concept of "good" is conditioned by our limited knowledge of, and limited power over our environment, therefore fallacious when applied to the absolute.
1.1.9.3.2.1.2. Con: Yet God passed his word/law onto his people, and by following that law/those words he deems them "good" enough to achieve eternal salvation. God, in the hypothetical of him existing, created us and our understanding of "good".
1.1.9.3.2.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.1.4.2.
1.1.9.3.2.2. Con: A necessary being cannot "become". God, as a necessary being, is fully actualized, has no potentiality, and cannot change.
1.1.9.3.3. Con: God's omnibenevolence can be exaggerated.
1.1.9.3.3.1. Con: A maximally-great being would be the greatest possible good; therefore, it is impossible to conceive of any greater good. This allows no possibility of exaggeration.
1.1.9.4. Pro: If your actions have to be benevolent, then you are not capable of doing anything.
1.1.9.5. Pro: An omnipotent being would be able to create something "unknowable", even if just a 'secret'. This contradicts omniscience.
1.1.9.6. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.10.
1.1.10. Pro: It is logically impossible for God to be omnipresent and having been there when there was nothing yet. The quality of God would have to include both 'existing' and 'not existing' simultaneously, therefore God is simply non-existent.
1.1.10.1. Pro: The dilema of an absolute being that contains everything is a variation on the paradox of the irresistible force. It is a false dilema. In this case it is a false dilema that points out that the "existence" cannot possibly be an atribute of an all-containing being.
1.1.10.1.1. Con: Other than a few panentheistic religions, few would support the idea of God being "containing" in any fashion.
1.1.10.2. Pro: Existence as a definition cannot explain the qualities of both [immanence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immanence) and [transcendence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendence_\(religion\)) inherent to the "classical" view of God.
1.1.10.3. Con: Our incapable minds are insufficient to comprehend the wonders of God, and just because we don’t understand something does not mean that it is false.
1.1.10.3.1. Con: If we do not understand something, we should approach everything we think we know about that something with a certain amount of doubt. This does not seem to be the prevalent approach to God, however.
1.1.10.3.2. Con: While it is certainly true that our incapability to understand something does not make it false, there exist simpler explanations and theories for God's \(non\)existence than assuming some mystical ways in which to exist and not exist makes sense, which we can then just label wonders of God and be done with it.
1.1.10.3.3. Pro: There are many things in this Universe that we do not understand yet and there is possibility that God is one \(or all\) of these things.
1.1.10.3.4. Con: Our incapable minds are insufficient to comprehend many things, but for everything else, there is evidence that we can look for or look at, experiments that we can try to gain more knowledge and so on.
1.1.10.3.4.1. Pro: The only testifiable and shareable piece of evidence for God's existence are words and books made of these words.
1.1.10.3.4.1.1. Pro: A lot of claims in, for example, Bible have been proven as false, such as the existence of global flood at some point in our history, the age of the Earth and the Universe and so on.
1.1.10.3.4.1.1.1. Con: The Bible makes no claims regarding the age of the Earth, nor the universe. Some interpretations argue for an old earth; some for a new earth, but the bible itself does not directly address the issue.
1.1.10.3.4.1.1.1.1. Con: The issue is directly addressed in the Bible. 
The gospel of Luke contains a [genealogy of Jesus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genealogy_of_Jesus#Luke's_genealogy). Comparing with the [genealogy from Genesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genealogies_of_Genesis#Genesis_numbers), one finds that the Earth and the universe should be approximately 6000 years old.
1.1.10.4. Pro: One of the cornerstones of the classical God is that it is everywhere, though it 's nowhere. It exists yet you cannot point your finger at it. Existence is too human a term to define such a being, regardless it actually may or maynot be.
1.1.10.4.1. Con: Omnipresence excludes being "nowhere". Nothing in the classical definition of God says that God is nowhere.
1.1.10.4.1.1. Con: The fact that God is a creator implies that it was there before there was anything.
1.1.10.4.2. Con: Being present does not necessarily imply being visible. Oxygen is present in the air, yet you cannot see it.
1.1.10.5. Pro: For God to be both the ultimate and encompass everything, they would have to include both 'existing' and 'not-existing' simultaneously.
1.1.10.5.1. Con: It is incoherent to say that something exists and does not exist simultaneously. Nothing in the classical concept of God indicates that even deity is capable of a logical impossibility.
1.1.10.6. Pro: God's higher property beyond existence is probably unfathomable to the human mind. Existence is therefore an unadequate term to use when talking about God.
1.1.10.7. Con: When "there was nothing yet", there was no place in the universe for God to be. Omnipresence is the quality of being at all places at all times. It is not inconsistent with omnipresence to say that God is not in a location that does not exist.
1.1.10.8. Con: -> See 1.1.10.5.1.
1.1.10.9. Con: God both 'existing' and 'not existing' simultaneously is not a mere contradiction that implies his non-existence. It looks more like a quantum superposition. [Schrödinger's cat.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger%27s_cat)
1.1.10.10. Con: This is based on the assumption that God is part of the universe, rather than separate from it. That is a logical fallacy in and of itself, since it would result in causa sui.
1.1.11. Pro: The classical definition of God ignores the elephant in the room, that it describes a being that deserves belief \(rather than just belief in its existence\) and everything that implies.
1.1.12. Pro: Attempting to define God without referencing belief is absurd.
1.1.12.1. Con: One or more powerful beings may exist, but attributing spiritual importance to such is specious.
1.1.12.1.1. Pro: Because the classical definition of God implies that God is spiritually important, the classical definition of God is specious.
1.1.13. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.
1.1.14. Con: If God cannot in principle exist, we should not be able to believe God exists.
1.1.14.1. Con: Children are able to believe in Santa Claus and other fairy tale characters, despite their non-existence. The belief in something does not imply its existence.
1.1.15. Con: Gödel has proven that any axiomatic system is either incomplete or contradictory. Therefore it is proven that truth beyond logic is possible.
1.1.16. Pro: God is perfection in it's being by man's definition. Imperfection would have to exist to prove perfection. So God would have to be imperfect to need imperfection to exist with God's perfection. Imperfection could not be if there is a God. God would not have need of anything imperfect if God is perfect.
1.1.16.1. Con: This does not follow. Perfection \(a standard\) is required for imperfections to exist, even in theory; but a standard of perfection can theoretically exist without anything failing to meet that standard.
1.1.17. Pro: The definition of God is self-contradictory in that he is all-knowing \(omniscient\) but there are things that God cannot know.
1.1.17.1. Con: -> See 1.1.1.3.
1.1.17.2. Pro: Humans \(and perhaps animals\) have free will. For this to be true, God could not know what their actions were going to be in advance, otherwise it wouldn't truly be free.
1.1.17.2.1. Con: Norman Swartz contends that arguments using free will to deny omniscience commit the [modal fallacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argument_from_free_will#Omniscience_and_free_will).
1.1.17.2.2. Con: If God is outside time, as is often understood, then God sees all of history at the same time. We mortals are yet to discover what we will freely decide - but it is all visible to God.
1.1.17.2.3. Con: Even if God's foreknowledge must result in a fully determined universe, this would not make free will impossible.
1.1.17.2.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.
1.1.17.2.4. Pro: Free-will means that humans are free to make choices, which necessarily implies that their choices cannot be known a-priori. If an all-knowing God knows every choice we make before we make it, then it cannot be said that we have free-will.
1.1.17.2.4.1. Con: According to [compatibilism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compatibilism) free choices can be made even if God knows them beforehand.
1.1.17.2.4.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.6.
1.1.17.2.4.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4.
1.1.17.2.4.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.1.2.1.1.1.
1.1.17.2.4.2. Con: If I have a son who I know has an anger problem and already know that if event B follows event A, then he will respond in a certain way, then I have foreknowledge of his actions, and he has the free will to take such actions or to not.  This is an example of human foreknowledge in relation to the reality of free will.  God trancends even this in that he creates each individual human with traits such as confidence, kindness, self-control and love.  He knows all things and allows us free will.
1.1.17.2.4.3. Con: A-priori here means in advance and refers to something that does not exist. In other words, a non-being. God knows all the being, meaning all there is to be known.
1.1.17.2.5. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.1.1.4.2.3.
1.1.17.2.6. Pro: We seem to have free will because we are conscious.
1.1.17.2.6.1. Con: Our consciousness seems not to require free will as it happens in deterministic settings \(ie. cells\).
1.1.17.2.6.2. Con: Consciousness does not seem to require free will, as sugested by the ever more complex digital intelligence and understanding which don't rely on free will at all.
1.1.17.2.6.2.1. Con: A simulation of consciousness does not imply consciousness. The "complex" digital intelligences we have today are [vastly inferior](https://www.newscientist.com/article/dn25560-sentient-robots-not-possible-if-you-do-the-maths/) to a human mind.
1.1.17.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.1.
1.1.17.4. Pro: The definition of God is self-contradictory in that he is omniscient but also [impassable](http://www.theopedia.com/Impassibility_of_God). Either God has experiential knowledge of suffering \(which violates impassibility\) or does not \(which violates omniscience.\)
1.1.17.4.1. Con: -> See 1.1.1.3.
1.1.17.4.2. Con: Not all classical theists would agree that God is impassable.
1.1.17.4.3. Con: -> See 1.1.1.3.1.1.
1.1.17.5. Con: God \(as recognized by the three largest monotheistic religions\) is a being of incomprehensible nature. To posit that a human can even fathom what it means that God "knows" is to admit to a fundamentally flawed understanding of omniscience
1.1.17.5.1. Con: That omniscience is something we cannot fathom is not a relevant or valid definition.
1.1.17.6. Pro: -> See 1.1.1.3.2.
1.1.17.7. Pro: Classical theists, like other varieties of theist, hold that god is both all-powerful and all-knowing.  All knowing, by any reasonable standard, would include knowledge of the future. The only way this can be accomplished is via determinism. The problem is that an all-powerful entity has no limits to what it can do \(besides logical impossibilities\). But if the universe is deterministic, then god has no free will; he can't even really make decisions.
1.1.17.7.1. Con: It's possible that God has both made His decisions in advance, and is spontaneously capable of changing His mind and doing something else.
1.1.17.7.2. Con: -> See 1.1.4.3.7.1.3.
1.1.17.7.3. Con: Omnipotence only implies determinism for a certain account of freedom, namely that for a being to be free it must be able, all other things being equal, to do otherwise. If the free action of the creature is posited as one of the effects of God's deeds, then the creature is not free in this sense. However this definition of freedom is rejected by many classical theists \(cf Thomas Aquinas for whom freedom arises from intellectual universality\) and therefore the argument does not follow.
1.1.17.7.4. Con: Even if the universe is deterministic, God, as creator of the universe, is immune from such determinism.
1.1.17.7.4.1. Pro: The creator must be capable of existing without the creation.
1.1.17.7.4.1.1. Pro: If an eternal God requires the existence of His creation, then He could not have existed without it to have created it. This would make the universe either God, or a part of God. This is the god of pantheism or panentheism, not the God of classical theism.
1.1.17.7.4.2. Pro: If the universe is deterministic, then it is the creator who made it so. Because God is immutable, it follows that once God decided \(determined\) what would happen in the universe, He would not change his mind. In short, it is possible that God freely chose to create a deterministic universe.
1.1.17.7.5. Con: There is no contradiction between all-knowing and all-powerful, unless you mistakenly assume that God is an entity inside of the universe. Classically, he has been conceived of as "outside" of the universe, including time itself. If God is outside of time, then his knowledge of the future does not imply strict determinism.
1.1.17.7.5.1. Pro: God is outside the Universe in the same way a computer programmer is "outside" his program. He can control and affect the program and can see the beginning and end parameters. If the computer program is "artificially intelligent," it can be said to have a measure of "free will," even if the parameters are limited and/or predetermined.
1.1.17.7.6. Con: If the universe unfolds at each step according to the will of God; then the future is knowable by God as it is determined according to God's free will.
1.1.18. Con: Most believers do not adhere to the classical definition of God as presented here - they modify it just a little bit, by introducing the problem of free will, or by using a more nuanced definition of omnipotence. This whole part of the discussion is to a large degree a strawman.
1.1.18.1. Con: The existence of a god which embodies omniscience, and the existence of free will are contradictory. If a god is omniscient then it must know the outcome of the all peoples lives and choices before they have occurred, which must mean that they are predetermined. Thus people would not have free will.
1.1.19. Con: Arguing definitions is not the same as arguing about what they are referring to, it only leads to a need to refine the definitions
1.1.19.1. Con: Refining the classical definition of God would amount to denying that God, as classically defined, exists.
1.2. Pro: There is empirical evidence supporting the existence of God.

1.2.1.1.2.5. Con: Resurrection stories and claims of witnesses are not uncommon at that time in history. Attis of Phrygia, Horus, Krishna, etc are other such figures.
1.2.1.1.2.5.1. Con: None of them was documented in script less than half a century after it happened and sparked a movement similar to 1st century Christianity.
1.2.1.1.2.6. Con: Even as recently as 2014 people have been [buried alive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Premature_burial#Unintentional_live_burial) unintentionally. A lack of medical understanding is a more likely reason that led people to believe that Jesus Christ was dead, in turn leading to stories of resurrection.
1.2.1.1.2.6.1. Con: The Romans were expert executioners. It is highly unlikely that they would fail to successfully execute a condemned man.
1.2.1.1.2.6.1.1. Pro: Before Jesus was even put on the cross he had received beatings considered equivalent to execution.
1.2.1.1.2.6.1.2. Pro: Jesus was put up on the cross and stabbed in the side with a spear so he would bleed out to ensure he was dead by the time he was taken down from the cross.
1.2.1.1.2.6.1.2.1. Con: [Rasputin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grigori_Rasputin) was poisoned with enough cyanide to kill several men, and shot three times before he died. He was presumed dead after the first gunshot wound and left alone for some time, before leaping up and attacking his assailants when they returned. The only thing the crucifixion of Christ proves is that some people are more resilient than others.
1.2.1.1.2.6.1.3. Con: Something that is highly unlikely is not impossible. Even if the probability of surviving a Roman Crucifixion were .01%, and Jesus Christ was the only one to ever survive it, and everyone assumed he was dead because no one had ever survived an execution before, it is still possible that it played out exactly that way.
1.2.1.1.2.6.2. Con: Under normal conditions, one who had suffered a Roman Crucifixion and then lain in a tomb for days without medical care could not inspire others to believe they had conquered death.
1.2.1.1.2.6.2.1. Con: The Gospel of John records that Nicodemus brought myrrh and aloes \[John 19:39\]. These healing plants, particularly aloe plants, are considered medicinal and applied to wounds.
1.2.1.1.2.6.2.1.1. Con: While herbs and aloes are medicinal, even today's modern medicine is not advanced enough to restore a victim of a Roman scourging and crucifixion to full or near-full health in such a short amount of time.
1.2.1.1.2.6.2.1.1.1. Con: The tale could have been wildly exaggerated to accentuate suffering and other characteristics of the protagonist of the story and basic medical care given to Jesus \(being the herbs and aloes indicative of medical care\).
1.2.1.1.2.6.2.1.1.1.1. Con: "Could have been" implies what is possible, but possibility does not equal probability. We have documentation attesting to the severity of Roman scourging, the [brutality](http://www.newsweek.com/how-romans-used-crucifixion-including-jesus-political-weapon-318934) [of](http://www.newsweek.com/how-romans-used-crucifixion-including-jesus-political-weapon-318934) [crucifixion](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1420788/), and the efficiency of the [Roman](https://stephenmillerbooks.com/2016/03/crucifixion-in-naked-gritty-detail/) death squads to successfully fulfill their assignment.

Without supporting evidence, this claim is pure speculation.
1.2.1.1.2.6.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con: The probability of someone being effectively dead for a couple days and then miraculously coming back to life is still vastly inferior to the account having been wildly exaggerated.
1.2.1.1.2.6.2.1.1.2. Con: We could never know this unless we subject various people to beatings and nail them on a cross for a few hours. The claim seems subjective.
1.2.1.1.2.6.2.1.1.3. Pro: Crucifixion and scourging resulted in massive tissue \(and even organ\) damage, blood loss, and physical trauma. Modern medicine is often unable to save victims from car accidents and other severe traumatic events resulting in injuries that appear less severe than this. And the ones that can be saved from comparable trauma are in no shape in three short days to appear "victorious over death" in any meaningful way.
1.2.1.1.2.6.2.1.2. Pro: The idea that medicinal plants cured Jesus is not more preposterous than the idea that Jesus died and then was miraculously alive again.
1.2.1.1.2.6.2.1.2.1. Con: This is true, only when assuming that Jesus rose by naturalistic means. This is not the Christian assertion. 

Christianity holds that the God who created the universe and all that is in it raised Jesus back to life. To ignore this is to beg the question of the discussion by asserting that God does not exist.
1.2.1.1.2.6.3. Con: The theory that Jesus was not dead fails to account for other aspects of the passion story.
1.2.1.1.2.6.3.1. Pro: The Swoon theory fails to explain how or why the stone was moved from the door of the tomb.
1.2.1.1.2.6.3.1.1. Pro: Moving such a stone would be impossible for a recently-crucified man.
1.2.1.1.2.6.3.1.1.1. Pro: Jesus lost voluminous amounts of blood from the scourging, the crown of thorns \(which pierced the scalp, a part of the body that bleeds freely\), the crucifixion itself, and finally, the spear. 

In addition, he had already lost blood while praying in the garden. By the time he died, he would have suffered from massive dehydration.
1.2.1.1.2.6.3.1.1.2. Pro: The Roman scourging alone often caused death. It caused deep lacerations, torn and bruised muscles.

While Jewish law called for no more than 39 lashes, Roman law had no such limit, and was continued until the commanding officer chose to end it.

For a victim of such a scourging to be able to remove the stone from the door of a tomb less than a week later, having received no medical attention, would take a miracle, almost on the same level as the resurrection.
1.2.1.1.2.6.3.1.1.2.1. Con: Leveraging, it can move mountains \(or at least a ton-heavy blocks\). \(Exaggeration also does miracles for a story.\)
1.2.1.1.2.6.3.1.1.3. Pro: The wounds from the nails in a crucifixion victim's hands and feet would make it impossible to exert enough force to move a stone from the door of a tomb.
1.2.1.1.2.6.3.1.1.4. Pro: The stones used to seal burial tombs in ancient Israel could weigh more than 1 ton.
1.2.1.1.2.6.3.1.1.5. Con: It is more likely that someone else moved the stone to assist Jesus, than it is that he moved it himself.
1.2.1.1.2.6.3.1.1.5.1. Con: We have no historical accounts of anyone either overpowering the posted guard, or asking/bribing them to move the stone.

Men will often die for what they believe to be true; but never for what they know to be false.

While the Jewish leaders did claim that the tomb was empty because the body had been stolen, we have no evidence at all that anyone ever searched for the body of the dead Jesus, or the living Jesus who somehow survived execution, despite having a huge number of ancient manuscripts that document the event.

Celsus \(175AD\): "he acquired certain \(magical\) powers"

1.2.1.1.7.1.3.3. Con: Jesus might have been a sorceror whose magics appeared divine but were not actual miracles.

1.2.1.1.8. Pro: There is more historical evidence that Jesus existed than of many other men who existed before the printing press. This same Jesus professed to be God.
1.2.1.1.8.1. Con: We have documentation of the existence of Jim Jones, who almost 1000 people professed to be God, but clearly the existence of Jim Jones is not evidence of the existence of God.
1.2.1.1.8.1.1. Con: Because one person's claim is a lie does not have any bearing on another's claim. This statement neither supports nor undermines the parent claim.
1.2.1.1.8.2. Con: Outside of the bible there are only two references \(josephous and tacitus\) both of which have been shown to be forgeries.
1.2.1.1.8.2.1. Con: [This is factually incorrect.](http://www.rationalchristianity.net/jesus_extrabib.html)
1.2.1.1.9. Con: Jeremiah 31 establishes others won't die for your sins. "But every one shall die for his own iniquity: every man that eateth the sour grape, his teeth shall be set on edge." This is confirmed by Ezekiel 18:4 "The soul who sins will die." Jesus could not die for the sins of others according to God.
1.2.1.1.9.1. Pro: This is further clarified in Ezekiel 18:20 "The soul who sins shall die. The son shall not suffer for the iniquity of the father, nor the father suffer for the iniquity of the son. The righteousness of the righteous shall be upon himself, and the wickedness of the wicked shall be upon himself."
1.2.1.1.10. Con: Jesus Christ's existence in history is not evidence that a god exists. i.e.: If a woman bears a child, she can claim the father to be whomever she wants, that claim has no bearing on the actual father.
1.2.1.1.11. Con: Jesus Christ did exist, but there was a council \(The [First Council of Nicaea](https://www.livescience.com/2410-council-nicea-changed-world.html), in 325AD\) that decided on whether Jesus would be portrayed as human or divine \(was concluded as divine\). Therefore the existence of Jesus provides no direct proof of the existence of God.
1.2.1.1.12. Con: There is no evidence that Jesus Christ was a god.
1.2.1.1.12.1. Pro: Jesus is not a theistic god in the classical meaning of the word.
1.2.1.1.12.1.1. Con: Many Christians are classical theists, and they believe that Jesus is, in a nuanced way, a theistic God in this sense.
1.2.1.1.12.2. Con: -> See 1.2.1.1.3.1.2.
1.2.1.1.12.3. Con: Jesus claimed to be God, and others agreed. Testimony is evidence.
1.2.1.1.12.3.1. Con: Many human beings with followers who enthusiastically agree have claimed to be a god. There's no clear reason to accept Jesus's divinity but reject the others.
1.2.1.1.12.3.1.1. Con: Considering the unique impact of the person of Jesus Christ in history there is absolute reason to consider him as entirely different from any other.
1.2.1.1.12.4. Con: -> See 1.2.1.1.7.1.3.
1.2.1.1.12.5. Pro: Many people throughout history have claimed to be prophets, messiahs, gods, or otherwise, or been labeled so by others, but this does not make such claims true, regardless of the amount of people who believe it.
1.2.1.1.13. Pro: Jesus' disciples, who were witness to his life and miracles, identified him as being the Son of God. \(See Matthew 16 verses 13-17\)
1.2.1.1.13.1. Con: Nothing of Jesus' life that was written about him during his alleged lifetime has survived to this day.
1.2.1.1.13.2. Con: The disciples never wrote their own accounts of their experience of Jesus. The earliest copies of the books we now call the Gospels date to decades after the occurance of the proported events, and were not authored by the men whose names they are title after.
1.2.1.1.13.3. Con: Rather then reports of witnesses, what is provided is hearsay in the form of posthumously written texts.
1.2.1.1.13.4. Con: This testimony is only of a man performing some kind of miracles. There are [many examples](https://theconversation.com/tricking-the-brain-how-magic-works-56451) of miracles and other forms of magic being faked throughout history.
1.2.1.1.13.4.1. Pro: People today fake divine power and deeds on YouTube and Christian television shows.
1.2.1.1.13.4.1.1. Pro: Some [videos](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hqPc9btW1rM) have shown how fake healing shows generate illusory miracles.
1.2.1.1.13.4.2. Pro: Even in the information age, people people are still deceived by rather obvious and well known gimmicks \(for instance the [leg extention](https://youtube.com/watch?v=wW2IgE78qzQ) "miracles", which are [not miraculous](https://youtube.com/watch?v=wW2IgE78qzQ) at all\).
1.2.1.1.13.4.3. Pro: Even in cases where faking miracles is not intentional, a person's actions can be exaggerated and distorted by those who tell and retell the episode.
1.2.1.1.14. Con: Ultimately the role of God being fulfilled by Jesus doesn't even exist in the Bible and came about as a result of a conflict between the different sects of Christianity in ancient Rome.
1.2.1.1.14.1. Con: The Bible identifies Jesus as God. See [Hebrews 1:3](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Hebrews+1%3A3&version=NIV), [Hebrews 1:6-9](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Hebrews+1%3A6-9&version=NIV), John 14:8-11.
1.2.1.1.14.2. Con: The Bible identifies Jesus as being present and involved in the creation of the universe. See [Colossians 1:16–17](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Colossians+1%3A16%E2%80%9317&version=NIV), [John 1:1-5](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=john+1%3A1-5&version=ESV).
1.2.1.1.14.3. Con: The Bible identifies Jesus as having authority to forgive sins, while also qualifying that God forgives sins. See [Mark 2:1-12](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+2%3A1-12&version=NIV),  [Luke 7:47–50](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+7%3A47%E2%80%9350&version=NIV)
1.2.1.1.14.4. Con: The Bible identifies Jesus as being the judge of the living and dead. See [John 5:26-27](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=John+5%3A26-27&version=NIV), [Matthew 25:31-46](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+25:31-46).
1.2.1.1.14.5. Con: There is no historical evidence that intrachristian conflict in Rome actually led to a perversion of what the first Christians and their Scriptural authors believed.
1.2.1.1.14.5.1. Pro: [Christians believed in the Trinity prior to the Council of Nicaea](https://historyforatheists.com/2017/05/the-great-myths-4-constantine-nicaea-and-the-bible/).
1.2.1.1.14.5.1.1. Pro: In John 8:48-59, Jesus tells the Pharisees, "Before Abraham was, I AM". \(v58\)  "I AM" is a reference to the name of God given to Moses at the burning bush. The Pharisees knew exactly what he was saying, for in verse 59, they take up stones to stone him for blasphemy. John included this affirmation that Jesus was God long before Nicaea.
1.2.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.3.1.4.2.1.2.
1.2.1.3. Con: Historical and anecdotal evidence are both highly susceptible to bias, manipulation, tampering, accidental or purposeful translation errors, etc. This invalidates it for use in proving the existence of God.
1.2.1.3.1. Pro: The Torah was originally written in Sanskrit and not in Hebrew. The God of the Torah does not exist because the Hebrew word for God is a mistranslation of Sanskrit words meaning the Lunar Dynasty of ancient India. [See David's Note](https://www.facebook.com/DavidWLundman/posts/1901710463426158)
1.2.1.3.1.1. Con: Even if the Hebrew word for God is a mistranslation, it does nothing to prove that the Abrahamic God does not exist.
1.2.1.3.1.2. Con: There are religions other than those descended from Judaism that believe in the God of classical theism.
1.2.1.3.1.3. Con: Belief in the Torah, or in any particular religion, is not necessary for belief in a classical theistic God.
1.2.1.3.1.4. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.1.7.
1.2.1.3.1.5. Con: The view that the Torah was written in Sanskit is not a commonly accepted view, or at least not universal or conclusive enough to disregard the evidence it provides.
1.2.1.3.1.6. Con: Some parts of the Torah can be confirmed to be written in periods and cultures where Sanskrit would not have been written.
1.2.1.3.1.7. Con: Regardless of the supposed origin of a particular name, the actions described by the entity represented by it clearly represents traits which would qualify it as a classical theistic God.
1.2.1.3.2. Con: Universally ignoring historical and anecdotal evidence would imply an inability to learn anything from either history or eyewitness testimony.
1.2.1.3.2.1. Con: Being skeptical about historical and anecdotal evidence is not the same as ignoring it. From historical evidence of religious ideas we have learned much about culture, politics, and psychology.
1.2.1.3.2.1.1. Con: Historical evidence has a hard limit: scientific plausibility.
