1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2. Con: The contingency argument is self-defeating since it implies that God must also have a sufficient reason to exist.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1. Con: The Principle of Sufficient Reason suggests that everything requires a sufficient reason for its existence, but says nothing about whether this reason is internal or external to the thing itself.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1. Con: If God could be the sufficient reason for his own existence, so could the universe be it's own reason for existence.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1. Con: The universe requires a necessary cause whose nonexistence would be impossible. The universe itself is known to not be necessary, and its nonexistense is entirely conceivable.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.1. Con: There is no reason to assume God's nonexistence is impossible.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.1.2.2.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.2. Con: Although it is describable as an empty point in space with nothing, not even space, on all sides, minus the point, an actual conception of it's non-existence, and actual conception of nothing, is at the very least quite hard.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.1.2.2.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.6. Con: The universe does not require a cause.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.6.1. Pro: If there was no time before the universe, there was nothing causally prior to the universe.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.6.2. Pro: If the [b-theory of time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B-theory_of_time) is true the universe needs no cause.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.1.1.6.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.1.2. Con: It is circular reasoning to claim that something's existence is the reason for its existence.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.2.2. Con: God exists by the necessity of His own nature.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.3. Con: If accepted, this would only show that God is necessary when the universe exists, so God, in this case, is only as necessary as the universe is.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.3.1. Con: The "necessity" referred to in the contingency argument is modal necessity, meaning that God's nonexistence would be logically impossible - not that God is "necessary" to explain some data. Equating the two commits the [fallacy of equivocation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equivocation).
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.3.2. Con: God's necessity in the contingency argument is a premise which is defended separately, unrelated to the existence of the universe.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.3.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.1.2.2.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.3.3. Con: While an intelligent cause may exist without a particular effect, an effect cannot exist without a cause. DaVinci would have existed had he not painted the Mona Lisa; the Mona Lisa could not have existed without DaVinci.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4. Con: God, with the qualities of [Classical Theism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_theism), is not necessarily the root cause of the universe.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1. Con: If [evil](https://oregonstate.edu/instruct/phl201/modules/Philosophers/Augustine/augustine_evil.html) is a [privation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Privation) of good, then a thing that has no flaw is perfectly good by definition. The Unmoved Mover is Pure Act \(containing no potentiality\) and therefore is the perfect realization of its own essence. But if it is perfectly good, then it is "omni-benevolent."
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.1. Con: There is no reason to assume that good is the natural state that evil is a privation from.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.1.1. Pro: Evil has not been established to be accurately described as a mere lack of good. For example, if donating a billion dollars to a specific charity were good, failing to make that donation would not mean you are evil.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.2. Con: Flawlessness does not necessitate moral goodness.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.2.1. Con: Flawlessness by definition necessitates moral goodness. For if a thing was not morally good, how could that thing be flawless? Its lack of moral goodness would be a flaw. But if it truly is flawless, then it is necessarily morally good.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.2.1.1. Con: There are no mentions of moral goodness in the [definition for flawlessness](http://www.dictionary.com/browse/flawless). Furthermore, morally neutral things can be considered flawless.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3. Con: If evil is just a privation of good, then nothing is wrong. It could only be properly called "less good". This would mean that murder, rape, etc, are not wrong, they are simply less good.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.1. Con: The words "evil" and "wrong" still have meaning in the context of the privation view of evil. "Wrong" would refer to some action which corrupts the good. And evil could refer to a state of affairs strongly contrary to the good. But thinking of things as "less good" is in fact a more precise characterization, as it contains the privation aspect of evil, where the simple word "evil" can be misunderstood to imply a separately existing standard or entity by which evil is measured.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.1.1. Con: If things can only exist on different gradations of goodness, then rape and murder must be inherently good in some way. The fact that they exist does not make them good.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.1.1.1. Pro: If a god punishes things that are good, then the god lacks justice and is therefore not omnibenevolent.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.1.1.2. Con: Rape and murder are actions which corrupt that which is already good. To the extent they fail to completely destroy the goodness in a thing, they fail to be completely evil. But in order for a rape or murder to take place, there must be a living innocent to start with. There must be some good which is corrupted \(or destroyed\) by the evil act.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.1.1.2.1. Con: If it were true that things were evil solely because of the of good they destroyed, murdering an innocent 4 year old would be less wrong then murdering an old rapist, but these acts are equally wrong, therefore, it can not be true that things are evil solely because of the amount of good they destroy.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.1.1.2.2. Con: This implies that if a person were not innocent or good, that they cannot be raped.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.1.1.2.2.1. Con: Anyone being raped by definition is innocent. No one deserves rape. That's what rape is: undeserved, forced sex.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.1.1.2.3. Con: If rape and murder are evil, and rape and murder exist, then evil exists. If evil exists, it is not a mere lack of good.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.1.2. Con: If evil is contrary to "the good" then it cannot be considered a privation. It must be its own idea.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.3.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.2. Pro: Nothing is purely 100% totally "wrong" in this sense, because everything that exists, no matter how corrupted it be, still exists, and existence itself is good.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.2.1. Con: If things can only exist on different gradations of goodness, then rape and murder must be inherently good in some way. The fact that they exist does not make them good.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.3.3. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2. Con: If there is an Unmoved Mover at the root of all change in the universe, then that means every potentiality which is actualized, or even could be actualized is fundamentally derivative of that Mover. To be able to actualize every possible potentiality is just what it means to be "omnipotent."
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.1. Con: This kind of "omnipotence" need only be in place at the time of the universe's creation, and does not need to be an active force, making the Unmoved Mover a deistic God, rather than theistic.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.1.1. Con: The Unmoved Mover is not proved by reference to a chronological beginning of the world, but rather a hierarchical "beginning" of the world, which is here and now, at every moment of time in action.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.2.2.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.1.1.2. Pro: In fact, Aquinas considered that reason alone could not prove that the world had a beginning. Aristotle considered that the universe had always existed, yet he made a similar first-way [argument](http://www.logicmuseum.com/ontological/aristotleontological.htm) for the existence of God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.14.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.1.3. Con: The Unmoved Mover is outside of time \(eternal\), therefore it is present before the begining of time and has no end.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.2. Con: The ability to actualize is distinct from actualization. The ability to actualize requires decision making power, whereas intelligence is not required for actualization. The ultimate cause of the universe only requires actualization.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.3. Con: If there is NOT an Unmoved Mover at the root of all change in the universe, then that means no potentiality which is actualized, or even could be actualized is fundamentally derivative of such an Unmoved Mover. No such Unmoved Mover has been demonstrated to exist, let alone to exist and be omnipotent. If an Unmoved Mover does not exist, then it can have no direct effect on the real world and is therefore not omnipotent in reality.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.4. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.3. Pro: God being the likely \(but not certain\) cause of the universe does not prove the existence of God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.3.1. Con: An argument doesn't need to logically prove a claim to act as supporting evidence.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.14.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.5. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.2.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.6. Con: Free will exists within the universe, and can only be caused by another free will. Thus the thing which caused the universe had free will, and must be an intelligent being.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.6.1. Con: The assumption that things must come from things like them is false, as the rocks, and sticks that start a fire are not hot.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.6.2. Con: There is no objective evidence for the existence of free will in the universe, thus free will does not exist.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.6.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.17.2.6.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.6.2.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.6.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.6.2.3. Con: -> See 1.1.3.1.2.1.1.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.6.2.4. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.3.2.4.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.7. Pro: -> See 1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.8. Con: Possible causes of the universe other than God are extremely unlikely.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.8.1. Con: God as a cause of the creation of the universe is at least as extremely unlikely as any other of the extremely unlikely causes.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.8.2. Con: To accept one particular claim on the grounds that other claims appear unlikely is a false dichotomy \(there may be other claims we have not evaluated\) and a shifting of the burden of proof.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.9. Con: The law of entropy indicates that the cause of the universe must be infinite and completely lacking entropy.  If God's entropy is 0 and he is infinite than he must necessarily be good at least to the extent that entropy is defined as lack of order or chaos.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.9.1. Con: The law of entropy does not require the universe be infinite, or to lack entropy at the beginning.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.9.2. Con: Saying something could be the answer does not make it more likely then other scenarios.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.9.3. Con: There's no reason to believe the second law of thermodynamics matters in respect to metaphysical claims. It doesn't even necessarily affect all physical claims because we still haven't figured out how the laws of pshysics worked during the beginning of the universe.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.10. Con: Being the ultimate cause of everything that has a cause would be omnipotence in the purest form.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.11. Pro: Even if there were some force that pushed the universe to existence, there is no evidence it would resemble the humanity's understanding of the God rather than simply another law of physics. For example, there is no evidence that the force that created the universe has any knowledge of Earth or humans, or that it is even capable of such a thing.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.12. Pro: Just because something may have created the universe doesn't mean the force that did knows about humans or Earth in any way. Seeing the number of objects in space, it is incredibly unlikely.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.13. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.5.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.14. Con: There is an extensive amount of evidence suggesting the cause of the universe fits the properties of the classical God, as gathered by Aquinas in the [Summa Contra Gentiles](http://dhspriory.org/thomas/ContraGentiles1.htm).
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.15. Con: This is a red herring; even if true, it does not refute the claim it purports to refute.

It also ignores that these attributes of God are established by other arguments for the existence of God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.16. Con: Intelligence is suggested by the incredibly precise conditions found in the universe that allow life to exist.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.17. Con: The Kalam cosmological argument doesn't claim God is omnibenevolent.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.5. Pro: The universe is contingent \(meaning its nonexistence is possible\).
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.5.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.6. Pro: The existence of contingent things must ultimately be grounded in some necessary thing, or else they wouldn't exist at all. This necessary thing would be God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.6.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.1.2.2.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.6.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.6.3. Con: A necessary thing cannot alone give rise to a contingent thing. Since a necessary thing must in all its qualities and details exist, anything it alone gave rise to must likewise exist and is not contingent.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.6.3.1. Con: Contingent existence does not entail autonomous entities entirely separate from the necessary thing. A necessary thing can communicate complexity in its qualities and details without simple duplication. Contingent things share in those qualities and details, and do not possess them simply.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.7. Con: The contingency argument begins with a generalization about things that exist, namely that they require a reason for existence. However, the generalization is formed entirely based on knowledge of things that exist within the universe, bound by the laws of the universe. There is no basis for assuming that the laws that govern the things that make up the universe would also apply to the universe itself. This is the fallacy of composition.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.7.1. Con: The principle of sufficient reason only states that for every thing that exists, there exist or existed conditions sufficient for it to exist. This seems tautologically true - if conditions weren't sufficient for something to exist, then that thing wouldn't exist, by the definition of "sufficient."
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.8. Con: God's necessity is an assumption. It cannot be proven.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.8. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.9. Con: There is no need to resort to God to explain the changes we see, as a chain of mundane causes could stretch back into infinity.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.9.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.2.2.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.10. Con: There does not necessarily need to be an unmoved mover to explain efficient motion in the world. The world could be explained by circular causes.  For example, A cause B, B caused C, C caused D, and D caused A.  Reality could be structured that acts of potency change into acts of actuality in a circular fashion, hence an unmoved mover would not be necessary to explain original physical motion.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.10.1. Con: Such a structure of reality seems to require an infinitely-old universe with an infinite regression of causes and events. Such a regression is logically impossible, and stands at odds with scientific evidence for a finite universe.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.11. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.2.2.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.12. Pro: [This](https://docs.google.com/presentation/d/1c2V7wEJB5dFkLgDPqnnCE3rrFUGX80GH4EVFwoeiUY4/edit#slide=id.p) is yet another formulation of the argument in sequential fashion.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.12.1. Con: No proof is offered in this link unless we assume the nature of humans to be an arbitrarily defined philosophical entity; something which is virtually impossible to test scientifically or verify
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13. Con: An "Unmoved Mover" \(aka a being of Pure Actuality\) is not the same thing as God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.2. Con: The Unmoved Mover is by definition the the ultimate cause of the existence of things, and whatever else God is supposed to be, He is the ultimate cause of things.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.2.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.3. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.4.2.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.4. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.5.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.5. Con: This is an issue of semantics. Call it God, call it Great Green Arkleseizure, we are talking about the same thing.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.5.1. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.14.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.6. Pro: The Unmoved Mover and the First Cause describe a deistic god, not a classical theistic God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.6.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.6.2. Con: It is not necessary for a single line of evidence to completely describe all features of God. It is enough to say that the evidence supports the existence of a being that meets the classical existence of God. Other lines of evidence may supply more information of God's other attributes.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.6.3. Con: According to classical theism, God is the ["absolutely metaphysically ultimate being"](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Classical_theism). If a being is a First Cause, this this the same thing as being metaphysically ultimate, therefore the First Cause is God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.6.3.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.6.3.2. Con: The claim defines "absolutely metaphysically ultimate being" as simply the first cause. While the link defines classical theism differently by adding personhood and multiple attributes to the first cause that hasn't been demonstrated. Using the two interchangebly is a fallacy of equivocation.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.14. Con: This involves "change" and "motion" are used as a mere synonym for "coming into existence"/actualization. Since some things do not exist, this entails the Unmoved mover is not perfect, not omnipotent, or both. Therefore, the Unmoved mover is not God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2. Con: The neo-platonic model claims that God, a being that is all-powerful, all-knowing, and exists outside of time and space, is absolutely simple. This is contradictory.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1. Con: All that is meant by "absolutely simple" is that [God is without parts](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Divine_simplicity). The being of God is identical to the "attributes" of God. In other words, such characteristics as omnipresence, goodness, truth, eternity, etc. are identical to God's being. There is no contradiction inherent here.

In fact, an absolutely simple being is of necessity pure Actuality \(as opposed to potentiality\) and thus is necessarily all-powerful, all-knowing, immaterial, etc.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3. Con: Then an absolutely simple thing is anything that is, by definition, itself. This can be literally anything, making the argument pointless.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1. Con: Anything that is composed of potentiality and actuality is not simple. Which is \(nearly\) everything you interact with on a daily basis.

There is a simple test for simplicity: can the thing be logically divided into discrete parts?
If yes: it is not simple.
If no: it is simple.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.1. Con: Electrons, photons, quarks, and gluons all fit this definition. Claiming it is God is still unnecessary and arbitrary.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.1.1. Con: The parts of the atom are composed of two metaphysical parts: potentiality and reality. They are real to the extent that any given neutron is really present. But they are potential to the extent that any given neutron could be in a different location, or energy state, or really could be changed in any way. Since the parts of the atom are all changeable they are not metaphysically simple.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.1.1.1. Con: The definition of simple that was provided says nothing about not being changeable.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Changeability implies composition of the metaphysical parts: [act and potency.](http://edwardfeser.blogspot.com/2009/05/act-and-potency.html) Composition is contrary to the definition of simplicity.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: If Composition is contrary to simplicity and changeability means composition, then any description of god changing in any way is proof that god is not simple. God becoming his own son, or creating new laws at any point, for instance, point to god being changeable. Therefore, many descriptions of a god are changeable and cannot be considered simple.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.1.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.1.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.1.2. Con: Even the elementary subatomic particles may not be simple, but may be composed of simpler things, such as strings.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.1.2.1. Con: Then those simpler things are not composite.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.1.3. Con: It is fallacious to claim that because two objects share one property in common, that they must be identical.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2. Con: Many descriptions of God can be split into smaller parts.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.1. Con: While the statement "God is omnipotent" does not mean the same thing as "God is omniscient", it doesn't follow that they are not statements about the same one reality.

[Gottlob Frege](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Gottlob_Frege) famously distinguished between the ['sense' of an expression and its 'reference'](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Sense_and_reference).

"The evening star" does not have the same sense as "the morning star", but both expressions refer to one and the same thing--namely, the planet Venus. Similarly for descriptions of God.

\(from p 77 of [5 Proofs of the Existence of God](https://www.amazon.com/Five-Proofs-Existence-Edward-Feser-ebook/dp/B0754MJFMG/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&qid=1503973077&sr=8-1)\)
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.14.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.2. Con: When we talk about a human's power, or his intellect, we are talking about actually different things. But precisely because God is noncomposite \(lacking distinct parts\), we cannot apply these terms in exactly the same sense. We must apply them in an [analogical](http://www.iep.utm.edu/rel-lang/#SSH2b.iii) sense. \(as opposed to a [univocal](http://www.iep.utm.edu/rel-lang/#SSH2b.ii) or [equivocal](http://www.iep.utm.edu/rel-lang/#SSH2b.i) sense. [see also](http://www.philosophyzer.com/univocal-and-equivocal-language/)\)

Under an analogical use of language describing God, the many descriptions of Him fail to be split into smaller parts, as they all remain descriptions of the same thing.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.2.1. Pro: Equivocal Term: A term that has a variety of different meanings. An associate equivocal term has completely different intentions once it’s used. For instance, the term “Chihuahua” will signify \(a\) a breed of dog; \(b\) a state of Mexico.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.2.2. Pro: Univocal Term: A term that has only one meaning. That is, it signifies only one thought, and therefore corresponds to only one definition. Words mean the same thing even when applied in different contexts, for example, black shoes, black hair, black car.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.2.3. Pro: Analogous Term: A term that is supposed to convey one or additional similar characteristics that exist between two ideas.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.2.4. Con: Premises 2 and 3 of the Analogous terms are unsupported.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.3. Pro: Brahman in Hinduism is split into smaller gods.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.3.1. Con: If Brahman can be split, he is not simple, ergo he is not the God of classical theism.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.3.1.1. Con: This description of a god meets the criteria for the definition of a god under the classical theistic definition. Therefore, it is fair to discuss.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.4. Pro: In Catholicism, the Holy Trinity is composed of the Father, Son, and Holy Spirit.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.4.1. Con: Whether the Trinitarian description of God compromises the absolute simplicity of God is another debate entirely. This debate is about the classical theistic conception of God, which is not exclusive to the Catholic Church.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.4.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.3.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.2.5. Con: A description of an entity is not the entity itself.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.3.1.3. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.2.1.4. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.3. Con: Aquinas's conclusion only uses the word god as an end point. There is no justification for its use in his argument.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.3.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.2.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.2. Pro: The cosmological arguments add additional details to a "rule" so that God can be excluded from that rule e.g. "everything that exists had a cause for its existence" becomes "everything that begins to exist has a cause for its existence".
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.2.1. Con: There is no reason to make a distinction between things that exist and things that began to exist, other than to tailor-fit the pre-conceived conclusion that God exists.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.2.1.1. Con: On the contrary... the difference between necessary and [contingent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contingency_%28philosophy%29) objects was recognized and discussed by the ancient philosophers. The added phrase is a clarification that is required to allow for the very existence of necessary objects.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.3. Con: The argument from the act/potency divide \(at least in its [valid](http://www.aquinasonline.com/Topics/firstway-analysis.pdf) [form](https://proofs.herokuapp.com/theorems/63)\) does not commit special pleading, because it does not "state that all things have a cause." What it says is that any *change* requires a cause \(where change is when something goes from potential to actual\).

This is very different from saying that *everything whatsoever* has a cause.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.3.1. Con: Stating that every change has a cause does not necessarily require a first cause, as there can be an infinite chain of causes in an infinite universe.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.3.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.2.2.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.3.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.3.3. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.5.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.4. Con: The properties of God defined in the OP indicate God is "totally independent of space-time" and therefore would never begin to exist or require a creator because a beginning is time-dependent. It is not special pleading to adhere to the definition of terms specified
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.5. Con: If God created time \(the existence of a beginning and an end\), then it stands to reason that God lives outside of these bounds and has no beginning nor end. Therefore it is not Special Pleading and is merely a logical characteristic of the creator of time.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.3. Con: The argument presupposes that something non-composite and immaterial can cause something material to come into existence.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.3.1. Con: The argument does not "presuppose" that something non-composite and immaterial can cause something material to come into existence, but rather the argument \(purports to\) shows that something non-composite and immaterial can cause something material.

The difference is in assuming as a premise or in accepting as a conclusion.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.4. Con: Composition is a mental abstraction. No composition can be said to be 'caused' by anything because it is not an event. Things may become stuck together or even amalgamated into something new but seeing them as a composite is a mental shift, not a change in the real world.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.5. Con: Max Tegmark's 'Mathematical Universe' argues that the foundational 'component' of composite things is mathematics, not God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.5.1. Con: The proof from composition states that composite things are caused by, not composed of, God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.6. Con: Subatomic particles form composites, including more complex subatomic particles, as well as composites known as atoms, due to natural affinities caused by natural forces according to the natural laws of physics.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.6.1. Con: These "natural forces" or "laws of physics" would then be the \(intermediate\) cause which keeps composite entities together. If the laws themselves are in some way composite, then they too require a cause. If they are not composite, then they are absolutely simple, which has been argued elsewhere to equate to God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.6.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.6.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.2.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.6.1.3. Con: Natural causes are still natural causes regardless of whether they are composite natural causes or non-composite natural causes.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.6.1.4. Con: Simply because it has been claimed elsewhere that God is absolutely simple and implied that God is the only such being, does not mean that anything absolutely simple has been shown elsewhere to equate to God. Claims are neither proof nor evidence. They are claims.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.7. Con: Changes can accumulate within a complex environment of interacting atoms and molecules. If chemicals accumulating are inorganic, then this is an example of inorganic evolution. If they are organic, then this is an example of organic evolution. If they are molecules which carry mutable information inherited by any biological organism, this accumulation of inherited change is an example of biological evolution. This explains how composites form naturally, not supernaturally.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.7.1. Con: Despite the [numerous experiments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miller–Urey_experiment), and even assuming the conditions in which they were preformed matched the conditions of the early earth \(rather than favoring positive results\), they all failed to produce life out of lifeless matter.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8. Pro: A composite thing exists at any moment only insofar as its parts are combined at that moment.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.1. Con: Such composites can naturally form, naturally evolve, and naturally break down, but have no existence separate from their parts, so it is merely their current state and not their current existence which depends upon their current combination of parts.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2. Pro: This composition of parts requires a concurrent cause. \(It requires some outside thing or things to cause the parts to be combined.\)
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.1. Con: Not all composites require concurrent causes.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.1.1. Con: No composite entity can be named which does not require a concurrent cause or series of causes to hold it together. Thus, all composites require concurrent causes.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.1.1.1. Con: The universe is a named entity which does not require a concurrent cause.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.1.1.1.1. Con: It isn't known with certainty that the universe has no concurrent cause. Such a cause may exist and be called God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.1.1.2. Con: This sentence required non-concurrent keystrokes to be typed.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.1.1.2.1. Con: The cause of non-concurrent keystrokes is a concurrent brain state including an idea, the desire to communicate it, a plan for how to communicate it using a keyboard, and an intention to complete the plan.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2. Pro: Any composite thing has a cause of its existence at any moment during which it exists.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.1. Con: No moment to moment cause of existence is needed for a stable composite to continue existing. Both the formation of and stability of composites are well understood natural phenomena.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.1.1. Con: To the extent that a "stable composite" depends for its existence on the continued action of the laws of nature, that composite is caused from moment to moment by they continued action of the laws of nature. Thus a moment to moment cause is present in every physical composite object.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.1.1.1. Pro: Consider the example of a simple stone at rest. The stone is a composite of many molecules, all of which are held together by the continually acting laws of chemistry.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2. Pro: Each of the things of our experience has a cause at any moment during which it exists. \(Everything we see is being caused as we see it\)
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.1. Con: Some parts of nature are very interactive and some parts of nature are very stable, but continuation of existence does not require an unchanging form. Just because something changes enough that humans might choose different labels or descriptions for it does not mean that anything ceased to exist. Composite things form and break down. These are well understood natural events, not mysterious supernatural events.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.1.1. Con: It is a red herring to mention "unchanging form" in the context of the argument from composition, because that argument makes no mention of "unchanging form."
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.1.2. Con: The argument from composition does not talk about things forming or breaking down, whether "naturally" or supernaturally. It only depends on one instant of any single composite entity existing.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2. Pro: If the cause of a composite thing's existence at any moment is itself composite, then it will in turn require a cause of its own existence at that moment.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.1. Con: Such claims of composites needing a cause of existence are unsubstantiated.

Changing or retaining a given form will not make something cease to exist, so claims of needing moment to moment causes of existence are nonsensical.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.1.1. Con: It is not unsubstantiated to say that composite things require a cause. In fact, the whole argument from composition purports to show why they need such a cause, and why that cause must be simple and non-composite. It will not do to merely declare the argument to be unsubstantiated without pointing out a flaw in the premise, or in the reasoning from point to point.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2. Pro: The regress of causes this entails is hierarchical in nature \(a per se causal chain\), and such a regress must therefore have a first member.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.2.2.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.2. Con: Such a causal chain is not a chain of caused existence but of cause change.  Change happens. Change accumulates. When change accumulates, that is called evolution. Evolution does not require a starting point but merely something in which change can accumulate.

No God required.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3. Pro: Only something absolutely simple or non-composite could in principle be the first member of such a series.

So, the existence of each of the things of our experience presupposes an absolutely simple or non-composite cause.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.1. Con: There are zero scientifically validated examples of a thinking entity which is more simple than a hydrogen atom, which is a complex composite of subatomic particles. Therefore it would be ludicrous to assume from that claim that one could logically conclude such a hypothetical first cause to be capable of thought. Therefore, it could not be a God, at least in the classical theology sense.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.1.1. Con: God is immaterial. Science confines its study and its methods to only what portions of the material world are measurable. Therefore the lack of "scientifically validated examples" is irrelevant. It is like expecting a yardstick to be able to gauge what something sounds like. Science is the wrong tool for the job of analyzing God. Science is the right tool for analyzing His creation.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.1.1.1. Con: If there is no way to measure a god then there is no reason to believe a god exists.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.1.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.2.1.2.1.3.2.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2. Pro: God is this absolutely [simple or non-composite](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Divine_simplicity) cause of all composite things.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.1. Con: No justification is given as to why the argument determines that the non-composite entity is God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.2. Pro: A pure deterministic cause of everything could not produce a world with randomness. A pure random cause of everything could not produce a world with order. A pure will can produce a world with a mix of determined, random and free causes. If the absolute cause of this world is simple, then it has to be a pure will.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.3. Con: To define a God of classical theology to be basically the simplest thing imaginable is to define away everything that defines it as a God according to classical theology. There are no known mechanisms by which an unchanging nondescript simple thing with no moving parts can accomplish anything at all. That's not a God. It's more like an infinitesimal neutrino field without even a neutrino. Basically as close to nothing as anything can be. You might as well say God is nothing.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.3.1. Con: The classical definition of God historically includes [Divine Simplicity](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Divine_simplicity).
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.3.2. Con: [Divine Simplicity](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Divine_simplicity) implies many of the other features of God:
There can be only one absolutely simple thing. The absolutely simple thing must be unchanging \(no potentiality\). Something devoid of potentiality must inherently be immaterial, for material implies potential. Thus already we have Oneness, Unchanging, Immateriality, all common features of the classical definition of God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.3.2.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.5.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.3.2.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.3.3. Con: [Divine Simplicity](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Divine_simplicity) does not imply smallness. It actually implies immateriality which makes the concept of physical size inapplicable.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.3.4. Con: To be absolutely [simple](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Divine_simplicity) is a very different thing from nothing. Existence is absolutely contrary to non-existence. There are no "gradations" of existence. Something either exists or it does not.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.3.5. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.5.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.3.6. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.2.4. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.13.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.3. Con: This argument is fallacious because it assumes a single first cause instead of multiple first causes.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.3.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.4.2.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.3.2. Con: Ockham's razor implies that one should not unnecessarily multiply causes.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.4. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.3.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.3. Con: The evolution of a composite is merely the result of the composite accumulating changes and is a well understood process which does not require existence to be caused at each moment in time. Anything which changes has its former state and the change as the causes of its new state. No moment to moment cause of existence is needed for a composite to change or to be in its current state.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.3. Con: A composite can change and accumulate changes without ceasing to exist and therefore does not depend upon its combination of parts at any given moment for its existence but rather depends on its evolution for its current state.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.9. Con: Composite things are not "caused by" simpler things, but "composed of" simpler things. For example, if a human being produces a teardrop, that teardrop is composed of things which are smaller and simpler than itself but it was certainly not caused by something simpler than itself. The claim made effectively argues that God is simple and practically powerless but that there are lots of him from which to make stuff. That's not the God of classical theology.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.9.1. Con: The argument from composition does not claim that God is "practically powerless," or that there are "lots of him." The argument in fact claims that the God of classical theism, who is [absolutely simple](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Divine_simplicity), [omnipotent](https://www.infogalactic.com/info/Omnipotence), and one \(singular\), exists.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.9.1.1. Con: The claim made from an argument doesn't protect the argument from alternate or additional implications in it's structure.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.9.2. Con: The argument does not claim that composite objects are caused by simpler things, but that they are caused by simple parts being held together by some thing or things outside of the composite object.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.9.2.1. Con: Then that claim would disagree with physics, since the forces which hold such composites together are carried by the parts.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.10. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.11. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.8.2.2.2.2.2.3.3.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.12. Pro: [This](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=oYZ7Hz99xjE) is another formulation of the proof of God's existence from the composition of things.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.4. Con: The Cosmological arguments commit the [composition fallacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fallacy_of_composition).
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.5. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.6. Con: The universe and everything in it is part of an infinite cycle of probabilistic events.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.7. Con: Cosmological arguments arrive at a God that is not shown to be necessary. One could posit any sufficient entity and it would be just as valid. Universe creating pixies that disappear after creating the cosmos are just as sufficient.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.7.1. Con: Any sufficient entity, such as a universe-creating pixie, would be considered God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.7.2. Con: The Aristotelian argument from the Act/Potency divide does in fact show that a god meeting the formal definition of God, is necessary.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.7.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.8. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.1.4.3.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.9. Con: The cosmological argument involves [special pleading](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_pleading). This is granting God an unexplained exemption from the rule: all things must have a creator.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.9.1. Con: The cosmological argument doesn't have to special plead \(i.e. make an exception for God without explanation\). It claims that members of an established class of things \(observed things, moving things, etc.\) require other things to exist, not that all things do. Thus it can conclude that something outside of the class must ground the existence of all members of the class, since each member is in need of grounding by virtue of being in the class, even if it grounds other members.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.9.1.1. Con: These examples still constitute special pleading. By making all of existence one "established class" and god another, you're still making him an exception.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.10. Con: If a creator of the Universe has always existed, it has existed in infinite time prior to the Universe. That which exists in the infinite past would need infinite time to reach the present, thus it never would.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.10.1. Con: "Infinite time prior" is nonsensical. It describes something that cannot exist, and is not a part of any cosmological argument.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.10.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.1.2.4.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.10.2. Con: God is typically understood to be outside time; to have created time. As such he has no beginning. He just is. See [Psalm 90:2](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Psalm%2090:2)
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.10.2.1. Con: It seems an event, even a thought, requires time. It's doubtful things of the scantest duration can happen or be caused to happen in a timeless ether.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.10.2.1.1. Con: The decision to create was co-incidental with the act of creation.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.10.2.2. Con: Using a Bible reference to support a cosmological argument seems incongruous. The Bible does not accurately describe the cosmos. In Genesis 1, the cosmos is water into which the sky is centrally placed, and two great lights are placed in the sky to rule day and night \(the moon produces light\).
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.10.2.3. Con: Having God be outside of time requires us to have numerous assumptions on how that could work
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.10.3. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.5.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.11. Con: Cosmological arguments commit the [God of the gaps](https://rationalwiki.org/wiki/God_of_the_gaps) fallacy.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12. Pro: There are various formulations of the [Kalam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kalam_cosmological_argument) cosmological argument for God's existence.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2. Pro: If the universe has a cause, the best candidate for that cause is the God of classical theism
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1. Pro: The law of [conservation of mass](http://www.infogalactic.com/info/Conservation_of_mass) states that matter cannot be created or destroyed. Given that matter does exist, and must have come into existence at some point, its cause must have broken this law and thus transcend the laws of physics. Such a cause could be considered God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.1. Con: Inflationary models of the universe posits a material that has a density that is "hard to dilute" \(Max Tegmark, The Mathematical Universe\). Such a material is a material with a negative pressure, which results in gravity having a repulsive effect \(instead of an attractive one\). When such a material expands, it gains mass from gravity \(under E=mc^2\).
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.2. Con: Conservation of mass does not imply conservation of matter since matter can be converted to and from energy. 

It is an error to claim that the law of conservation of mass is that "Matter cannot be created or destroyed." 

Therefore any argument based on such a claim is invalid.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.3. Con: Assuming that any massive change happened in a moment implies that natural processes had no time to take place, but there is no reason here to make such an assumption with regard to the formation of matter since science had proven that the process of matter being converted to and from energy has not in fact stopped happening.

The existence of God is not supported by the claim that "there was a moment when all of the matter in the universe came into existence", as it still happens naturally.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.4. Con: The event known as the Big Bang is not accurately described as "matter that was shot outward" but rather as "energetic space that rapidly expanded" and in fact evidence indicates that it still continues to expand.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.4.1. Pro: Well known physical phenomena such as the formation of particles from in high energy space, the emergence of virtual particles in low energy space, and Hawking radiation, are various ways that matter is known to form, without any supernatural intervention required.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.5. Con: We do not know where all the matter/energy and space/time originally came from. 
So one cannot claim that a God is responsible for this without arguing for \(or claiming to know\) the unknowable.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.5.1. Con: It does not follow that because some people believe that we do not know X, it is not possible to know X.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.6. Con: \>Matter/energy cannot be created or destroyed.
\>God created all of matter and energy, and this is how everything began.

This two statements contradict each other, unless you say God is just naturally omnipotent and therefore is simply just capable of doing this, but saying so contradicts the first statement. Can matter/energy be created/destroyed or can't it?
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.6.1. Con: These two statements do not contradict for the obvious reason that the first statement is clearly meant to apply to the laws of nature, which by definition are transcended by a supernatural God.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.7. Con: There are [processes](http://www.nytimes.com/1990/08/21/science/new-direction-in-physics-back-in-time.html?pagewanted=all) through which, in rare circumstances, matter can be created from a vacuum, or nothing, depending upon what we define as nothing.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.7.1. Con: These processes are still part of a physical, natural order which demands explanation.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.8. Con: This ignores other possibilities such as the matter always existing, a multiverse, or  any other natural or sufficient causes \(quantum fluctuations, universe creating pixies\). The conclusion that it must be God requires more evidence.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.8.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.7.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.1.8.2. Con: All of these solutions \(except pixies\) rely on the existence of a natural, physical order, which brings the question back to why such an order is in place. By definition, anything which can be the cause of the natural order is supernatural.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.3. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.4. Pro: The cause of the universe \(i.e. all time and space\) must have a will in order to be able to exist in a timeless state and act as the cause of a single universe \(or finite number of universes\).
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.4.1. Pro: A non-personal cause of the universe \(e.g. quantum vacuum fluctuation\) can only create an infinite number of universes or no universe. This is because a non-personal cause is defined such that it always acts when a set of sufficient antecedent conditions is met. In a timeless state, if those conditions are ever met, then they are always met and such a non-personal cause would generate an infinite number of universes.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.4.1.1. Con: We have no way to determine whether there are or are not infinite universes besides our own.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.4.2. Pro: Our first hand experience of our own actions evidences that while antecedent causes play a necessary and often pivotal role in the actions we take, they are not alone sufficient as in the end we must finally choose to will the actions we take, where this willing is not determined by those antecedent causes
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.4.2.1. Con: This boils down to the assertion that free will exists, with an argument that it does, and whether free will exists remains disputed.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.5. Pro: If the cause of the universe has a will, then it must be a person since there are no other candidates which possess a will
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.2.6. Pro: The cause of the universe must be timeless, spaceless, and immaterial
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3. Pro: Anything that "begins to exist" \(defined [here](https://www.reasonablefaith.org/writings/scholarly-writings/the-existence-of-god/must-the-beginning-of-the-universe-have-a-personal-cause-a-rejoinder/) at definition A5\), must have a cause that explains how the thing came to exist
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.1. Con: The phrase "begins to exist" cannot be adequately defined such that it applies to the universe but not to God \(see [Bobier 2013](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11841-013-0362-4) or this [blog](http://philosophicaldisquisitions.blogspot.com/2015/08/beginning-to-exist-and-kalam.html) summarizing the paper\)
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.2. Con: Using the above linked definition of 'begins to exist', the universe did not begin to exist. Time exists within the universe \(as a direction in spacetime\), and not the other way around. Therefore it doesn't make sense to say 'The universe exists at time t.'
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.2.2.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.3. Con: The law of causality works within the universe, where time exists.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.3.1. Pro: Causes and effects are events in the universe. They occur at a given time, in a given place. There is no event where and when 'The universe began to exist'.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.3.2. Con: Cosmologists who consider multiverse theory assume that laws of causality still hold outside of our universe in the larger multiverse. In other words, they see no reason to offer a defense of the laws of causality when moving to a contexts where the laws of physics might be entirely different. This is suggestive that we all intuitively think of the law of causality as a metaphysical principle that applies to any physical reality and not just to our particular universe.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.4. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.1.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.5. Con: There is no support for this argument beyond human intuition, and human intuition is known to be unreliable in many fields of study.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.5.1. Con: The support for all beliefs, philosophical and scientific, eventually work their way down to fundamental intuitions for which no external evidence can be offered.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.5.2. Con: While one can safely question/disregard the philosophical intuitions of a single person or small group of people, to give no credence to the shared philosophical intuitions of large groups of sincere thinkers, if not the majority of thinkers in this case, undermines rational inquiry.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.5.2.1. Pro: "All things that begin to exist require a cause" is assumed true in all other other notable contexts. In other words, there is no independent case in either philosophy or science where this principle is not assumed true by the majority
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.5.3. Con: This fails to distinguish gut reactions \(e.g. this person seems untrustworthy\) and intuitive speculation \(e.g. the stock market is going to go up\) from philosophical intuitions \(e.g. the people I experience have minds like my own\). Most evidence of "human intuition" being unreliable covers only the first two cases.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.6. Pro: The rejection of this principle undermines science as it becomes acceptable to hypothesize that a given phenomena \(e.g. unexpected galaxy rotation curves, species distributions across continents, changes in atmospheric temperatures\) lacks a cause
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.7. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.3.5.2.1.
1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.13. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.
1.2.2.4.1.2. Con: "God exists" is not a falsifiable hypothesis, and therefore not viable to proper research. For a hypothesis to be falsifiable, there has to be a "if A were to be discovered, that would disprove the hypothesis". For the hypothesis that God doesn't exist, A could be for example an unadulterated video recording of God in his true \(distinguishable from human\) form. For the inverse hypothesis, there is no possible A where "if A is found, the hypothesis is disproven".
1.2.2.4.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.2.1.2.2.
1.2.2.4.2. Pro: In investigating a hypothesis, there is always a margin of error. In scientific research, this margin of error \(most often 5%\) is preset to determine when a result, until falsified, can be considered evidence. This means that even if you perform research in the most stringent of settings, you will have 5% false positives \(i.e. falsehoods retained as evidence\). So by that factor alone some evidence could virtually be found to support any  hypothesis, regardless of truth.
1.2.2.4.3. Con: Relational claims contingent on the character of being are not subject to strict prediction testing. E.g. I might say "If John loves Mary, John will buy her flowers," but that is only a statement of probablility, and not subject to the same predictable requirements of natural science. The same is true with the predictions we have about a personal God.
1.2.2.4.3.1. Con: The primary thesis of this discussion does not claim that the god in question is a "personal God".
1.2.2.5. Pro: Human desires, like hunger and thirst, come from the biological drive to seek something in the world \(e.g. water and food\). As there is a desire to believe in God it is likely that this desire exists because God is a real entity.
1.2.2.5.1. Con: Some people have the craving to engage in intercourse with tentacle monsters. This does not mean tentacle monsters who could satisfy this desire exist.
1.2.2.5.1.1. Con: A sexual desire suggests a sexual need. They may think that they require a "tentacle monster" but that need could be filled by a man or woman \(depending on persuasion\). Sexual needs prove the existence of something that can fulfill that need. We may be mistaken about what satisfies our need, but there is a thing of some form that will satisfy that need. It does not prove which God exists, just that a god exists in some form.
1.2.2.5.1.1.1. Con: The thing that fulfills our craving for something beyond the physical world need not be God.
1.2.2.5.1.1.1.1. Con: Even if it is not God in the sense that we see Him, the divine does in form exist. Food can not satisfy thirst, and water can not satisfy hunger. If we hunger for the divine, then the divine must exist because only the divine can satisfy it.
1.2.2.5.1.1.1.1.1. Con: We could think we have some hunger for the divine, but instead just have some existential angst, or desire to know our place in a cruel, cold universe. We can misinterpret what our desires point to.
1.2.2.5.1.1.1.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.14.
1.2.2.5.1.1.2. Con: If "We may be mistaken about what satisfies our need," then we may also be mistaken that the desire to believe in god will be satisfied by a god. It may be satisfied by further understanding of the universe, or it may even be conquered as we discover that we can reprogram our thinking to realize we don't actually need a god to live our lives and be happy, morally strong citizens of the world.
1.2.2.5.2. Con: These desires can be explained in neurological terms without the existence of an unseen realm.
1.2.2.5.2.1. Con: We developed a sense of hunger because we need food. This is a neurological function yet we do not disregard the reality that food must exist. Otherwise we would not have developed a sense of hunger. Reducing something to a neurological function does not invalidate the reality of the need or desire nor the object that will satisfy it.
1.2.2.5.2.1.1. Con: We can actually sate hunger, and we developed hunger because we can sate it. On the other hand, something we seek but cannot find is not comparable.
1.2.2.5.2.1.2. Pro: The fact that we hunger for something does not suggest we will be satisfied, just that the object exists. A person stuck on an island will be hungry, but may starve to death. It is possible some people may never see God even though they have a yearning for what is beyond our world.
1.2.2.5.3. Pro: The inherent requirement and craving for God apparent in virtually every culture suggests an evolutionary benefit for acceptance of God at the least and a priori existence of God can be inferred.
1.2.2.5.3.1. Con: God was created for a craving by humans,  implying that god is only a craving and doesn't actually exist.
1.2.2.5.3.1.1. Con: C.S. Lewis pointed out in "Mere Christianity" that we thirst, and there is water. We hunger, and there is food. For every "craving" we have, there exists an object of that craving.

It is illogical to believe that we have a craving for something that does not exist. Far from being evidence that God does not exist, mankind's craving to know his creator is evidence that our Creator is there.
1.2.2.5.3.2. Con: The selection pressure to conform to religious belief / practice was man-made through punishment, isolation, and other disadvantages. This had nothing to do with God's existence.
1.2.2.5.3.3. Con: If anything, this evolutionary benefit would imply that only those who have a tendency to be gullable or compliant are more likely to survive, this can in no way be considered to be real, acceptable evidence for the existence of God.
1.2.2.5.3.4. Con: Humans evolved to be superstitious, because this conferred a survival benefit.  This does not mean everyone's superstitious—be they God-shaped or not—are real.
1.2.2.5.3.5. Con: The "Requirement and craving for God" is more likely a craving for understanding of the unknown, which is then filled with a created "God" because it is convenient to give the answer: "God made it that way," and be satisfied.
1.2.2.5.3.6. Con: With each generation indoctrinating the next well before critical thinking develops, it becomes ingrained, and is as much cultural as religious. It is a "requirement," especially in religiously homogeneous regions. The existence of a tradition alone, does not prove its worth, or that it is logical.
1.2.2.5.4. Con: The cravings for something beyond the physical are not universal in the way hunger and thirst are universal.  Their absence have no verifiable ill effects on those lacking them.  This is seen in interviews of atheists and agnostics who feel no particular spiritual drive and none the less lead perfectly fulfilling, happy lives.
1.2.2.5.5. Con: That "something beyond the physical exists" does not necessarily equate to "God."
1.2.2.5.6. Con: The analogy between food and spiritual desires is weak, which invalidates the argument.

The desire for food and the desire for something "beyond the physical world" are too dissimilar.
1.2.2.5.6.1. Pro: Food is needed for subsistence while something "beyond the physical world" is a convenient explanation for how the world works \(or for what happens after death\).
1.2.2.5.6.2. Pro: Desire for food is seen throughout the animal kingdom while the desire for something "beyond the physical world" is seen only in humans.
1.2.2.5.7. Con: Biological drives evolve because the drive itself provides a survival benefit. That benefit need not be tied to the physical existence of something. Otherwise, all superstitions would be real.
1.2.2.5.8. Con: Evolved characteristics can be superfluous or be misused.
1.2.2.5.8.1. Pro: A dog licks it's wounds even when it is [incorrect](https://www.petmd.com/blogs/dailyvet/2009/december/07-5101) to do so.
1.2.2.5.8.2. Pro: Cancer is a case of misuse of evolutionary traits \(such as cell growth, replication, irrigation\).
1.2.2.5.9. Con: Paul Kurtz argued that because nature is full of chaos and impermanence, it can cause great unease. Some seek to counter that with something stable and eternal. This is what "God" gives them; he's a safe harbor in a storm.
1.2.2.5.10. Con: Deducing God must have been there because some people search for Him is like deducing thirst creates water without ever having seen any water. \([Post hoc ergo propter hoc fallacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post_hoc_ergo_propter_hoc)\)
1.2.2.5.11. Con: This argument is perfect example of confirmation bias.
1.2.2.6. Con: According to most God beliefs, God is described as a separate entity that exists outside of time and space.  Such a claim can never be demonstrated as true by its own definition, since we can only demonstrate things that exist in time and space.
1.2.2.6.1. Con: In the Judeo-Christian tradition while God transcends time and space he can be manifest in time and space as made explicit in the incarnation of the Son of God.
1.2.2.6.2. Con: The laws of logic transcend time and space, yet we must use them to make a claim on this board. This defeats the idea that "we can only demonstrate things that exist in time and space".
1.2.2.6.2.1. Con: The laws of logic describe things we know to be true. 'The universe does not conform to logical absolutes because someone thought them up and is holding reality to that standard. These absolutes exist [purely to describe patterns that we have observed as true in reality](http://www.atheistrepublic.com/blog/arminnavabi/do-laws-logic-prove-existence-god)' \(in time and space\).
1.2.2.6.2.1.1. Con: Because the described patterns exist in time and space does not imply that the laws of logic exist in time and space.
1.2.2.6.3. Con: The existence of a thing that does not exist in time and space can be inferred. It can also be used as the basis of entirely real world physical constructions. The square root of minus 1, for example, has no physical reality, yet as a concept, is required in the construction of [electric circuits](https://www.quora.com/in/What-are-the-main-practical-uses-of-i) \(and therefore most machines\).
1.2.2.7. Con: The existence or non-existence of God cannot be established through the scientific process.
1.2.2.7.1. Con: The existence or non-existence of any being can be established as much as anything else. God is not a special case in that sense. There have been various testable predictions about God and they have failed.
1.2.2.7.1.1. Pro: [Prayer studies](http://www.nytimes.com/2006/03/31/health/31pray.html) are evidence that a God who intervenes to answer prayers does not exist.
1.2.2.7.1.1.1. Con: It was said, that it wasn't sure how much prayers did control group get from family and friends, therefore it is hard to determine whether there wasn't possible impact of prayer on that group.
1.2.2.7.1.1.2. Con: Not necessarily. This claim is based on the premise that if God exists, He would by necessity answer to prayers in a prayer study, which is not self-evident.
1.2.2.7.1.1.2.1. Pro: This study shows that God to which people adress preyers doesn't intervene, not that there is no such God - they might have been praying to the wrong one.
1.2.2.7.1.1.3. Con: There are well documented cases of [scientifically inexplicable recoveries after prayer](http://www.is-there-a-god.info/life/tenhealings/).
1.2.2.7.1.2. Pro: [Biological evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution) demonstrates that a God that directly created [humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adam_and_Eve#Historicity), animals and plants does not exist.
1.2.2.7.1.3. Pro: According to [1 Kings 18:24](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1%20Kings%2018:24) God would answer in very specific ways when you call on his name.
1.2.2.7.1.4. Pro: According to the [Mark 16:18](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+16%3A18&version=NIV) if God existed people who believed in Him would be able to drink deadly poison and not be hurt at all. That has been [demonstrated false](http://www.pulse.ng/gist/5-church-members-die-after-pastor-made-them-drink-rat-poison-id6253951.html).
1.2.2.7.1.4.1. Con: Not everything included in Bible is literal in eyes of every Christian. Therefore proving that some fragment is not literaly true is not enough to show that main thoughts of Bible are false.
1.2.2.7.1.4.2. Con: Even if it would prove the non-existence of Christian God it is not a proof for the non-existence of a classical God, which is the topic of this discussion.
1.2.2.7.1.4.3. Con: The linked incident does not meet the conditions that are described in Mark. Notably, they violate scriptures [prohibiting tempting the Lord](https://www.kingjamesbibleonline.org/Matthew-4-7/), or attempting to make Him perform just to see a miracle \(Matthew 4:7\).
1.2.2.7.1.4.4. Con: Acording to [Luke 17:6](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+17%3A6&version=NIV) if we would have faith as a mustard seed we could move mountains; even greatest saints haven't done that, therefore it is hard to determine whether people included have enough faith.
1.2.2.7.1.5. Con: There are mathematical theories which prove that some theories can't be proven nor disproven. Same could apply to some establishing existence of some being.
1.2.2.7.1.5.1. Pro: [Undecidable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Undecidable_problem?wprov=sfla1) problems cannot be solved.
1.2.2.7.2. Pro: The broad world-view establishes and limits the uses of Occam's razor vis the problem. [Gray, Scott David \(2017\), Musings on the Limitations of Science](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1ruEIedns9d3Df902EDR2gWvzPRvjhRvlqSHn1LG770I/edit)
1.2.2.7.2.1. Con: Source cited appears to be unreliable.
1.2.2.7.2.2. Con: Occam's razor does not depend on a persons view, but on the number of required assumptions.
1.2.2.7.3. Pro: Even if we could prove everything logically, then still there is place for God. There would be no faith without doubt, so God could have created world in such way that finding proof for his existence is impossible, to give us possibility of believing - not knowing for sure.
1.2.2.7.4. Pro: Science has room for the unknown, and certain metaphysical issues are resistant / inaccessible to scientific investigation.
1.2.2.7.4.1. Pro: Every supposed act of God / miracle etc. could be interpreted as something not yet understood from a scientific perspective. Neither side could disprove the other, because of the limitations of our scientific knowledge, which would always be present.
1.2.2.7.4.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.2.1.2.2.
1.2.2.7.4.2. Pro: As a fully metaphysical being, God is out of bounds of physics, which could suggest that he is also out of bounds of any other scientifical, even logical, bounds.
1.2.2.7.4.2.1. Con: "Out of bounds of physics" means not being able to have an effect on anything in the universe.
1.2.2.7.5. Pro: Claims about God have to be falsifiable before they can be experimentally tested. [Falsifiability in wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falsifiability?wprov=sfla1)
1.2.2.7.5.1. Con: Infinitely many claims are infalsifiable. Assuming they are not true is pragmatic.
1.2.2.7.5.1.1. Con: Assuming the claims as false gives such claims more credibility than they actually deserve.
1.2.2.7.5.2. Pro: Unfalsifiable claims are neither true nor false. ["It is not even wrong"](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Not_even_wrong?wprov=sfla1)
1.2.2.7.6. Pro: There is no demonstrable scientific evidence that proves the presence of God, not the existence of God. God, by His nature, is transcendent, which will therefore render the scientific method as futile.
1.2.2.8. Pro: The existence of self aware life is scientific evidence that God exists.
If God did not exist, the fact that 14 billion years of physics can produce life out of an ocean of 'conscious-less' hydrogen and energy is evidence that consciousness is either a fundamental characteristic of the universe, or an emergent property. In the former case, the universe is God. In the latter case, the universe can create God.
1.2.2.8.1. Con: Actually in the latter case, the universe has the ability to create consciousness \(not necessarily "God"\) via "emergence."
1.2.2.8.2. Con: The classical definition of God, which is used in this discussion, holds that God is uncreated.
1.2.2.8.2.1. Con: No, it says that God is a absolutely metaphysically ultimate being. That does not preclude such a God creating itself.
1.2.2.8.2.1.1. Con: A being that exists is greater than a being that is identical in every way, but with the sole exception of being non-existent.

 If it is possible for God to exist, but at any point He does not \(both of which must be true if God created Himself\), then God, when he did not exist was not an absolutely metaphysically ultimate being. Therefore, by definition, existence is entailed in the concept of an absolutely metaphysically ultimate being.
1.2.2.8.2.1.2. Con: The idea of X creating X is a logical impossibility.
1.2.2.8.2.1.2.1. Pro: Existence is required for an actor to act.
1.2.2.8.3. Con: A trans-personal reality \(God\) can not be found out by a personal reality \(Human Being\), unless a person has lived a trans-personal experience.
1.2.2.8.4. Pro: There's no logical reason to think that human brain capacities are the limit, therefore it is logical to think that a much more powerful brain exists, and this being would be equivalent to the concept of God.
1.2.2.8.4.1. Con: This is a bit of a non sequitur. It doesn't follow that, because it is possible for a brain to exist beyond human capabilities, that such a brain actually exists.
1.2.2.8.4.2. Con: It doesn't follow that, because it is possible for a brain to exist beyond human capabilities, and that such a brain does exist, that this brain must be God. If this same statement is considered from a gerbil's perspective, it becomes apparently false, as human brain capabilities far exceed that of rodents, yet we are not God.
1.2.2.9. Con: A reason to believe that god exist is not an evidence of so. Such reason can be defined as a mere "hole" in the scientific theory that need to be scientifically filled, not religiously.
This can be easily seen in the evolution of politheistic religion, in which every deity had a "power" \(Zeus-\>lightning, Apollo-\>Sun\) that were easily eradicated with the comprehension of the real world through science.
1.2.2.10. Pro: Science can easily prove the existence of a world that we can not see. Digital objects can have substance without being physical \(like the code that defines the substance of this page, for example\).
1.2.2.10.1. Con: There is physical substance to digital objects - electrons or charged particles in computer's processor. But even if there was a proof of something withou any substance, that does not support the existence of anythig else.
1.2.2.10.2. Con: This has not be done for a god.
1.2.2.11. Pro: The second law of thermodynamics has no known exceptions. The claim that the universe can bring the order we see around us from the disorder of the big bang is incompatible with this law.[www.personal.psu.edu](http://www.personal.psu.edu/faculty/j/m/jmc6/second_law.html)
1.2.2.11.1. Pro: The second law of thermodynamics indicates that the universe is running out of usable energy. If the universe had existed for an infinite number of past days, then all usable energy should have disappeared long ago.
1.2.2.11.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.
1.2.2.11.1.2. Con: Scientists do not claim that the universe has existed for an infinite number of past days. The current estimate is about [13.8 billion years](https://www.space.com/24054-how-old-is-the-universe.html).
1.2.2.11.2. Con: Entropy is not exactly a measure of disorder. Instead, it is a measure of the possible microstates of the system. The entropy of the early universe was lower than today, perfectly in agreement with the second law of thermodynamics: [www.forbes.com](https://www.forbes.com/sites/startswithabang/2017/04/15/ask-ethan-what-was-the-entropy-of-the-universe-at-the-big-bang/#427aa5757280)
1.2.2.12. Pro: -> See discussion #10223: Faith and Science Can Co-exist
1.2.2.13. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.12.
1.2.2.14. Con: The fact that faith wholly underpins the belief in god, suggests that scientific evidence is not available to support it, or faith would not be required.
1.2.2.15. Con: Most claims the God exists posit something that has human like characteristics. The nature of the universe we see doesn't look like anything that would be created by a human. Therefore what most people conceive of as God probably doesn't exist.
1.2.3. Con: There are many claims of evidence of the handiwork of God, but no actual evidence that anything is God's handiwork as opposed to naturally formed or the handiwork of something other than God.
1.2.3.1. Pro: Nothing can be held to exist until it is proven to exist. There is no categorical proof that God does exist, so we cannot state that “God Exists.” The noun, God, boils down to the name given to the cause of the what where when why and how of the Universe. Scientists ponder over all aspects of the Universe and dream of one day finding a theory of everything. God is the precursor to The Theory of Everything.
1.2.4. Pro: Testimonial evidence supports the existence of God.
1.2.4.1. Pro: Testimonial evidence is used in courts of law to establish facts beyond reasonable doubt.
1.2.4.1.1. Con: In a court of law testimonial evidence is rarely accepted by itself.
Testimonial evidence will be rejected when the claim is extraordinary or outside of our normal understanding of physics.
No eyewitness is going to be accepted in a court of law for something like resurrection that would require something like specialist medical expertise.
1.2.4.1.1.1. Con: Testimonial evidence is acceptable on its own if there are many independent reliable witnesses, and if the testimony includes facts that can be established objectively.
1.2.4.1.1.1.1. Con: If it is accepted with many other statements, then it isn't on its own.
1.2.4.1.1.2. Con: Judges will accept testimony that doesn't not have a scientific explanation if it has been undeniably witnessed, since they are bound only by fact and not constrained to the scientific method. Judges accept that minds exist, for example.
1.2.4.1.1.3. Con: Specialists medical experts are not needed to know that crucified men don't come alive.
1.2.4.1.1.3.1. Con: They might be needed to ascertain whether or not a person was actually dead.
1.2.4.1.2. Con: This claim is highly country dependent, some court of law will give more value to witness testimony than the other.
