1.2.4.2. Pro: The best psychological explanation for [Christian martyrdom](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Christian_martyrs) is that God exists.
1.2.4.2.1. Con: The best psychological explanation for Christian Martyrdom is that people sometimes voluntarily make sacrifices in the name of strongly held community beliefs. 
This also explains phenomena like young men going to war, [government officials sticking to their posts during a Japanese Tsunami](https://listverse.com/2013/01/15/the-top-10-most-inspiring-self-sacrifices/) and parents insisting on female circumcision in their daughters.
1.2.4.2.1.1. Con: People don't die for beliefs that are verifiably false, where admitting the truth would save their life.
1.2.4.2.1.1.1. Con: Existence of God is not verifiably false. That's why this dscussion exists in the first place.
1.2.4.2.1.1.1.1. Con: For those present in first-century Jerusalem, the resurrection of Jesus was much more verifiable than it is today.
1.2.4.2.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: The earliest Christians claimed to have seen Jesus alive after the crucifixion.
1.2.4.2.1.1.1.1.2. Pro: The earliest Christians could have visited the tomb \(as they did, according to the Bible\), or asked the Chief Priest to view the body.
1.2.4.2.1.2. Pro: There are many types of martyrdom with religious martyrdom being only one type. There is no reason to believe that the reasons for religious martyrdom differ from reasons for other type of martyrdoms.
1.2.4.2.1.3. Con: Whilst people are often willing to die for what they believe, people are unlikely to die for what they know is not true. The early martyrs would have known whether Jesus had resurrected or not, and could have saved their lives by simply saying that it had not happened. It is psychologically improbable that multiple people were willing to die for such a readily verifiable false belief.
1.2.4.2.1.3.1. Con: The stories about early martyrs might not be true.
1.2.4.2.1.3.1.1. Pro: History is written by the victors. The stories of early martyrs might have been "improved" in order to paint the whole religion in a better light.
1.2.4.2.1.3.1.1.1. Pro: People have a psychological [inclination to root for the underdogs](https://www.psychologytoday.com/gb/blog/the-big-questions/201006/the-appeal-the-underdog). Stories of extreme martyrdom and great deeds inspire devotion and sympathy.
1.2.4.2.1.3.1.1.2. Con: Christianity has plenty of examples of early Christian cowards \(Peter\), people slow to understand \(the disciples\) and other embarrassing testimony. It is not a movement that seems to recast it's characters as heroes. Peter was called "Satan" by Jesus. The disciples fled in terror while the women were found at the cross and were the first to the tomb... in a society that did not value women or their testimony! The only "hero" in the story is Jesus himself.
1.2.4.2.1.3.1.1.2.1. Con: The narrative of "bad" turning into "good" with Christianity as a catalyst of that change is one of the central parts of Christian lore and teaching. The existence of said examples does not contest the possibility of existence of embellished martyrdom stories.
1.2.4.2.1.3.1.2. Pro: Stories often embellish the truth and exaggerate, mainly if their main purpose is to spread a message or persuade someone.
1.2.4.2.1.3.2. Con: Only Saint Stephen \(34 AD\) and James, son of Zebedee \(44 AD\) were near in time the to reported resurrection, and they were not witnesses. Their knowledge of the resurrection wouldn't have been much better, if at all, of those who came later. Stephen's death does not appear to be voluntary. He angered a crowd of Jews while preaching to them, and they killed him.
1.2.4.2.1.3.3. Con: As the martyrs were not witnesses, they heard of the resurrection second hand. There seems no way to readily verify if they were deceived or not.
1.2.4.2.2. Con: A strongly held belief that God was real would be as motivating as God being actually real.
1.2.4.2.2.1. Con: In the case of the 11 faithful apostles, this is obviously not what happened. In their case, they were in a place to know what happened. They were eyewitnesses. Yet to a man, they never recanted their story, even in the faith of persecution and death. To believe 11 men maintained a lie for decades, even to their own disadvantage, is illogical and unreasonable.
1.2.4.2.3. Con: There are a number of [other explanations](http://uo-martyrs.weebly.com/psychology.html) for martyrdom, including a steady desensitisation and role modelling by authority figures.
1.2.4.2.4. Con: If I am willing to suffer or die for something, it does not mean that that something is right or true. It means that I believe it is.
1.2.4.2.5. Pro: -> See 1.2.4.2.1.3.
1.2.4.3. Pro: Testimonial evidence is the chief tool of the apostles.
1.2.4.3.1. Con: Witness testimony is the lowest form of evidence, both in the court room and in scientific inquiry. In fact, the reliance on testimony is one of the major weaknesses of the notion that God exists.
1.2.4.3.2. Con: Prior to Emperor Constantine there were only [21 books that Christians could agree on](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Development_of_the_Christian_biblical_canon#Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon). 10 others were disputed and some labelled heresy. The [Council of Nicea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea) wasn't called until 325CE and the canon's completion took until [397CE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Testament). Many of the books that went into the finished product were fabricated, the alleged authors centuries dead. Only the original 21 books stand any chance of being at all authentic.
1.2.4.4. Con: Testimonial evidence has been already proved to be unreliable. Not only people can lie but also hallucinate, have memory distortion etc. Testimonials are far from being a scientific evidence.
1.2.4.4.1. Pro: The concept of evidence in general relies on the perception of the individual perceiving it. Peer reviewed science is subject to the same flaws, so either our perception is reliable, or it isn't; But saying that we can't trust testimonial accounts would undermine all efforts, religious or not, to understand the universe and/or it's creator.
1.2.4.4.1.1. Con: The scientific method is not based on testimony, it is based on data collection, and testable theories. If repeated tests illicit the same results, we accept it as a truth, i.e. I drop something and it falls to the ground. That is not a testimony because you could perform the same test and get the same result.
1.2.4.4.2. Con: This claim is itself "testimonial evidence", and therefore, by its own standard, is proven unreliable. Whether the claimant is lying, hallucinating that they've seen evidence to support their assertion, or just remember the evidence incorrectly is unknown. 

Even so, the claimant is providing testimony regarding the unreliability of testimony. As such, the claim can be disregarded, as either incorrect, or unreliable.
1.2.4.4.3. Pro: The game / experiment [Chinese Whispers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_whispers) easily show how unreliable a testimony can be.
1.2.4.5. Pro: Taking large doses of [DMT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N,N-Dimethyltryptamine) provides the journeyman an exploration of consciousness and an unprecedented  understanding of reality that can only be presumed to have been designed by a greater being.
1.2.4.5.1. Con: Just because an understanding is unprecedented, does not make it correct.
1.2.4.5.2. Con: This claim refers to a psychedelic hallucinogen that induces varying results which is hardly reliable evidence in a testable setting.
1.2.4.5.3. Con: People under the influence of DMA have yet to create equally valid evidence while sober.
1.2.4.6. Con: A testimony is not worth much without supporting tangible or testable evidence.
1.2.4.7. Pro: Witness testimonies of new life offer the best evidence of an active loving God
1.2.4.8. Con: There are human experiences \('divine moments', 'spiritual moments', moments of ultimate joy, serenity or peace\) that can easily be attributed to a god but can also be explained in other, more rational ways. It is not necessary to attribute these experiences, which might just be biological processes and "epiphanies" in our brains, to a superior being.
1.2.4.9. Con: Testimonial evidence supports the existence of [Flying Spaghetti Monster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flying_Spaghetti_Monster), [Yeti](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yeti), [Chupacabra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chupacabra) and many others.
1.2.4.9.1. Pro: According to [Russell's teapot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russell%27s_teapot) argument, the burden of proof for testimonial evidence lies with the party making the claim, not the party refusing it. No definitive or testifiable proof has been provided by any religion so far.
1.2.4.9.1.1. Con: If Russell's teapot says that the burden of proof lies on the party making the claim, then produce someone who can prove the existence of the FSM. Testimonial  evidence will be accepted ... but everyone knows that the FSM is a parody, so it is rejected.
1.2.4.9.2. Con: No one has ever given testimony in support of the Flying Spaghetti Monster. People who "believe" in the FSM have openly and knowingly stated that it is a parody. The Yeti, Chupacabra, Loch Ness Monster, etc. are all within the realm of possibility but highly unlikely.
1.2.4.10. Pro: Demons exist and can be [exorcised](https://www.washingtonpost.com/posteverything/wp/2016/07/01/as-a-psychiatrist-i-diagnose-mental-illness-and-sometimes-demonic-possession/?noredirect=on&utm_term=.d73ad79e5bcf). Something more powerful than demons must exist, which is likely God.
1.2.5. Pro: The existence of Christianity supports the existence of God.
1.2.5.1. Pro: It is highly unlikely that anyone would have become a Christian in the first century without good reason to believe that Jesus had risen from the dead.
1.2.5.1.1. Pro: The founders and early adopters of Christianity faced lose of prestige, possessions, and even death for their belief. Without good reason to believe that Jesus actually rose from the dead, Christianity would not have been widely adopted with these deterrents.
1.2.5.1.1.1. Con: Christianity did the same thing to Pagans and other religions during the Crusades, and they refused to change their beliefs and were slaughtered for it as well. So if Christianity was that much more true than any other religion, everyone would have converted. It seems it is just harder to shake a belief system once it takes hold, than it is to prove one specific belief system.
1.2.5.1.1.1.1. Con: The above claim explains why people would hold onto their beliefs in the face of adversity, but it fails to explain why people would choose to abandon their beliefs in favor of Christianity while it was still new and persecuted; it fails to address the point of its parent claim.
1.2.5.1.2. Con: Many people become Christian because their parents are Christian.
1.2.5.1.2.1. Con: While this is true, to assert that this invalidates the claim is a [Genetic Fallacy](https://yourlogicalfallacyis.com/genetic).
1.2.5.1.3. Con: Jesus' resurrection is unconvincing evidence for God as it is unlikely that the resurrection occurred.
1.2.5.1.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.1.2.6.
1.2.5.1.3.2. Pro: The story of the resurrection is at odds with reality as we know it. It being so wildly contradictory with our collective experience that it would require an incredible miracle \(not even a garden variety one\) to be true is reason enough to be nigh certain that it did not occur \(at least the way it is claimed in biblical texts\).
1.2.5.1.3.2.1. Con: A miracle is, by definition, an extremely rare event. Each miracle claim must be evaluated on its own merits. Stating that miracles don't conform to our normal experiences is simply a recognition of the nature of miracles, not a refutation of them.
1.2.5.1.3.2.1.1. Con: All claims of miracles are unlikely to be true a priori by their very classification. On their own merits, only claims that can be shown to be true with sufficient certainty to counter that a priori likelihood of being false should be accepted \(and people's testimony is not extremely strong evidence\).
1.2.5.1.3.2.1.2. Con: Rare events are not evidence of the existence of God.
1.2.5.1.3.2.2. Con: A miracle is, by necessity, an extremely rare event. If the purpose of a miracle is to communicate something from a transcendent God to humanity, then the event must be something that is inexplicable by any naturalistic means; otherwise, we would simply accept it as a natural event.
1.2.5.1.3.2.2.1. Con: A communication would be more successful if it was plausible that it happened. As it stands it is implausible.
1.2.5.1.3.2.3. Con: The recognition that the resurrection is more than just a "garden variety" miracle acknowledges that this singular event is the most important communication of God to mankind.
1.2.5.1.3.2.3.1. Con: The lack of supporting evidence for such singular event calls into question whether it happened.
1.2.5.1.3.2.3.2. Con: Rather than acknowledging that such an event existed in contradiction with all other real events, it reasonably demonstrates that such an event did not happen.
1.2.5.1.3.3. Pro: The resurrection account is very weak evidence since the claim is a matter of individual revelation \(even though it was several of Jesus' disciples who may have made the claim\).

[Individual revelation](https://www.hidabroot.com/video/177507/Rabbi-Lawrence-Kelemen/A-Rational-Approach-to-the-Torahs-Divine-Origin) \(from 12:00 to 12:40\) has an extremely low value \(near 0%\) as scientific evidence. The evidence relies on whether we believe the disciples of Jesus not.
1.2.5.1.3.4. Pro: Views on the afterlife. The Sadducees said there was none. The Essenes said it was spirit only, no body. The Pharisees \(Jesus\) believed in the "resurrection" which was body & spirit. The spirit lived on after death, but the faithful would get their body back at the end-times for use in heaven. This was a belief they held that was likely embellished or mistranslated.
1.2.5.1.4. Con: People frequently have bizarre beliefs that they are convinced are true and are willing to sacrifice for the belief. Early Christianity argued about the resurrection and Virgin birth. It was a council of men in 325 that decided current Christian dogma. A social psychological study in 1950s followed people who gave up jobs and school because they strongly believed the world was about to end. They joined a cult of true believers..much like the early Christians.
1.2.5.1.5. Con: Christianity was highly fractured in the first century. The New Testament was assembled over time and was not complete by the First Council of Nicea in 325 AD, when the trinity was adopted. Some groups believed in a divine, yet non-resurrected Jesus.
1.2.5.2. Con: The existence of a mythology does not inherently support the existence of the gods in said mythology. If that were the case, you would have to acknowledge the existence of Zeus, Ra, Shiva, Odin, etc. alongside the Christian God. The existence of those gods would then render the claim of the Christian God being the only god or "one true god" a falsehood.
1.2.5.2.1. Con: Jesus’s ressurection, proof of God, is separated from myth by a number of historical facts that are required for Christianity to have become as widespread as it is.
1.2.5.2.1.1. Con: Christianity isn't all that widespread.
1.2.5.2.1.2. Con: Many ancient myths were also based in historical fact, or at least what was perceived as fact. The only fundamental difference between 'Myth' and 'Religion' is the amount of people currently practicing it.
1.2.5.2.1.2.1. Pro: When the Pagans were all but wiped out by the Catholics, their faith died with them. The same is true for the Ancient Romans, Greeks, and Egyptians. No one believes those stories anymore, yet at the time they were held as truths. In another 1,000 or 2,000 years from now, if Christianity gives way to another religion, it will also be viewed as a mythology.
1.2.5.2.1.3. Con: -> See 1.2.5.1.3.
1.2.5.2.1.4. Pro: The tomb was empty.
1.2.5.2.1.4.1. Pro: The Jewish leaders who had Jesus killed took measures to ensure the body of Christ could not be stolen.
1.2.5.2.1.5. Pro: The deciples bore testimony to having witnessed the resurrected Jesus along with many others. They bore this testimony even at the cost of their lives.
1.2.5.2.1.5.1. Pro: The deciples had no reasonable motivation to lie about having witnessed the resurrected Jesus.
1.2.5.2.1.5.1.1. Con: They may very well have witnessed Jesus, who had nearly died, and returned having narrowly escaped death.
1.2.5.2.2. Pro: The concept of God/s has changed through time reflecting cultures and cultural understandings.  A particular concept of God/s that arose in a definite place and time \(within history\) cannot be separated from its circumstances.  We might as well argue whether Odin exists or Artemis or Ahura Mazda.  God \(YHWH\) is merely one more cultural artifact.
1.2.5.2.2.1. Pro: There are as many concepts of God as cultures, but are "models" \(approximations\) of God adapted to the reality of each culture / depend on each culture. The reality of God must be beyond / beneath the cultures.
1.2.5.2.3. Con: A continued strong and active belief in God in spite of extreme persecution over centuries is not the same as a continued knowledge that a people group once believed something, such as in the case of Greek or other mythology. Those belief systems essentially died out and simply became part of history when they went out of political/social favor.
1.2.5.3. Con: That is just an [argumentum ad populum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argumentum_ad_populum) \(a [fallacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_fallacies), an error in reasoning that even has its own name\).
1.2.5.3.1. Con: This is not argumentum ad populum, since it does not refer to modern Christians, but to the apostoles and other early followers, who knew Jesus personally. Thus, it is a historical argument. Using a relation of witnesses and pointing out that these witnesses had nothing to gain by lying is not the same as using ad populum.
1.2.5.4. Pro: The earliest Christians were Jews who had nothing \(worldly\) to gain and much to lose by abandoning Judaism to follow Jesus. Something powerful must have happened in their lives to convince them that a crucified carpenter was their promised Messiah.
1.2.5.4.1. Pro: The Jews already believed they were "God's chosen people" and were destined for Heaven.  Early Christians risked being ostracized from society and alienated from their families to become followers of the Way.
1.2.5.4.2. Pro: [Jesus was made a curse](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Galatians+3&version=NKJV) \(v13\) \(or accursed\) when he was crucified by gentiles. The Jews of that day [found it hard to believe](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1%20Corinthians+1&version=NKJV) \(v23\) in a crucified, suffering messiah. It would have taken a lot of evidence to convince a self-respecting Jew to follow such a man.
1.2.5.4.3. Con: Just because early Jesus followers believed he was the messiah, doesn't mean he actually was.
1.2.5.4.4. Con: By this logic every other religion and God is as true as Christianity. There were always people switching faiths, it proves or disproves nothing, the point is moot.
1.2.5.4.4.1. Con: The point is not moot, the point is that, generally, people will not die for what they know to be a lie. People changing faiths has no bearing on the idea that early Christians would have known the truth and wouldn’t have willingly died if they knew it was fake.
1.2.5.5. Con: God was active with humanity in the Bible.  If he exists he should still be active.
1.2.5.6. Pro: The existence of the Bible, as the word of God, provides evidence for the existence of God.
1.2.5.6.1. Pro: There is a circular pattern of 12 in Bible & universe that shows the existence of God by order & design, purpose, and connectivity to God's Word the Bible.  \(I will work on a link\).
1.2.5.6.1.1. Con: The human brain is very good at pattern recognition, so good, that we often see patterns that are meaningless or coincidental.
1.2.5.6.2. Con: It has not been demonstrated that the Bible is the word of any god.
1.2.5.6.3. Pro: God's nature is literally that of "The Word"; as evidenced by John 1:1.
Because the word "God" exists, God exists.
1.2.5.6.3.1. Con: The word "dragon" exists; this doesn't mean that dragons exists in reality.
1.2.5.6.4. Con: This is circular reasoning.  You cannot use scripture to prove itself legitimate.
1.2.5.6.4.1. Con: This claim is not about the Bible claiming it is true. It is about the Bible containing scientific facts and ethical principles which are ahead of their time and predictions of the future which can reasonably be explained by devine inspritation.
1.2.5.6.5. Con: God makes a number of appearances in the Old Testament right up until writing is invented, and the stories are set out. After that, He never appears before men again.
1.2.5.6.5.1. Con: Writing was invented 3000 BC, which is well before the events of Exodus where the Bible claims that God appeared to Moses.
1.2.5.6.6. Con: The Bible exists, but much of its authorship is unknown, even as to the New Testament. Without knowing the authors, we can never know what their motivations were for writing it.
1.2.5.6.6.1. Con: No matter what the authorship is, it is the content of the Bible that speaks for itself.
1.2.5.6.7. Pro: The Bible contains ethical and sometimes even scientific principles which are far ahead of their time. \(e.g. Exodus 20:12; Leviticus 11:28; Deuteronomy 22:25; Job 26:7; Matthew 5:44; Galatians 3:28\)
1.2.6. Con: No peer-reviewed articles in any reputable scholarly journal have ever provided evidence for the existence of God\(s\). The claim that God exists is a claim about the nature of reality, and must be judged by the same standards as other claims about the nature of reality.
1.2.6.1. Con: Scientific peer reviewed journals are written by scientists, who by necessity \(and correctly\) perform their research under the assumption of methodological naturalism. Thus supernatural explanation is ruled out as a first principle. Inference to supernatural explanation is a philosophical question, which science, for better or worse, rules out by definition.
1.2.6.1.1. Con: While true, the claim in no way shows that it is improper to use the conclusions of scientific research as evidence for non-scientific conclusions. Nor is it improper to use certain non-scientific conclusions as scientific evidence.
1.2.6.1.1.1. Pro: The "scientific method" is not derived from science. Indeed, it could not be, for that would mean that it existed before it was derived. Therefore, science itself is based on a philosophical, not a scientific foundation.
1.2.6.2. Con: Peer reviewed article on the effects of prayer:[ncbi.nlm.nih.gov](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20686441)
1.2.6.3. Con: Scientific research can only be used on the observable universe and can only indirectly prove in God's existence.
1.2.7. Con: There is no proverbial "smoking gun." At best, the evidence is circumstantial. In truth it is highly subjective, because no one has ever produced evidence of God that lends itself to only one interpretation.
1.2.8. Con: In fact there are no physical or materialistic evidences that one can see that supports existence of God. All "evidences" are actually either claims or feelings.
1.2.9. Pro: There are probabalistic suggestions that God exists
1.2.9.1. Pro: The Modern Synthesis of Evolution implies that it is highly likey that intent was present for the creation of animals because the alternate "random" mutation of genes detailed in evolutionary theory makes the evolution of organisms as complex as they are highly improbable [sciencedirect.com](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0022283604007624) [witpress.com](https://www.witpress.com/elibrary/dne-volumes/4/2/399) \(from [www.kialo.com](https://www.kialo.com/argument/2629.5756)\)
1.2.9.1.1. Con: The concept of "specified complexity" is [disputed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Specified_complexity#Criticisms)
1.2.9.1.2. Con: The evolution of organisms as complex as humans is necessitated by the [anthropic principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropic_principle); the possible worlds where nothing intelligent enough to observe things as we do evolved, do not include any observations of how complex or simple life on Earth is.
1.2.9.2. Con: The [Kolmogorov complexity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kolmogorov_complexity) of the God hypothesis is very high
1.2.10. Con: Throughout the history of mankind we have attributed every event that we didn't understand to God, while the safest answer to those should simply be "we don't understand", as chances are those event justifying the existence of God will eventually find a scientific explanation.
1.2.10.1. Pro: Our ancestors used to think natural events like thunder was an evidence of God.
1.3. Pro: Revelation through faithful practices and religious experiences support the existence of God.
1.3.1. Con: Because it's impossible for anyone to know that God exists unless they are God, there is no reason to believe in the existence of God.
1.3.1.1. Con: God can know that He exists and He can tell that to others.

1.3.2.2.2.2. Pro: People don't have to feel guilty for normal behaviours such as \(but not restricted to\) masturbation or out-of-wedlock-sex; indeed religions tend to shame such practices whereas they are actually [normal and healthy](http://www.independent.co.uk/life-style/love-sex/masturbation-the-health-benefits-10252487.html). The guilt that such activities bring can prevents people from doing them, or cause mental distress when people do.
1.3.2.2.2.3. Pro: One can eat whatever one wants \(beef is forbidden in the major branches of Hinduism, meat in general in some branches, pork in Islam and Judaism\), whenever one wants \(Ramadan in Islam, Lent in Christianity\), [unlike](https://www.britannica.com/topic/dietary-law/Rules-and-customs-in-world-religions) many religions.

1.3.2.2.3.2. Con: Believing in something doesn't mean it exists.
1.3.2.2.3.3. Con: Given any particular religion in 2017, there are at least twice as many non-followers as followers in the world. Would that mean that God mostly does not exist?
1.3.2.2.3.4. Con: Many people also believe in faeries,  which is why we have to temper the opinion of the many with the wisdom of the few according to Aristotle.
1.3.2.2.4. Pro: Adopting a similar philosophy of people with whom you can benefit by association is a time-honored practice.

Although this doesn't necessarily contribute to any material evidence that such beliefs are true.
1.3.2.3. Pro: There are no empirical proofs or conclusive logical-philosophical demonstrations of the existence of God. God is only known in faith. You can never 'prove' the nature of that experience to those without faith, as by definition that experience is personal.
1.3.2.3.1. Con: The word translated as "faith" in English is the Greek word "pistis", a word used in the technical language of Ancient Greek rhetoric. Aristotle gives the definition in his book "Rhetoric" as "forensic proof" when used as a noun, and "trust based on evidence" when used as a verb. This changes the Biblical definition of faith from the more modern "belief in something you cannot prove". The NT provides historical claims to public acts that Jesus performed as evidence for "pistis" in Christ.
1.3.2.3.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.2.1.2.1.
1.3.2.3.3. Con: All experiences are personal.
1.3.2.3.4. Con: Any  argument without evidence to support it is not a good reason to believe.
1.3.2.3.4.1. Con: Mathematical proofs do not have evidence, yet we take them as good reason to believe their conclusion.
1.3.2.3.4.1.1. Con: A mathematical proof is, itself, the argument which proves a theorem with evidence.
[en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_proof)
1.3.2.3.4.2. Con: An argument is evidence, in and of itself.
1.3.2.3.4.2.1. Con: An argument is not evidence in the exchange in which it is used, but may be evidence merely that an argument was stated.

For example, if I have a red pen and argue that "this pen is blue", my argument is not evidence that the pen is blue, but my statement could be used as evidence that I lie.
1.3.2.3.5. Con: This is not an argument for the existence of God as much as it is an argument against reason and rational inquiry. If this argument is accepted, the entire concept of rational debate on which this thesis is founded is internally inconsistent and the discussion cannot continue. This would not prove the existence of God, it would only prove that a rational debate cannot be had as to whether God exists.
1.3.2.3.6. Con: Faith \(or the experience of God\) is a form of empirical knowledge, but it cannot be used in a proof, because it is personal in nature.
1.3.2.3.6.1. Pro: Incorrect things can be believed by faith. As such, it is not a form of [empirical proof](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empirical_evidence).
1.3.2.3.6.1.1. Con: Incorrect things can be believed by empirical proof. This is the basis for almost all illusions, such as optical illusions.
1.3.2.3.7. Pro: Faith and belief are cognitive functions the holder possess because of the inability to explain super complex ideas and sensations beyond the mental maturity of the holder.
1.3.2.3.8. Con: Arguments for theism are not designed to show "proof" \(100% certainty\), but rather are designed to show that the existence of God is more compatible with the evidence we have than the non-existence of God.
1.3.2.4. Pro: The Catechism of the Catholic Church [\(#47\)](http://www.scborromeo.org/ccc/p1s1c1.htm) declares that "the one true God, our Creator and Lord, can be known with certainty from his works, by the natural light of human reason \(cf. Vatican Council I, can. 2 § 1: DS 3026\)."
1.3.2.4.1. Con: This is circular reasoning.  An institution that depends upon the existence of god produces literature that self-verifies its own claims.
1.3.2.5. Con: Faith \(in the sense of non-empirical knowledge\) is indistinguishable from a claim of faith. In other words, true faith and false faith both manifest as claims of faith. Therefore, faith claims cannot be used to justify belief over disbelief.
1.3.2.6. Con: It is possible to know that God exists without faith.
1.3.2.6.1. Pro: There is a difference between personal knowledge, and abstract understanding. Aristotle reasoned his way into abstract understanding of the existence of God, but \(as far as we know\) did not have the gift of the Holy Spirit, or any other personal experience of God.Thus it is possible to know that God exists without faith.
1.3.2.7. Con: Faith as an epistemology can and is used to come to mutually exclusive conclusions and as such can be discarded as a reliable means for deriving truth. It's subject is often asserted without reason and defended against all reason. Any rational process working toward understanding of reality will therefore not be based on faith.
1.3.2.8. Con: There is a cart before the horse problem here. "One must believe that God exists," is being used to support the above claim that "supports the existence of God."
1.3.3. Pro: Many people have religious experiences involving God. Not just beliefs, but actual experiences.
1.3.3.1. Con: People have had similar experiences regarding e.g. pagan gods and nature spirits, so unless we accept that all of these exist we cannot use this as proof of God's existence.
1.3.3.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.1.1.1.
1.3.3.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.3.1.4.
1.3.3.2. Con: An experience of god doesn't suggest the existence of god. The term religious experience is defined by a religious person, not by an atheist. If religion provides self certification of a religious experience, then that claim can't be tested. That means that experience may or may not be true.
1.3.3.2.1. Con: An atheist who has a compelling religious experience would no longer be an atheist, so by this standard no account of personal revelation would be valid no matter how trustworthy or reliable. It's like a flat-earther denying all orbital photographs of the Earth's curve that weren't taken by a fellow flat-earther.
1.3.3.3. Con: Lighter and more complete theories than "God" can explain extraordinary phenomenons.
1.3.3.3.1. Pro: For example, Lourdes is regarded as a sanctified site for many Catholics where miraculous healing would appear, but in fact, there are no more cancer healings in this site than there are spontaneous unexplained cancer healing in any other place.
See [Les miracles, Lourdes et les probabilités \(French\)](http://www.charlatans.info/lourdes-miracles.php), quote: "We can say that with 67 healings in slightly more than 150 years, results are not more impressive in Lourdes that in hospitals."
1.3.3.4. Pro: God the Father and Jesus Christ visited Joseph Smith. [First Vision](https://www.lds.org/topics/first-vision?lang=eng)
1.3.3.4.1. Con: Joseph Smith was a well known charlatan for his time, and ended up being murdered by his own followers after it was revealed he was deceiving them.  There's no evidence that the Book of Mormon is anything other than fiction.[Joseph Smith](http://freethoughtpedia.com/wiki/Joseph_Smith)
1.3.3.5. Pro: God spoke to Moses "as a man speaketh to another." [Exodus 33:11.](https://www.biblestudytools.com/kjv/exodus/33-11.html)
1.3.3.5.1. Con: You cannot use the book stating that God exist as a proof that God exist.
1.3.3.5.2. Con: There is no archaeological or paeleological [evidence](https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/worldviews/wp/2014/12/10/was-moses-real/?utm_term=.e6ec959d58f2) to suggest Moses existed. In fact, most biblical historians do not believe he existed.
1.3.3.5.3. Con: -> See 1.2.5.6.4.
1.3.3.6. Pro: The Apostles were eye witnesses to miracles, which are evidence that God exists.
1.3.3.6.1. Con: We cannot asses how reliable the Apostles themselves are. People tell lies, or just are wrong sometimes, and that might be the case for them too.
1.3.3.6.2. Con: There is no contemporary or direct testimony on Jesus.  While there are records from Rome during the time he lived, there are zero contemporary references.  The gospels appear to have been written at least 70-300 years after the fact.[Who would have noticed in Jesus' time?](http://freethoughtpedia.com/wiki/A_Silence_That_Screams)
1.3.3.6.3. Con: Attempting to prove existence by means of “evidence” found within the pages of questionable texts is a circular argument that requires a leap of faith.
1.3.3.7. Pro: Altered states of consciousness including dreams, hallucinations, astral projections and other out-of-body experiences, often are of a spiritual nature or are instructive, hinting at a greater intelligence or at least the existence of something beyond the physical.
1.3.3.7.1. Con: Dreams, hallucinations, astral projections and OBEs find physical expressions and can be interpreted as body phenomenons. Psychological experiences can explain and reproduce such phenomenons without involving "God".
1.3.3.7.2. Con: Dreams and hallucinations can be interpreted in any way to confirm your beliefs. If you believe in 'anything', you can then twist dreams to fit within the narrative of your beliefs. Who's to say dreams don't 'actually' show that we are living in a virtual universe created by omnipotent chickens?
1.3.3.7.3. Con: All mental states can be proven to be a function of the material operation of the brain.  There is no science suggesting there is anything supernatural going on. When a brain dies, it ceases any mental activity.
1.3.3.8. Pro: The fact others have personal experiences of God should be accepted as a good reason to believe God exists.
1.3.3.8.1. Pro: Rejecting this would be like assuming a missing soldier is dead, while ignoring individual reports that he is alive.
1.3.3.8.1.1. Con: Rejecting this would be like accepting you don't know whether or not a missing soldier is dead, regardless of reports he is alive, because he has not yet been found in either state.
1.3.3.8.2. Con: Consistent application of this standard would require one to accept any and all such claims.  But we know contradictory experiences are commonly reported by adherents of different religions.
1.3.3.8.3. Con: Accepting this would be like assuming fairies exist because people claim they've had a lost tooth replaced with money by the tooth fairy at night.
1.3.3.9. Pro: There are experiential arguments for the existence of God as a universal power. Those reports come from some practitioners of Yoga, Meditation, and other spiritual practices. Even though these reports are not yet scientifically verified, the accounts are nearly identical.
1.3.3.9.1. Con: The account of a report that is not scientifically verified cannot afford a scientific impact.
1.3.3.9.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.2.1.2.2.
1.3.3.9.2. Con: The practices of yoga and meditation do not require a belief in a god to be effective. Since a god is not common in all of these practices, it is not required to get the effects.
1.3.3.10. Con: Where truth is defined as empirical knowledge of reality, personal revelation cannot provide truth.  Any two people can individually boil water under the same conditions and it will boil in the same way regardless of what either of them may believe.  But experiences with gods can yield widely varying ideas about what god is for any two individuals-- hence all the various religions.  There is no truth \(as defined above\) related to gods because gods are not empirically observable.
1.3.3.11. Con: Individual experiences are not sufficient evidence as people are able to experience things that do not match reality.
1.3.3.12. Con: Anecdotal evidence is the least reliable kind of evidence. This means experiences \(in this context with god\) have little to no value when it comes to proving whether a proposition \(the existance of god\) is true or false.
1.3.3.13. Con: People of all religions claimed to have had religious experiences. Evangelical Christians claim to have experienced the presence of Jesus. Catholics have experienced the Virgin Mary or a saint. Muslims claim to have had a religious experience with Allah. Buddhists with Buddha and Hindus with Krishna or another deity and so on and so on. This is not evidence for the existence of God but evidence that religious experiences are psychological in nature.
1.3.3.14. Con: Such experiences do not necessarily validate God specifically. They could validate any of numerous other things. The individual might only interpret the experience as religious as a result of confirmation bias.
1.3.3.14.1. Con: Lack of validation is no indication of the opposite. Even the scientific method is not interested in validation, only falsification and partial support, which is offered by personal religious experiences.
1.3.3.14.1.1. Con: The scientific method definitely cares about validation precisely because so many scientific observations are of effects with only statistical results.   The more independent trials validating the result the more unlikely the negative is. What you are observing is a publication bias caused by journals so aren't interested in publishing results like "Yes, relativistic effects have been demonstrated yet again", etc.
1.3.4. Con: There's no evidence to show that divine experiences are anything other than wishful-thinking, fallacious reasoning, or fraudulent reporting.
1.3.4.1. Con: 1. The view that the 5 senses are the only ways people can perceive evidence is unnecessarily limited. 
2. Well over half of all people in the world believe in God, many of whom claim that they can perceive God in a way that they don't trace to the 5 senses. 
3. Therefore, it is not irrational to consider the possibility of God's existence and that people may be perceiving God in a way other than through the 5 senses.
1.3.4.1.1. Con: There are more then 5 senses, but there is no reason to believe any of them can sense something divine. The "divine sense" just moves the problem of not having a reason to believe in God to one of not having a reason to believe in the sense of God.
1.3.4.2. Con: The currently accepted standards for science and epistemology may not be adequate to account for true religious experience, even if it were assumed there was such a thing.
1.3.5. Pro: Thousands of Christian martyrs have been giving their lives annually, adding up to millions over the centuries. They all were killed for asserting the truth of Jesus's deity, authority, death, and resurrection \(i.e. in support of the Christian God\). These sacrifices have been going on since the Acts of the Apostles.
1.3.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.3.1.4.2.1.2.1.1.1.1.1.1.4.1.2.2.
1.3.5.2. Con: New Testament scholar Candida Moss argues that ["Christians were never the victims of sustained, targeted persecution."](https://www.salon.com/2013/02/24/the_myth_of_persecution_early_christians_werent_persecuted/)
1.3.5.2.1. Con: Moss claims that Christians weren't persecuted for their beliefs, but does state that they were executed for "publicly denying the divine status of the emperor" - which is a fundamental part of Christian belief \(that there is only one God\).
1.3.5.2.2. Pro: In the Annals 15.44, written by Roman historian Tacitus, Jesus and the Christians are mentioned. It [recounts](http://law2.umkc.edu) how Emperor Nero went after Christians in order to draw attention away from himself after Rome's fire of 64 AD.
1.3.5.2.3. Pro: According to [Harari](http://www.ynharari.com/book/sapiens/), the Roman persecutions of Christians over five centuries amounted to the deaths of no more than a few thousand people. This a fraction of what Christians did to each other within a  [24 hour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/St._Bartholomew%27s_Day_massacre) period in 1572.
1.3.5.3. Con: Martyrs may be wrong in their belief.
1.3.5.3.1. Pro: Throughout history, people have chosen to die for causes, some of which have proven to be [unworthy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jonestown).
1.3.5.3.1.1. Pro: Many people have died and continue to die on both sides of religious wars and for religious causes. They cannot all be right. So people dying for a cause simply can't be taken as it being righteous.
1.3.5.3.2. Pro: A thing is not necessarily true because a man [dies](https://books.google.com/books?id=F6skDQAAQBAJ&pg=PT310&dq=%22A+thing+is+not+necessarily+true+because+a+man+dies+for+it%22&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwidoYW8uLDZAhWIvVMKHUCnCbYQ6AEIQjAF#v=onepage&q=%22A%20thing%20is%20not%20necessarily%20true%20because%20a%20man%20dies%20for%20it%22&f=false) for it.
1.3.5.3.3. Con: Just because someone believes in something that is *possibly* wrong does not make the belief completely unsubstantiated. They could be wrong, yes, but they could also be right. Stating that something could be wrong is not an argument against it.
1.3.5.3.4. Pro: The Aztecs used human sacrifices to keep the sun aloft.
1.3.5.4. Con: Most religions with different concepts of God have "Martys", in thousands. There is no yardstick to measure whose Martyrdom was for the truth.
1.3.5.5. Con: The action here, becoming a martyr, doesn't support the existence of a god; it only demonstrates the degree to which an individual believes in the existence of a god. The fact that these individuals number in the millions doesn't add credibility anymore than the existence of Santa Clause is made credible by the millions of children who believe in him.
1.3.6. Con: The same experiences involving God happen with other deities, such as Shiva, Ogum, and any other kind of god or spiritual entity. If we agree that all one of these experiences are real, then the God of classical theism cannot exist.
1.3.6.1. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.1.1.1.
1.3.6.2. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.3.1.4.
1.3.6.3. Con: The fact that the belief in God is prevalent, even among groups who refuse to believe or accept any of the other claims from competing groups, tends to add further weight to the belief in God.
1.3.6.3.1. Con: Many of the religions referred to are polytheistic rather than monotheistic, and therefore a belief in gods, rather than a belief in God.
1.3.6.3.2. Con: The "further weight to believe" is not evidence of existence.  All religions cannot be right at the same time, because their gods and beliefs contradict each other.
1.3.6.4. Pro: Classical theism is monotheistic.
1.3.6.5. Pro: What you see in a near-death experience, is influenced by your cultural background, including your [religion](http://www.horizonresearch.org/near-death/intro-to-the-nde-phenomena/religion-culture-and-near-death-experiences/). People that see an angel or a god, only see ones consistent with their own religious beliefs \(regardless of whether they're f.e. Christian or Hindu\).
1.3.6.5.1. Con: Cultural background definitely shapes up the way the experience is presented, however, universal elements such as seeing deceased relatives, a supreme being, a white light tunnel do exist.
1.3.6.6. Pro: The differences between deities experienced in revelations are surface-level differences. The same ultimate being is at the root of some, many, or all of them.
1.3.6.7. Con: Hindu scriptures solve the contradiction of multiple Gods in two ways. One is by explaining them as avatars - all Gods are manifestations of a [Supreme being](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brahman). The other is to say that since the Ultimate, by description, is all-everything, it must therefore be beyond even intellectual form - so Shiva and Ogum and God cannot help but be human/ 4 dimensional/ geography specific approximations of the Supreme god \(or theistic deity\).
1.3.6.8. Con: Christianity, which endorses classical Theism, does not claim that God is the only spiritual entity in the universe. Encounters with a spiritual entity do not necessarily mean that such encounters are with God Himself. Therefore, a spiritual encounter with a being who is not God in no way implies that God does not exist.
1.3.7. Con: Anecdotal evidence doesn't provide a quantifiable amount of verifiable information to use as a determination for whether a given claim is true or not true.
1.3.7.1. Pro: If anecdotal evidence were legally enough evidence to convict a person of a crime then it would only take a small group of people making a claim that a crime took place to convict the accused, which would make it easy to unjustly convict people. Thus, anecdotal evidence in favor of the existence of God should be ignored.
1.3.7.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.3.
1.3.7.3. Pro: Arguments from personal experience are highly subjective and not verifiable or observable.
1.3.7.4. Con: Alternately personal experience is fairly used to verify information for one's self.  

The possibility of revelation and direct experience gives reason to give some credence to claims, and while not being enough for an external determination is very compelling to the one who experienced it first hand.

Even if the only way for a hypothetical external observer to test this is to cease being an external observer this is a relevant point to the enquiry \(as none of us are truly external\).
1.3.7.4.1. Con: Since we want to know whether god exists in our shared world \(reality\), rather than in some person's halucinations and mistakes, personal experience is unfit and unreliable for a task that is a prori implausible \(unlike the more commonplace cases where due to similarities with prior confirmed cases we already have basis to affirm plausibility in addition to the personal experience\).
1.3.7.4.1.1. Con: Because Merriam-Webster includes a definition of "a priori" as "[presupposed by experience](https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/a%20priori)", it seems that personal experience is precisely the tool required to evaluate a priori assumptions.
1.3.7.4.2. Pro: I myself can use my own experience as proof of God. All experience,even A priori experience is personal. Any specific experience of any certain individual is either proof of Creation or proof of Entropy. One may say he doesn't believe in God. This means that certain individual is an unbeliever in God therefore a believer in the absence of God which is death or entropy. The physical proof of this claim is the relationship between choice and consequence: Good choices lead to good consequences.
1.3.7.5. Con: Once you analyse all the known points of view of a certain Historical Data from all the dimensions you may now interpret that historical data\(i.e.cultural context of the time,human habits of that period, the roots and all known coincidences \(i.e. The existence of dinosaurs discovered through bones in different parts of the world\) one develops distinct capacities in relation to that certain data. This simply means:You become a master of it and your logic may filter the bias. You become a scholar.
1.3.8. Pro: God uses prophets to bear witness of his existence, his nature, his attributes, and how to live in mortality. Through prophets, God has said that if you strive to emulate his nature, attributes, and follow his directives, you will know the truth of his existence. God has stated that you can know whether a prophet is true or false by the results of their teachings.
1.3.8.1. Con: The claim that the aforementioned prophets are endorsed by God lacks confirmation.
1.3.8.2. Con: Prophets often endorse contradictory claims \(example: [Matthew 5:44](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+5%3A44&version=NIV) and [Koran 2:191](http://corpus.quran.com/translation.jsp?chapter=2&verse=191)\).
1.3.8.2.1. Con: It is not surprising to find prophets from two different religions making contradictory claims.
1.3.8.2.1.1. Pro: Two prophets from two dissimilar religions worshiping different concepts of God \(one or both of which may be false Gods\) provide no basis for an expectation of consistency.
1.3.8.3. Con: This point is circular within the broader argument, because it essentially makes the claim: “We know how to know God, because God told the prophets and they told us.” If god doesn’t exist, he didn’t tell the prophets anything.
1.3.8.4. Con: No prophet has ever been shown to have specific, precise, and accurate knowledge of the future that cannot be explained by chance or their personal influence.
1.3.8.4.1. Pro: There have been many supposed prophets since the scientific revolution, and so have been many opportunities for these people to prove themselves to be genuine, if they wished.
1.3.9. Pro: A person can come to know God exists by learning about his will and then practicing it for a period of [time](http://biblehub.com/john/7-17.htm). If what you have learned and implemented is true, God will [communicate](https://biblia.com/bible/esv/Matt%2016.13-17) it to you, and by this you will know that God exists.
1.3.9.1. Con: At it's core, this argument claims "God exists because the Bible says that God exists and the Bible is the word of God." This is a blatant fallacy, [begging the question](https://yourlogicalfallacyis.com/begging-the-question). Using statements from a Christian text that presupposes the existence of God as "proof" of God's existence is circular logic.
1.3.9.1.1. Con: "Using statements from a Christian text that presupposes the existence of God as 'proof' of God's existence is circular logic" is only true if the trust in the Christian text is warranted exclusively by its claims to be God's word. Most Christian scholars evaluate the trustworthiness of the Bible as they do any other text, including the use of [textual criticism](https://www.britannica.com/topic/textual-criticism).
1.3.9.1.2. Con: Such circular logic is valid, but the "proof" from the parent claim would be considered independent in its source \(i.e from God\). This is because the teachings/words themselves are not evidence of God but rather the consequences of it.
1.3.9.2. Con: This relies on subjective experiences. When a person affirms that God is communicating with him/her we don't have any mean to verify if that is true.
1.3.10. Con: Hallucinations are regularly experienced.  However fascinating, these experiences provide no evidence supporting the existence of anything observed or experienced in the hallucination
1.3.10.1. Pro: There is no objective standard for whether a given religious experience is a hallucination, or a revelation. It is judged differently by observers, in accordance with their bias.
1.3.10.2. Con: Hallucinations are not shared. They take place in one mind independent and irrespective of another. Eye witness accounts by a large number of people cannot rationally be explained as a shared hallucination.
1.3.10.2.1. Con: There have been shared hallucinations recorded as well. The phenomenon is called ["Folie à deux"](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Folie_%C3%A0_deux).
1.3.10.2.1.1. Con: Folie a deux is shared delusion not hallucination.
1.3.10.3. Con: This claim equates, without defense of such equivocation, an individual's religious experiences and hallucination. Without evidence to show that these are the same, this claim becomes a straw man argument.
1.3.10.4. Con: Stating that "Hallucinations are regularly experienced" implies that ones experience with a higher power is always linked to hallucinating.
1.3.11. Con: God is perfectly capable of existing regardless of whether humans know of his existence or not. 

For many years, men believed that [coelacanths](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coelacanth) were extinct, until one was caught. Our lack of knowledge did not prevent the fish from existing.
1.3.11.1. Con: There were fossils indicating the previous existence of the fish, which is evidence for their existence even though they were not yet observed.
1.3.11.1.1. Con: Fossils were strong evidence that the fish had existed... not that they still exist to this day. To use another analogy, atoms existed long before they were discovered.
1.3.11.1.2. Con: The claim makes a serious category error. Looking for fossils of a non-corporeal being is like searching for love in a fishbowl... it's logically incoherent.
1.3.11.1.3. Con: The claim commits the fallacy it attempts to refute. The claimants lack of knowledge of such fossils do not require that they do not exist.
1.3.11.2. Con: It was still known that the fish had at least existed once, unlike God, who we have no evidence for whatsoever.
1.3.12. Con: A claim that rests on something being divine assumes the existence of a divinity. If the existence of God is in question, the interlocutor cannot accept divinity as part of the argument \(as that would be circular\).
1.3.12.1. Con: Just as with the use of assumptions in logic, the existence of the divine can be assumed in order to build on the argument with additional evidence and reasoning.
1.3.13. Con: In most instances, followers are born into their religion. Their parents expose them to faithful practices and religious experiences early on. This is similar to indoctrination practices, which provide false sense of belonging and a distaste for divergent thought.
1.3.13.1. Con: This is a [genetic fallacy](https://yourlogicalfallacyis.com/genetic). The origins of one's belief in a proposition has no impact on whether the proposition is true.
1.3.13.1.1. Con: It's not a matter of where someone was brought up, but how.
1.3.13.1.1.1. Con: This is still a genetic fallacy. How someone was brought up has no bearing on the veracity of their beliefs. Each belief must be evaluated in terms of the evidence for it. Upbringing has no impact on this evidence.
1.3.13.1.1.1.1. Con: A genetic fallacy would describe an argument based on someone's nature, or the biological makeup of a person. The grandparent claim is one based on nurture, and this 100% has an influence on what people believe and how they practice their beliefs.
1.3.13.1.2. Con: With all of the religions in the world, either all of them are wrong, or all but one are wrong.
1.3.13.1.2.1. Pro: With all the variations of god, the main problem facing humanity, the means of overcoming the problem to attain salvation, and the concept of salvation, it is probable that one religion has a correct worldview. But, because of these variations, if one religion were correct, all the others would be wrong.
1.3.13.1.2.2. Con: By simply following the [common](http://www.religioustolerance.org/reciproc2.htm) moral code, or the [law of reciprocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_Rule), one can attain salvation \(or in religions that believe in reincarnation, be closer to attaining it.\)
1.3.13.1.2.3. Pro: Given N differing propositions, either N or N-1 must be fallacious.

One of them might be true, or they all could be false. But because they all differ, they can't all be true.
1.3.13.1.3. Con: This is a [fallacy fallacy.](https://yourlogicalfallacyis.com/the-fallacy-fallacy) The grandparent claim doesn't invalidate the great grandparent claim on the basis of genetics or nature of the followers, but the nurture or the means in which they were instructed to believe. The parent claim incorrectly asserts the opposite.
1.3.13.2. Pro: Either one religion is right, and the rest are wrong, OR all of them are wrong. This would suggest the number of people who are groomed to believe an incorrect religion is very high, if not the majority of believers.
1.3.13.3. Pro: Stating that the means of obtaining information is tainted, therefore the information gained from it is too, is nothing more that an example of [Fruit of the poisonous tree.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fruit_of_the_poisonous_tree) This is an argument that is good enough for the U.S. judicial system.
1.3.13.4. Con: This danger also possible in non-religious families. Darwin's grandfather Erasmus Darwin must have influenced him [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erasmus_Darwin)
1.3.13.4.1. Con: The scientific method means the point is moot: any inherited knowledge would be verifiable, and one does not assume that a theory is correct without adequate proof.
1.3.14. Con: The belief in God can be explained and predicted using rational thought: sociology, psychology, history, etc.
1.3.14.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.3.1.1.
1.3.15. Con: Even if the revelation is truly from God and is properly interpreted by the one receiving revelation, such an experience is personal and only justifies belief for that individual. However, it cannot be used as part of an argument to persuade others who have not had that experience.
1.3.15.1. Con: Many revelations and experiences, especially miracles, can be shared. Modern experiences include claims of individuals being healed by prayer in which others can confirm that symptoms seem much better after the prayer. \(Not to mention religious texts like the Bible recording instances where groups experience miracles together, such as the parting of the Red Sea\).
1.3.16. Con: The symptoms of some mental conditions cause people to hear voices in their head. Without modern understanding of this condition, an Iron Age man could perceive it as divine revelation, or at the very least, supernatural.
1.3.16.1. Con: Ancients had some understanding of mental illness \(mentions of "madness" and "demon possession" are in ancient texts\), even if it wasn't as comprehensive as ours. Even so, groups often accepted an individual's claims of revelation, choosing not to believe that it was madness or possession.
1.3.17. Con: Humans report religious experiences and develop religious narratives, including the existence of God, as a result of their painful experiences and hardships. If you alleviate the hardships, there would be no need to attribute success to God.
1.3.17.1. Pro: It seems God is held responsible for both good & bad tidings when He may not even exist. E.g., a regular church-going friend lost his spouse to death & stopped attending church, blaming God for her death.
1.3.17.2. Con: Some have reported about God without \(or at least before\) having any major hardship. King Solomon is one example.
1.3.18. Con: The object of ones faith and the subsequent practices, as well as the context of ones experiences can all be based on provable falsities; hence any claim of revelation is only in support of the existence of revelation and not in support of the existence of God
1.4. Pro: Philosophical arguments support the existence of God.
1.4.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.
1.4.2. Pro: [Axiological arguments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiology) support the existence of God.
1.4.2.1. Pro: [Moral arguments](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/moral-arguments-god/) support the existence of God.
1.4.2.1.1. Con: If you listen to people, God has been for, or against whatever happens to be in their best interest at the time. If one is looking for humans determining for themselves what is moral without the application of reason, they thus need look no further then a theist.
1.4.2.1.1.1. Con: The selfish nature of humanity does not disprove the existence of God. It only proves that many theists are selfish or insincere. The flaws of mankind are distinct from the existence of God.
1.4.2.1.1.1.1. Con: Those that follow God-free, logic-based moralities are much better behaved, and consistent than theists do in no small part to fundamental foundational problems with theism.
1.4.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Since the ability to determine whether a person who claims to follow some moral code actually does is limited to him being honest, there is no reliable method to evaluate what every person does in relation to the moral code he claims to follow.
1.4.2.1.1.1.1.2. Pro: The horrible practice of the [gospel of wealth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prosperity_theology) is an example of theistic indiscretion.
1.4.2.1.1.1.1.3. Pro: Theistic sources are contradictory, allowing one to choose morals convent for them, rather then to be consistent, or good. For example, the bible argues both [for](http://www.evilbible.com/evil-bible-home-page/slavery/) and [against](https://www.openbible.info/topics/anti-slavery) slavery. It is impossible to be good when what is good isn't even consistently defined.
1.4.2.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.
1.4.2.1.3. Con: In Plato's Socratic Dialogue "Euthyphro," it is proved that morality cannot feasibly come from a god, and thus must either exist or not independently. Thus moral arguments for god are all based on a shaky standing.
1.4.2.1.3.1. Pro: If God commands actions because they are right, then morality isn't grounded in God but instead supersedes him. But if actions are right because God commands them, then morality is arbitrary, as it is subject to the whims of God.
1.4.2.1.3.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.
1.4.2.1.3.1.2. Con: Morality isn't subject to God's whims because God is necessary and unchanging.
1.4.2.1.3.1.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.1.2.2.1.1.
1.4.2.1.3.1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.5.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3. Con: Morality isn't arbitrary because God is defined as being perfect, or maximally great.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.1.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.2. Pro: Many ontological arguments use God's perfection/maximal greatness as a premise to prove His existence.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.13.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.4.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.2.3. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.5.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.2.4. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.12.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3. Con: Without a measurement to use, there is no maximum, or minimum. If morality isn't arbitrary due to God's greatness, there must be an objective yardstick for God to be the top of. If there is such a yardstick, then God is not the measure of morality. If not, then, once again, morality would be arbitrary.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.1. Con: Measurements can be based on similarity to a given reference \(such as the nature of God's mind\), without a need for an "objective yardstick."
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.1.1. Con: The concept of a "reference" is functionally identical to the concept of an "objective yardstick."
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.1.1.1. Con: One point of reference is insufficient to create a system of measurement, as what is being measured is unknowable. For example, if God is the only reference for morality, then if someone was stupider, or less wise then God, if they preferred cheese more, or ham less, they would be just as immoral as a murderer, or a rapist.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.1.1.1.1. Con: Morality is relative, not absolute. Not every difference from God would have to be equally immoral.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Without a measuring stick, it is impossible to tell which aspects of God matter for the measurement of morality, and which ones don't.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.1.1.1.2. Con: A second point of reference isn't necessary to determine what is being measured.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.1.1.1.2.1. Pro: Scientists can compare the relative sizes of the Earth and the Sun without getting confused by the fact that the Sun is hotter than the Earth, or concluding that the size difference must therefore be greater than what is physically measured.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.1.1.1.2.1.1. Con: This is because measurements of size already exist, as does it what size is that is being measured. One doesn't measure the size of the sun by merely asking how different is is from the earth.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.1.1.1.2.2. Con: The only comparison here given is one where an existing scale already exists.
1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.2. Con: -> See 1.4.2.1.3.1.3.3.1.1.
1.4.2.1.3.2. Con: Euthyphro's dilemma could be regarded as a false dilemma.
1.4.2.1.3.2.1. Pro: One could keep asking why is whatever moral standard considered such moral standard in an infinite regress.
1.4.2.1.3.2.1.1. Con: Things could be bad because if you don't do them, you will be punished.
1.4.2.1.3.2.1.2. Con: Things could be good because they bring about a desirable end for the individual.
1.4.2.1.3.2.1.3. Con: Things could be good because they bring about a positive end in the aggregate.
1.4.2.1.3.2.1.4. Con: Things could be bad because if other people did them, it would bring harm to one's self.
1.4.2.1.3.2.1.5. Con: Things could be good because people with certain traits do them, or bad because people with other traits do them.
1.4.2.1.3.2.1.6. Pro: Euthyphro's dilemma could be applied to any objective moral standard.
1.4.2.1.3.2.1.6.1. Con: "is what is morally good commanded by \[objective standard\] because it is morally good, or is it morally good because it is commanded by \[objective standard\]? "
Well since it is an objective standard, the answer is the latter. So no, Euthyphro's dilemma does not at all apply to objective moral standards, because it does not create a dilemma.
1.4.2.1.3.2.2. Con: It seems highly unlikely that neither Socrates, not Euthyphro, both great thinkers, one on either side of the debate, saw this supposed problem while they were discussing this dilemma.
1.4.2.1.3.2.3. Pro: It commits the [fallacy of false dilemma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/False_dilemma), in which something is falsely claimed to be an "either/or" situation, when in fact there is at least one additional option.
1.4.2.1.3.2.3.1. Pro: If morality originates in God's nature rather than His command Euthyphro's dilemma is avoided.
1.4.2.1.3.2.3.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.
1.4.2.1.3.3. Con: -> See 1.4.2.1.3.2.3.1.
1.4.2.1.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.
1.4.2.1.5. Con: The moral argument for God's existence is not sound as it presupposes that objective moral laws exist, which has not been demonstrated.
1.4.2.1.5.1. Con: In support of premise \(1\), [Lewis](http://www.paul-gould.com/2012/12/19/c-s-lewis-the-moral-argument-for-god-and-the-gospel/#_ftn2) argues that we all have within us the sense of right behavior and character. There is a sense of “oughtness” that presses upon us. He says, “human beings, all over the earth, have this curious idea that they ought to behave in a certain way, and cannot really get rid of it.” \[Mere Christianity p 8\]
1.4.2.1.5.1.1. Con: The moral laws that apply to human beings should not be confused with a "Universal" moral law, which applies to everything.
1.4.2.1.5.1.1.1. Con: Applicability to everything is not necessary for a law to be objective. The statement "water is a liquid at room temperature and sea level pressure" is objectively true, despite only applying to water. This type of objective truth is all that is necessary for the moral argument.
1.4.2.1.5.2. Con: The existence of objective moral laws has been persuasively defended by many philosophers, including many philosophers who are not theists, such as [Russ Shafer-Landau](https://www.amazon.com/Moral-Realism-Defence-Russ-Shafer-Landau/dp/0199280207/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&qid=1528209042&sr=8-1&keywords=moral+realism%3A+a+defence), [David Enoch](https://www.amazon.com/Taking-Morality-Seriously-Defense-Realism/dp/0199683174/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&ie=UTF8&qid=1528209100&sr=1-1&keywords=taking+morality+seriously), and [Michael Huemer.](https://www.amazon.com/Ethical-Intuitionism-Michael-Huemer/dp/0230573746/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&ie=UTF8&qid=1528209133&sr=1-1&keywords=ethical+intuitionism)
1.4.2.1.6. Pro: Morality is a purpose given to us by a creator.
1.4.2.1.6.1. Con: Being created for a purpose is not warrant for the goodness of that purpose. Building a gun to kill does not make it a moral good for that gun to fire and kill even though it does what it was designed to do.
1.4.2.1.6.1.1. Con: A gun being built for murder and then being successfully used for murder does make it a correctly functioning, and therefore "good", gun, even if it doesn't justify the moral actions taken by the human using it.
1.4.2.1.6.1.2. Con: Under this theory, manmade purpose isn't identical to morality, only Godmade purpose is.
1.4.2.1.6.2. Con: This doesn't solve the [is/ought problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Is%E2%80%93ought_problem) and therefore doesn't explain morality as understood by human nature. The fact that humans were given a purpose doesn't mean their purpose is "correct".
1.4.2.1.6.3. Pro: In the same way that a clock that doesn't tell the time is a bad clock, a human who doesn't live a life of love and compassion is a bad human.
1.4.2.1.6.3.1. Con: A clock that doesn't tell the time is only subjectively a bad clock according to \(a subset of\) humans. In the same way, even if God did exist, that would not make morality objective.
1.4.2.1.6.4. Con: This claim is completely unsubstantiated.
1.4.2.1.7. Pro: [C.S. Lewis](http://www.paul-gould.com/2012/12/19/c-s-lewis-the-moral-argument-for-god-and-the-gospel/) reasoned from universal experience that universal law exists and thus, a universal law-giver exists.
1.4.2.1.7.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.
1.4.2.1.7.2. Con: There's a difference between prescriptive and descriptive laws. Those set by a law giver, like the ones passed by the legislative branch, are prescriptive, while the ones humans develop through observation and analysis are descriptive. As the laws of nature scientists have discovered are descriptive, it would be invalid to deduce that a law giver must exist.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1. Pro: Laws do not require lawgivers.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.1. Con: The moral law is prescriptive, and we have no other example of a prescriptive law without a law-giver.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.1.1. Pro: Morality certainly isn't descriptive, in that it doesn't describe human behavior. Almost every moral realist will agree that humans break the moral law more often than they follow it.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.1.2. Con: "If you don't eat, you will die" is an example of a prescriptive natural law.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.1.2.1. Con: This is only an example of a true if-then statement, not a law.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.1.2.1.1. Con: all laws are if-then statements, if it is not consequential it is a definition, not a law.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.1.2.2. Con: If someone does not value their life, they won't have a reason to "follow" this. The statement itself doesn't force someone to put value on their own life.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.1.2.2.1. Con: the law does not force someone to follow it, it merely enacts consequences.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.1.2.2.1.1. Con: This is equivocation. The moral law isn't \(necessary\) one which enacts consequences for actions, but only prescribes the right actions to do regardless of its results.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.1.2.2.1.1.1. Con: ignoring moral law has consequences, just as with physical laws, like guilt, lack of depth of experience, lack of social progress, and social estrangement.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.2. Pro: Lewis doesn't explain why moral laws require a law-giver, even though there are other laws that are not dependent on minds. Laws of Physics, for example.
1.4.2.1.7.2.1.2.1. Con: This is an equivocation of the word "Laws". Natural laws are simply descriptions of what would happen in nature under the given circumstances. The moral law describes what should happen under the given circumstances. The two are not equivalent.
1.4.2.1.7.3. Con: -> See 1.4.2.1.5.
1.4.2.1.7.4. Pro: For a universal Moral Law to exist, there must be a sort of 'order' in the universe. This order in the universe is what dictates humans acting/reacting according to the universal Moral Law.
1.4.2.1.7.4.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2.1.
1.4.2.1.7.5. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2.1.
1.4.2.1.7.6. Con: Lewis is pushing back against the supposed absence of objective moral law in an atheistic universe. However, he fails to provide objective morals in a theistic universe. In his view, God is creating the law. The laws are conceived of entirely in the mind of God, and hence subjective. The only difference between God's subjective morals and human's subjective morals is that God has enough power to enforce his morals \(might makes moral right\).
1.4.2.1.7.6.1. Con: The unchanging and universal moral law still suffices as an explanation for our experience, even if it is "subjective" from the perspective of God.
1.4.2.1.7.6.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.
1.4.2.1.7.6.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.7.1.1.1.
1.4.2.1.7.6.4. Con: Lewis does not argue that God creates the law by His divine subjectivity or His will to impose them. That view is associated with nominalism and theological voluntarism, which Lewis rejected in favor of something more like Christian Platonism.
1.4.2.1.7.6.5. Con: The argument that God's morals are subjective does not apply if God is of a different kind and mind than us. If we are created rather than begotten \(that is being of different, more limited kind merely resembling the creator\) then our perception of available morality is not going to be equal to that of God.
1.4.2.1.7.6.5.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.2.1.2.4.6.1.
1.4.2.1.7.7. Con: CS Lewis's second premise \("If there is a universal Moral Law, there is a Moral Law-giver"\) is unsubstantiated.
1.4.2.1.7.7.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.2.1.7.2.1.
1.4.2.1.7.7.2. Con: To be an inherent pull towards following any sort of universal Moral Law, there must be a sort of 'order' to the universe. This order implies a force or being with greater complexity to implement this order. As a starting point for understanding this force or being that implements order, God can be assumed.
1.4.2.1.7.8. Con: CS Lewis's third premise \("If there is a Moral Law-giver, it must be something beyond the universe."\) is unsubstantiated.
1.4.2.1.7.8.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.
1.4.2.1.7.8.2. Con: Morals cannot be dictated by matter, space or, time.
1.4.2.1.7.8.2.1. Pro: By definition, morals deal not with what is but what ought to be.
1.4.2.1.7.8.3. Pro: Laws can be from internal sources. Many laws that affect the United States were written after its foundation by its citizens.
1.4.2.1.8. Con: Humans would have morals regardless of God. Things like murder are wrong because it hurts the survival of our species.
1.4.2.1.8.1. Con: Without objective moral axia, there is no way to derive that we ought not hurt the survival of our species \(i.e. that hurting the survival of our species is wrong\).
1.4.2.1.8.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.
1.4.2.1.8.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.2.1.5.
1.4.2.1.8.2. Con: To say that humans need God in order to know/understand morality is mischaracterizing the moral argument - that is a statement about moral epistemology \(why we know about morality\), whereas the moral argument is about moral ontology \(why morality exists in the first place\).
1.4.2.1.8.2.1. Con: If a God is not necessary from the moral argument then the moral argument does not imply God's existence.
1.4.2.1.8.3. Con: The survival of our species is not a moral priority.
1.4.2.1.8.4. Con: Eugenics and social darwinism help the survival of humanity by removing unfit genes. Forced conversion of homosexuals also helps human survival by increasing reproduction rate. Yet most agree that these actions are still morally abhorrent. Therefore, the survival of humanity is not identical to morality.
1.4.2.1.8.4.1. Con: Social darwinism is not a moral framework that came out of evolution - rather it is a moral framework intentionally designed by people drawing on a parallelism with evolutionary principles.
Conversely the moral framework that came out of evolution does take into account the well being of individuals.
1.4.2.1.8.5. Con: It is not self evident why the survival of our species would be morally good without God.
1.4.2.1.8.5.1. Pro: The [is-ought problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Is–ought_problem) in philosophy states that you cannot derive what ought to be true solely from what is true.
1.4.2.1.8.5.2. Con: Immoral behavior harms the ability of humanity to survive.
1.4.2.1.8.5.2.1. Pro: Murder decreases the population.
1.4.2.1.8.5.2.2. Pro: Theft removes resources from those who can put them to good use.
1.4.2.1.8.5.3. Con: Moral behavior increases survival odds.
1.4.2.1.8.5.3.1. Pro: Saving lives keeps the genetic pool diverse.
1.4.2.1.8.5.3.2. Pro: Moral behavior promotes group cohesion.
1.4.2.1.8.5.3.3. Pro: Taking care of the needy discourages unrest.
1.4.2.1.8.6. Con: As society becomes increasingly atheistic, it also tends to moral laxity, which implies that many \(if not all\) moral codes stem from God.
1.4.2.1.8.6.1. Con: -> See 1.4.2.1.8.2.
1.4.2.1.8.6.2. Con: Most morals that have laxed in recent times, such as the lack of 'chastity', 'reproductive duty', and 'decency', are based on theism or natural law. This only shows that those particular morals are connected to God existence or lack thereof.
1.4.2.1.8.6.2.1. Pro: Morals justified by other moral systems, like the utilitarian view that giving to people with less than you is good, have remained steady.
1.4.2.1.8.6.3. Con: Secular nations/societies are typically healthy. They tend to have decreased instances of homicide, STDs, and abortion, as well as increased life expectancy. [moses.creighton.edu](http://moses.creighton.edu/JRS/2005/2005-11.pdf)
1.4.2.1.8.6.4. Con: Theocracies have higher instances of terrorism.
1.4.2.1.8.6.4.1. Pro: Afghanistan, Iran, Mauritania, Saudi Arabia, Sudan, and Yemen are theocracies.
1.4.2.1.8.7. Pro: Morality is sufficiently explained by evolution and the relationships between individuals. These objective features do not require a God.
1.4.2.1.9. Con: One of the tenets of a moral argument for the existence of God is a knowable abstract moral absolute. If the AMA is any of those demonstrated by known gods, the values have shifted over time. All deities have shown fluctuations in morals.
1.4.2.1.9.1. Con: The AMA isn't necessarily represented by the god of any particular religion.
1.4.2.1.9.2. Con: Subscribing to a religion doesn't require that you believe everyone who claimed to speak about God's morals was telling the truth and unmotivated by personal goals. Thus, any perceived shifts in God's morals can be ascribed to shifts in people, rather than God.
