1.4.2.1.9.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.2.1.1.1.
1.4.2.1.9.3. Pro: In the case of the Judaeo-Christian god, the old testament god who demands virgin sacrifice seems to modernize as humanity does. If there were an AMA by a perfect being it should stand out among the bronze age human-sacrifice rituals, not blend in with them.
1.4.2.1.9.3.1. Con: There is only one story in the [Old Testament](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binding_of_Isaac) where God commands human sacrifice, and this was only a test which was revoked before the sacrifice actually happened.
1.4.2.1.9.3.2. Con: There is no evidence within the Bible that the Judeo-Christian notion of God evolved and modernized. The God portrayed in Revelation has all of the characteristics of the God portrayed in the early Old Testament.
1.4.2.1.10. Con: Morality does not factor into the existence, or non-existence of an entity.
1.4.2.1.10.1. Con: There would be no reason to be moral without the existence of a higher entity.
1.4.2.1.10.1.1. Pro: The word ‘reason’ is used intentionally here. Without a higher being, we can ‘explain’ moral behaviour through evolution or the social contract theory etc. But without a higher being keeping us accountable - providing we could get away with something and avoid punishment - there is no ‘reason’ to be moral.
1.4.2.1.10.1.2. Con: Rational actors behaving 'morally' often achieve maximal combined utility outcomes.
1.4.2.1.10.1.3. Con: There are psychological reactive mechanisms in healthy people with trigger negative emotions in reaction to negative actions as well as positive actions. Humans have what is called empathy-based guilt reactions when they do something that harm others. \([researchgate.net](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/268206560_Empathy-Based_Pathogenic_Guilt_Pathological_Altruism_and_Psychopathology)\) Likewise, empathy also rewards us positively when we do something good for others. Humans are already born with an instinctual barometric reaction mechanism for behavioral regulation with regards to how our actions affect others.
1.4.2.1.10.2. Pro: Morality itself doesn't support or refute anything as a fact but rather provides an argument why one action should be taken above another. Take the trolley dilemma as an example. Should the lever be pulled to save more lives or should no action be taken?
1.4.2.1.10.2.1. Con: This is an example of normative ethics, the discussion of what is right and what is wrong. The moral argument is an exercise in metaethics, the question of what moral statements mean and how they come to exist, which is very different.
1.4.2.1.11. Con: All morality is necessarily situational. Take a moral pronouncement and you can derive both a situation where it's justified and an alternate situation where it is not.
1.4.2.1.11.1. Con: If there isn't a standard morality, then there is no need to qualify any action by its context, because all things would be acceptable across all situations.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1. Con: [Moral relativism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moral_relativism) holds that actions can be considered right or wrong, but that such a judgment depends on the context.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.1. Pro: Agent relativism states that the morality of an action can only be determined by the person taking the action.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.1.1. Con: Agent relativism isn't intuitive or held by many people - for instance, we still consider a murderer to be wrong, even if the murderer thinks that murder is morally permissible or even obligatory.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: Under agent relativism, since moral framework is decided entirely by the individual, individuals should be able to change their moral framework whenever it's convenient to them or they have a whim to. In reality, people don't treat morality as if it's subject to their own whims in such a way, and will deem it right to adhere to it even when it's inconvenient.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.1.2. Con: Agent relativism isn't supported by the human instinct to justify one's own actions based on moral standards. For instance, if someone steals, they will attempt to justify the theft by saying that they need the stolen thing more than the original owner does, rather than simply saying that stealing is morally right to them. This implies that morality is based on something outside of the agent.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.1.3. Con: Under agent relativism, morality is only motivated by internal beliefs, not by any external motivators. However, there are many examples of people's views of morality being changed by external motivators. For instance, imagine a man who believes that poor people deserve to suffer for the choices they make, but changes his mind after spending time with a poor family and coming to care about them. This scenario would not be possible under agent relativism.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.2. Pro: Speaker relativism states that moral statements are true or false only according to the one who makes the statement.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.2.1. Con: According to speaker relativism, when a claim is made, that claim is referring to the moral framework of the speaker. As such, when two people disagree about morality, they are speaking about two separate frameworks - and, as such, do not have shared meaning. Without shared meaning, real disagreement is impossible. Intuitively, this is not what moral disagreement is.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.2.1.1. Pro: If one person says that planes fly through the air, and another person states that they don't, believing the word "plane" to refer to a [woodworking tool](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plane_\(tool\)), then the two people don't truly disagree because they aren't referring to the same thing.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.2.2. Con: Speaker relativism isn't intuitive or held by many people. For instance, if someone says that murder is permissible, we intuitively believe that they are wrong, rather than just stating a truth about their own moral framework.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.4. Con: All forms of moral relativism fail to account for the general agreement amongst all of humanity of basic moral principles.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.4.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.4.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2.1.
1.4.2.1.11.1.1.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2.1.
1.4.2.1.11.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2.1.
1.4.2.1.11.2. Con: Morality can be both situational and objective. Water is a liquid at certain temperatures/pressures and a solid at others, but the definition of water is still objective. In the same way, "it is wrong to do x in this situation" can still be a statement with an objective truth value.
1.4.2.1.11.3. Con: Most theists reject moral absolutism \(that certain actions are always either right or wrong, regardless of situation\). Moral realism \(that the rightness or wrongness of any given, situational action is objective\) still holds, and is all that is needed for the moral argument to work.
1.4.2.1.11.3.1. Con: -> See 1.4.2.1.8.7.
1.4.2.1.11.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.5.1.1.1.2.1.
1.4.2.1.12. Con: There are as many moral arguments against the existence of God.
1.4.2.1.12.1. Con: There are no arguments against God from the existence of morality.
1.4.2.1.12.2. Pro: The god of Abraham is described as genocidal. Genocide is immoral. Therefore, moral arguments for the god of Abraham are invalid if they are based on omnibenevolence.
1.4.2.1.12.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.1.5.1.1.
1.4.2.1.12.2.2. Con: The "genocides" depicted in the Old Testament were God's punishment against evil people for their immorality. As such Genocide is not intrinsically immoral.
1.4.2.1.12.2.2.1. Con: Hitler's attempted genocide was punishment for his perceived evil nature of Jewish people, yet it was still immoral.
1.4.2.1.12.2.3. Pro: According to [Genesis 6-9](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Genesis+6-9&version=NIV), God was the first entity to commit genocide, thereby inventing the practice.
1.4.2.1.12.2.4. Pro: According to [Exodus 11-12](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=exodus+11-12&version=NIV), God committed genocide against the first born of Egyptian households.
1.4.2.1.12.2.5. Con: -> See 1.1.4.4.3.1.
1.4.2.1.12.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.
1.4.2.1.12.4. Pro: Many theists base their belief in God on good in the world, while the stories in their religious texts tend to depict the Gods of classical theism as selfish evil tyrants with bad attitudes, short tempers, lust for death and destruction, and a general disregard for the value of anything other then themselves, even when those same texts defined such Gods as being good no matter what they do.
1.4.2.1.12.4.1. Con: The Christian God is depicted as slow to anger and rich in mercy.  Jonah actually complains to God that he is not nearly vengeful enough. \(Jonah 4\)
1.4.2.1.12.4.2. Con: God's justice and wrath against immorality is not evidence that God is an evil, selfish, tyrant.
1.4.2.1.12.5. Con: If God does not exist, there is no basis upon which to affirm objective moral values and duties. Therefore, talking about the morality of God is meaningless.
1.4.2.1.12.6. Con: If God exists, you can disagree with Him or reject Him as a moral monster. However, it makes no sense to disagree with or reject a God that doesn't exist. Therefore, moral objections to God’s existence are pointless.
1.4.2.1.13. Pro: Various basic human instincts such as responsibility \(and other similar instincts such as gratitude and awe\) are implicitly theistic. Being responsible is universally considered a virtue. This raises the question, to whom are we responsible? Is to ourselves \(like appointing your chief accountant to audit your company\)? To one another? By analogy with Aquinas' 5 Ways we can trace back the chain of responsibility to God the Judge before whom we are ultimately responsible.
1.4.2.1.14. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.
1.4.2.2. Pro: There are [aesthetic arguments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argument_from_beauty) for the existence of God.
1.4.2.2.1. Pro: There isn't any reason a priori to expect the world to be fundamentally beautiful - however, if it was created by a perfect God, it would almost certainly be. As such, the fact that we exist in a fundamentally beautiful universe counts as evidence for a perfect Creator.
1.4.2.2.1.1. Pro: The Likelihood Principle states that if some observation O is likely under hypothesis A and less likely under hypothesis B, then O counts as evidence for A over B.

The existence of a fundamentally beautiful universe \(O\) is likely assuming a perfect creator \(A\), and less likely assuming the absence of such a creator \(B\).
1.4.2.2.1.2. Con: Much of the world isn't beautiful - for example, the atrocities committed by man and animals.
1.4.2.2.1.2.1. Con: God gave men and animals free will, including the freedom to defile natural beauty. The works that God is solely responsible for, excluding those sullied by free creatures, are fundamentally beautiful.
1.4.2.2.1.2.1.1. Con: Natural disasters or infections are not works of free creatures, but they are not beautiful. Which means God, if he exists, did not create a fundamentally beautiful universe.
1.4.2.2.1.2.1.1.1. Con: Even the case of the vast majority, but not all, of the universe being fundamentally beautiful is very unlikely without assuming intentional design.
1.4.2.2.1.2.1.1.2. Con: Natural disasters may wipe out much moral evil, and so may have the beauty and awe of divine justice.
1.4.2.2.1.2.1.1.3. Con: During natural disasters, many heroic acts are done, which are beautiful in their own right, and only possible in such dire situations, thus natural disasters can be made beautiful by acts of heroism.
1.4.2.2.1.2.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.1.1.4.
1.4.2.2.1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.4.2.2.1.2.1.1.1.
1.4.2.2.1.3. Con: It's possible that the human conception of "beauty" adapted from experience of the outside world, and so it would be natural for humans to consider nature "beautiful."
1.4.2.2.1.3.1. Con: Many things not visible to early humans - such as planets, nebulae, and galaxies, or the microscopic structures of many materials - are still considered beautiful, despite humans having no time to adapt to them and come to see them that way.
1.4.2.2.1.3.1.1. Con: Our value of these other structures may be evolutionary [spandrels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spandrel_\(biology\)).
1.4.2.2.1.3.2. Con: God may have [guided evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theistic_evolution).
1.4.2.2.1.4. Con: The universe is not fundamentally beautiful - for example, life is impossible in 99.99% of the universe, and millions of people die every year because of natural disasters.
1.4.2.2.1.4.1. Con: From a distance, the violent scenes of the cosmos are extremely beautiful, and since no life is at stake, they are not horrifying, yet remain awe inspiring.
1.4.2.2.2. Pro: There is no particular reason a priori to expect natural laws to be elegant or beautiful, or even [so accurately described by mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Unreasonable_Effectiveness_of_Mathematics_in_the_Natural_Sciences). However, intentional design by a perfect God could easily account for such elegance. As such, the fact that physical laws are mathematically elegant counts as evidence for a perfect Creator.
1.4.2.2.2.1. Pro: The fundamental laws of our universe are extremely elegant, whereas the vast majority of possible sets of natural laws are incredibly arbitrary and complex. This suggests maximization by some agent.
1.4.2.2.2.1.1. Con: Elegance in scientific theories is an illusion.
We, as pattern finding masters, project the elegance into these equations and modify them to fit our aesthetic values.
1.4.2.2.2.1.2. Con: Proving the universe has a purpose or was "maximized by some agent" does not prove the existence of a classical theistic God. It merely proves the existence of "some agent", which does not need to be God, or that the universe has a purpose.
1.4.2.2.2.1.2.1. Con: An agent that can maximize some quality about the universe would have to be transcendent and enormously powerful, if not omnipotent.
1.4.2.2.2.1.2.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.2.1.1.1.
1.4.2.2.2.2. Con: While this could be considered evidence, this evidence is not sufficient or compelling. Aesthetics are subjectively pleasing, and something being found aesthetically pleasing doesn't necessitate it being made for personal appreciation. "Someone made this for me to enjoy," does not logically follow from "this is elegant and beautiful, in my opinion."
1.4.2.2.3. Pro: If aesthetic judgments have objective truth, [there must exist](https://philosophicalnarratives.wordpress.com/2017/11/16/why-beauty-points-us-towards-the-existence-of-god/) an objective standard or anchor point to justify them. Such an aesthetically perfect, objective, and transcendent anchor would be considered God.
1.4.2.2.4. Con: There is no thing necessitating objectivity in aesthetics. Indeed art, literature or any other thing that exhibits or conjures beauty is subjective in appreciation. One may fail to find beauty even where a billion others succeed, no known physical laws deny the possibility.
1.4.2.2.4.1. Con: A difference in appreciation of art need not be grounded in mere difference of opinion. It may just as well be a consequence of some sort of defect in the one person over the other.
1.4.2.3. Pro: Without a transcendent creator and arbiter which gives objective value to truth, there is no objective value to the pursuit of truth. The purpose of debates, such as the existence of God, are to pursue truth. Therefore, if none exists, the discussion is moot.
1.4.2.3.1. Pro: Truth has no inherent value. Truth has value if and only if there is an arbiter of mankind which can give value to truth.
1.4.2.3.1.1. Con: Truth has objective value due to it's nature of being able to predict future events.
1.4.2.3.2. Pro: The discussion of whether God exists is not a question of the effectiveness practicality of the belief, but of the truth of the belief. Therefore, the discussion itself only has value if truth has value.
1.4.2.3.3. Con: Objective value of truth can come from utilitarian point of view \(truth is valuable because it helps us in achieving our everyday goals\). Science is inherently valuable because it works \(produces useful technologies\), and science needs to stick to truth in order to work.
1.4.2.3.4. Con: If the value attributed to something were only subjective rather than objective, that's not an argument that the value is zero value.
1.4.2.3.5. Con: An alternative to divine truth might be considered to be consensus truth which would prevent this discussion from being rendered moot.
1.4.2.4. Con: Even if we accept the dubious premise that objective values exist, and even if we further argue that such values can only be given by a creator, it is a much simpler claim to argue that human beings \(beings for which we have empirical evidence\) are the creators of such objective values.
1.4.2.4.1. Con: Since humans don't have necessary existence, anything created or sustained by humans cannot itself have necessary existence. As such, any values caused solely by humans could not be objective.
1.4.2.4.1.1. Con: Necessity is not a precondition of objectivity.
1.4.2.4.1.1.1. Pro: We say the discoveries of the natural sciences are objective, yet they are all contingent.
1.4.2.4.1.2. Con: Objectivity is an ambiguous notion.
1.4.2.4.1.2.1. Pro: 'Objective' may be interpreted to mean either 'mind independent' or 'factual', while 'subjective' may mean 'mind dependent' or 'mere opinion'. 

Now it is a fact that I have a mind, but that fact is dependent upon my mind, so it is objective in that it's factual, but subjective in that it's mind dependent. Hence, there is ambiguity.
1.4.2.4.1.2.1.1. Con: This is a quite clever, but fallacious, equivocation.

Mind-independence in this context does not entail just one specific mind, but ALL minds. While the existence of the claimant's mind is, of course, dependent on the claimant's mind, the objective existence or non-existence of said mind is mind-independent of every other mind in existence.

There is no ambiguity in stating that the existence of one's mind is dependent on the existence of that mind--It's tautological.
1.4.2.4.2. Con: This would require a demonstration of humans being able to create objective values.
1.4.2.4.3. Con: Human beings have perception. This perception is subjective. For all have a different perception of things. Therefore it is very difficult for a person to be objective. If there is an allknowing god, who oversees all things, than He can be objective.
1.4.2.4.3.1. Con: An all-knowing God who oversees all things has the subjective perception of a being who sees all things. This is just another different perception of things, quantitatively more in what is seen but not any more objective in quality.
1.4.2.5. Con: Value is subjective.
1.4.2.6. Pro: The ontology and existence of logic cannot be justified in a naturalistic universe.
1.4.2.6.1. Pro: The laws of logic are immaterial.
1.4.2.6.1.1. Pro: The laws of logic cannot be changed by altering any physical substance.
1.4.2.6.1.2. Pro: The statement "No matter exists" would be true and logical if matter did not exist; therefore logic cannot depend on matter.
1.4.2.6.2. Pro: The laws of logic require a mind to exist; they have no reason to exist in a universe with no minds.
1.4.2.6.2.1. Pro: The laws of logic are rules for correct thinking. Thinking requires a mind. It is unlikely in a naturalistic universe that laws for correct thinking would exist platonically, waiting for minds that might or might not appear.
1.4.2.6.2.1.1. Con: The minds that conceived of logic are the minds of humans \(or, in a more basic sense, animals\). Patterns however don't require a mind to exist.
1.4.2.6.2.1.1.1. Con: Logic was not invented by humans; it was discovered and used by humans.
1.4.2.6.2.1.1.1.1. Pro: Humans could not have made the [law of non-contradiction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_noncontradiction) irrelevant to our universe. They discovered the law, they did not create it.
1.4.2.6.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con: That law is not a feature of the universe, it only codifies a pattern observed in various instances in the universe. The existence of such instances does not require a mind.
1.4.2.6.2.1.1.1.2. Pro: Humans could not have made the [law of the excluded middle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_excluded_middle) irrelevant to our universe. They discovered the law, they did not create it.
1.4.2.6.2.1.1.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.4.2.6.2.1.1.1.1.1.
1.4.2.6.2.1.1.1.3. Con: Logic codifies patterns. Logic itself is a system made by humans.
1.4.2.6.2.2. Con: -> See 1.4.2.6.2.1.1.
1.4.2.6.2.3. Con: The statement "there are no minds" would be true and logical in a universe where no minds exist.
1.4.2.6.3. Pro: The laws of logic would exist even if human or animal minds did not exist.
1.4.2.6.3.1. Pro: In the early seconds after the big bang, the statement "No human minds exist" was true and rational. However, no human existed to have a mind capable of processing that statement.
1.4.2.6.3.1.1. Con: Without minds to contain the sentence "no human minds exist" the claim was meaningless, rather than logical, true or rational.
1.4.2.6.3.1.1.1. Con: If it is rational to believe that a mind-dependent entity must exist even if human or animal minds do not, then it is logical to assume that some other mind exists.

The objection assumes that no other types of minds exist, which begs the question of the discussion. God is not human; it is plausible that a concept can exist in the mind of God, even if humans do not exist.
1.4.2.6.3.2. Con: That is at the very least baseless and at most completely incorrect. What would be justified to say is that patterns existed, not logic. Logic was invented later by humans to codify predictions regarding patterns of nature.
1.4.2.6.3.2.1. Con: -> See 1.4.2.6.2.1.1.1.
1.4.2.6.4. Con: Our explicit conceptions of the laws of logic may transcend a naturalistic universe, however they have [derived](https://absoluteirony.wordpress.com/2014/04/23/laws-of-logic/), implicitly, from a naturalistic universe.
1.4.2.6.4.1. Pro: Our utterly unthinking ancient ancestors displayed behaviours consistent with logical thought. Human's "laws of logic" simply codified these responses into rules.
1.4.2.6.4.1.1. Con: This argument acknowledges that logic can exist, even if all human ancestors are "unthinking"--for otherwise, the claim that the unthinking ancestor's actions were logical would either be false, or meaningless.

Therefore this claim supports, rather than rebuts, the parent claim.
1.4.2.6.4.1.1.1. Con: The claim talks about the predictability of the naturalistic universe giving rise to the possibility of logical thought. It therefore rebuts the claim that logic is not possible in a naturalistic universe.
1.4.2.6.4.2. Pro: Logic can itself be conceptualised in differing ways. For example the "[law to exclude the middle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_excluded_middle)" can be [written as](https://absoluteirony.wordpress.com/2014/04/23/laws-of-logic/): \(P∨∼P\) or \(P|P\)|\(\(P|P\)|\(P|P\)\). A different approach to the world would have led to a differing representation of a transcendent rule, derived from the same observations of nature.
1.4.2.6.4.2.1. Con: This addresses the conceptualization of logic, not the ontology of logic. The conceptualization of an entity does not explain why or how that entity exists.
1.4.2.6.4.2.1.1. Con: But that is what logic is: conceptualization. Patterns on the other hand don't really require an explanation, they can just be.
1.4.2.6.4.3. Pro: The predictability of the universe is sufficient for utility codifying a system of logic. As intelligent beings evolved the use of patterns was favored as it aided in survival, therefore giving rise to more and more complex logical systems.
1.4.2.6.4.3.1. Con: This system would necessarily prefer survival over truth. Natural selection of logic would allow an entity to believe a beneficial lie rather than a harmful truth. Such an entity would have no reason to believe that his thoughts \(including the claim above, or this claim\) were true.
1.4.2.6.4.3.1.1. Con: While people do believe in some beneficial lies over some harmful truths, the subset of beliefs that are part of the system we call logic do not share that trait \(in other words those are cherry picked ideas for the sole purpose of inducing truth\).
1.4.2.6.4.3.1.2. Con: The fact that these ideas are empirically verified to hold true is sufficient to establish that they hold true.
1.4.2.6.4.3.1.2.1. Con: If the parent claim is true, then we have no reason to trust our empirical verifications. These would be evolved beliefs and subject to the same filter of natural selection, which favors survival, not truth.
1.4.2.6.4.3.1.3. Con: Correct reasoning often does help in survival.
1.4.2.6.4.3.1.3.1. Pro: Correctly predicting the direction a predator is going improves the chances of escaping.
1.4.2.6.4.3.1.3.2. Pro: Understanding that if an individual predator is in a given place it is not in another place improves the chances of not being in the same area as a predator \(consequently being at a lower chance of getting preyed upon\).
1.4.2.6.4.3.1.3.3. Pro: Extrapolating that if a tree bears one fruit that isn't poisonous the other fruits are probably not poisonous either can aid in eating safer foods.
1.4.2.6.4.3.1.3.4. Con: While this is true in the examples given, it is irrelevant to the point. A false belief that was beneficial would be selected and reinforced in exactly the same way.
1.4.2.6.4.3.1.3.5. Con: An ability to properly interpret our environment does not necessarily imply an ability to determine truth about abstract and metaphysical claims.
1.4.2.6.4.3.1.3.6. Con: If the belief that "correct reasoning often does help in survival" is false, but beneficial to survival, then that belief would be selected and reinforced. On materialism, we have no way to rise above our programming to determine whether this is or is not true.
1.4.2.6.4.3.2. Con: If logic developed through evolution, then it is necessarily true that it could have developed differently than what it did. This would mean that the law of non-contradiction could have been false.
1.4.2.6.4.3.2.1. Con: It is not the usefulness of the law of non-contradiction that evolves, it is the ability to apply it, which not all things have.
1.4.2.6.4.3.3. Con: Evolution leads to diversity; yet we see one uniform law of logic across the universe. The law of non-contradiction is not true for one species but false for another.
1.4.2.6.4.3.3.1. Con: -> See 1.4.2.6.4.3.2.1.
1.4.2.6.5. Con: The contradiction between a naturalistic world and the certainty that we experience through logic is a falacy, since logic itself is not certain, nor truly objective.
1.4.2.6.6. Con: The naturalistic view can contain a mind, and through self assuring entropic development, a consciousness. Consciousness itself is closely resembling an entropic process of self-modification like DNA evolution.
1.4.2.6.6.1. Pro: There's no need for any additional mystic or supernatural quality to explain the existence of consciousness.
1.4.2.6.6.1.1. Pro: In The Emperor's New Mind \<[en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_New_Mind)\>, Penrose proposes a very plausible explanation for the apearence of conscioussness.
1.4.2.6.6.1.1.1. Con: AI researchers, philosophers, computer scientists and other experts deny the plausibility of the thesis contained in [The Emperor's New Mind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_New_Mind).
1.4.2.6.6.1.2. Con: Such a naturalistic consciousness would necessarily be deterministic, and any conclusions derived from that consciousness would be the product of naturalistic biochemical reactions and non-directed processes; more akin to a cosmic Plinko game than rational thought. 

If true, then the conclusion reached in the parent claim should not be assumed to be true, as the claimant could not have come to any other conclusion regardless of the evidence.
1.4.2.6.6.1.2.1. Con: Natural biochemical reactions are not deterministic, they derive from a great deal of chaos and randomness.
1.4.2.6.6.1.2.1.1. Con: "Chaos" and "randomness" in a naturalistic universe merely imply that too many variables exist to be tracked by humans or our tools. Neither chemistry nor physics are random; they behave according to well-established principles which permit us to understand our universe using the tools of science.
1.4.2.6.6.1.2.1.2. Con: If the parent claim is true, then consciousness is the product of chaos and randomness, and would produce random thoughts, not rationality. Therefore our reason could not be trusted.
1.4.2.6.6.1.2.2. Con: The fact that no supernatural explanation is required does not mean that such consciousness is deterministic. Quantum mechanics are not supernatural and are not deterministic
1.4.2.6.6.1.2.2.1. Con: It has not been shown that quantum events are non-deterministic. Some interpretations, such as the De Broglie–Bohm theory, have deterministic implications.
1.4.2.6.6.1.3. Pro: AI investigation will eventually be able to explain the rise of conscioussness.
1.4.2.6.6.1.3.1. Con: This is not only speculation, it is a "science of the gaps" argument. In other words, "we don't understand this, but someday, science will be able to fill in the gaps of our knowledge".
1.4.2.6.6.1.3.2. Con: In [The Emperor's New Mind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_New_Mind), Penrose proposes that consciousness can never be modeled by a Turing machine. This includes modern AI systems.
1.4.3. Pro: [Teleological arguments](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/teleological-arguments/) support the existence of God.
1.4.3.1. Con: All forms of teleological arguments require "teloi" \("purposes"\). Until its existence is proven, we should assume that there is no such thing as purpose. Thus, teleological arguments all fail until purpose is shown to exist.
1.4.3.1.1. Con: Artificial purposes exist, such as the purpose of an oven is to heat food.
1.4.3.1.1.1. Con: Artificial purposes do prove an agent with intent, but artificial purposes do not assist a teleological argument. Natural purposes are required for teleological arguments.
1.4.3.1.1.1.1. Con: Teleological arguments infer the intent of a particular agent, the hypothetical creator of the universe. It's unclear how anything more is required for the argument to work.
1.4.3.1.2. Pro: Natural purposes do not exist. Assuming purpose to a natural thing, such as a rock, a tree, or a planet, assumes a creator to prove a creator.
1.4.3.1.2.1. Con: Natural purposes do exist. The heart's purpose is to pump blood throughout the organic system. The blood's purpose is to distribute nutrients \(and take waste\) to/from the organic system. Without these real purposes, the organic system would cease to function. Thus, teloi \(natural and unnatural\) exist.
1.4.3.1.2.2. Con: Assuming natural things do not have purpose assumes a lack of a creator to prove a lack of a creator.
1.4.3.1.2.3. Con: This only shows that the assertion of natural purposes is fallacious, but it doesn't follow from that that it is untrue.
1.4.3.1.3. Con: Not all teleological arguments assume intrinsic purpose, despite the etymology of the argument's name.
1.4.3.1.3.1. Pro: Life in our universe would be impossible if any of a number of [laws, constants, and initial conditions](https://calumsblog.com/2017/07/25/full-defence-of-the-fine-tuning-argument-part-4/) were changed even [unimaginably slightly](https://www.kialo.com/god-exists-3491/3491.0=3491.1+3491.641/+3491.641/comments). This implies the strong probability that the universe was created for the purpose of harboring life.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1. Con: Fine-tuning arguments can be explained via [multiverse theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiverse), without resorting to God as the source of order.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.2. Con: Multiverse theory and anthropic reasoning can successfully explain why the laws of nature permit living beings to exist, but cannot explain why the laws of nature are so simple and elegant, since the vast majority of possible worlds with intelligent beings have extremely convoluted and arbitrary natural laws.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.2.1. Con: We only know about our own universe and therefore have no frame of reference for how simple and elegant our universe is.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.2.1.1. Con: Other sets of natural laws are conceivable, and those are often very complex. This is all that's required for them to be considered possible worlds under modal logic, which serves as our frame of reference to judge this universe.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.2.1.1.1. Con: if they are conceivable they are presentable, and they have not been presented.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.3. Con: Multiverse theory attempts to resolve the origins of the universe by allowing random chance to compensate for the lack of a creator, but it fails to explain the source of the multiple universes. The question of a creator still remains.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.4. Con: No system has been proposed that would result in randomized laws, constants, and quantities in a multiverse.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.4.1. Pro: Although pseudo-random events seem to be common within our universe, due to unpredictable events and chaos theory, this would not apply outside of our universe without a similar system in place. We can't assume that randomness is the default state without reason.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.4.2. Pro: There is no theory in physics that explains how physical laws themselves would change or come into being, and so we have no basis on which to assume that this would happen at random in a multiverse.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.4.2.1. Pro: It could be argued that this would be outside of the jurisdiction of physics, since physics deals with the results of physical laws, not the laws themselves. No amount of understanding of physics can conclude *why* physics exists.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.4.2.2. Pro: By contrast to the multiverse, the god of classical theism would have the means to create and change physical laws due to its omnipotence.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.4.3. Pro: Perfect randomness may be a concept that could only exist within a mind.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.5. Con: If an infinite multiverse existed, then not only would implausible beings like Santa Claus and the Easter bunny exist, but so would every pagan/animist god and goddess. Therefore not only is a multiverse lower probability than virtually every religion, but belief in it entails beliefs in gods.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.5.1. Con: Santa Claus and the Easter bunny are only implausible within our universe. Their existence in other universes, where they may be perfectly plausible and probable, doesn't count as evidence against the multiverse theory.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.5.2. Con: Pagan gods existing in causally disconnected universes are irrelevant to our lives, and as such their existence can be safely ignored.
1.4.3.1.3.1.1.5.2.1. Con: Depending on how disconnected they actually are, if they have followers in this world, they are influenced by these Gods, and warrant attention.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2. Con: The fine-tuning argument carries the implied proposition that the universe is designed for life to exist, rather than assuming that life exists as a result of adapting to the universe.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.1. Pro: The universe is so unimaginably immense, has existed and will exist for such a huge amount of time that it's not statistically impossible for the conditions for life to exist at least at some point of the universe, at some period of time.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.1.1. Con: The argument from fine-tuning isn't concerned with the likelihood of life existing within the universe, but with the likelihood that the universe would exist in such a way that it permits life at all.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.2. Con: The purpose of the universe is the conclusion of the fine-tuning argument, not any of its premises.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.3. Pro: With an infinite amount of time \(including whatever existed before time started\), and an infinite number of universes, even low probability events become quite likely.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.4. Con: The fine-tuning argument refers to constants and quantities that, if changed, would not allow for the formation of stars, matter, or complex chemistry, or else would result in near-instant heat death or collapse of the universe. It's clear that no universe such as these could support any form of life.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.4.1. Con: Our current understanding of physics partially supports this claim, however our current understanding is incomplete and physical behavior if the constants were outside the goldilocks zone can only be extrapolated based on our incomplete understanding.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.4.2. Con: The origin of these physical constants and the origin of the physical forces they apply to is unknown, there could be a common as yet undiscovered constant or variable controlling the values of these constants which can explain fine tuning without resorting to God.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.4.2.1. Con: Arguments hinging on evidence that may be discovered in the future are exceedingly weak. The claim could be made that it's possible we'll discover a faraway planet with the entire New Testament carved into its side, so God almost certainly exists. We can't investigate arguments like these because we don't have the hypothetical evidence in front of us.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.4.2.2. Con: Although a shared constant may lessen the amount of fine-tuning required, it would not eliminate it, since such a constant would itself be arbitrary and thus need to be finely tuned.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.4.2.3. Pro: The fine tuning could stem from the theory of multiple/infinite daughter universes posed by quantum physics: there could be a universe with any configuration of forces, and the chances are one of these would have the correct values for our observable universe to exist as it does, as unlikely as it is
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.4.2.3.1. Con: This assumes that the values of the fundamental forces are assigned probabalistically, of which there is no evidence
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.4.2.3.2. Con: This assumes that the daughter universes theory is true, which has no evidence
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.5. Con: There is no contradiction between believing that the universe was fine-tuned to permit life to exist and that life arose naturally within the universe and adapted to its environment.
1.4.3.1.3.1.2.5.1. Con: The fine-tuning argument's conclusion that the universe was created to support life is what is contradicted by the idea that life exist by the sheer size of the universe, and ability of natural adaption to occur.
1.4.3.1.3.1.3. Pro: The Likelihood Principle states that if some observation O is likely under hypothesis A and less likely under hypothesis B, then O counts as evidence for A over B.

The existence of a life-permitting universe \(O\) is likely assuming a creator with the intent to create life \(A\), and less likely assuming the absence of such a creator \(B\).
1.4.3.1.3.1.3.1. Con: The Likelihood Principle is only useful on its own when the probability distribution over the hypothesis space is uniform. The model of the Universe being used which assigns that probability distribution is key: Creationism isn't the only model. The prior probability that there is a creator in the first place also needs to be taken into account.
1.4.3.1.3.1.3.1.1. Con: The previous argument didn't assume there was only one possibility. It explicitly allowed two: God's existence or non-existence.
1.4.3.1.3.1.3.1.2. Con: [Bayes rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bayes%27_theorem) shows that the principle applies regardless of your prior.
1.4.3.1.3.1.3.1.3. Con: It not been established that the prior probability of God existing is so low that any evidence of God's existence can be ignored.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4. Con: The low amount of life found in the universe so far suggests that it is not particularly hospitable to life.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1. Pro: That the universe should be so vast while also being so uninhabited, argues against there being a god. If God exists, there should be more life, or less universe.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.1.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.3. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.2.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.4. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.3.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.5. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.4.1.1.1.4.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6. Con: We don't have a good grasp on how much of the universe is inhabited.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1. Con: We know that we haven't found life anywhere we've looked for it, and have looked in a lot of places.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1.1. Con: To date we have only discovered [fewer than 4,000 exoplanets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exoplanet), which is a minuscule number compared to the expected amount.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1.1.1. Con: 1 in 4000 is a speciously low hit rate for a god.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1.2. Con: We don't yet have the technology to say with absolute certainty that any given planet or moon is lifeless, especially outside of our solar system.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1.2.1. Con: If there is life there, it's nowhere near the level we are at.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1.2.1.1. Con: We don't yet have [the technology needed](https://what-if.xkcd.com/47/) to detect civilizations like ours from interstellar distances.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1.2.1.2. Con: A creator may not specifically prefer civilized technological life.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1.2.1.2.1. Con: From this, one would necessarily conclude that God hates us. Although it seems consistent with other information, it's not a popular position.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1.2.1.2.1.1. Con: It's unclear how not having a specific, individual preference for something necessarily implies hatred.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1.2.1.2.1.1.1. Pro: I don't always prefer pizza to burgers or vice versa, but that doesn't mean that I hate either or both.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.1.2.1.3. Con: The technology of an alien civilization may be unrecognizable to us.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.6.2. Pro: The space we consider as empty may be bursting with life in parallel dimensions.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7. Con: The universe may be infinitely large and contain an infinite number of inhabitants.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.1. Con: The universe is not infinitely large. It is expanding at the speed of light, but it not infinite. [We know this because we know that at the time of the big bang, all of space and time was limited to a single point.](https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/according-to-the-big-bang-1999-10-21/)
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.1.1. Con: [Certain measurements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe#Curvature_of_the_universe) imply that the curvature of the universe is flat, and a flat universe would likely be infinitely large.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.1.1.1. Con: All a flat universe implies is that it is expanding in different places. It is still finite in size from moment to moment.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.1.1.2. Pro: If the universe is Euclidean, which is the simplest shape that fits with its flatness, [then it is infinitely large](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe#Universe_with_zero_curvature).
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.1.1.3. Con: The world is flat enough that we can, for almost all purposes, treat it as flat, but is still a sphere, it's just really big, [this is how integers work](https://www.mathsisfun.com/calculus/integration-definite.html), by dividing the data up until there is local flatness, the same thing could be true of the universe, we might just be to small to see the curves.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.1.1.3.1. Con: Curvature gets smaller at smaller scales, but doesn't disappear completely. This doesn't fit with the data - namely, that absolutely no global curvature has been detected in the universe.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.1.1.3.1.1. Con: The data may not be precise enough to answer this question.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.1.2. Con: The size \(infinite or finite\) of the universe is [currently unanswered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe#Infinite_or_finite) by cosmology. It hasn't been proven by the existence of the Big Bang.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.2.2.1.1.2.1.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.2. Con: Even if the universe had an infinite number of inhabitants, that would not explain the lack of density of life.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.1.1.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.4. Pro: If the universe has an infinite number of inhabitants, there couldn't even conceivably be any more. A God who only prefers a large number of inhabitants would therefore be satisfied.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.4.1. Con: There are levels of infinity, some are quite bigger then others, for example, the set of real numbers includes the set of natural numbers.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.4.1.1. Con: Uncountable infinity cannot be reached from countable infinity by addition or multiplication, which is what would occur if the density of creatures in an infinitely large universe increased.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.4.1.1.1. Pro: [Hilbert's paradox of the Grand Hotel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert%27s_paradox_of_the_Grand_Hotel) shows that adding to or even doubling the number of inhabitants filling an infinitely large space doesn't actually increase that number.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.4.1.1.2. Pro: Uncountable infinity refers to sets that can be separated into infinitely smaller amounts, such as the set of all rational numbers between 1 and 2. It doesn't apply to counting whole entities, such as living inhabitants of a universe.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.4.1.2. Con: This [doesn't apply to all infinities](http://mathforum.org/library/drmath/view/59138.html). For instance, there are as many even integers as there are all integers, because the even integers are defined as being any integer multiplied by 2 - despite it intuitively seeming as if there should be only half as many even integers.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.4.1.2.1. Pro: The presented example does show the problem of density, the number of even integers is clearly less dense then the number of all integers.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.7.5. Pro: We can destroy all life on our planet. If God favors life, He would not keep all eggs in the same basket.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.8. Con: The universe could be so vast so that the life that is there has a place to grow into.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.9. Con: The universe being so vast and relatively uninhabited is not an argument against God's existence. At best it is an argument that you would have created the universe differently than God did.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.2. Pro: The universe does not appear to be fine tuned for life. It appears to be better tuned for making black holes.
1.4.3.1.3.1.4.3. Con: In the context of this argument a hospitable universe is one that supports life, not necessarily produces life.
1.4.3.1.3.1.5. Con: -> See 1.4.2.2.2.1.2.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6. Pro: Purpose-based inductive reasoning is used all the time. For instance, it is far more likely that a log cabin in the woods resulted from a man chopping down trees to build a home, rather than a series of nearby trees falling in a very specific pattern.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1. Con: The problem with this line of thinking is that you still need to explain where the human came from. The reason the log cabin was likely constructed by a man chopping down trees is because there is evidence of the existence of a man, but there is no evidence for the existence of a God.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1. Con: Even if a structure was discovered in the middle of a desert which was thought uninhabited, or on an uncolonized planet, it is still more rational to conclude that at least one sentient creature lives there than that the structure formed by random chance.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1. Con: The probability of a complex creator is much lower than the probability of a life-permitting universe with no creator.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.1. Pro: The arguments related to the [Ultimate Boeing 747 Gambit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultimate_Boeing_747_gambit) provides some insight into why a complex creator is less likely than no creator.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.1.1. Con: The classical God is a disembodied mind. Since the universe already contains at least 7 billion other minds, and has contained many more over its lifetime, God is clearly not significantly more complex than the universe.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Many religions consider God to be omniscient,  omnipotent and omnipresent. Each of these qualities requires the God to be at least as much and possibly significantly more complex than the universe which is predicted, controlled or embodied by God.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: God as classically defined is perfectly simple. Knowledge, power and location do not require complexity if they are absolute and unchanging. God is not relative to the universe.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.2. Pro: In terms of probability, P\(life-permitting universe and sentient designer\) \< P\(sentient designer\) \< P\(life-permitting universe\). Of course P\(life-permitting universe given sentient designer\) is very high, but we have not been given the existence of the designer, that is what the debate is about.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.2.1. Con: The relevant probabilities are P\(life-permitting universe|designer\) and P\(life-permitting universe|¬designer\). According to the Likelihood Principle, if the former is more likely than the latter, then that counts as evidence for the existence of a designer.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.2.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.1.3.1.3.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.2.1.2. Con: What we have for certain is a life-permitting universe, and we are estimating the probability of the designer. 
Let Designer be D and Universe be U
1. P\(D|U\) = P\(D ∩ U\)/P\(U\)
and 
2. P\(¬D|U\) = P\(¬D ∩ U\)/P\(U\)
since the P\(U\) term is common in both 1 and 2, the higher of  P\(D ∩ U\) and  P\(¬D ∩ U\) determines which is more likely.
formulating in terms of P\(U|D\):
P\(D ∩ U\) = P\(U|D\)*P\(D\)
P\(¬D ∩ U\) = P\(U|¬D\)*P\(¬D\)
P\(U|D\) \>\> P\(U|¬D\)  does not automatically lead to P\(D|U\) \> P\(¬D|U\) if P\(D\) \<\< P\(¬D\)
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.2.1.2.1. Con: Given physical calculations involving the life-permitting ranges of physical constants, P\(U|¬D\) seems to be vanishingly small - so much so that any realistic prior value given to P\(D\) still works for this argument.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.2.1.2.1.1. Pro: Just one initial condition of the universe - the mass and energy distribution of the singularity - is fine-tuned to a precision of [1/10^\(10^123\)](https://books.google.co.uk/books?id=yX6591VNz4IC&pg=PA157&lpg=PA157&dq=Initial+condition+of+the+universe+123&source=bl&ots=yNnnKSWrm4&sig=5jP22gIyUrDaCZKlac8qIGTT4nI&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwjcudrHjsvZAhXGJsAKHXhVCz0Q6AEIOzAD#v=onepage&q&f=false). No intellectually honest non-theist would claim that the prior probability of God's existence is significantly lower than that, especially considering that this mind-boggling improbability is raised multiplicatively with many other similarly large figures.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.2.1.2.2. Con: It's not obvious that P\(D\) \<\< P\(¬D\), at least to an extent relevant to this discussion.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.2.1.2.2.1. Pro: P\(D\), or more specifically P\(D|k\) where k is our background knowledge, is raised significantly by other arguments for God's existence, as well as historical and anecdotal evidence.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.2.1.2.2.1.1. Con: In the original claim U is considered to be  the same as K in this claim and therefore P\(D\) doesn't change because of any other information, they have already been factored into the formulation.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.2.1.2.3. Con: The Likelihood Principle only claims that the data counts as evidence - that is, it raises P\(D\) \(and thereby lowers P\(¬D\), since P\(D\) + P\(¬D\) = 1 by the law of the excluded middle\) - not that it necessarily demonstrates that the hypothesis is the more likely one, or P\(D\) \> P\(¬D\).
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.2. Con: The primary flaw in this argument is that we are trying to ascribe the existence of a partially complex object by claiming the existence of a significantly more complex being. This raises the question of the origin of the creator which is an infinite regress.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.2.1. Con: Proposing an explanation for some data doesn't require an explanation for that explanation. When arguing that a certain death was a murder rather than an accident, one doesn't need to explain the murderer's diet, place of birth, and current living conditions before making the claim.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.2.1.1. Con: This analogy is not appropriate because there is irrefutable evidence for the existence of murderers, making the existence of murder a likely possibility and the origin of the murderer irrelevant. The existence of God however has not been irrefutably proven, hence the origin of God is key if the hypothesis that God exists is accepted without irrefutable evidence.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.2.1.2. Con: Why doesn't that explanation need an explanation? If one suggests that complex things need a creator, you need to explain why the creator doesn't need a creator, otherwise you have created an infinite loop which means eventually "something" comes from "nothing", thus circumventing the need for God at all as a possible explanation.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.1.1.
1.4.3.1.3.1.6.2. Con: We only know this to be the case because we can compare naturally occuring objects with objects we know to be designed. If all things were designed, the cabin would be a single cabin in a world of other cabins, and there would be no undesigned objects to compare the cabin to. We recognize design because we know what things that weren't designed look like.
1.4.3.1.3.1.7. Pro: Some interpret [biological structuralism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Structuralism_\(biology\)) to support a creator, as they argue it requires physical constants to be [fine tuned](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_dYRvujeUFE) to enable the life forms we see.
1.4.3.1.3.1.7.1. Pro: According to process structuralism, biological systems would be a bit like crystals, in that they're likely to form and propagate wherever conditions are right \(instead of only in individual, random circumstances\).
1.4.3.1.3.1.7.2. Pro: Since the adaptation and shape of life would result directly from the physical laws of the universe, process structuralism suggests that if the universe is fine-tuned, it's not just for life - it's fine-tuned for exactly the type of life that exists, and for humans as well.
1.4.3.1.3.1.7.2.1. Con: Saying because we observe a form of life, the universe must be made for that type of life is equivalent to saying because a random number generator happened to spit out a random sequence of numbers in one instance, it must be made to spit out that particular string of numbers. It might make sense to say something like it was made to make a recognizable pattern if we happen to see a pattern we recognize, but not to say it was made for that pattern, even by the pattern logic.
1.4.3.1.3.1.8. Con: The [anthropic principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropic_principle) observes that, for conscious beings to wonder about the possibility of their existence in the universe, conscious beings must exist in the first place. Therefore, asking such a question is pointless because the fact of asking it implies a positive answer.
1.4.3.1.3.1.8.1. Con: The anthropic principle doesn't have any explanatory power. Take the analogy of a man sentenced to death by firing squad, who finds after all the shots are fired that each trained marksman missed him entirely. If he had died he wouldn't have been able to wonder about the situation, but the fact that such a thing happened clearly doesn't make it less unlikely or less requiring of an explanation.
1.4.3.1.3.1.8.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.1.3.1.3.
1.4.3.1.3.1.8.1.2. Pro: The fact that we can know a certain event happened only gives us certainty that it happened, not why it happened. The latter is what the fine-tuning argument is concerned with.
1.4.3.1.3.1.8.2. Con: The anthropic principle, when used correctly, only applies to scenarios where there are many possible spaces observers could occupy, but only a few wherein observers could actually come to exist/survive. For instance, it's pointless to wonder why we find ourselves on a planet with liquid water, rather than another planet - if there were no liquid water, we couldn't exist. The same doesn't apply here, where no probability spaces other than our universe are known to exist.
1.4.3.1.3.1.8.3. Con: The ability to ask a question doesn't factor into whether the question is meaningful. I can ask why my parents got together, even though if they hadn't I wouldn't be able to ask such a question - there is still a meaningful explanation for their getting together.
1.4.3.1.3.1.9. Con: -> See 1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.2.
1.4.3.1.3.1.10. Con: -> See 1.4.3.1.3.1.6.1.1.1.
1.4.3.1.3.1.11. Con: It's possible that the constants actually have only one possible value, which would mean the universe couldn't be tuned for life or anything else
1.4.3.1.3.1.11.1. Con: The constants referred to are [dimensionless](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dimensionless_physical_constant), meaning their value doesn't depend on any other value or measurement. It's unclear how these values couldn't be different.
1.4.3.1.3.1.11.2. Con: The initial quantities of the universe, such as the mass/energy distribution and the entropy of the singularity, are also very finely tuned - but there is no obvious reason to believe that the contents of the universe could not have been different.
1.4.3.1.3.1.11.3. Con: No argument has been given for the logical impossibility of a different set of physical laws. In the absence of such an argument, our default assumption should be that since a physically different universe is conceivable, it is possible.
1.4.3.1.3.1.11.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.2.
1.4.3.1.3.1.11.3.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.2.3.
1.4.3.1.3.1.11.4. Pro: As far as we know, the universal constants are constant. There is absolutely no evidence to suggest any other value is possible. Therefore, variations are not probable
1.4.3.1.3.1.11.4.1. Con: Asserting that variations are not probable because we are aware of no evidence to suggest other possibilities is a non sequitur. One could as easily say, "We have no evidence to suggest that the universal constants must necessarily have their known values, therefore variations are probable." 

Neither position is warranted.
1.4.3.1.3.1.11.5. Con: The possibility of having only one possible set of constants does not imply the plausibility of such a universe. We have no warrant to reject the possibility of other values for these constants without evidence that such a constraint exists.
1.4.3.1.3.1.11.6. Con: This does not refute the finely-tuned relationships between the constants that are required support life. In fact, with only one chance to get it right, the fact that the universe supports life would be even more miraculous.
1.4.3.1.3.1.12. Con: My existence in our universe would be impossible if any number of laws, constants, and initial conditions were changed even unimaginably slightly. Following the logic of the above statement, it would imply the strong probability that the universe was created for the purpose of harboring me!
1.4.3.1.3.1.12.1. Con: As a member of the set of "life", the argument is expected to apply just as well to you as to any other creature or to life as a whole.
1.4.3.1.3.1.12.2. Con: A theist would typically agree that the universe was made for the purpose of hosting you, in a sense, since God has an individual purpose for everyone.
1.4.3.1.3.1.13. Con: People are bad at understanding probabilities, and this is formalised as the Lottery Fallacy. The chance of me winning the lottery is astronomically small, but every week or so someone has a big win.
1.4.3.1.3.1.13.1. Con: The problem of fine-tuning isn't based on popular understanding of probability, but on formal probability such as the Likelihood Principle and Bayes' Theorem. [See here](https://calumsblog.com/2017/07/25/full-defence-of-the-fine-tuning-argument-part-3/) for an in-depth explanation of the logic behind it.
1.4.3.1.3.1.13.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.1.3.1.3.
1.4.3.1.3.1.14. Con: We have no reason to assume that the values a universal constant could take are equally likely. we cannot say anything about the probability that
1.4.3.1.3.1.14.1. Con: Without a mechanism proposed to explain the universal constants, sharing equal probability space is the simplest explanation. To assert anything else without solid reason is ad hoc.
1.4.3.1.3.1.15. Con: There are [other known sets of laws of physics congenial to life](http://web.uni-plovdiv.bg/marta/life_in_the_multiverse.pdf), that have significant difference to our own, including ones with an entire fundamental force missing and these lie in the tiny region of laws of physics we're able to conceive of and simulate.
1.4.3.1.3.1.16. Con: [Conway's game of life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway%27s_Game_of_Life) shows that life like phenomenon can emerge from radical different "laws of physics". The hostility of similar laws to our own in no way extrapolates to laws distinct from our own. As such it's possible that most laws pass the level of congeniality to life, and we just exist in an island surrounded by hostility.
1.4.3.1.3.1.17. Con: Suppose the universe was created for the sole purpose of creating life, our lives specifically. It is just as likely that humans, or some other race, advanced technologically far enough at some point in the future to crack time and dimension travel and go back before the Big Bang to make it happen ourselves knowing that we wouldn't ever exist without it, as it is that there is a God. We could be our own gods by creating a time-loop paradox. The fact is, there is no way to know yet.
1.4.3.1.4. Con: This cycles back to the original thesis of proof for God's existence and does not reach an end in itself.
1.4.3.2. Con: Some things have meaning, order and design.  Many things do not.  The teleological argument for God's existence makes the mistake of assuming that the traits of some objects must belong to all objects. That assumption is not justified.
1.4.3.2.1. Con: That the universe has purpose is the conclusion of the teleological arguments, not one of their premises.
1.4.3.2.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.1.1.7.1.2.1.1.3.
1.4.3.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.1.3.1.
1.4.3.4. Con: Teleological arguments based on the existence of specific things are nullified if natural explanations are discovered for those things.
1.4.3.4.1. Pro: Many teleological arguments are from the complexity and diversity of life. These arguments have been disproven by the discovery of evolution, which can explain the appearance of life without a divine designer.
1.4.3.4.1.1. Con: Evolution does not explain the appearance of life - rather only how it changes and adapts. The appearance of life is referred to as [abiogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis). At present there is no single generally accepted model for abiogenesis.
1.4.3.4.1.1.1. Con: given what we know of the world before, and after that point, this argument seems equivalent to the famous 'missing link' argument, while this is something we need to work on, it seems that in order for simple facts to be accepted by some, we must map out the location of every proton from the start of the universe to present.
1.4.3.4.1.2. Con: Biological evolution, if true, still requires that the laws of physics and chemistry be in a very particular form. If they were even slightly different, evolution would likely be impossible. So that evolution is even possible implies a teleology to the natural order.
1.4.3.4.1.2.1. Con: This isn't necessarily true. If the laws of physics or chemistry were different, we may have simply seen a different form of love evolved to function in the universe as it would be under different circumstances. There is no way to evaluate the probability of this being plausible or implausible.
1.4.3.4.1.2.2. Con: The particularity of forms do not imply or necessitate "purpose" or teleological meaning.
1.4.3.4.1.3. Con: This is assuming macro-evolution is indeed true, which is, itself, an unverifiable theory based on conjecture and estimations.
1.4.3.4.1.3.1. Con: The unprovability of science is a myth based on a failure of our education system to properly communicate the scientific method.
1.4.3.4.1.3.2. Con: There are [multiple](http://darwin-online.org.uk/converted/pdf/1861_OriginNY_F382.pdf) [sources](http://www.bbc.com/earth/story/20150803-how-do-we-know-evolution-is-real) which contradict the idea that macro-evolution is an unverifiable theory.
1.4.3.4.1.3.2.1. Con: Both sources are biased towards a particular outcome, and are thus unreliable.

The BBC piece itself admits that current research methods into evolutionary science are yet incomplete to fully determine how distinct species can somehow transition into an entirely distinct species.
1.4.3.4.2. Con: Here John Lennox's [arguments](http://augustinecollective.org/god-in-mathematics-and/) come into play. A natural explanation based on laws is unsatisfactory unless we know how those laws were put in place. Laws come from lawgivers.
1.4.3.4.2.1. Con: Equating cosmological constants to laws dictated by minds is a false equivalence. Natural "laws" are not dictated rules that must not be broken. They are observations about the natural world.something that simply is does not indicate a need for someone who declares it to be that way.
1.4.3.4.3. Pro: The absence of a presently adequate explanation is not reason to assume there is no adequate natural explanation. Every explanation we currently have was true and adequate before we understood it.
1.4.3.4.4. Con: This argument does not follow because "natural explanations" and "teleology" are not mutually exclusive. Natural explanations can only answer the question, "How?" but teleology addresses, "To what end?"
1.4.3.4.5. Con: So far such natural explanations have not been found \(i.e. modern evolutionary theory and the precise assignation of the values of the fundamental forces\)
1.4.3.5. Con: Teleological arguments are [god-of-the-gaps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/God_of_the_gaps) arguments. This makes them a kind of [argument from ignorance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argument_from_ignorance).
1.4.3.5.1. Con: Not all are 'God of the Gaps' arguments, because some point to features of the world, e.g. order and regularity in the universe, that not only aren't explained by current science, but couldn't even in principle be explained scientifically at all.
1.4.3.6. Con: Charles Darwin refutes the Teleological Argument. "The old argument of design in nature, as given by Paley, which formerly seemed to me so conclusive, fails, now that the law of natural selection has been discovered."

[On the Origin of Species](https://books.google.com/books?id=eTfRotZTXI0C&pg=PA431&lpg=PA431&dq=The+old+argument+of+design+in+nature,+as+given+by+Paley,+which+formerly+seemed+to+me+so+conclusive,+fails,+now+that+the+law+of+natural+selection+had+been+discovered.+We+can+no+longer+argue+that,+for+instance,+the+beautiful+hinge+of+a+bivalve+shell+must+have+been+made+by+an+intelligent+being,+like+the+hinge+of+a+door+by+man.+There+seems+to+be+no+more+design+in+the+variability+of+organic+beings+and+in+the+action+of+natural+selection,+than+in+the+course+which+the+wind+blows.+Everything+in+nature+is+the+result+of+fixed+laws.&source=bl&ots=r_-4RLU_U6&sig=h3X6c7cJ9O5q1r1UUhSum_iOrug&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwjA1uWhwbnYAhXB7yYKHYj1CisQ6AEIMzAC#v=onepage&q=The%20old%20argument%20of%20design%20in%20nature%2C%20as%20given%20by%20Paley%2C%20which%20formerly%20seemed%20to%20me%20so%20conclusive%2C%20fails%2C%20now%20that%20the%20law%20of%20natural%20selection%20had%20been%20discovered.%20We%20can%20no%20longer%20argue%20that%2C%20for%20instance%2C%20the%20beautiful%20hinge%20of%20a%20bivalve%20shell%20must%20have%20been%20made%20by%20an%20intelligent%20being%2C%20like%20the%20hinge%20of%20a%20door%20by%20man.%20There%20seems%20to%20be%20no%20more%20design%20in%20the%20variability%20of%20organic%20beings%20and%20in%20the%20action%20of%20natural%20selection%2C%20than%20in%20the%20course%20which%20the%20wind%20blows.%20Everything%20in%20nature%20is%20the%20result%20of%20fixed%20laws.&f=false)
1.4.3.6.1. Pro: Richard Dawkins [refutes](https://www.huffingtonpost.com/richard-dawkins/why-there-almost-certainl_b_32164.html) the Teleological Argument. "Natural selection is so stunningly powerful and elegant, it not only explains the whole of life, it raises our consciousness and boosts our confidence in science's future ability to explain everything else"
1.4.3.6.2. Con: The Theory of Evolution doesn't cover the entire scope of the teleological argument.  Arguments like fine-tuning via the cosmological constant or nuclear force, the strength of gravity etc, cannot be answered by evolution, because evolution has organized matter as a presupposition, but without these features, there is no organized matter.
1.4.3.6.3. Con: Natural selection can only act upon the random variation that occurs in the genetic code.  The Modern Synthesis of Evolution has been challenged upon the grounds that the random variation does not have the probabilistic resources sufficient to explain either [functional proteins](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0022283604007624), or [larger complex features](https://www.witpress.com/elibrary/dne-volumes/4/2/399).
1.4.3.6.4. Con: -> See 1.2.2.2.4.
1.4.3.7. Pro: Arguments from [consciousness](http://www.philosophyofreligion.info/theistic-proofs/the-teleological-argument/the-argument-from-consciousness/) support the existence of God.
1.4.3.7.1. Pro: Adams \(1992\) and Swinburne \(1979\) [argue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argument_from_consciousness) deductively from the disjunction  that mental events either have a personal or natural scientific explanation. Given that mental events are genuinely nonphysical, a personal explanation demonstrates the mental and physical event type correlations. If the explanation is personal, then it is theistic.
1.4.3.7.1.1. Con: Mental events are thoughts, which are the product of mind, an emergent property of brain. They are very physical.
1.4.3.7.1.2. Con: Deductive reasoning presumes accurate premises. The non-physical nature of 'mental events' is not a premise with any backing, thus nullifying the deduction.
1.4.3.7.2. Pro: The ability to reason must be a dispensation from a higher being because it can't come from the irrational.
1.4.3.7.2.1. Pro: As [C. S. Lewis identified](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argument_from_reason) arguing from reason that God doesn't exists means someone has to  "ask me to accept a conclusion while discrediting the only testimony upon which that conclusion can be based" because irrational processes can't produce the ability to reason.
1.4.3.7.2.1.1. Con: Evolution is the only thing we know has ever produced reason, and lacks any mind or rationality.
1.4.3.7.3. Pro: As philosophers like Nagel, Levine and Chalmers have shown, conscious states cannot be identified with physical / natural states. Hence, conscious states are incompatible with physicalism/naturalism, though they are compatible with theism. Thus, conscious states give us reason to prefer theism over physicalism/naturalism.
1.4.3.7.3.1. Con: As [most philosophers are physicalists](https://philpapers.org/archive/BOUWDP), Nagel, Levine and Chalmers do not seem to have sufficiently shown this.
1.4.3.8. Con: The teleological argument has repeatedly been demonstrated to be fallacious and invalid. The appearance of design can arise through natural processes and this appears to be the case with living organisms, stars, crystals and other supposed evidences of design. In fact the opposite appears to be true: all so-called intentional design arises from physical laws acting on human brains and bodies and thus arises naturally as well. There is no evidence that unnatural design is possible.
1.4.3.9. Pro: The universe is way too complex and intricate for it to have been a product of pure coincidence. Our bodies are organisms that function as a microcosms, with everything being coded in our DNA from head to bottom. The multiplicity of species, of systems, of connections, and nexuses between things don't just fall into place on their own. A Supreme Being put them together so that they could function autonomously with everything having an exact role and function.
1.4.3.9.1. Con: The fact that a natural explanation for a phenomenon isn't currently known \(even when this is true\) is poor evidence that no natural explanation exists.
1.4.3.9.1.1. Pro: This is often phrased as "God of the gaps." When science explains all the gaps, there is no more God.
1.4.3.9.1.2. Pro: At one time it was believed that the animacy of living things was inexplicable to science and could only be explained by an irreducible [élan vital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%89lan_vital); this was obviously false, as biology has shown itself reducible to ordinary physical processes.
1.4.3.9.2. Con: The complexity of biology is not consistent with the work of an intelligent designer.  There are many evolutionary "wrong turns" that make sense in the context of natural selection, but not in the context of intelligent design.

For example, it makes no sense for an intelligent designer to make lizards with vestigial \(non-functioning\) eyes.  However, the vestigial eyes make sense as an adaptation to the pitch-dark cave environment the lizards inhabit.
1.4.3.9.2.1. Con: According to Christianity, initially creation was good, but then creation was cursed, bringing death into the world. With this understanding of our current place in history there is plenty of room for imperfect creations.

For example: God makes lizards with functioning eyes, then the world gets bad, then the lizards go underground, and after natural selection, we have lizards with blind eyes.
1.4.3.9.2.1.1. Con: This argument uses natural selection to explain the existence of vestigial organs and concedes that vestigial organs are not consistent with an explanation by intelligent design. 

"Cursed creation" cannot possibly explain all examples.  For instance, avian species form a yolk sac during the course of their development, while mammals form and then degrade a yolk sac.  Yolk sac formation in mammals is consistent with an evolutionary hold-over, but not consistent with a "cursed creation."
1.4.3.9.2.2. Con: To claim an evolutionary wrong turn creates a false equivalency between man and God. If God intentionally created lizards with vestigial eyes, who are we then to call that a mistake? Our limited capacity for understanding can often cap our sense of perspective. Modern science has yet to come to a consensus on why we yawn or the function of one's appendix, but this does not mean they are fundamentally meaningless or inherently flawed.
1.4.3.9.2.2.1. Con: Evolution can explain these wrong turns quite well, there vistiua traits made to solve a form of a problem that doesn't exist, and not adapt to what they are currently needed to do.
1.4.3.9.2.2.2. Con: There are apparent contradictions in biology between form and function.  Many species have vestigial organs which perform no apparent function.  From a molecular standpoint, many species posses genes which have been silenced, but which still function in ancestor species.  While intelligent design cannot provide an explanation for these apparent contradictions, the theory of evolution by natural selection is consistent with these apparent contradictions.
1.4.3.9.2.3. Con: Complex biology and organisms could have been created by an Intelligent Designer and later have natural selection act on them.
1.4.3.9.2.4. Con: Natural selection could be a mechanism established by the Designer to ensure His creation's adaptation and survival.
1.4.3.9.2.5. Pro: The eye is a good example of suboptimal design. A liquid filled eye in a gaseous environment is less effective than a gas filled eye in a gaseous environment. Evolutionists believe this happened because the eye evolved in aquatic animals first. It seems a theist would be left to believe god chose to make humans sub-optimally or lacked the ability to make a gas filled eye.
1.4.3.9.2.5.1. Pro: The issue is one of [refraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refraction), when light passes from our gas filled environment to a liquid filled eye, the light distorts. The human brain adapts but our vision could be better without the refraction problem.
1.4.3.9.3. Pro: DNA is understood universally to be an extremely complex code that details every aspect of an organism's appearance, behavior, and physical description. Any code that precisely conducts a complex set of commands with regard to computers and technology is understood to have an author who wrote it. Yet, the most complex code in existence - DNA - supposedly just wrote itself. This defies basic logic and common sense.
1.4.3.9.3.1. Con: There is nothing contradictory about complex systems arising from random chance.
1.4.3.9.3.2. Con: Each gene describes a protein or number of proteins that will be expressed by an organism.  In fact, if we look across species, we can observe a series of gradual changes to the same genes, causing modifications to the expressed proteins.  We can also observe that some genes, while still expressed in species of early origin, are silenced in species of later origin.  In summary, evidence for the natural selection of genetic change exists.
1.4.3.9.3.3. Con: Your inability to comprehend how DNA could arise through natural processes is not evidence that didn't happen. It's an argument from personal incredulity. Plenty of people do understand how this could have occurred. It is a false equivalence fallacy to assume that because one complex code has an author that all complex codes have an author. Complexity is not the component that implies authorship.
1.4.3.9.3.3.1. Con: Origin of life studies continue, but we still do not understand how the first life came to exist.  At the time of this writing, this has not been demonstrated in a lab, nor has it been observed in nature.
1.4.3.9.3.3.2. Con: DNA is a language, in the same way that a computer language is a language, complete with syntax, punctuation, phrases, etc. and has a high level of specified complexity.

No other known code with a similar level of specified complexity has ever been discovered that was not designed and created by an intelligent agent. Therefore, it is reasonable to assume that the DNA code has an author.
1.4.3.9.3.4. Con: Without comprehensive examples of complex systems which were not authored/designed, we cannot know with certainty the nature of such systems.
1.4.3.9.3.5. Con: The [appeal to common sense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divine_fallacy) is a known logical fallacy.
1.4.3.9.3.6. Con: Software applying evolutionary algorithms has proven to be able to design complex designs beyond the [capability \(and sometimes understanding\) of humans](https://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/st-5/main/04-55AR.html) without being intelligent or conscious.
1.4.3.9.3.7. Con: A chain of DNA can be extraordinary long, but there are only four possible rungs on the ladder that vary along its length. It may contain vast information, but it is simple in its constituent parts.
1.4.3.9.4. Con: -> See 1.4.3.9.3.1.
1.4.3.9.5. Con: The quality known as "complexity" is relative to the limits of  human language and intellect.
