The laws of the universe appear to be complex, and they require sophisticated language to communicate, but this is only proves that humans struggle to explain the universe.  This fact communicates nothing about the origin of the universe.
1.4.3.9.6. Con: Complexity is not synonymous with intention.
1.4.3.9.6.1. Pro: A hallmark of intentional creation is simplicity, not complexity. It is the lack of redundant features for achieving a purpose that starts to point to design.

1.4.3.9.9. Con: Our universe is not a product of random coincidences. Evolution is a result of cumulative selection guided by natural selection. The only random aspect is random mutation in gene replication. Each aspect moving backwards from the formation of ecosystems, planets, stars, the universe itself have similar explanations that do not come down to coincidence.
1.4.3.9.9.1. Pro: Our understanding of genetics and mutation adequately explain minor changes through the course of reproduction.
1.4.3.9.9.2. Con: To claim that natural selection is "well understood" minimizes the breadth of data and research that can never be done since institutions of higher learning have only sprung up recently. Processes that take billions of years can be surmised, hypothesized, and even generally accepted, but they are not well understood.
1.4.3.9.9.2.1. Con: Bacteria and other microorganisms have extremely short lifespans and thus evolution through natural selection can-and has been- directly observed.

1.4.3.9.9.3.1. Pro: The origin of the first cell is outside the scope of evolution, just as the origin of the sun is.

1.4.3.10. Pro: [All things](https://www.lds.org/languages/eng/content/scriptures/bofm/alma/30) denote there is a God. The laws that are in our universe [dennote](https://www.lds.org/languages/eng/content/scriptures/bofm/2-ne/2)there is a God.

1.4.5. Pro: The [simulation hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simulation_hypothesis) holds that reality is simulated by an external being, and there is good reason to believe that such a being would be the classical God.
1.4.5.1. Pro: It is likely that our universe is simulated.
1.4.5.1.1. Pro: If entities capable of, and willing to, simulating universes are able to develop, then simulated universes are [likely to outnumber real ones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simulation_hypothesis). According to the [Copernican principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copernican_principle), we likely exist in the least special, or most numerous, type of surroundings that can support observers. As such, we likely exist in a simulated universe.
1.4.5.1.1.1. Con: Such a being wouldn't necessarily meet the classical definition of God \(omnibenevolence, timelessness, simplicity, transcendence, immutability, etc\).
1.4.5.1.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.14.
1.4.5.2. Pro: The entity simulating our universe is God \(theological [idealism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Idealism)\).
1.4.5.2.1. Pro: Consciousnesses such as ours cannot be simulated in a computer, but only within another consciousness - a mind.
1.4.5.2.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.1.1.1.
1.4.5.2.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.3.6.9.3.1.1.2.
1.4.5.2.2. Pro: There are three broad types of things that may be simulating our universe: a quantum computer in a universe such as ours \(see Bostrom's ancestor simulation hypothesis\), a classical computer in a classical universe, or a mind. We do not ultimately exist in either a quantum or classical computer, so we must exist in a mind.
1.4.5.2.2.1. Pro: An ancestor simulation or other simulation within a quantum computer would have to exist in a quantum world very similar or identical to ours. According to the law of [indiscernibility of identicals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Identity_of_indiscernibles), if our universe is likely simulated, then any identical universe is also likely simulated. So any quantum simulation must itself ultimately exist within a mind.
1.4.5.2.2.2. Pro: In order for a classical computer to simulate a universe it must contain as many states as the universe it is simulating, and thus be larger than it. A computer larger than our universe would be incredibly inefficient and impractical to build for any entity or civilization that isn't omnipotent. Therefore, we are not simulated in a classical computer.
1.4.5.2.2.2.1. Con: A simulated universe/reality could be much smaller than the entire universe that we believe exists, such as a simulation of only our Solar System.
1.4.5.2.2.2.2. Con: A simulated universe could drastically simplify many aspects of the universe if the simulators were not interested in them; most stars could be modelled as perfect spheres with the properties expected of those stars without us being any the wiser.
1.4.5.2.2.3. Con: Even if we exist in some kind of a mind, there is no reason to call that mind "God".
1.4.5.2.3. Con: The simulation could be created by a species not that much more advanced than humans.
1.4.5.2.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.5.1.1.1.
1.4.5.2.4. Con: Your mind of this moment, complete with memories, could have multiple instantiations over time or on different computers, with different owners or multiple owners. They are not the classical God.
1.4.5.2.5. Con: It is not likely that God would need a simulation of reality when it has the ability create reality.
1.4.5.3. Con: The simulation hypothesis seems to be untestable, which means it doesn't provide a reliable path to the truth about the existence of an entity simulating our universe, leave alone that this entity is a god.
1.4.5.4. Con: The simulation hypothesis is an independent hypothesis from the god hypothesis, and it does not support or detract from it.
1.4.5.4.1. Con: Both hypotheses have far-reaching implications over an entire worldview if one believes in them, and for the hypotheses to be truly independent, those implications would have to \(improbably\) never interact.
1.4.5.5. Con: The simulation hypothesis was created specifically to avoid invoking any deities to explain our existence.
1.4.5.6. Con: This does not escape the necessity of explaining God's existence.
1.4.5.6.1. Con: There is no necessity of explaining Gods existence.  The discussion is on if “God exists”, not why God exists.
1.4.5.7. Con: The universe as it exists cannot be simulated.
1.4.5.7.1. Con: Our universe could be easily simulated in reality based on higher number of dimensions, just as for us it's much easier to simulate 2D.
1.4.5.8. Con: The simulation hypothesis involves rationalising things outside the universe as if they were within the universe, there is no evidence to suggest this is valid.
1.4.5.9. Pro: Within a simulation, origin notwithstanding, a non-human designer may be omnipotent, omniscient, for all intents and purposes omnipresent, and perfectly benevolent.
1.4.5.9.1. Con: This is begging the question. There is no reason provided to explain why a designer would be inherently benevolent. Following your logic, God is God to me as Will Wright is God to [the Sims](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Sims); yet, even in that context, we'd be hard-pressed to call Mr. Wright omnipotent, omniscient, and omni-benevolent, even relative to the Sims themselves these attributes are vacuous.
1.4.5.9.2. Con: They *may* be these things, but our universe does not appear as if it were designed or managed by one who is omnipotent and perfectly benevolent.
1.4.5.10. Con: The simulator would not have the personal awareness of each human being that the classical God has. Nor the absolute goodness, mercy, etc
1.4.5.10.1. Con: At least in the case of "personal awareness of each human being", if the simulator's mind is running on hardware comparable to what it's using to run the simulation, there's no reason it couldn't have such extensive knowledge.
1.4.5.11. Pro: The universe itself was created by an intelligent mind out of a informational model.
1.4.5.11.1. Pro: Intelligent minds exist \(Humans for example\) and can create informational model's of the universe \(like string theory\).
1.4.5.11.2. Pro: [Digital physics](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_physics) implies that the universe is fundamentally information.
1.4.5.12. Con: We could be very well simulated in the sense that we are a mathematical model that can be expressed in a mathematical universe. No consciousness is required to simulate us.
1.4.5.13. Con: It is hubris to suggest that only Omnipotence could simulate \(or create\) reality as we know it. It's possible that between man and God \(should such exist\) there could be any number of beings capable of such.
1.4.5.14. Con: There is no "good reason" to believe such a being would be the classical God. 
The statement also assumes the "classical God" in question is the Christian one,  \(and not - say - the Hindu one\), nothing suggests that humans are created in "his" image.
1.4.5.14.1. Con: This argument is simply detracting from the semantics of the argument and not addressing whether the hypothesis actually supports the existence of a higher entity regardless of if it is regarded as a "classic God" or not.
1.4.5.14.1.1. Con: In a debate over whether God exists, it's not mere semantics to point out that some nebulous "higher entity" is not necessarily God.
1.4.5.15. Pro: -> See discussion #9858: We exist within a [Simulated Reality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simulated_reality).
1.4.5.16. Con: The simulation hypothesis is currently a matter of debate itself. Evidence that its predictions from matematics and theoretical physics are supported by observation is still missing, so it can't be used further than to claim "It may be a simulation". Even if it where true, the starter of the simulation might have finite computational resources, which would be incompatible with an omnipotent God
1.4.6. Con: Agnosticism holds that the lack of evidence makes it impossible to know whether God exists.
1.4.6.1. Con: There is no reason to think why we, in principle, could not know. It might be the case that if all the arguments in favor of God's existence are not on balance enough to justify belief that we do not know, but there is no reason that we could not possibly know.
1.4.6.2. Pro: Justified belief in the claim "God Exists" requires relevant, compelling evidence.
Because there is no such evidence, belief in the claim "God Exists" should be withheld.
1.4.6.2.1. Con: There is good reason to believe that God exists.
1.4.6.2.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.
1.4.6.2.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.
1.4.6.2.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.2.
1.4.6.2.1.4. Pro: -> See 1.4.2.1.
1.4.6.2.1.5. Pro: If one has taken none of the necessary steps to know God, one cannot claim that He cannot be known.
1.4.6.2.1.5.1. Con: Many atheists come from religious families and/or have backgrounds backgrounds and have taken various steps in search of truth regarding God, eventually arriving at the conclusion that there is no valid reason to believe that God exists.
1.4.6.2.1.6. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.
1.4.6.2.1.7. Con: The teleological, moral, ontological, and cosmological arguments have repeatedly been demonstrated to be fallacious and invalid.
1.4.6.2.1.7.1. Con: It is only accepted among atheists that the arguments in favor of God's existence are demonstrated to be fallacious. It remains hotly contested that these arguments have \(all\) been shown to be false.
1.4.6.2.1.7.1.1. Con: William Lane Craig, a professional philosopher and one of the foremost living Christian apologists, has infamously attested that his belief in God is ultimately grounded in the "inner witness of the Holy Spirit," with evidence and rational argument playing a subsidiary role. This suggests that religious apologists will continue to believe in God and contest the validity of these arguments even if they are demonstrably fallacious.
1.4.6.2.1.7.1.2. Con: The other half of that coin is: it is only among theists that the arguments in favor of God's existence are not demonstrated to be fallacious. It is yet to be shown that any of those arguments is sound, all of them having the validity of their premises or assumptions hotly contested.
1.4.6.2.1.7.1.3. Con: This argument is disjointed from what it is replying to. If not all arguments in favour of God's existence are false, then it does not follow that any subset of arguments are true.
1.4.6.2.1.7.2. Con: This is a response to certain philosophical arguments and not all philosophical arguments, as such this is disjointed from the parent claim it is refuting. Any subset of God proving arguments could be fallacious and just as long as there is one philosophical argument that is non fallacious then God would be proven. \(Perhaps the responses should be moved under their positive counterparts as cons and vice versa?\)
1.4.6.2.2. Con: This is an unsupported claim. It asserts that the arguments for God’s existence fail on the grounds that the evidence provided is not compelling, yet provides no epistomological definition of what constitutes acceptable evidence.
1.4.6.2.3. Pro: In what may well be an apocryphal story, the great atheist philosopher Bertrand Russell was once asked what he would say if he found himself standing before God on the judgement day and God asked him, "Why didn’t you believe in Me?" Russell replied, "I would say, ‘Not enough evidence, God!  Not enough evidence!'"
1.4.6.2.3.1. Con: Russell said this a long time ago. We have more evidence now that we should look at ourselves.
1.4.6.2.3.1.1. Pro: Evidence for universal fine-tuning \(as opposed to the fine-tuning of only the Earth's environment\) only began to be discovered in the 1960's, near the end of Russell's life, and has been steadily piling up ever since.
1.4.6.2.3.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.1.3.1.
1.4.6.2.3.1.2. Pro: Several ontological arguments, such as Plantinga's and Maydole's, weren't written until after Russell's death. Gödel's wasn't discovered and published until after his death either.
1.4.6.2.3.1.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.13.
1.4.6.2.3.1.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.5.
1.4.6.2.3.1.2.3. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.6.
1.4.6.2.3.2. Con: A single person lacking evidence, even if he were telling the truth, neither implies that evidence does not exist, nor does it imply that the thing in question does not exist.
1.4.6.2.4. Con: The argument here rests on the principle of non-contradiction. We are justified in believing that principle, even though we lack sufficient evidence for it. \(There are no good arguments for it, and what sensory evidence there may be for it is inadequate.\)
1.4.6.2.5. Con: Lack of evidence is immaterial to beliefs in general. Almost all claims which are ostensibly rejected for lack of evidence are actually rejected due to various elements of prior implausibility.  Russell's teapot is an ironic example. Believing in the teapot is not problematic because of insufficient evidence, but rather because a teapot in orbit is extremely improbable either to have originated naturally or to have originated artificially.
1.4.6.2.5.1. Con: It is epistemically wrong for anyone, anywhere to believe anything on insufficient evidence. \(Modified [Clifford's Dictum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethics_of_belief)\)
1.4.6.3. Con: Belief in something does not require 100% proof but sufficient evidence.
1.4.6.3.1. Pro: Epistemologically, the only undeniable statement is "I think, therefore I am". All other claims weighed by partial evidence, or by rules such as "justified true belief", rather than requiring that we "know for sure".
1.4.6.3.1.1. Con: Epistemologically "I think, therefore I am" is no more sound an argument than "I am the subject of a proposition, therefore I am". We could stop at "there is a thought" and that would be pretty much sound. But I don't see how that entails the existence of a thinking being. The word "I" is illegitimate since it does not describe a datum, though it is convenient in normal language.
1.4.6.3.2. Con: The example of the pancreas does not apply to God because the basic scientific model of the pancreas is well defined in scientific community and proved in accordance with scientific criteria, whereas neither the scientific community nor the religious communities have a formal and unified definition of God.
1.4.6.3.3. Con: Infering information based on proof of related phenomena is the cornerstone of all research and is a fantastic tool, however this does not have an analoguous point in the search for god. We have ancient writings of people who already believed in god/gods unquestionably, and offered us no proof other than parables, myths and stores of happenings that are both unverifyable and as believable as any piece of work written by those long since dead.
1.4.6.3.3.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.2.1.
1.4.6.3.3.2. Pro: There is no demonstrable scientific evidence that proves the existence of God.
1.4.6.3.3.2.1. Pro: Every claim of observable features of the world that point to the actions of gods or other mythical beings has been shown to be caused by natural means. While theists believe the origin of the natural world is a miracle, empirical facts point to natural explanations for the origins of life, Earth, and the universe without need to appeal to any mythological explanation. For those hoping to find empirical evidence for their faith, "God of the Gaps" is the only \(fundamentally flawed\) proposal left.
1.4.6.3.3.2.1.1. Con: The claim is too broad. The universe itself is an observable feature of the world; yet cannot be shown to be caused by natural means.
1.4.6.3.3.2.1.1.1. Pro: According to our best scientific data, time, space, matter, and energy \(the "universe", or "nature"\) came into existence. It is impossible for something that does not exist to act in any way, and therefore, "nature" cannot bring about its own existence.
1.4.6.3.3.2.1.1.1.1. Pro: The system mentioned does not perfectly support itself \(even as a whole the universe is slowly coming to a halt\).
1.4.6.3.3.2.1.1.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.11.1.
1.4.6.3.3.2.1.2. Con: The presupposition here is that God can only be an explanation for physical and contingent things. This is simply not true, and trying to appeal to science is the ''crackers in the pantry'' fallacy. You don't prove God like you don't prove the law of logic with science. God's Mind is what links immaterial forces of logic and number to be coherent instead of dialectical insanity working against each other.
1.4.6.3.3.2.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.2.1.2.2.
1.4.6.3.3.2.3. Con: Science is incapable of proving or disproving the existence of God because it only tells us the method by which things occur and not the reason why they occur.
1.4.6.3.3.2.3.1. Con: Science suggests that how an event occurs is by way of thermodynamical processes, which is precisely the same as the reason why they occur i.e. because of thermodynamical processes
1.4.6.3.3.2.4. Pro: Science & research is the only tool which has ever reliably proven or disproven anything with consistent results and so should be adhered to
1.4.6.3.3.2.4.1. Con: The problem is in the term 'anything.' The scientific method has a limited domain. For example, developments in history like the Fall of Rome resist reductive scientific methods.
1.4.6.3.3.2.4.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.2.1.2.2.2.3.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5. Pro: The [Sagan Standard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sagan_standard), "The weight of evidence for an extraordinary claim must be proportioned to its strangeness" often paraphrased as "Extraordinary claims require extraordinary evidence"; implies that there needs to be a lot of very convincing evidence for the existence of God, which is lacking.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.1. Con: That the Sagan Standard is valid is, itself, an extraordinary claim, yet it is accepted with no evidence whatsoever.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.1.1. Con: The Sagan Standard is an easily derived pattern from probabilities of claims being true given their similarity with events known to be true. Thus, the Sagan Standard is neither necessarily accepted without evidence nor is it extraordinary.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.2. Con: The "Sagan Standard" presupposes the uniformity of nature, which itself would be very extraordinary without a god, but which lacks any proof.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.2.1. Con: The uniformity of nature has been amply demonstrated.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.2.2. Con: The existence of a God plus the uniformity of nature is more unlikely than the sole existence of uniformity of nature.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.3. Con: If one is willing to accept miracles, then they would count as sufficiently extraordinary evidence.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.3.1. Con: Miracles are generally events unlike any other, taken as true based on evidence that does not at all satisfies the Sagan Standard.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.4. Con: No claim is intrinsically strange, though some claims seem strange to some people. If Sagan were right, then the amount of evidence needed for a claim would depend on whose perspective we were taking.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.4.1. Con: "Strange" there simply means how \[dis\]similar to claims that are actually the case a claim is. That does not depend on an individuals perspective.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5. Con: The Sagan Standard can also apply for the claim that any god doesn't exist; that is, it is unscientific to claim no god exists when there is no extraordinary evidence proving said god's nonexistence \(especially considering we have no way of observing or measuring that which exists "superior to" or outside of "creation" or the expanding universe\).  In as much, Agnosticism makes more scientific sense than Atheism.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1. Con: [A priori](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori), the existence of God is improbable.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.1. Pro: Out of a myriad of possible scenarios contradictory with God's existence, God is just one possible case. That puts God at a lower than average a priori probability of being real.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.1.1. Con: This is a misapplication of statistical reasoning. With billions of persons in the world, it is highly unlikely that precisely this small subset of persons would collaborate on this discussion; yet here we are.

It is absurd to say that two plus two cannot equal four because the odds of the correct answer being four out of an infinite number of integers is \(literally\) infinity to one. Yet that is an exact analog to the argument presented here.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.1.1.1. Con: Something being the case does not mean that a priori \(ie. without specific information\) it is not highly unlikely.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: It is highly unlikely that I am in any specific galaxy in the universe, yet I am in the Milky Way - which is only likely given certain a posteriori information \[for instance information about me or about humans\].
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.1.1.2. Con: At least this discussion is likely to have people in it, since it is linked to a network that makes it likely \(ie social network\). The existence of God has no such reasons to be believed a priori.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.1.1.3. Con: Two plus two are known to equal 4 a priori, since they are part of a framework that follows logically from a priori conditions in Math. Taking any two random symbols representing quantities between -infinity and +infinity however makes it quite certain that the results will not equal four. And that is a lot more analog to the argument presented.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.2. Pro: The existence of God is asserted on basis that would be \(and often are\) unacceptable for other fictional characters.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.3. Pro: Most people who believe in God don't have sound justifications for their beliefs.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.4. Con: The argument of "improbability" is not a sound way of disproving the existence of God. Many things, even the development of human life through evolution, could be regarded as "improbable" and yet that does not serve as a refutation against the existence of human life.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.2. Pro: Applying the Sagan Standard to the atheistic assertion that the universe came into existence from a state of non-existence without an external agent \(which is also an extraordinary claim\), would seem to imply that such an agent should exist, or that the universe should not exist. As it is self-evident that the universe exists, it follows that the Sagan Standard supports a theistic position.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.3. Con: [Agnosticism](http://www.dictionary.com/browse/agnostic) is not exclusive from either theism or atheism. [Gnosticism](http://www.dictionary.com/browse/gnostic?s=t) deals with knowledge; [Theism](http://www.dictionary.com/browse/theism?s=t) deals with belief.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.6. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.7. Con: Even if the Sagan Standard were a legitimate criterion, it remains to be shown that there is anything strange or extraordinary about the claim that God exists.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.7.1. Con: The lack of examples of gods is proof that "God exists" it is an extraordinary claim.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.7.2. Con: -> See 1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.8. Con: The Sagan Standard is unsupported, as there is [no clear reason](https://calumsblog.com/2017/07/25/do-extraordinary-claims-require-extraordinary-evidence/) to expect the level of evidence for a claim to be proportionate to its oddity.
1.4.6.3.3.2.5.8.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.3.3.2.5.1.1.
1.4.6.3.3.2.6. Pro: Experiences that may prove the existence of God lack reproducibility.
1.4.6.3.3.2.6.1. Pro: Considering a God which answers to prayers. One may pray months and get nothing that \(s\)he awards to God, whereas one may never pray and award something to God. Then, this God's existence theory is not reliable.
1.4.6.3.3.2.6.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.4.2.2.
1.4.6.3.3.2.6.1.2. Con: The definition used in this debate does not require that God answer intercessory prayers.
1.4.6.3.3.2.7. Con: "Demonstrable evidence should not be appealed to in this case, because humans as 3rd dimensional beings are incapable of perceiving a higher dimensional being."
1.4.6.3.3.2.7.1. Pro: God's existence can be proven on a person-by-person basis through the practice of meditation, deep devotion, or the path of wisdom.  This is where the individual becomes a spiritual scientist using the laboratory his/her own consciousness to discover the consciousness of God within him/herself.  Since science depends on the testimony of the senses, it is too limited to discover God.
1.4.6.3.3.2.8. Pro: [Falsifiability](http://whatis.techtarget.com/definition/falsifiability) is required to prove that something is or is not true and cannot be achieved without a measurable focus. As god is not measurable you can not use scientific method to test the validity of god.
1.4.6.3.3.2.8.1. Con: To the extent that you can not use the scientific method to test the validity of God this is true. However this suggests that, as God is not a scientific theory, the scientific approach is wrong. It would be the same to try to scientifically demonstrate the existence of love or other abstract concepts.
1.4.6.3.3.2.8.1.1. Con: There is no problem to demonstrate existence of love scientifically. It exists as a social phenomenon and behaviour of people.
1.4.6.3.3.2.8.1.1.1. Con: This commits a category error. 

Behavior is observed, but is not love. Love may motivate behavior, but it cannot be directly observed.
1.4.6.3.3.2.8.1.1.1.1. Pro: Providing medical care can be an act of love. However, a prison doctor may provide medical care to the serial killer that murdered his family out of duty rather than love. The observed behavior is the same, but the motivating force is different.
1.4.6.3.3.2.8.1.1.1.2. Con: There is behaviour associated with love that is distinct from love as a source of motivation \( both exist\). The behaviour has been studied scientifically, e.g. in Kinsey reports.
1.4.6.3.3.2.8.1.2. Con: Abstract concepts can be and have been modelled scientifically for example in computer science. [Software Design patterns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Software_design_pattern) [Abstraction in computer science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstraction_\(computer_science\)) [Category theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Category_theory)
1.4.6.3.3.2.8.1.2.1. Con: A software design pattern is an abstraction... until it is implemented. Then it is a concrete implementation of an abstract pattern.
1.4.6.3.3.2.8.2. Con: Falsifiability serves as a test for a scientific theory, but positive evidence can maintain a theory as scientific despite failing the falsifiability test. 

For example, Darwin said that the fossil record is incomplete and serves as the biggest objection to his theory. The fossil record still poses a problem for evolution, but in light of other positive evidence the theory continues to be accepted by the majority of scientists.
1.4.6.3.3.2.8.2.1. Con: Falsifiability or other scientific principles would not require completeness of the fossil record. It does require that when new measurements are made, the theories adapt to include the new evidence to the extent it's valid. Usually accepted theories are very resistant to new evidence \( they include it already \).
1.4.6.3.3.2.9. Pro: Given God is all-powerful then God must be part of nature, therefore God would be detectable using the same methods by which we detect other natural entities.
1.4.6.3.3.2.9.1. Con: God us understood to be the creator of nature. Just as an artist is not in his painting, nor a sculptor in her sculpture, nor the author of "The Sims" a part of the Sims universe, God is not a part of nature. The creator transcends and is in no way dependent on the creation, although he may interact with it from time to time.
1.4.6.3.3.2.9.2. Con: It does not follow that, if God is transcendent to nature, that such a God would be "limited".
1.4.6.3.3.2.10. Con: Human life - self aware, conscious and free willed - is evidence of consciousness being either a fundamental characteristic or an emergent property of the universe itself. Given that [scraps of DNA have been found even on asteroids](https://sservi.nasa.gov/articles/dna-building-blocks-can-be-made-space/), its likely that life is fairly common, though it does require [Goldilocks conditions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circumstellar_habitable_zone) to manifest as human-level \(or higher\) forms. The fact that all of this happened naturally - on its own - is evidence that a theistic God could have set up the board.
1.4.6.3.3.2.11. Con: Absence of demonstrable scientific evidence would not show that an entity does not exist. The Higgs Boson existed before we knew of the existence of atoms.
1.4.6.3.3.2.11.1. Con: That doesn't apply to the case of God, people claimed the existence of god before any evidence.
1.4.6.3.3.2.11.2. Con: -> See 1.4.6.3.3.2.5.5.1.
1.4.6.3.3.2.12. Con: Few things are actually "provable". While it may be true that scientific evidence cannot "prove" God's existence, many lines of scientific evidence for theism are presented in this very discussion. The question is not whether they prove God's existence conclusively, but whether the evidence indicates that it is more likely than not that God exists.
1.4.6.3.3.2.13. Con: -> See 1.2.2.7.
1.4.6.3.4. Con: Sufficient evidence is the same as 100% proof.
1.4.6.3.4.1. Con: Many persons have been thrown in prison by the standard of "sufficient evidence", only to be set free later when other evidence \(perhaps via DNA or video\) was found that offered "100% proof" of their innocence. The claim is demonstrably false.
1.4.6.4. Pro: There is no reasonable and consistent standard of evidence that allows for belief in the existence of a god.
To be consistent, a theist must believe in Bigfoot, faeries, and all other imaginable things. But this is not reasonable.
To be reasonable, a theist must not believe in Bigfoot, faeries, and all other imaginable things. But this is not consistent.
1.4.6.4.1. Con: There isn't a clear reason to put God in the same category as mythological or imaginary things, and [arguments based on such a categorization fail](https://calumsblog.com/2012/12/24/why-i-do-not-think-god-is-supernatural/).
1.4.6.4.2. Con: It is consistent to believe in the classical God based on the arguments and evidence set forth in this discussion, but reject mythological and imaginary entities such as fairies since they don't have similar arguments or evidence.
1.4.6.4.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.2.1.
1.4.6.5. Pro: There is also no firm basis upon which to judge that God doesn't exist either. The hypothesis that a God exists has to be treated as possible since there is no counter fact that proves this hypothesis wrong.
1.4.6.5.1. Con: An argument presented without proof can be dismissed without proof.
1.4.6.6. Con: The existence of agnosticism as a position on the topic of God's existence is not a counterpoint to the claim that philosophical evidences support the existence of God.  One could very well take a position as an agnostic while acknowledging some arguments and evidence for God exists, but still determining it is impossible to have justified belief or true knowledge of God's existence.
1.4.6.7. Pro: Like [Russell's Teapot](https://infogalactic.com/info/Russell%27s_teapot), claims about God's existence need to be proved before they are believed. The burden of proof lies with theists.
1.4.6.7.1. Pro: There is not enough scientific evidence to prove that leprechauns do not exist, nor that unicorns and the flying spaghetti monster do not exist.  If your standard for acceptance of a given belief is simply a lack of scientific disproof, then you must also believe in leprechauns, unicorns, the flying spaghetti monster, and any other ridiculous thing that I might want to suggest. This is why there is a burden of proof before things must be believed.
1.4.6.7.1.1. Con: Theists have accepted the burden of proof and provided evidence for the existence of God.
1.4.6.7.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.

1.4.6.7.1.1.5. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.
1.4.6.7.1.1.6. Con: The arguments used for God's existence could also be used to support the existence of the [Flying Spaghetti Monster.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flying_Spaghetti_Monster?wprov=sfla1)
1.4.6.7.1.1.6.1. Pro: These arguments about FSM were explicitly designed to be similar to arguments about God that are considered fallacious by supporters of FSM.
1.4.6.7.1.1.6.2. Pro: Any approach to argumentation that cannot distinguish God from FSM during the argument would be rejected because it is against the principle of freedom of religion of either the believers of God or of believers of FSM.
1.4.6.7.1.2. Con: Theists do not make positive claims to know of the non-existence of leprechauns or flying spaghetti monsters.
1.4.6.7.1.2.1. Con: Atheists do not make positive claims to know of the non-existence of god.
1.4.6.7.1.3. Con: Unlike creatures like monsters and leprechauns, the existence of God is a universal, or nearly universal belief, which transcends cultures and history. While this itself does not prove its truth, it provides a stark contrast to purely fantasy constructs.
1.4.6.7.1.3.1. Con: Fictional characters such as monsters and leprechauns are universal and transcend culture and history as well.  Though descriptions vary, nearly all cultures have monsters and various fantastic creatures in their folklore.  The idea of God does not stand in stark contrast to these other fictive constructs.
1.4.6.7.1.3.2. Con: There is no single universal notion of God. Instead there are multiple different and incompatible notions. This variation occurs because the concept of God contains pure fantasy elements.
1.4.6.7.1.4. Con: -> See 1.2.2.2.1.2.1.3.1.
1.4.6.7.2. Con: We believe and investigate some claims without proof. There is no scientific proof or evidence that alien life exists and yet there are no objections to the continued search for it. The search for and belief in God are no different.
1.4.6.7.2.1. Con: That the search for extraterrestrial life is based solely on a belief is clearly stated, and never confused for the statement that this life exists.
1.4.6.7.2.2. Con: The existence of alien life is an empirical probability, based on the number of planets close enough to their sun to form a habitable atmosphere. God has no such basis.
1.4.6.7.2.3. Pro: There are no objections to the continued scientific search for God either, so long as this research is scientifically formulated ─ which at its most fundamental level means that it is argumented in terms that can be falsified.
1.4.6.7.3. Con: -> See 1.4.6.7.1.1.
1.4.6.7.4. Con: -> See 1.4.6.3.
1.4.6.8. Pro: An all knowing, all seeing god would speak \(or act\) to make his presence known. Indeed, most early writings of major religions support this notion that god makes his will clearly known by communicating to humans. Yet, no such communication exists today.
1.4.6.8.1. Con: The followers of Jesus and Jesus himself claimed that God had made himself known through both the prophetic writings and the person of Jesus - that he himself is the communication of the presence and will of God. Those writings are available today, and claim to be a communication from God to humans.
1.4.6.8.2. Con: The presumption that an all knowing and all seeing God would speak or act to make its presence known is logically unfounded. An artist, for example, does not maintain eternal vigil alongside the works they have created.
1.4.6.8.2.1. Con: If we presume that certain sacred documents are divinely inspired, dictated, or authored, then it seems to follow that God has at least some interest in communicating with humanity, thereby making its presence known.
1.4.6.8.2.1.1. Con: God may have had interest in communicating with humanity historically, but may believe that past communication was sufficient enough and is not necessarily tied to an eternity of such communication.
1.4.6.8.2.1.2. Con: The interest in communicating with humanity may not stem from a desire to make its presence known - correlation is not causation. The existence of such documents merely indicates a supernatural presence. For an idea of its interests, one has to gather more information, say from the documents themselves.
1.4.6.8.3. Con: Mormons believe that God continues to communicate with humans today through living prophets today as he did in ancient times as recorded in the Bible and the Book of Mormon. [\(see Prophets\)](https://www.lds.org/topics/prophets?lang=eng)
1.4.6.8.4. Con: The Bible is said to be God's communication to men. In the Bible it states why man is left alone without communication for the time.
1.4.6.8.4.1. Con: Historical records \(including some present within the Bible\) suggest it was written and compiled for political purposes. Particularly gaining support for: the attempted annexation of Judah to Israel \(~8th century BCE\), the Maccabean Revolt \(167 BCE - 160 BCE\) and the First Jewish-Roman War \(66 CE - 70 CE\).
1.4.6.8.4.2. Con: Nothing within the Bible suggest it was truly inspired by a higher power.
1.4.6.8.4.2.1. Con: Being unable to see that something is inspired by a higher power is not evidence that it wasn't inspired by a higher power.
1.4.6.8.5. Pro: The way to check for God would be prayer studies. Results showed prayer does not work.[STEP study](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16569567)
1.4.6.8.5.1. Con: Biblically prayer is an act of faith, and must be undertaken with a believing heart.  A prayer study is by nature and act of skepticism or doubt, and therefore by nature is faithless.  Therefore, the nature of the study is self defeating; as a person of faith does not require a prayer study.
1.4.6.8.6. Con: There is no current intellectual consensus on what would constitute proof positive of such communication, which might be a reason why there appears to be no communication at all - if the folder doesn't exist, there can't possibly be any documents in it.
1.4.6.8.7. Con: At a personal level, many believe the Vedas to be divinely inspired, if not dictated. Similarly for the Bible and the Quran.
1.4.6.8.7.1. Pro: In traditional Hinduism, even the Mahabharata is considered a true tale of direct divine intervention in human affairs. As for whether it truly is an example of divine communication - the story has been studied and commented on by several generations of Indian writers, and has been found to contain deep lessons in philosophy, statecraft, human morality and psychology. In many ways, it is a textbook for human psychological development. \(It's also a damn good story, but I may be biased.\)
1.4.7. Con: Atheism holds that the absence of evidence for God's existence is good evidence that God does not exist.
1.4.7.1. Pro: Whether there are good reasons or not to believe in something does not affect its existence. By this logic, if God did not exist, and I had a reason to believe God existed, God would suddenly exist.
1.4.7.1.1. Con: Personal reasoning is not the same as evidence. While there might be a reason/belief behind believing that a God does not exist, there is evidence/fact to prove that there is a God who exists.
1.4.7.1.2. Pro: The same logic also holds for the contrary. Even if everybody were to believe that God does not exist, it would not affect God's existence.
1.4.7.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.2.
1.4.7.3. Pro: Source [physicalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physicalism) is many times more probable than omni-theism.
1.4.7.3.1. Pro: [Occam's Razor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occam's_razor) suggests physicalism should be the preferred explanation over the existence of God.
1.4.7.3.1.1. Con: Occam's Razor only holds in the case of equally strong competing explanations. There are good explanations of the existence of God, which, if they are stronger than the explanations of his non-existence, invalidate Occam's Razor.
1.4.7.3.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.2.1.
1.4.7.3.1.1.2. Con: Occams razor works everywhere we're trying to explain observations.
1.4.7.3.1.1.3. Con: Occam's razor only applies to testable ideas. Since there are no tests available for or against a god's existence, Occam's razor does not apply to god claims.
1.4.7.3.1.1.3.1. Con: Occam's Razor has nothing to do with testability whatever, and is in fact most decisive where we do not have recourse to empirical tests.

For example, the hypothesis that "toys come to life when they are not being observed, but then return everything to exactly the position that it would have been once humans can directly or indirectly perceive them" is not testable, but we reject it because the rival \(but empirically indistinguishable\) explanation is simpler.
1.4.7.3.1.1.3.2. Con: Occam's Razor can apply to an untestable hypothesis. It is based on the principle that a simple hypothesis is more testable and therefore more useful than a complex hypothesis. If a hypothesis is so complex as to be unfalsifiable, the Razor says that it should be discarded.
1.4.7.3.1.1.3.2.1. Pro: Applying Occam's Razor to claims in favor of a god's existence means that those claims should be discarded since they are not falsifiable.
1.4.7.3.1.1.3.3. Con: The classical theistic definition of a god is testable. Therefore, Occam's razor can be used.
1.4.7.3.1.1.3.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.
1.4.7.3.1.2. Con: Occam's Razor is just a useful heuristic and should never be used as the final arbiter to decide which theory is to be favored.
1.4.7.3.1.2.1. Con: It's purpose is as a means of deciding which of available solutions should be favored.
1.4.7.3.1.3. Con: -> See 1.4.7.3.1.1.3.
1.4.7.3.1.4. Pro: Materialism, and the study of matter, has produced consistent understandings of physics, biology, mathematics and technology all over the world. In contrast, belief in god, as well as beliefs about his intentions and values, vary from person to person and between the [hundreds](https://simple.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_religions) of religious sects worldwide.

It follows that physicalism, as a heuristic, yields a more objective, robust and useful explanation of and approach to the universe and our place within it.
1.4.7.3.1.4.1. Con: Scientific progress doesn't rely on physicalism in the sense of rejecting all non-material claims, only the belief that the material world follows knowable rules. Belief in or rejection of God has no relation to that.
1.4.7.3.1.5. Pro: Bertrand Russell's [teapot analogy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russell%27s_teapot) asks if one imagines a teapot was in orbit around the sun, would we consider the fact that it's very small, or perhaps that the sun blocks it from view, be reasons to believe such a teapot exists? 

Russell was employing Occam's heuristic within this analogy by asking is a thing which cannot be observed, measured or seen to have any [consistent or reliable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/God_Moves_in_a_Mysterious_Way) effect on our lives, worth believing in for any reason other than mere self-[consolation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consolation)?"
1.4.7.3.1.6. Pro: Throughout history god has been cited as the cause for mental illness, the weather, viral and bacterial infection, volcanic eruptions, it was only because certain people preferred physical, measurable explanations of these phenomena that the human race now has facilities for psychology, forecasting weather and natural disasters as well as modern medicine.
1.4.7.3.1.6.1. Con: Citing unexplained naturalist phenomenon is no more reliable of an explanation for unknown causes than that of direct intervention.
1.4.7.3.1.7. Pro: In the absence of scientific explanations for natural phenomena, sentient beings are very likely to come up with the idea of god to explain why natural phenomena occur. The concept gains credence due to the power that comes with it. One individual can use the concept of god\(s\) to exert influence over the other \(e.g. "do this or the gods will be angry"\). This has been repeated across the world with the creation of many different gods.
1.4.7.3.1.7.1. Con: The fact that different unrelated cultures have developed ideas of God, divinity or transcendence is an argument for a diferent origin to those ideas other than cultural heritage.
1.4.7.3.1.7.2. Con: Modern theological arguments \(teleological, moral, ontological, cosmological\) arrive at the existence of a God deductively, by revealing a situation that could only be properly explained by something we'd call a God, not by seeing unanswered questions and proposing a God to explain them.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3. Con: A tendency for creatures to believe in God is exactly what we'd expect if God created humans to believe in Him.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1. Con: Atheists exist which is not what we would expect if a god existed
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1. Pro: An omniscient being who wants to be noticed should be impossible to miss, so to even merely lack belief rather than disbelieve should require ample evidence that such a God exists. 

To actually maintain a positive belief in God should require even more substantial evidence than would be required merely to be agnostic.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.1. Con: Classical theism says nothing about divine desire for being noticed, so divine hiddenness is not an argument against the classical theism.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2. Pro: If God does exist, then he is a terrible communicator. This would seem to undermine the theistic notion that he cares about people and wants them to be able to know, love, follow, and worship him.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2.1. Con: A God that issues specific instructions to every last human is not producing life in its own image - it would be producing slaves. To acheive God's own image requires that humans also have free will. The proper exercise of any ability - including free will - requires a period of 'training wheels off' practice. In this context, that means a God that privileges free will over obedience to divine command. Even if that produces 'errors' like evil.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2.1.1. Con: False dichotomy, there's a lot of space between God personally telling everyone exactly what to do, and the lack of evidence we have in reality.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2.2. Con: Consider the difficulty of an immortal being passing along teachings to very short lived creatures. Hands on, constant instructions would require almost infinite repetition and eternal presence - God would pretty much have to devote an inordinate amount of time \(a limited resource in this universe if God obeys its own laws\) to each generation of humans. Also, if you're training someone to think, then puzzles and quests are far more efficient than explicit instructions.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2.2.1. Con: "If God obeys \[his universe\]'s own laws" is a big assumption to make of a character defined by his omnipotence.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2.3. Con: Where the 'burden of understanding' lies - with the speaker of with the listener - is also a relevant aspect. In kindergarten, the burden is on the speaker - the teacher must simplify everything to the target audience's limits. As ability grows - say college onward - the burden of understanding shifts to the student.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2.3.1. Con: Between a puny mortal human and an allegedly infinite God, the relationship would seem to much more closely resemble that between a kindergarten teacher and their students than a college professor.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2.3.2. Con: Kindergarten teachers and college professors share the important quality of not being invisible to their students. At no point in education is "does the teacher exist?" a question the educational body has any reason to make its students ask.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2.4. Con: Empirical evidence would suggest a contrary conclusion to the claim that "God is a terrible communicator."

Christianity is the most recognized brand in the world. The biblical text and church are enduring religious phenomenon that has reached millions of lives throughout their lifespan.

If the God of Christianity is the theistic God we are addressing, then He has done a wonderful job of communicating to a large number of people throughout the ages.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2.4.1. Con: Creating an impressive organisation by human standards is a terribly low bar for someone who's supposed to be infinitely great
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.2.4.2. Con: How large Christianity is relative to Islam and Hinduism, or rather how large it isn't - the latter combined have more believers than the former - does not make sense if the God of Christianity is real and doing any job at all, even a terrible one, of communicating to humanity.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.3. Pro: The fact that people have to tell you about the existence of a god, is pretty good evidence that they do not exist.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.4. Con: God is impossible to miss except when people are blinded by certain socially constructed habits of thinking which are hostile to Them.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.4.1. Con: One can just as easily assert that God's non-existence is obvious except to people blinded by certain socially constructed habits of thinking.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.5. Con: This argument falsely equivocates an omniscient being wanting to be known and said omniscient being providing irrefutable, scientific/empirical evidence of Their existence.

If an omniscient being wanted to be noticed, from the dawn of humanity until now, They would have to appeal to some other form of human understanding than scientific reasoning, which has only existed for a little over 400 years.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.5.1. Con: It seems just as valid to assert either that:
A\) An omniscient being should wish to be noticed and so should prefer clear, reliable communication
B\) An omniscient should not wish to be noticed and so might prefer vague, unreliable communication.

But since theists also claim that God has an interest in the wellbeing of humanity. If God did exist, and still did prefer option B, then it seems unlikely that God really does have an interest in the wellbeing of humanity.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.5.1.1. Con: Whether or not God is interested in the wellbeing of humanity does not support or refute His existence.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.5.2. Con: The scientific method has only existed for a few centuries, but this is just the codified form of "believing things based on evidence", which is as old as the ability to learn and predates humanity by millions of years.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.6. Con: God does not reveal himself in order to give people the free choice of faith. In other words, God is running a simulation to distinguish those who believe from those who don't.
1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.2. Con: A free will allowing God would allow atheism to exist.
1.4.7.3.1.7.4. Con: A different order of events is even more likely. Sentient beings, having the idea of supernatural being, assign natural phenomena they cannot explain to that being. So God is at least equally or even less likely the consequence of ignorance about nature, He is rather a cause of supernatural explanation of the puzzling phenomena. Why would anybody invent God to justify ignorance? Any ignorance should remain just a simple ignorance, no need for God.
1.4.7.3.1.7.4.1. Con: It is reasonable to believe that people invented God to answer many questions about our universe. People don't like unanswered questions.
1.4.7.3.1.7.4.2. Con: In order to survive, we have evolved the ability to conjecture a cause to a phenomenon, even with incomplete data, as evidenced by our ability to come up with answers at all, but sometimes, this system gets it wrong.
1.4.7.3.1.7.4.2.1. Con: Not related. I never argued against assigning a cause to a phenomena. My claim is about the rank: 1. unexplained phenomena causing the idea of God or 2. already having the idea of God, then assigning unexplained phenomena to Him. We are well aware of ignorance, no need to assign a particular ignorance to God. "I don't know who took my wallet - wow! - it must have been God!" Second option is thus more probable.
1.4.7.3.1.7.4.2.1.1. Con: Although humans like answers, this does not blind them to questions, we are naturally curious, and defiant, and will generally only fall back on an easy answer if we can't find a better alternative.
1.4.7.3.1.7.4.2.1.2. Con: There is a stark difference between questions relevant to everyday survival, and function, and questions we want answered, but aren't easily applicable to our life.
1.4.7.3.1.7.5. Pro: The fact that you can track the evolution of religion to what we currently have suggests that it is a human construct and that no such power/presence exists.
1.4.7.3.1.7.5.1. Con: The fact that religion by definition is a human construct does not disprove God in the philosophical sense.
1.4.7.3.1.7.5.1.1. Con: The classical god supposes that god would want humans to know about its existence, so there should be one accurate religion not many contradictory religions
1.4.7.3.1.7.5.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.1.1.1.4.
1.4.7.3.1.7.5.1.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.1.1.1.
1.4.7.3.1.7.5.2. Con: The idea of the divine permeating culture is evidence of a common origin from a divine creator rather than invention.
1.4.7.3.1.8. Con: Occam's Razor supports other forms of monism, such as nondualism, idealism, panpsychism or computationalism just as well as it supports physicalism, because conscious experience is the only thing that we know for certain exists. Physicalism requires the assumption of new entities which are being perceived yet still 'exist' outside of perception.
1.4.7.3.1.9. Con: Occam's razor prefers the simpler theorem. It a simple explanation that an infinite omnipotent created the universe. A far more complicated theorem is required in order to explain how the universe \(and physics itself\) could come into existence in the absense of God.
1.4.7.3.1.9.1. Con: The theory of God's existence requires you to make a number of assumptions:
1. assume God exists
2. assume him to be powerful enough to create the universe
3. assume that he wants to do so
1.4.7.3.1.9.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.10.2.
1.4.7.3.1.9.1.2. Con: Any theory of the origin of the universe will require the pre-existence of at least something. Even naturalistic explanations such as the big bang start with presupposing some laws of physics.
1.4.7.3.1.9.1.2.1. Con: Laws of physics are testable and demonstrably true. God's existence is not \(or has not been\).
1.4.7.3.1.9.1.2.2. Con: It is true of both naturalistic explanations and the God explanation, that any theory of the origin of the universe will require pre-existence of at least some thing.
1.4.7.3.1.9.2. Con: God creating the universe is less simple according to Occam's Razor. To explain the universe from a non-theistic point of view requires one less thing to explain.
1.4.7.3.1.9.2.1. Con: Removing God from creation does not remove one fewer explanation, but removes the most simple explanation, thus contrasting Occam's Razor.
1.4.7.3.1.9.2.1.1. Con: As long as there is no extensive understanding on how a god would need to be in order to be able to create, god can not be classified as a simple explanation.
1.4.7.3.1.9.2.2. Con: The application of Occam's Razor prefers fewer variables. In theism, the only variable behind the universe is the will of God. Without theism, there can be many variables and brute principles which lie behind the operation of the laws of physics and the origin of the universe.
1.4.7.3.1.9.2.2.1. Con: All of the variables that exist without theism also exist with theism. Theism only adds another variable.
1.4.7.3.1.9.2.2.1.1. Con: Theism unifies diverse things under one single variable, the will of God. This is simpler than having a large number of "brute fact" variables without a unifying source or explanation.
1.4.7.3.2. Con: There is no contradiction between God's existence and the created order operating along physicalist lines, and most evidence we have for physicalism \(above all, its success in guiding scientific hypotheses\) concerns its being true of the world in which we are in. Therefore, most evidence for physicalism is not evidence against theism, even if theism is incompatible with more strict versions of physicalism.
1.4.7.3.3. Con: Physicalism is supported only by Occam's Razor, whereas theism is made even more probable by other factors.
1.4.7.3.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.2.1.
1.4.7.3.4. Pro: -> See 1.4.7.3.1.6.
1.4.7.3.5. Con: Probabilities and evidence are largely irrelevant to the question of God's existence. If you agree that the world exists, and that change occurs, then that is all the evidence that [Aquinas' First Way](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Five_Ways_\(Aquinas\)) requires to logically demonstrate God's existence.
1.4.7.3.5.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.7.4.
1.4.7.3.5.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.1.1.
1.4.7.3.5.3. Con: If probabilities and evidence are largely irrelevant to the question of God's existence, that means there is no debate. But such debates exist.
1.4.7.3.5.4. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.2.3.1.
1.4.7.3.6. Con: There is no known way to calculate the probability of any metaphysical statement. Therefore, probability can't be effectively used for metaphysical study, and other forms of reasoning \(like logical deduction\) are required.
1.4.7.3.6.1. Con: Because of uncertainty in our own reasoning abilities, we can be in a position of regarding in principle a priori positions as more or less likely.

For instance, prior to its more rigorous proof, most mathematicians regarded Fermat's Last Theorem as much more likely to be true than not.
1.4.7.3.6.2. Con: Metaphysics places itself outside measurement and examination. Thus it is unfalsifiable, by definition a fallacy, therefore unworthy of reasonable consideration when hypothesising real explanations for anything.
1.4.7.4. Pro: That which is presented without proof may be dismissed without proof.
1.4.7.4.1. Con: Theism rests on existing, mystifying phenomena such as the fact that humans exist at all. Or that our universe is apparently capable of evolving self aware, free willed creatures out of a lake of hydrogen and energy, and also capable of exploding out of a knot in space time *by itself*. The question might have more to do with what atheists will accept as proof rather than what proof actually exists.
1.4.7.4.2. Con: Atheism is presented without proof and thus could be dismissed for the same reason.
1.4.7.4.3. Con: To present a philosophical concept, proof is not required. Try proving randomness, for instance.
1.4.7.5. Con: We accept the vast majority of meaningful human knowledge without scientific evidence, particularly in the case of social knowledge. This truth criterion would eradicate almost all perfectly useful knowledge.
1.4.7.5.1. Pro: For example, there is no "evidence" that a particular couple are married \(instead, we accept the authority of particular individuals and organisations to come to that conclusion, as people do with God\), but it would be a strange thing to deny that a particular couple were married simply on those grounds; it is, instead, what Emile Durkheim referred to as a "social fact" \(we accept it on the grounds of social convention\).
1.4.7.5.1.1. Con: The existence of God is not a social fact like marriage, it is a fundamental fact about the origins of the universe. As such, it does not depend on social convention.
1.4.7.5.2. Pro: Similarly, I accept the fact that a particular ship is called the QEII because of similar social conventions associated with the naming authority of the person that launched her \(in this case, Queen Elizabeth II\), an aspect of philosophical truth claims based on Austin and Searle's notion of "natural language philosophy".
1.4.7.5.3. Pro: We accept the notion that the physical human body in front of us is a person, not on the grounds of objective evidence from physics, chemistry or biology, but rather on the grounds of what Daniel Dennett calls the "[intentional stance](http://www.timeshighereducation.com/features/a-grand-unified-theory-of-man/414269.article)" in human perception; no actual evidence, but socially necessary. \(see the linked article on this debate with precise reference to theological claims\).
1.4.7.5.4. Con: The parent claim does not specify scientific evidence, so this criticism does not apply.
1.4.7.6. Con: Our knowledge of the existence of something cannot be justified by our senses alone.
1.4.7.6.1. Con: It can be justified by senses and the processing that goes on in the brain.
1.4.7.7. Pro: -> See 1.4.7.3.1.7.3.1.1.
1.4.7.8. Con: Atheism/ atheists do not have a uniformly agreed upon standard of proof for what constitutes evidence for God's existence. That lack of a standard is why there appears to be a lack of evidence, not for an actual lack of data.
1.4.7.8.1. Pro: It is impossible to prove or disprove a thing that has not been described, and Atheism has no description of the God that it claims does not exist.
1.4.7.8.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.7.5.
1.4.7.9. Con: The definition of the theistic deity - the absolutely metaphysically ultimate being - actually implies that everything in existence is proof of God, because at some level, as the universal being, God *is* everything. This may appear a meaningless technicality, but it has profound consequences when it comes to explaining phenomena like the existence of human life.
1.4.7.9.1. Con: The unproven assumption that a God exists is the only thing supporting the claim that "everything" is evidence of God. \(It can be reformulated as: if God exists God is "everything", if God does not exist then God is not "everything".\) This is a circular argument.
1.4.7.10. Con: Atheism makes no claims, only denies the presence of evidence for a god. People who claim there is evidence a god does not exist aren't atheists but believers.
1.4.7.10.1. Con: People who believe that a God does not exist, do not believe that a God exists. Therefore they are atheist \(gnostic atheists, as oposed to agnostic atheists\).
1.4.7.10.2. Con: Agnostic atheists make no claims, while gnostic atheists make the claim that god does not exist.
1.4.7.10.3. Con: A denial is a claim. This is a flawed/illogical argument.
1.4.7.11. Con: Atheism is traditionally the absence in belief in deity.  If atheists, instead, accept a belief with no proof \(even in the non-existence of deity\), then they have essentially become a religion which cannot prove their own claims.
1.4.7.11.1. Con: A religion is more than a simple assertion on a single topic \([dictionary](http://www.dictionary.com/browse/religion)\). Belief in God is not a religion. Belief in the non-existence of God is similarly not a religion.
1.4.7.11.2. Con: The assumption that gnostic atheists can't adequately substantiate their position is unwarranted.
1.4.7.11.2.1. Pro: Many gnostic atheists claim that conceptions of God are incoherent \(either with themselves or with each other\).
1.4.7.11.3. Con: Atheism encompasses all people who do not believe that a deity exists. That includes gnosticism and agnosticism.
1.4.7.12. Con: -> See 1.4.6.2.1.
1.4.7.13. Con: Absence of evidence is not [evidence of absence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evidence_of_absence).
1.4.7.13.1. Con: Absence of evidence when evidence is expected is [evidence of absence](https://rationalwiki.org/wiki/Absence_of_evidence).
1.4.7.13.1.1. Pro: If your neighbor never buys dog food, never walks a dog, and you never hear any barking, that's evidence they don't have a dog.
1.4.7.13.1.1.1. Con: There could always possibly be information that one isn't aware of, or that isn't obvious.  For example, it is possible that \(heaven forbid\) the owner locks the dog in their basement, never to be seen by the world.
1.4.7.13.1.1.1.1. Con: Absence of evidence is not proof of absence, and the parent didn't claim it was. Of course you can think of situations where evidence is absent yet the neighbor still owns a dog.
1.4.7.13.1.2. Con: The claim assumes that we have sufficient knowledge to know when evidence is expected. A neighbor that never buys dog food, never walks a dog, and from whose house you never hear barking may in fact own a dog... that still lives at their parent's house on the other side of town.
1.4.7.13.1.2.1. Con: Evidence is expected exactly when it is expected \(from your perspective, when you expect it\). The claim assumes you have sufficient knowledge to know whether you expect evidence or not.
1.4.7.13.1.2.1.1. Pro: The parent of the above claim conflates "evidence is expected" with "evidence should be expected"
1.4.7.13.1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.4.7.13.1.1.1.1.
1.4.7.13.1.3. Pro: [www.youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=qiNiW4_6R3I)
Let X be any proposition, and E the proposition 'There is evidence in the world that X is true'. 
3 assumptions:
1\)"evidence is expected": given X is true, it is more likely that E is true than false: P\(E|X\) \> P\(not E|X\). Therefore P\(not E|X\) \< 1/2
2\)"evidence is absent": P\(not E\) ≈ 1
3\)It is possible that X is false: P\(X\) \< 1
Proof:
1\)Bayes' theorem: P\(not E|X\) = P\(X|not E\)*P\(not E\)/P\(X\)
2\)Using the assumptions and algebra: P\(X|not E\) \< 1/2
3\)P\(X|not E\) \< P\(not X| not E\) Q.E.D.
1.4.7.14. Con: There is evidence all around in the form of unlikely things.
1.4.8. Pro: Arguments derived from game theory and utilitarianism support belief in the existence of God.
1.4.8.1. Pro: Consider the following: the benefit of belief in the existence of God if correct is eternal reward in the afterlife; if incorrect, there is no negative consequence. There is no benefit of disbelief in the existence of God and being correct; the consequence of disbelief and being incorrect is eternal punishment in the afterlife. Thus there is much to gain and nothing to lose by belief in God's existence.
1.4.8.1.1. Con: That is the Pascal's Wager, a fallacious argument \([inauthentic belief](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal%27s_Wager#Argument_from_inauthentic_belief), [certain failure in the landscape of possible gods](https://rationalwiki.org/wiki/Pascal%27s_wager#Infinite_number_of_gods)\).
1.4.8.1.2. Con: Consider also the contrary claim that the belief in God is punished by eternal damnation. There is no benefit to believing in God and there is infinite consequence to the belief in God. There is nothing to since unsubstantiated claims are counteracted by their opposite. In believing only in what is true at least at least the benefit of knowing the truth is gained \(which I assume is in the interest of a reasonable person\).
1.4.8.1.3. Con: There is much to lose to religion.
1.4.8.1.3.1. Pro: Time is lost praying and attending sermons.
1.4.8.1.3.2. Pro: Money is donated.
1.4.8.1.3.3. Pro: Control of your own morals is given to the religion.
1.4.8.1.3.4. Pro: Independence—Your views become secondary to that of the religion.
1.4.8.1.3.5. Pro: Joining a religion requires granting it a significant amount of social power
1.4.8.1.3.5.1. Pro: The nature of power granted varies arbitrarily from religion to religion, but can include control over your guilt, your reproductive process, what you wear, or who you are allowed to love.
1.4.8.1.4. Con: Utilitarian claims, either positive or negative, are not evidence.
1.4.8.2. Con: Belief in something does not justify the claim that the thing in question actually exists.
1.4.8.2.1. Con: Yet, the existence of beliefs like Karma, do have a positive impact on behalf of civilization by "taming" rough aspects of mankind. To some extent the positive outcome of such beliefs make them true by self-justification and have verifiable effects.
1.4.8.2.1.1. Con: This reasoning only works if belief in X=existence of X. This can work with Karma and \(the value of\) money. But not with other things like God.
1.4.8.3. Con: Utilitarianism, while being productive for morals, often leads to factually false conclusions when applied to truth \(due to a duality between what should be and what is\).
1.4.9. Con: Philosophical arguments for God all originated at a time and from people who were not capable of providing sound arguments for their beliefs. This is because social pressure gave people no option to consider other viewpoints
1.4.9.1. Con: Correct arguments remain correct whoever makes them.
1.4.9.2. Con: There always was an option to believe in God or not to believe in God. Atheism is not a new concept.
1.4.9.3. Con: Many instances of a person defending something they believe in, in an argument, should be an indication/testimony of evidence for how reasonable the belief is.
1.4.9.4. Con: To reject a claim solely because it comes from someone who believes in it is foolish. One would never suggest that we reject the theory of relativity because Einstein believed in it!
1.4.9.5. Pro: It's understandable that if something happens \(like a thunderstorm or rainbow\) and one does not understand why it happens due to lack of scientific knowledge, the person figures it "must" have been done by a god to punish, to help or give a sign they hoped for.
1.4.9.6. Con: Social pressure is now moving the other way, and atheism is becoming the more politically-correct view. This does not provide any evidence supporting or refuting atheism. In the same way, the origin of any argument for God's existence does not provide evidence supporting or refuting theism. This is a classic [genetic fallacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_fallacy).
1.4.9.7. Con: Philosophical arguments for the existence of God where made by people like Aristotle, Plato, Leibniz, and Einstein. The great intellect of these people and their contributions are without question. They were definitely capable of providing sound arguments for their beliefs.
1.4.10. Con: According to the nihilist viewpoint, God does not exist.
1.4.10.1. Con: God can be subjective and still exist.
1.4.10.1.1. Pro: Many argue that numbers and even "random" ideas have real existence \(platonism\).
1.4.10.1.1.1. Con: [Platonism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonism) is not subjective. Platonists hold that abstract objects exist in a "third realm", not that they exist as subjective ideas. [Subjectivism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subjectivism) as a philosophy holds that no external truths exist.
1.4.10.1.2. Con: If God is subjective, then being "God" is not a property of the object, but intrinsically an ideal ascribed within the view of someone looking at the object from a certain prism.
1.4.10.1.3. Con: The God of classical Theism is the uncreated creator of all created things. A god that is subjective is, by definition, a created thing. Thus the two definitions are completely incompatible.
1.4.10.1.3.1. Con: That "creator God" does not exist, as creating requires time and there was no time before the big bang.
1.4.10.1.3.2. Con: That "creator God" does not exist if the [B theory of time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B-theory_of_time) is true.
1.4.10.1.3.2.1. Con: The B theory of time does not eliminate the need for a creator, who could have created and determined the entire course of history.
1.4.10.1.3.2.1.1. Con: If the universe always existed \(as per the b-theory of time\) then it was not created.
1.4.10.1.3.2.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.
1.4.10.1.3.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.
1.4.10.1.3.3. Con: It is compatible with the definition of God being "ultimate metaphysical being".
1.4.10.2. Pro: If all things are metaphysically equal, there is no metaphysical ultimate or supreme.
1.4.10.2.1. Con: Assuming that all things are metaphysically equal assumes naturalism, so this claim begs the question.
1.4.10.3. Pro: The idea of a ultimate metaphysical being \(and therefore it's mapping onto any thing\) is subjective. God's existence is therefore purely subjective.
1.4.10.3.1. Pro: Conceptions of ultimate beings can, and often do, differ between people and cultures. Examples: extremely simple or extremely complex; incapable of thought or endowed with extreme forethought; beyond the universe or the universe; perfectly good or simply uninterested or even severe and totalitarian.
1.4.10.3.1.1. Con: Inconsistent concepts of God may show that some \(or all\) aspects of a given concept is incorrect. It does not show that no concrete object exists behind the concepts.
1.4.10.3.2. Con: That persons subjectively perceive a thing, even in different forms, does not entail that the thing is *merely* subjective.
1.4.10.4. Con: From a nihilistic viewpoint, every argument is ultimately meaningless including arguments for nonexistence.
1.4.10.5. Con: Nihilism, just as religion, is a viewpoint and does not provide any proof to the existence, or non-existence of God.
1.4.10.5.1. Con: For something to be considered divine the attribution of meaning is necessary, which disproves the fundamental existence of divinity.
1.4.10.5.1.1. Con: If God exists necessarily \(which implies immutability\) and eternally, then meaning has also existed necessarily and eternally in the mind of God, which provide divinity with its fundamental existence.

1.4.12. Pro: Many-worlds interpretation of quantum mechanics \(so called "Multiverse" idea\) implies that there are an infinite number of universes. Therefore there must be a universe where God exists.

[General description of the "many worlds interpretation"](https://www.forbes.com/sites/chadorzel/2016/01/05/what-the-many-worlds-interpretation-of-quantum-physics-really-means/#27173be01102)
1.4.12.1. Con: If God were immanent and transcendent, he would exist in all of them simultaneously.
1.4.12.2. Con: The existence of an infinite number of universes doesn't imply that anything you can think of has to exist in one of the universes. For example, you can have an infinite number of universes where each of the universes is identical, except for the position of a single particle. Such an infinite set of universes would not have a universe with God in it.
1.4.12.2.1. Con: The multiverse above with a single particle moved is a very large, finite set of universes, not an infinite set.
1.4.12.2.1.1. Con: It would indeed be an infinite set. There is [an infinity of available places](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Three-dimensional_space) for the particle to be at.
1.4.12.2.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.1.1.1.1.1.3.2.
1.4.12.2.3. Con: The existence of infinte universes just moves the problem from one universe to many without providing an actual solution to the dilemma
1.4.12.3. Con: An infinite number of universes is not a consistently rational idea and thus not possible. There may be multiple, but not infinite universes. If there were infinite universes there would also be a universe where there are not, there would be a universe that consumes all other universes and already has done so. The existence of multiple universes ensures that there are not multiple universes and therefore impossible.
1.4.12.4. Pro: As there is an infinitude of universes, there may be an infinitude of Gods, or, Creators of these infinitude of universes.
1.4.12.4.1. Pro: There could be a hierarchy of them with a Higher Intelligence created by an even Higher Intelligence who is in turn created by a still Higher Intelligence & so on & on ad infinitum.
1.4.12.5. Con: The many-worlds interpretation of quantum mechanics describes universes branching off by natural, material, causal events, into two universes with the same history but a different future. A supernatural and eternal God cannot be created by such events.
