1.4.25.6. Con: "Existence" is absolutely a finite notion. God, by definition is an infinite notion \(the definition being taken from the ontological "proof", which is not a proof but provides a definition of God: "the highest conceivable entity"\). In this case the whole idea of God falling within some finite, falsifiable system of proof/disproof is meaningless.
1.4.26. Con: Philosophical arguments support the existence of god, the non-existence of god, and the concept that any attempt to prove or disprove the existence of god is futile. Therefore, philosophical arguments are not a reliable source which can be used to support the existence of god.
1.4.26.1. Pro: If god created humans and all that humans are conscious of, it is fair to reason that he may have allowed for no manner by which his existence could be either proven or disproven by the humans he created.
1.4.26.2. Pro: Philosophical arguments can support anything that you want to be true by using the right words and rhetoric. Masters in such discussions in ancient Greece were called [sophists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sophist).
1.4.26.2.1. Pro: In the logical arguments for God's existence, God is always defined such that the conclusion of the argument can be ''God exists''.
1.4.26.2.2. Con: Philosophical arguments are often confused with rhetorical arguments. Sophists were masters of rhetoric, they were not philosophers. Rhetoric can be used to support unsubstantiated claims through use of fallacy, philosophy contrarily relies on internal and external consistency, and therefore excludes fallacy.
1.4.26.3. Pro: More things are plausible than true. Supporting something with a philosophical argument is not the same as establishing its likelihood of actually being true.
1.5. Con: Empirical evidence indicates the non-existence of God.
1.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.7.
1.5.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.
1.5.3. Con: Most empirical evidence against God derives from an absence of observations of God. A lack of identified evidence is not a positive argument for the non-existence of God.
1.5.3.1. Con: Lack of evidence for a claim, when evidence for that claim is actively being sought, is indeed an argument for the opposite.
1.5.3.1.1. Con: Claiming that God does not exist because we have not found evidence for his existence is committing the fallacy of [arguing from ignorance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argument_from_ignorance).
1.5.3.1.2. Con: An all-powerful God by definition would be capable of keeping their existence hidden, assuming that doing so is in the best interests of ultimate morality.
1.5.3.1.3. Con: A claim can be deemed irrational, but without falsifiability it can not be claimed false.
1.5.3.2. Pro: Consider the claim, "There are an even number of electrons in the universe." If there is no good reason to believe this, it would be absurd to then conclude that "There are an odd number of electrons in the universe."
1.5.3.3. Pro: Seeing as science never answers a question to it's full extent, every conclusion opens a new question ad infinitum. In this sense, it is impossible for science to prepotently disregard that God may exist within infinity.
1.5.3.3.1. Pro: Science disregards many claims, due to the existence of better explanations. In fact science only regards claims when there is evidence in the first place.
1.5.3.4. Con: The burden of proof for any non-trivial claim rests on the claimant. A claimant who asserts that "the moon is made of marshmallow" should not expect readers to accept the premise without supporting evidence.
1.5.3.5. Pro: Absence of evidence does not entail evidence of absence.
1.5.3.5.1. Pro: Elementary particles existed apart from us being able to generate the evidence.
1.5.3.6. Con: Many of the empirical pieces of evidence of the non-existence of God is derived from instances where there should be divine intervention.
1.5.3.6.1. Con: This claim is subjective, and assumes that the claimant can accurately identify when divine intervention should take place.
1.5.3.6.1.1. Con: A God that is good and all powerful cannot allow suffering, there should therefore be intervention and there isn't in very numerous cases where it is objectively necessary.
1.5.3.6.1.1.1. Con: A God that is all-good must necessarily bring about the greatest good. Personal difficulty and trials are known to build character and foster personal growth, and may be better for the individual in the long run than deliverance from all suffering.
1.5.3.6.1.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.1.1.7.
1.5.3.6.1.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.3.5.1.5.2.
1.5.3.6.1.1.3. Con: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.3.5.1.
1.5.3.6.2. Pro: -> See 1.5.3.6.1.1.
1.5.3.7. Pro: The evidence of the non-existence of God is relative to our time, it may be the case in the future the evidence prove this thesis as incorrect.
1.5.3.7.1. Con: Both the arguments and the counter-arguments on God has been around for thousands of years.
1.5.3.7.2. Pro: Empirical evidence once indicated that the earth was flat.
1.5.3.8. Pro: We have been unable to measure or observe God as they are largely not perceivable with our senses.
1.5.4. Con: Omniscience implies that God knows what we don't know, omnipotence includes the capacity to withhold evidence, and goodness suggests that God's actions are motivated by good things that we are likely unaware of from our point of view. Thus, if God exists, a lack of scientific evidence to support that existence indicates that there is a good reason for God to withhold that kind of evidence from us.
1.5.4.1. Con: Omnipotence / Omniscience are more of an idea. The terms hold no value until God has proven to exist by other means.
1.5.4.2. Pro: God may actively remove or otherwise manipulate empirical evidence of his existence to maintain the need for faith.
1.5.4.3. Con: This argument takes as given that God exists and that there is a lack of evidence for him, so has no bearing on either of those issues and is irrelevant.
1.5.4.4. Con: Withholding information in this manner is deceptive and thus can be classified as lying. In ideologies involving an omniscient, omnipotent and omnibenevolent god, such as Christianity, it is often taught that lying is fundamentally bad. This would make god hypocritical and thus not omnibenevolent.
1.5.5. Con: Since evolution appears to be linear - with greater ability arising as time passes - it is likely that the universe contains higher forms of life than humans - gods, if not God. Projecting this still forward suggests that evolution may be destined toward a 'singularity of self awareness'.
1.5.5.1. Con: Evolution is not as teleological as this claim presents it to be. If you look at the [Galapagos finches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darwin%27s_finches) their beak size oscillates between small and large, in a generation, depending on climate conditions. That is the way evolution works it is not ability being increased but adaption to environment.
This evolve towards godhood is a strange form of god. it is not a god that creates the universe rather it is created by it. It is not a first cause conceptualisation of god.
1.5.5.1.1. Con: Repeated adaptation to environmental conditions seems to increase ability in the long term - single cell to multicellular, chimps to humans. Evolution does swing back and forth, but the overall history of life on earth seems to indicate a linear progression. This can still be viewed as a first cause form of god because the principles producing self aware life - and guiding evolutionary processes at the chemical level - appear to be independently present precursors of life - or God.
1.5.5.2. Con: The idea that evolution provides greater ability as time passes is known as orthogenesis. This idea \(beyond certain localised exceptions\) was abandoned by mainstream biologists in the 1940s - see [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthogenesis)
1.5.5.2.1. Con: Orthogenesis was abandoned as a concept because of its connotations - it implied a supernatural force, which is a priori anathema to hyper-rationalists - not because it is logically unsound. The idea behind orthogenesis is that genetic mutations are rarely 'lost' - they merely become inactive within the DNA strand. This means that a creature with 100 million years of evolution behind it will have greater complexity - whether manifested or not - than something with only 10 million years.
1.5.5.2.1.1. Con: Other reasons orthogenesis was abandoned include growing evidence of instances of [regressive evolution](https://news.nationalgeographic.com/2016/10/reverse-evolution-explained-hagfish-penguins-snakes-science/)
1.5.5.2.1.1.1. Con: There are only a small handful of examples of regressive evolution compared to millions of examples for progressive evolution. Additionally, while evolution does tick back and forth in response to environmental conditions in the short term, over the longer term \(say \>1 million years\) and when examined over multiple species, there is more evidence for an overall increase in biological ability than for the opposite.
1.5.5.2.2. Con: The 1940's were a highly biased time in human history, and scientists are human beings - subject to cultural winds. The hyper-rationalism of the age, which also contributed ideas such as eugenics, contributed to a disdain for anything to do with a higher power. What mainstream scientists thought \(and think today\) is a matter of social consensus, not logical coherence. Until a decade or so ago, plenty of mainstream scientists were of the \(published\) opinion that climate change was a hippie hoax.
1.5.5.2.3. Con: Orthogenesis is *one* of the scientific theories that argues for a linear progression in long term evolution. It was contentious because of its presumption that there was innate 'driving force' that produced this linear progression. The absence of proof for that innate force is what weakens the argument for Orthogenesis. This, however, does nothing to weaken the statement that there is an observed linear trend in evolution over the long term - single celled to multi cellular, apes to humans.
1.5.5.2.4. Con: This argument is a heuristic version of the argument from authority fallacy. Unanimity on a subject in a field does not determine correctness of a statement, rather, a discussion of the facts and theories does.
1.5.5.2.5. Pro: Evolution has a form of \(abstract\) [tree](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tree_structure?wprov=sfla1). [Phylogenetic tree in wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree?wprov=sfla1)
1.5.5.3. Pro: The presence of self aware life is evidence that the fundamental principles of the universe a\) can either generate self aware life through recombination b\) are themselves a manifestation of self aware life.
1.5.5.4. Con: Since humans are the "highest" known life forms, it is \(by your argument\) unlikely that God exists, that his existence would be fundamental, that He would be omnibenevolent, that He would be omnipresent, infinitely just or omnipotent.
1.5.5.5. Con: This merely moves the goalposts of what a god is and defines it as anything that is sufficiently more advanced than one's self.
1.5.6. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.3.3.2.
1.5.7. Pro: It is more likely that people invented the idea of God, than that he actually exists.
1.5.7.1. Con: Whether people invented the idea of God is irrelevant to whether God exists. If someone invented the idea of a dog, that would not make dogs exist any less. What matters for whether we should believe whether there is a God are the merits of the individual arguments, not the history or origin of the ideas or arguments.
1.5.7.1.1. Con: Since the existence of God is not falsifiable, having concrete evidence that someone wrote scripture they considered to be false would be valuable. If we had that evidence we should reasonably doubt the existence of the God referenced by the invented scripture, which may be as strong a statement as we can make given that the existence of God is not falsifiable.
1.5.7.2. Pro: Religion formed naturally from [hyperactive agency detection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agent_detection#Role_in_religion) - a tendency to attribute natural or unexplained events to a sentient agent \(in this case, God\).
1.5.7.2.1. Con: Studies have shown that, while believers in superstition and the paranormal [do show hyperactive agency detection](http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1053810013000974), believers in classical religion don't. In fact, some studies have found that atheists and skeptics often have [underactive agency detection](http://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0130422), meaning they deny the existence of agency even when it is actually there.
1.5.7.2.2. Con: -> See 1.4.7.3.1.7.2.
1.5.7.2.3. Con: -> See 1.4.7.3.1.7.3.
1.5.7.2.4. Pro: Psychological studies indicate people have tendency to shift responsibility to somebody else when many people are present. The God could be a target for such shift when no other target is available. [Groupthink in wikipedia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Groupthink?wprov=sfla1), [Diffusion of responsibility](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diffusion_of_responsibility?wprov=sfla1)
1.5.7.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.7.3.1.7.
1.5.7.4. Pro: Humans are likely to judge incorrectly when they want something to exist and are likely to anthropomorphize. Human error is relevant to the discussion.
1.5.7.4.1. Pro: The concept 'God' is a nothing more than a nature worship, but developed over thousands of years. This idea was able to spread due to people seeking the hope in the most horrible circumstances.
1.5.7.5. Pro: Non-human animals don't manifest a belief in God
1.5.7.5.1. Con: Theists largely agree there is a supernatural \(or, at least spiritual\) distinction between non-human animals and humans. For Christians, the Bible makes a clear distinction between man and animals: in the first letter to the Corinthians: "All flesh is not the same flesh, but there is one kind of flesh of men, another flesh of beasts, another of fish, another of birds" \([1 Corinthians 15:39](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1+Corinthians+15&version=NIV)\)
1.5.7.5.2. Con: Non-human animals lack the unique reasoning abilities of humans.
1.5.7.6. Pro: It is natural for any society to invent the concept of a God as a protector and to ensure divine justice. In a patriarchal society, this God would be an omnipotent father figure; the fact that God is male suggests God is a construction.
1.5.7.7. Pro: Modern religions have arisen from human superstition or misunderstanding of phenomena.[Cargo Cults](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cargo_cult)
1.5.7.7.1. Con: Cargo cults make runways and radios and reenact the movements of ground personnel. Their rituals are based on things that exist and events that really happened. Just because they are unable to summon an air drop at their command, does not mean cargo never arrived or that it will never do so again or that it doesn't exist.
1.5.7.7.2. Con: The origins of specific modern Religions and the origin of the Belief in Gods existence are separate questions. Deism for example is a belief in God without Religon. There also is natural theology.
1.5.7.8. Pro: The concept of God has a mundane explanation as a consequence of human desire for power. When individuals with conflicting desires form groups, the group assigns an individual with infinite power representing their shared goals in an attempt to dominate all others.
1.5.7.8.1. Pro: There is also a precedent for this behaviour from the social structures of our genetic ancestors as [primates](https://aeon.co/essays/how-monotheists-modelled-god-on-a-harem-keeping-alpha-male) dominated by a single alpha male - In The Naked Ape \(1967\), the zoologist Desmond Morris wrote that religion’s ‘extreme potency’ is simply a ‘measure of the strength of our fundamental biological tendency, inherited directly from our monkey and ape ancestors, to submit ourselves to an all-powerful, dominant member of the group’. Hector Garcia's [Alpha God](https://www.amazon.co.uk/Alpha-God-Psychology-Religious-Oppression/dp/1633880206) explores this connection in depth.
1.5.7.8.2. Pro: As Freud points out in [The Future of an Illusion](https://www.free-ebooks.net/ebook/The-future-of-illusion/pdf?dl&preview) \(p9-10\) this relationship to reality in the face of individual powerlessness is not a surprising one "It has an infantile prototype, of which it is in fact only the continuation. For once before one has found oneself in a similar state of helplessness: as a small child, in relation to one's parents. One had reason to fear them, and especially one's father; and yet one was sure of his protection against the dangers one knew."
1.5.7.8.3. Pro: Nietzsche argues in [The Genealogy of Morality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On_the_Genealogy_of_Morality) that the origin of Christian values is a rebellion of the oppressed against their oppressors by asserting power and value to their own way of life as slaves. An example that shows this is the statement "the meek shall inherit the earth". To further own this state of slavery a new master created in God, removed power from their real-life oppressors and then asserted that this new master also had power over them as well. [Here](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ti9zdpLlXf0) is a good explanation.
1.5.7.8.3.1. Con: The Geneology of Morality is a moral argument and does not address the existence of God
1.5.7.8.3.2. Con: Nietsche's writings suggest that arguments for the nonexistence of God are based on man's assumption that since God is unattainable by man, God cannot exist.
1.5.7.8.3.3. Pro: Nietsche's writings suggest that [God is dead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/God_is_dead) \(thus ceasing to exist\).
1.5.7.8.4. Con: This argument could be represented: 

1. If groups of people assign an individual that represents their shared goals with infinite power, then belief in God is a consequence of the human desire for power. 
2. Groups of people assign an individual that represents their shared goals with infinite power.
3. Therefore, belief in God is a consequence of the human desire for power. 
4. Therefore, God does not exist!?!

\(4\) in no way follows as a conclusion and is a non-sequitur.
1.5.7.8.4.1. Con: The argument is that belief in God as a big man in space who cares about humans and where they put their sexual organs is more likely explainable by human psychology and not a result of our own insight into the unknown.
1.5.7.8.5. Con: This argument, rather than engaging with the arguments for God’s existence or discussing what counts as warranted belief in God, seeks to dismiss belief in the thesis “God exists” by explaining how it originated. Thus, this argument commits the [genetic](https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=yv8L57GAVQ0) fallacy.
1.5.7.8.5.1. Con: Many of the attributes assigned to the concept ‘God’ - sentience, masculinity, have no basis except for the fact that people believe them. Thus how they came to believe them is important when it is the only factor which supports those assertions. Unless any other evidence for those attributes is given, they can be considered irrelevant. All arguments equating a ‘cause’ to ‘God’ need to provide additional justification for those extra attributes to be seriously considered.
1.5.7.8.6. Pro: It is likely that the concept of a God arose from the necessity of civilizations for stability and uniformity, as civilizations which had a God gave meaning to the people, also allowing cooperation between them.
1.5.7.9. Pro: The [Meme hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meme) is a more plausible explanation for the propagation of the God concept that his truthful existence.
1.5.7.10. Con: It is impossible to define "likeliness"—probability—in a context which cannot be duplicated. There is only one possible context for the reality or non-reality of God, and thus statistical comparisons to generate probabilities is impossible.
1.5.7.10.1. Con: The statement "there is only one possible context for the reality or non-reality of god" is false.  Mistaken belief can be understood and explained in the fields of Psychology, Neuroscience, Anthropology, and Biology.
1.5.7.11. Pro: [Historical Criticism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historical_criticism) is the multidisciplinary study of scripture as an academic subject. It demonstrates the human origins of religion by examining sources. For instance, the much older Epic of Gilgamesh was used as source material for, or substantially influenced the Bible, at [multiple points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epic_of_Gilgamesh#Relationship_to_the_Bible): the Genesis account of Noah and the flood uses the same events in the same order as Gilgamesh.
1.5.7.12. Con: It is unlikely that people who have such complex minds to invent deities or discuss topics such as the existence or nonexistence of God came to be without being created by God.
1.5.8. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.
1.5.9. Con: The evidence might originate from the enemy of the God, or might be placed as a temptation.
1.5.9.1. Pro: One of the crucial concepts in Bible is concept of snake which deceives Eve, and Satan as the tempter. It is possible he would provide false evidence to trick people
1.5.9.2. Con: Any evidence that does not suit the believer in God might be interpreted as false evidence provided by enemy of the God.
1.5.9.3. Con: Most definitions of a god see it as the creator of everything. The "enemy of god" would be created by god itself. Therefore, the god would hide itself and not actually be hidden by someone else.
1.5.10. Pro: The universe and nature show all the characteristics we would expect if there were no God.
1.5.10.1. Pro: Order and structure in the universe emereges when spans of time are so large that they permit the inefficient, non orderly things to weed themselves out of existence.
1.5.10.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.1.3.1.4.1.
1.5.10.3. Con: There is positive evidence for the existence of God as the cause of the order and complexity of nature.
1.5.10.3.1. Con: Because an event is rare doesn't mean it is impossible.
1.5.10.3.2. Con: That the universe appears ordered is at best evidence that the universe isn't chaotic. It does not prove that a god exists.
1.5.10.3.3. Con: Order is an emergent property to which humans ascribe meaning \(those emergent properties which are beneficial to humans are order\). It is a truism that in a universe where humans exist there will be phenomena that are beneficial to humans. This is in no way empirical proof for a god or any other mythological figure.
1.5.10.3.4. Pro: In nature, chaos is without order, yet the universe is ordered and complex.
1.5.10.3.4.1. Con: If we look to the clouds we can see faces and familiar shapes.  This is pure chaos, yet we create order in it.
If I throw stuff together in random, something that appears to be order will appear, that does not mean it was meant to exist.  Nor does it mean that our universe could not exist in a completely different form, as such order vs. random is not something we can confirm.
1.5.10.3.5. Con: Order is the result of randomness given meaning.  There are many things in nature that do not fit the prescribed order we have presented and labeled.  We are constantly relabeling, reordering, and re-describing nature as we observe it.
1.5.10.3.6. Con: Complexity is not evidence of creation.
A lot of created objects are extremely simple. Paper clip, post it notes etc. As opposed to extremely complex natural phenomenon, a dandelion seed pod.
Order is also not evidence of creation.
A Jackson pollock painting is extremely disordered as opposed to the growth rings of a tree.
1.5.10.3.6.1. Con: The fact that some created objects are simple does not prove that complex objects need not be. The example of "natural" complex objects does not provide logical evidence against creation.
1.5.10.3.7. Con: This is a fallacy called "personal incredulity". Complex subjects like biological evolution through natural selection require some amount of understanding before one is able to make an informed judgement about the subject at hand; this fallacy is usually used in place of that understanding.
1.5.10.3.8. Pro: A painting is evidence of a painter. A song is evidence of a composer. A house is evidence of a carpenter, roofer, plumber, electrician etc. The universe itself and everything in it is evidence for a creator.
1.5.10.3.8.1. Con: This is not true for non-human made objects. For example for organisms the evidence of their creator is natural selection.
1.5.10.3.8.1.1. Con: If there is a creator, it would work through the process of natural selection to create. This argument would be like saying “paint colours are evidence of differing wavelengths of light, not of a creator”.
1.5.10.3.8.1.1.1. Con: The claim that god works through the process of natural selection does not prove the existence of a god because by definition natural processes do not require a god. A god needs to demonstrate supernatural powers to qualify as such. When a painter creates a painting using a natural process like combining colors of differing wavelengths of light, he can claim to be a creator, but cannot claim to be a god because there is nothing supernatural about the process.
1.5.10.3.8.2. Con: The relationship between a painter and his painting is fully known. We can prove that the painter exists, and we can know fully that the painting was made by a painter by tautology. There is no such tautology or implied relationship between the universe and a god.
1.5.10.3.8.2.1. Con: The relationship between a painting and a painter is implied by the properties of the painting, even when the painter is unknown.
1.5.10.3.8.2.1.1. Con: Properties of nature point to a natural cause not to an intelligent creator.
1.5.10.3.8.3. Con: Unlike a painting there is little evidence that the universe has any overarching purpose.
1.5.10.3.8.3.1. Pro: The inherent presence of purpose is necessary for any kind of creator to be involved. If there was a purpose at the origin of the universe, that's the fulcrum that the religious or creator argument rests. If there is no purpose, and it is a random event, that proves that there is no creator, simply an event that has chain reacted.
1.5.10.3.8.3.2. Pro: Order, or its absence, alone is not evidence of intent and thus a creator. A [Jackson Pollock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jackson_Pollock) painting can be less ordered than spilt paint.
1.5.10.3.8.3.3. Pro: Complexity is also not evidence of intent or a creator. A stick figure can be less complex than spilt paint.
1.5.10.3.8.4. Con: If a house is "evidence of a carpenter, roofer, plumber, electrician etc", a whole team of people, then a neighborhood or town or city is evidence of a large number of teams, individuals, and organizations of people building these things - often without collaborating or even communicating with one another. The existence of the universe, with its multitudes of different things, is therefore not evidence of any one specific creator, any more than it is evidence of multitudes of creators or forces.
1.5.10.3.8.5. Con: A stalagmite is not evidence of a stalagmite maker. It is evidence of a natural process.
1.5.10.3.8.5.1. Pro: The same can be said of a river, a mountain, a canyon, a forest, a volcano, planets, the stars even life. They are all evidence of natural processes.
1.5.10.3.8.6. Con: If the universe needs a god to create it, then even God needs a creator. Conversely, if God can exist without a creator, so can the universe.
1.5.10.3.8.6.1. Con: Only things that begin to exist require a creator. The classical definition of God holds that He is eternal and uncreated. Any conception of a god who was created fails to meet the standard of God used in this discussion.
1.5.10.3.8.6.1.1. Con: The universe could also be eternal and/or uncreated in which case no god is necessary.
1.5.10.3.8.6.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.3.3.2.1.1.1.
1.5.10.3.8.6.1.1.2. Con: Philosophical evidence indicates that the universe cannot be past-eternal.
1.5.10.3.8.6.1.1.2.1. Con: The same evidence would then show that God also cannot be past-eternal. Either nothing is eternal or some things can be eternal. If some thing\(s\) can be eternal then the universe has to be eternal by its own definition.
1.5.10.3.8.6.1.1.2.1.1. Con: The evidence provided specifically attacks the time-dimension of the universe. As classical theism holds that God is timeless, and envisions Him as the creator of time, God is immune from an infinite regress of temporal events.
1.5.10.3.8.6.1.1.2.1.2. Con: It does not follow that because some thing\(s\) can be eternal that the universe must be eternal.
1.5.10.3.8.6.1.1.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.3.3.4.4.5.5.1.1.1.4.1.1.1.2.
1.5.10.3.8.7. Con: This argument confuses the content with the container \(the universe\), for which we have no other example to compare with.
1.5.10.4. Con: The Universe "and nature" show all the characteristics we would expect if there was a God \(parent claim is meaningless\).
1.5.10.4.1. Con: What "characteristics of the universe we would expect if there was a God" is elusive.
1.5.11. Pro: The God claim has never produced any discovery, testable theory or improved in any way our understanding of the natural world.
1.5.11.1. Con: Early scientists, Copernicus, Bacon, Newton, Kepler, Galileo, and others studied the universe precisely because they believed that a rational, logical God had created it according to principles that could be discovered. This led to an astonishing number of discoveries, and the development of the scientific method.
1.5.11.1.1. Con: The scientific method, and logic more generally, are independent from the \(non\)existence of God.
1.5.11.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.4.2.6.
1.5.11.1.2. Con: People can be inspired by things that do not exist or are not true, such as ideals, dreams or prejudice.
1.5.11.1.3. Con: Newton's claim that God was necessary for the existence of stable orbits in the solar system was proven false: [Principia](https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Page%3ANewton's_Principia_\(1846\).djvu/510) vs [modern simulations](https://www.astrobio.net/mars/solar-system-simulation-reveals-planetary-mystery/).
1.5.11.2. Pro: The accepted textbooks on God are the Bible and Qur’an. We all have access to them.  These books state, but don’t prove that God exists. Academics and clerics don’t have magic formulas that turn books into factual proof. They just interpret words. They can’t claim that God does exist until God’s existence is a proven fact. They don’t have proof. Without proof, the existence of God is no more than a theory put forward to explain Universal phenomena. Scientists too are working on such a theory.
1.5.11.3. Con: The debate over the existence of God is irrelevant when it comes to the study of the natural world. There is no such thing as atheist or theist science. There is only science.
1.5.11.4. Pro: [Young Earth Creationism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Young_Earth_creationism), is a testable claim that has been demonstrated false.
1.5.11.4.1. Pro: The history of [life](http://www.bbc.co.uk/nature/history_of_the_earth) on Earth began about 3.8 billion years ago, initially with single-celled prokaryotic cells, such as bacteria.
1.5.11.4.2. Pro: The [earliest known](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pottery#History) human pottery is approximately twice as old as the age of the Earth predicted by Young Earth Creationists.
1.5.12. Con: It is simply factually incorrect that there is "empirical evidence" that "indicates the non-existence of God".
1.5.12.1. Pro: There is no empirical evidence that God does not exist. There can't be. The concept of God is unfalsifiable.
1.5.12.1.1. Con: There are many concepts of God that are falsifiable and which have been falsified, indicating that this is a concept that likely does not correspond to reality.
1.5.12.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.3.4.
1.5.12.1.2. Con: Concepts of God have been falsified many times leading to a consequent retreat to ever more unfalsifiable versions.
1.5.12.2. Pro: The reliability of empirical evidence cannot be proved empirically and to assume it can is to reason in a circle.
1.5.12.2.1. Con: The reliability of empirical evidence based reasoning is evidenced by new evidence confirming that reliability \(not by the assumption that it is reliable\). It is therefore not circular reasoning.
1.5.12.3. Pro: Looking for empirical evidence for God is a category mistake.  Empirical evidence is something that you can test, observe, and repeat. You can not do this with an immaterial God.
1.5.12.3.1. Pro: Since God is a spiritual entity, there is enough evidence for his existence in the fact that we feel his presence in our hearts and acknowledge it as such through terms like the Holy Ghost. Feeling him is tantamount to seeing him.
1.5.12.3.1.1. Con: The "feeling" of god is anecdotal.

Anecdotal evidence is a place to start a hypothesis, but if it cannot be tested and validated it holds no philosophical or scientific weight. This is a known logical fallacy known as [Hitchens's Razor](https://rationalwiki.org/wiki/Logical_razor#Hitchens.27s_razor):
"What can be asserted without evidence can be dismissed without evidence"
1.5.12.3.1.1.1. Con: This argument is irrelevant because of its materialistic or positivist bias: it assumes evidence can only be physical and anything else is anecdotal. This needs to be proven before it can be used as evidence. Stating it is not enough.
1.5.12.3.1.1.1.1. Con: It's not a matter of bias, but rather to work inside the realm of possibilities. It could be stated "I feel this shouldn't be proved" as a response to your argument and it would be just as true as the "I feel god exists" argument because we can't discuss objective truth over feelings. Note that if an assertion is not proven to be false doesn't imply on it being proven true.
1.5.12.3.1.2. Con: This claim assumes that "everyone feels God's presence" is a fact when the feeling of God's presence is not clearly defined.
1.5.12.3.1.3. Con: Deriving a conclusion about the existence of a spiritual entity from a premise about the existence of another spiritual entity is circular and entails infinite regress.
1.5.12.3.1.4. Con: Many others do not share this subjective feeling of "God's presence," and may in fact have equally strong subjective feelings that God is an illusion or fantasy. Neither subjective feeling counts as evidence for or against the existence of God.
1.5.12.3.2. Pro: Positive evidence is not needed for belief in God.
1.5.12.3.2.1. Con: Belief is subjective, and is not relevant to a discussion regarding the objective ontology of an entity \(e.g. God\).
1.5.12.3.2.2. Con: Belief in the existence of God can be, and often is, held with no justification in regards to the truth of that idea. That is however not a reasonable stance and does not provide justification for the existence of God.
1.5.12.3.2.3. Con: For the belief in God to be justified, it needs something that justifies it. The evidence is one of the grounds for justification that theists lack.
1.5.12.3.3. Pro: Empirical evidence could not prove the existence nor the nonexistence of God, it could only prove the existence of physical things that you could feel and experience such as tables and chairs and the wind. Love or friendship are examples of things that cannot be proven with these methods.
1.5.12.3.3.1. Con: Yet there is empirical evidence for friendship, often found in the actions of friends. Same goes for love. The existence of evidence contradicting the expected actions of a friend or a god are evidence against their existence.
1.5.12.3.3.2. Con: While it might be true that it is impossible to empirically know or prove the existence of love between people, the behavior of the suspected lovers is knowable, and behavior is commonly accepted as evidence of internal emotional states.
1.5.12.3.4. Pro: An inmaterial God cannot interact with physical world. There is not empirical evidence for abilities of interacting with the physical world without being a material thing made by atoms. You can't interact with atoms if you are not made by elementary particles.
1.5.12.3.4.1. Con: Photons \(and subatomic particles\) interact with the physical world and are not made of atoms.
1.5.12.3.4.2. Con: [Psychosomatic reactions](https://www.answers.com/Q/What_is_psychosomatic_response\]) are a material body's reaction to an immaterial thought.
1.5.12.3.4.3. Con: Gravity is a force and it does not seem to have a material existence. It is action at a distance. We don't know what gravity is but we know that it is.
1.5.12.3.4.4. Con: The classical God is claimed to have multiple effects on the physical world, therefore observable evidence of its existence is rightfully expected.
1.5.12.3.5. Con: -> See 1.5.12.3.4.4.
1.5.12.3.6. Pro: If we accept that empirical evidence can conclude that God doesn't exist in this way, then we would also have to accept that we should conclude the immaterial mind doesn't exist, even though we know it does exist.
1.5.12.3.7. Con: There is no distinction between the idea of immaterial and nonexistent. The immaterial \(concepts, ideas, descriptors, abstractions\) is at best descriptive of things, but cannot rationally be claimed as existent.
1.5.12.3.7.1. Con: Being subjective does not exempt something from existence. Love, courage, skepticism, doubt, etc. are all said to exist because they are known and understood properties of the human experience
1.5.12.3.7.2. Con: Theism is a set of rational claims to God's existence
1.5.12.3.7.3. Con: One can rationally come to differing conclusions. Only a person with the ability to access all the information in the universe could come to a singular absolute truth with no alternative possibilities
1.5.12.3.7.4. Con: If "immaterial" equals "nonexistent", then only physical objects exist. This is an absurdly restrictive meaning of "existence", as it excludes, among other things, all thought and emotion.
1.5.12.3.7.5. Con: An idea can be, and in fact many times is, transformed into a material object. For example, the idea of a flying machine ultimately turned into an airplane. Therefore ideas exist as they're the proven source of later built objects.
1.5.12.3.8. Pro: Logical positivists argue that "one can not speak about causality on a meta-physical level". Therefore this argument is invalid.
1.5.12.3.8.1. Con: [Logical Positivism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logical_positivism)'s central tenet has been shown to be self-contradictory. The philosophy holds that "only statements verifiable through empirical observation are cognitively meaningful." \(See link.\) 

As that statement is not verifiable through empirical observation, the Logical Positivist cannot hold that statement to be meaningful.
1.5.12.3.8.2. Pro: [Logical positivists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logical_positivism) would also argue that such concept of God \(purely metaphysical\) is empirically unverifiable and therefore meaningless.
1.5.12.3.9. Con: If the universe is stated to have been created by god \(which is how god is defined in this conversation\), and the universe is material, this claim cannot be logically sustained. 

The universe is stated to be material evidence of god, redefining god so there can be no material evidence for a god constitutes an admission that the god, as defined here, doesn't exist. 

In this context the "immaterial" and the non-existent are the same thing.
1.5.12.4. Con: Different assumptions about God may be falsifiable, leading to conclusion that he does not exist.
1.5.12.4.1. Con: This conclusion would be premature. By disproving a related assumption one does not disprove the main premise, however removal of assumption may modify that premise
1.5.13. Con: Against all odds we exist as a part of a perfect system.
1.5.13.1. Con: Assuming that God exists does not change those odds, being therefore not even an explanation for the existence of this system.
1.5.13.2. Con: Since the outcome is known \(we do exist\), the probability accounts only for our ignorance of how it came to pass. It is then assumed from ignorance of cause that God is the cause. Ignorance is however neither reason nor evidence for the existence of God.
1.5.13.2.1. Con: Our "ignorance of cause" is not total. We inhabit a universe that shows every indication of being designed. In every single instance when we see such a system and the cause is well-known, a designer is responsible. It is reasonable to infer a designer of the universe based on that experience. This inference is not an argument from ignorance, but from knowledge.
1.5.13.3. Con: -> See 1.4.6.3.3.2.1.1.1.1.
1.5.13.4. Pro: Quantum physics has provided us empirical evidence suggesting the complete opposite of this statement. Against all odds all things should not exist and yet they do in a system of perfect balance.
1.5.13.4.1. Con: This is purelly a misconception regarding the concept of observation in science which does not refer to a conscious observer, but merelly to interactions between things.
1.5.13.4.2. Con: Quantum mechanics predicts that matter can spontaneously come into existence.
1.5.13.4.2.1. Con: Quantum mechanics predicts that [conservation of energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservation_of_energy?wprov=sfla1) is a valid principle that the universe conforms to.
1.5.13.4.3. Con: -> See 1.5.13.2.
1.5.13.4.4. Con: -> See 1.4.6.3.3.2.1.1.1.1.
1.5.13.4.5. Pro: Quantum physics has proven we as observers play a role in the unfolding of our reality which matches the practice of prayer and meditation in spirituality.
1.5.13.4.5.1. Con: There are interpretations of quantum mechanics where observation doesn't play a special role. Even in interpretations where observation is considered special, the 'observer' doesn't need to have consciouness - machines can be 'observers' too.
1.5.14. Con: -> See 1.2.2.7.5.
1.5.15. Con: "Empirical evidence" can neither prove that God does or does not exist.
1.5.16. Con: A study MRI study was conducted which showed that an individual's brain "lit up" when they felt the holy ghost.[archive.sltrib.com](http://archive.sltrib.com/article.php?id=4617561&itype=CMSID)
1.5.17. Con: A God, as described in this discussion, may hide any evidence of His existence and still being part of the reality.
1.5.18. Con: Empirical evidence deals with physical reality. The concept of God is the concept of a non-physical being, so you shouldn't expect to prove the existence of God empirically. That being said, God's existence is, in fact, an empirically adequate claim.
1.5.19. Con: Empirical evidence cannot possibly indicate the non-existence of morality, rationality, beauty, and other metaphysical concepts such as a deity.
1.5.20. Con: If God created this reality as a test to judge our faith, then real evidence of his existence would be deliberately made hard if not impossible.
1.5.21. Pro: The burden of proof for God's existence has not been met.
1.5.21.1. Con: This does not prove that God does not exist.
1.5.21.1.1. Con: The claims under the main thesis \(God exists.\) do not need to prove that God doesn't exist. \([burden of proof](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burden_of_proof_\(philosophy\))\)
1.5.21.2. Con: No finite being that is not omniscient is competent to adjudicate whether or not the "burden of proof" for God's existence has or has not been met.
1.5.21.2.1. Con: Consistent application of this standard would result in humans not being able to know anything at all, so this standard is useless in practice.
1.5.21.2.2. Con: This argument is circular. It boils down to: The only way to properly assess the evidence for the existence of God, is if God shows me how it should be assessed.
1.5.21.3. Pro: Knowing/Proving something exists annuls belief.
1.5.21.3.1. Con: By that standard you know of no thing that exists. Because any claim about existence requires some faith. We could always live in a simulated reality for example.
1.5.21.3.1.1. Con: Anything that can be observed exists in at least some form or capacity, even if that existence is contained within a single mind.
1.5.21.3.1.2. Con: Faith as taken from a religious perspective is completely different from the philosophical faith that science refers to. The first one does not require empyrical evidence, the second one must provide it.
1.5.21.3.2. Con: Theoretical physicists, as well as Astro-physicists and anthropologists, would certainly take issue with this as they believe in many things that can not be known absolutely and the proof they bring is purely abstract or theoretical itself. Despite this, because it is “proven” in the abstract \(often mathematically\) they claim it is “known” but ultimately it boils down to “belief”. Good examples would be string theory or the Big Bang or the transition from hunter/gatherers to agriculturalists.
1.5.21.3.3. Con: God does not only want us to have faith that He exists but also faith that He is who He says He is \(good, trustworthy, praiseworthy\). Thus, even if we could prove God exists, we still need belief \(faith\) to trust He is always acting in our good, etc. \(He 11:6\).
1.5.21.4. Pro: If a critical variable or constant \(a [topological invariant](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topological_property)\) in the universe function is to be required one must prove the function fails to evaluate if the variable is withheld.
1.5.21.4.1. Pro: Adding variables with no impact on the result of a function to the function is equivalent to adding zero to the function.
1.5.21.4.2. Con: If one is the critical variable the impact to the universe function would be equivalent to multiplying by one because one is omnipresent in all topologies.
1.5.21.5. Con: The burden of proof for a universe created from nothing has not been met.
1.5.21.5.1. Con: Red Herring argument. this does not refute the original point.
1.5.21.5.1.1. Con: You could argue that the original argument is a red herring from the start. You can claim that the existence of the universe itself and the laws of cause and effect are enough evidence for an ultimate cause, i.e. God.
1.5.21.5.1.2. Con: This is an argument from fallacy, simply showing a fallacy within a sub-argument does not suffice to actually address any of the argument's merits.
1.5.21.6. Con: 'Burden of Proof' is a subjective term which is unknowable and impossible to evaluate it's criteria. Everyone has a different standard of proof. Objectively, we can only evaluate God's existence on available evidence, which is a different argument.
1.5.21.6.1. Con: If the question posed is looking for an objective answer, the criteria is a significant majority positive confirmation by scientific communities around the world based on a reproducible test. Therefore, the burden of proof has indeed not been met.
1.5.21.7. Pro: A metaphysical being has not yet been definitively observed, and therefore, has not yet been definitively proven to exist.
1.5.21.7.1. Con: Metaphysical suggests highly abstract & beyond nature so observation is not a possibility. If it were to be observed it could no longer be considered metaphysical and would certainly not qualify as deity. Rather, like all metaphysical matters, God is not known by direct observation but an observation of His signs and indications in the physical or “natural” world. That there is a physical world is itself proof of a metaphysical will which chos to bring it into existence from pure non-existence.
1.5.21.8. Con: Having no proof of God's nonexistence, at least his existence cannot be dismissed as a clear false.
1.5.21.8.1. Con: Red herring fallacy: This argument bears no direct relationship to its parent claim. The truth value of the parent claim is unaffected by this claim's truth value, so even when this line of reasoning is sound, it can't be used to prove its opposite proposition is true.
1.5.21.9. Con: On the other hand, the proof of God’s non-existence could only be based on reasoning which could be controversial, for non-existence is nothingness, which is intangible or non-physical & could never be seen or heard, i.e., if God were indeed non-existent no one would be able to know for sure as hard incontrovertible physical or experimental evidence proving it, which is nothingness, is an impossibility.
1.5.21.10. Con: The existence of a perfectly moral "lawgiver" is the only rational way to explain objective morality, thus giving proof of God \(or at least as close as you can get to "proof"\).
1.5.21.10.1. Con: Objective morality does not exist, so there is no need to explain it.
1.5.21.11. Con: Irrefutable proof will never be found - because free will to believe or not believe is intrinsic to being a person. It is our right to not believe as much as its our right to believe. This is not a place of enlightenment but it is a place where facility is there if chosen.
1.5.21.11.1. Con: Whether God's existence or nonexistence is more than a mere fact about the universe, and has any more impact on our free will and humanity than knowing whether the sky is green or blue, is an assertion that requires justification.
1.5.21.12. Con: "Burden of proof" is a term used purely for determining when evidence obliges a particular response. Usually the one asking the other person to change their behaviour or to take action \(eg. asking the state to punish someone\) carries the "burden of proof". Since this debate is only about the truth of the matter and is not about how people should act "burden of proof" is meaningless in this context.
1.5.21.13. Con: There is not supposed to be proof of Gods existence. That would negate the idea of faith, which is the very basis of Judeo-Christian religion.
1.5.21.14. Con: The burden is not on the greater being to justify itself to the lesser being. If God exists you do not have the right to demand he submit to your authentication.
1.5.21.14.1. Con: This argument [begs the question](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Begging_the_question), as it self-admittedly only applies if God exists.
1.5.21.14.2. Con: Assuming God demands submission is a human condition thrust upon others, by a specific religious doctrine. The statement in and of itself, demonstrates more about your belief system than it does anything for the existence of God.
1.5.21.14.3. Con: This argument is a red herring. The burden of proof is placed on those making the claim that a god exists, not the god itself. The reason that this is the case is that the burden of proof is the duty placed upon a party to prove or disprove a disputed claim and is placed on the party making the claim, in this case monotheists.

1.6.10. Con: The concept of God is intuitive and resides in the psychological commons. Religion, on the other hand, is an invention to portray a certain understanding of God and it is a human creation.
1.6.11. Con: In many communist countries, God is banned to control the masses. So the concept of an absence of God must have been intentionally invented by humans.
1.7. Con: The formation of an all powerful consciousness has an unimaginably low possibility when compared to the formation of known complex entities
1.7.1. Con: God was not formed but has always been.
1.7.1.1. Con: The parent claim is about Occamian priors, "having always existed" is just another addition to the complexity of the God hypothesis making it yet more improbable.
1.7.1.1.1. Con: The parent isn’t an additional explanation but a correction of a straw man. The grandparent claim is that God was formed which is highly unlikely because we don’t see complex things forming very often by natural processes. God isn’t believed to have formed by natural causes.
1.7.1.1.2. Con: “God always existed’ is a claim of equal if not more simplicity to the claim that a singularity was the uncaused cause of the universe. To assert that the cause of the universe is not supernatural is complicated because one would have to explain how a cause is not the effect of another cause, which is the natural order of all observed causes in history. It is straightforward to say that the uncaused cause is supernatural \(not natural or beyond natural\).
1.7.2. Con: This claim is a misunderstanding of the definition of God provided in the stem claim.
1.8. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.
